Andy Stoneham, a tall youth with sallow face and fuzzy beard, edged over closer and closer. &ldquo;Reckon you&rsquo;re orful stuck up this mawnin&rsquo;,&rdquo; he drawled. Molly looked at the bullet holes in the wall of the old store. She had seen them before, and long ago when she was ten she had stuck her finger in them and wondered about the battle that had been fought there once. &ldquo;Goin&rsquo; up to Flag, huh?&rdquo; &ldquo;Do you think I&rsquo;d dress up like this for West Fork?&rdquo; inquired Molly, loftily. &ldquo;Wal, you used to, didn&rsquo;t you? You shore look purty. But I can&rsquo;t see you&rsquo;ve any call to get uppish. I&rsquo;ve seen you in thet rig before, haven&rsquo;t I?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t remember, Andy.&rdquo; &ldquo;Then you&rsquo;ve got a darn short memory,&rdquo; replied Andy, bluntly. &ldquo;Didn&rsquo;t I take you to the last dance in thet dress?&rdquo; &ldquo;Did you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I shore did. An&rsquo; didn&rsquo;t I hug you in it?&rdquo; &ldquo;Did you?&rdquo; queried Molly, flippantly. &ldquo;You bet I did.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve forgotten. But I&rsquo;ve heard it said you&rsquo;re so big an&rsquo; awkward you have to hold on to a girl when you dance. Else you&rsquo;d fall down.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, how aboot kissin&rsquo; you, too? On the way to the dance an&rsquo; drivin&rsquo; home?&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, did you!&rdquo; retorted Molly, her face hot. Andy&rsquo;s voice carried rather far. &ldquo;An&rsquo; what did I do?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I figger thet you kissed me back an&rsquo; then slapped my face.&rdquo; &ldquo;Andy Stoneham, you&rsquo;re a liar about that first.&rdquo; &ldquo;Haw! Haw! ... Say, Molly, there&rsquo;s goin&rsquo; to be a dance next week.&rdquo; &ldquo;Where at?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hall&rsquo;s Mill. Come on an&rsquo; go.&rdquo; &ldquo;Andy, I don&rsquo;t like that place,&rdquo; returned Molly, regretfully. &ldquo;Besides, I wouldn&rsquo;t go with you, anyway.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you shore air gettin&rsquo; stuck up. An&rsquo; why not?&rdquo; &ldquo;Because of what you said&mdash;about huggin&rsquo; an&rsquo; kissin&rsquo; me.&rdquo; &ldquo;What of thet? I did an&rsquo; you liked it. Aw, you&rsquo;re funny. Haven&rsquo;t all the boys done the same?&rdquo; &ldquo;They have not,&rdquo; declared Molly. &ldquo;Who ever said such a thing?&rdquo; &ldquo;I heerd Sam Wise say it. An&rsquo; Bill Smith laughed, though he didn&rsquo;t say nothin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;So that&rsquo;s the kind of fellows you are!&rdquo; exclaimed Molly. &ldquo;Talk about a girl behind her back? ... To kiss an&rsquo; tell!&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, at thet we&rsquo;re not so gabby as your cowboy admirers from Pleasant Valley. Take thet red-headed cowpuncher. Accordin&rsquo; to his talk he&rsquo;s a tall fellar with gurls. He shore had you crazy aboot him.&rdquo; &ldquo;He did not,&rdquo; said Molly, hotly. &ldquo;Wal, you acted orful queer then. Danced all the time with him. An&rsquo; three times walked out under the pines. Aw, I watched you. An&rsquo; come Saturday night he was drinkin&rsquo; heah, an&rsquo; accordin&rsquo; to his talk he could have had a lot more than huggin&rsquo; an&rsquo; kissin&rsquo; from you, if he only got you alone.&rdquo; &ldquo;Andy Stoneham!&mdash;You let him talk that way aboot me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, why should I care? You&rsquo;ve shore been mean to me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why should you, indeed?&rdquo; replied Molly, coldly, and turned away. At that juncture a horseman rode up and his advent not only interrupted Molly&rsquo;s argument with her loquacious admirer, but had a decided quieting effect upon the other occupants of the porch. He was a lean range-rider, neither young nor old, and he fitted the hard country. His horse showed the dust and strain of long travel. &ldquo;Howdy, Seth,&rdquo; said old Enoch Summers, rubbing his bristled chin and stepping out. &ldquo;&rsquo;Pears like you been humpin&rsquo; it along. Whar you come from?&rdquo; &ldquo;Me an&rsquo; Arch Dunn just rode over from the Diamond,&rdquo; replied the other. Molly&rsquo;s attention quickened to interest at the mention of her brother. Seth Haverly was his boon companion and they had been up to something. &ldquo;Wal, thar&rsquo;s news stickin&rsquo; out all over you,&rdquo; drawled Summers. &ldquo;Reckon so.&rdquo; &ldquo;Git down an&rsquo; come in. Mebbe a drink wouldn&rsquo;t go bad.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nope. I&rsquo;m goin&rsquo; home an&rsquo; get a snack of grub.&rdquo; One by one the men on the porch joined Summers. The fact that Seth Haverly did not want a drink, as much as his arrival, interested them. Haverly had a still brown face and intent light eyes. &ldquo;Enoch, you know thet drift fence we been hearin&rsquo; aboot for the last year?&rdquo; he asked. &ldquo;Reckon I heerd the talk.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, it&rsquo;s more&rsquo;n talk now.&rdquo; &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t say?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yep. Me an&rsquo; Arch rode along it, for ten miles, I figger. Straight as a bee-line. New three-wire fence, an&rsquo; barbed at thet!&rdquo; &ldquo;What you say? Barbed!&rdquo; &ldquo;You bet.&rdquo; Silence greeted Seth&rsquo;s nonchalant affirmative. &ldquo;Arch had a hunch aboot this fence goin&rsquo; up,&rdquo; went on Haverly. &ldquo;An&rsquo; in Flag we found it was a fact.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, who&rsquo;s buildin&rsquo; it?&rdquo; &ldquo;Traft.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. He could afford it. Wal, what&rsquo;s his idee?&rdquo; &ldquo;It ain&rsquo;t very flatterin&rsquo; to West Fork,&rdquo; drawled Seth, with a grin. &ldquo;We heerd some things thet&rsquo;d be hard for you old cattle-nesters to swaller, if they&rsquo;re true. But me an&rsquo; Arch only had the word of some idle cowpunchers. We couldn&rsquo;t get any satisfaction from Traft&rsquo;s outfit. New foreman. Nephew from Missourie, we heerd. Tenderfoot, but I agree with Arch, who said he was no fool. Anyway, we asked him polite like: &lsquo;Say, mister, what&rsquo;s the idee of this drift fence?&rsquo;&mdash;An he looked me an&rsquo; Arch over an&rsquo; said, &lsquo;What do you suppose the idee is?&rsquo;&rdquo; &ldquo;Short an&rsquo; sweet!&rdquo; ejaculated a man standing beside Summers. &ldquo;Wal, you two-bit free-range cattlemen can put thet in your pipes an&rsquo; smoke it.&rdquo; Whereupon he strode off the porch and down the road, erect and forceful, his departure expressive of much. &ldquo;Me an&rsquo; Arch was sure curious aboot this fence,&rdquo; continued Seth. &ldquo;We rode out of Flag an&rsquo; started in where the fence begins. It strikes south into the timber at Traft&rsquo;s line, an&rsquo; closes up every draw clear to the Diamond. At Limestone we hit into Traft&rsquo;s outfit. They&rsquo;ve got the job half done an&rsquo; by the time the snow flies thet drift fence will run clear from Flag to Black Butte.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ha! A hundred miles of drift fence!&rdquo; exclaimed some one. &ldquo;Ahuh,&rdquo; nodded Summers, sagely. &ldquo;An&rsquo; all the cattle will drift along to Black Butte an&rsquo; then drift back again.&rdquo; Haverly swung his spurred boot back to his stirrup and without another word rode away. Molly watched the departing rider as thoughtfully as any of the others on Summers&rsquo; porch. This drift fence must be going to have a profound significance for the few inhabitants along the West Fork of the Cibeque. Then down the road from the other direction appeared the See buckboard, sight of which brought Molly bouncing to her feet. To her relief young John See was not in the vehicle with his parents. John had more prospects than any of the young men Molly knew, but he also had more than his share of their demerits. The buckboard rolled to a stop. &ldquo;Hop up, Molly,&rdquo; called See, gayly. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re late an&rsquo; it ain&rsquo;t no fault of yours.&rdquo; &ldquo;Good mawnin&rsquo;,&rdquo; returned Molly, brightly, as she climbed to the seat beside Mrs. See. &ldquo;Mornin&rsquo; lass,&rdquo; replied the rancher&rsquo;s wife. &ldquo;You look like you could fly as well as hop.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, I&rsquo;m on pins,&rdquo; cried Molly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll never be able to thank you enough.&rdquo; &ldquo;Howdy, Caleb,&rdquo; spoke up Summers. &ldquo;Reckon you&rsquo;ve got time to come inside a minute.&rdquo; &ldquo;Mawnin&rsquo;, Enoch,&rdquo; replied See, which greeting included the others present. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m in a hurry.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, come in anyhow,&rdquo; returned Summers, bluntly, and went into the store. See grumbled a little, as he wound the reins around the brake-handle, and laboriously got down. He was a heavy man, no longer young. All the loungers on the porch followed him into the store, but Andy Stoneham remained in the door, watching Molly. &ldquo;That lout&rsquo;s makin&rsquo; sheep eyes at you, Molly,&rdquo; said Mrs. See. Molly did not look. &ldquo;He just said some nasty things to me,&rdquo; she confided. &ldquo;Then the fool asked me to go to a dance at Hall&rsquo;s Mill.&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly, you&rsquo;re growin&rsquo; up an&rsquo; it&rsquo;s time you got some sensible notions,&rdquo; said Mrs. See, seriously. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m goin&rsquo; to Flag,&rdquo; trilled Molly, as if that momentous adventure was all that mattered. &ldquo;Lass, you&rsquo;re a bad combination. You&rsquo;re too pretty an&rsquo; too crazy. I reckon it&rsquo;s time to get you a husband.&rdquo; Molly laughed and blushed. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s what ma says. But it&rsquo;s funny. I have to work hard enough now.&rdquo; Caleb See came stamping out of the store, wiping his beard, sober of face where he had been merry. Without a word he stepped into the buckboard, making it lurch, and drove away. Molly was reminded of the news about the drift fence. &ldquo;Mrs. See, while I was waitin&rsquo; for you Seth Haverly rode up,&rdquo; said Molly. &ldquo;He&rsquo;d just come in from the Diamond with my brother Arch. They&rsquo;d been to Flag. An&rsquo; he was tellin&rsquo; old Enoch Summers about a fence that was bein&rsquo; built, down across the country. A drift fence, he called it. What&rsquo;s a drift fence?&rdquo; While Mrs. See pondered over the query Caleb answered. &ldquo;Wal, lass, it&rsquo;s no wonder you ask, seein&rsquo; we don&rsquo;t have no fences in this country. On a free range cattle travel all over, accordin&rsquo; to water an&rsquo; grass. Now a drift fence is somethin&rsquo; that changes a free range. It ain&rsquo;t free no more. It&rsquo;s a rough country this side of the Diamond. All the draws head up on top an&rsquo; run down into the West Fork, an&rsquo; into the Cibeque. Water runs down these draws, an&rsquo; feed is good. Wal, a drift fence built on top an&rsquo; runnin&rsquo; from Flag down country will keep the cattle on top. They&rsquo;ll drift along an&rsquo; water down on the other side. Then they&rsquo;ll drift back.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why were they so serious about it?&rdquo; asked Molly, curiously. &ldquo;Isn&rsquo;t a drift fence a good thing?&rdquo; &ldquo;Reckon it is, for Traft an&rsquo; Blodgett, an&rsquo; the big cattlemen up Flag way. But for us folks, who live off the Diamond, it ain&rsquo;t so good.&rdquo; &ldquo;Most of us couldn&rsquo;t live much worse,&rdquo; replied Molly, thoughtfully. &ldquo;You bet you could, lass. Haven&rsquo;t you always had milk an&rsquo; beefsteak, an&rsquo; shoes to wear?&rdquo; &ldquo;Most always, but not always. Just now I&rsquo;m walkin&rsquo; in my bare toes,&rdquo; said Molly, with giggle. &ldquo;If I hadn&rsquo;t saved up money enough to buy stockings an&rsquo; shoes I&rsquo;d never come.&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly, you goin&rsquo; to have a new dress, too,&rdquo; declared Mrs. See. &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t tell you we are goin&rsquo; to a picnic. Goin&rsquo; to be a big time in Flag on Saturday, most like the Fourth.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, heavenly!&rdquo; exclaimed Molly, rapturously. &ldquo;An&rsquo; to think I almost didn&rsquo;t come! ... Mrs. See, you&rsquo;re awfully kind.&rdquo; Mr. See went on with something in his mind. &ldquo;No Molly, we&rsquo;ve been fair to middlin&rsquo; prosperous down here in the valley. But this drift fence will make a difference.&rdquo; &ldquo;Caleb, isn&rsquo;t the land owned by the government? Couldn&rsquo;t any man homestead it?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. An&rsquo; there&rsquo;s the rub. Traft has no right to fence this free range. But he&rsquo;s a rich, powerful old duffer an&rsquo; bull-headed as one of his steers. Who&rsquo;re we down here to go to law? An&rsquo; where&rsquo;d we go? Fairfield, the county seat, is farther away than Flag. It takes time an&rsquo; money to travel.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, dear!&rdquo; sighed the good woman. &ldquo;Then it&rsquo;ll mean hard times.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Susan, we can stand hard times, an&rsquo; I reckon come out ahead. But this drift fence means trouble. It&rsquo;s a slap in the face to every free ranger in this section. They&rsquo;ll all take it Traft accuses them of stealin&rsquo; unbranded stock that drifts down into the draws on the West Fork.&rdquo; &ldquo;What kind of trouble, Uncle Caleb?&rdquo; queried Molly, soberly. &ldquo;Lass, do you remember the Pleasant Valley war over across the mountains in the Tonto? Let&rsquo;s see, you must have been about six years old. Ten years ago.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, I remember, mamma wouldn&rsquo;t let me play out of the yard. We lived at Lunden then. But if I hadn&rsquo;t remembered I&rsquo;d sure know what that war was. Papa talks about it yet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Lass, some people say your dad was crippled for sympathy to one faction in that fight.&rdquo; &ldquo;Pa denies it. But he was on the side of the sheepmen. An&rsquo; that riles my brother Arch somethin&rsquo; funny. They never get along. Arch isn&rsquo;t much good, Uncle Caleb.&rdquo; &ldquo;Humph! I&rsquo;d not say that, for Arch has good parts. But he&rsquo;s much bad, an&rsquo; that&rsquo;s no joke.... Wal, if Traft&rsquo;s outfit ever finishes their fence&mdash;at least down in the Diamond, it&rsquo;ll be cut. An&rsquo; as Traft runs a lot of hard-ridin&rsquo; an&rsquo; shootin&rsquo; cowpunchers, there&rsquo;s shore goin&rsquo; to be blood spilled. It takes years sometimes to wear out these feuds. An&rsquo; we&rsquo;ve a lot of thick-headed hombres in our neck of the woods.&rdquo; His ominous reasoning had a silencing effect upon his hearers. The women of that country were pioneers in suffering, and there were many widows and orphans. Molly thought of her brother Arch. He was only twenty-two, yet he had killed more than one man, and through many fights, but few of them bloodless, he had earned a reputation that was no source of pride to his family. Arch not so long ago had been a nice boy. Lack of work, and drinking, and roaming the woods with fellows like Seth Haverly, had ruined him. Now it would grow worse, and that would make it harder for Molly&rsquo;s crippled father, who had to sit at home and brood. Molly conceived a resentment against the rich cattleman who could impose such restrictions and embitter the lives of poor people. And as for Traft&rsquo;s tenderfoot relative, who had come out from Missouri to run a hard outfit and build barbed-wire fences, Molly certainly hated him. Funny if she should meet him! What would he be like? A change from long-legged, unshaved, ragged boys who smelled of horses would be a relief, even if he was an enemy. It was unlikely, however, that she should have the luck to encounter Mr. Traft&rsquo;s nephew from Missouri, which fact would be good luck for him, at least. Molly would certainly let him know what she thought of him. It occurred to her presently that Arch had seen this new foreman of Traft&rsquo;s and could tell her all about him. How was Arch going to take this newcomer? Seth Haverly was as easy-going a boy as Arch, but dangerous when crossed. Molly was prone to spells of depression and she felt the imminence of one here. Wherefore, in order to shake off the insidious shadow, she devoted herself to the ride and to her companion, who needed a little cheering also. It had been years since Molly had been so many miles from the village. She did not remember the road. From her own porch she always had a wonderful view down the valley and across to the grand upheaval of earth and rock locally called the Diamond, and at the rugged black hills to the south. But now she was riding at a fast trot of a spirited team through a winding timbered canyon, along the banks of the West Fork. As there was a gradual down grade, the gray cliff walls grew higher until they were far above. Only a lone horseman was encountered in all the fifteen miles down to where the West Fork poured its white torrent into the Cibeque. Here Mr. See took the main road, which climbed and wound and zigzagged up the long slope. Molly looked down and back at the wilderness which was her home. All green and gray, and so big! She could not hate it, somehow. All her life she had known that kind of country. She had played among the ferns and the rock, and in the amber water, and under the brown-barked pines and spruces, where deer and elk and wild turkeys were as common as the cows she drove from pasture in the dusk. She felt that it would take a terrible break to sever her from this home of forest and gorge. There had been snow on the ground all winter, which accounted for the abundance of gramma grass, now beginning to bleach in the early summer sun. Cattle dotted all the glades and flats and wide silvery meadows; and toward afternoon, from a ridge top the vast gray-green range spread like a billowy ocean far as eye could see. Several ranches were passed at any one of which See would have been welcome to spend the night, but he kept going all of daylight, and by night had covered more than half the journey to Flagerstown. &ldquo;Wal, wife, we&rsquo;ve made Keech&rsquo;s, an&rsquo; that&rsquo;s good, considerin&rsquo; our late start,&rdquo; remarked See, with satisfaction, as he drove into a wide clearing, the hideousness of which attested to the presence of an old sawmill. Rude clapboard cabins and fences, not to note the barking dogs, gave evidence of habitation. The cabins, however, were more inviting inside, Molly was to learn, and that the widow Keech was a most kindly and loquacious hostess. She had two grown daughters, and a son about fourteen years old, an enormously tall boy who straightway became victim to Molly, a conspicuous fact soon broadly hinted by his elders. &ldquo;So this hyar is John Dunn&rsquo;s girl growed up,&rdquo; said Mrs. Keech. &ldquo;I knowed your father well, an&rsquo; I seen you when you was a big-eyed kid. Now you&rsquo;re a woman ridin&rsquo; to Flag.&rdquo; Molly, however, was not to be led into conversation. This adventure seemed to her too grand to be joked about. She was keen to listen, and during the dinner hour heard much about Flagerstown and the fair to begin there on the morrow, and to end on Saturday with a rodeo. Mrs. See had not imparted all this marvelous news to Molly and she laughed at the girl&rsquo;s excitement. &ldquo;What you know aboot this drift fence?&rdquo; finally asked See. &ldquo;Caleb, it&rsquo;s a downright fact,&rdquo; replied the widow, forcefully. &ldquo;Harry has seen it. Traft&rsquo;s outfit are camped ten miles north of us. They&rsquo;ll pass here this summer an&rsquo; be down on your Diamond by the time snow flies.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. So we heerd. But what&rsquo;s your idee aboot it?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Caleb, all things considered, it&rsquo;ll be good for the range. For no matter what folks say, cattle-rustlin&rsquo; is not a thing of the past. Two-bit stealin&rsquo; of calves is what it really is. But rustlin&rsquo;, for all that. An&rsquo; up this way, anyhow, it&rsquo;ll help.&rdquo; &ldquo;Are you runnin&rsquo; any stock?&rdquo; asked See, thoughtfully. &ldquo;Cows, mostly. I send a good deal of butter in to town. Really am gettin&rsquo; on better than when we tried to ranch it. I don&rsquo;t have to hire no-good punchers. People travel the road a lot these days. An&rsquo; they all stop hyar. I&rsquo;ve run up some little cabins.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; that&rsquo;s a good idee,&rdquo; said See. Molly listened to hear everything, and particularly wanted to learn more about the young Missouri tenderfoot who had come out West to build fences for Traft. He would certainly have a miserable existence. And it was most liable to be short. To Molly&rsquo;s disappointment, no more was said about the drift fence. &ldquo;Wal, we&rsquo;ll rustle off to bed,&rdquo; concluded See. &ldquo;Mrs. Keech, I&rsquo;ll want to leave early in the mornin&rsquo;.&rdquo; Molly shared one of the new cabins with Mrs. See. It was small, clean, and smelled fragrantly of dry pine. It had three windows, and that to Molly was an innovation. She vowed she would have one like it, where she could have light in the daytime and air at night. She was tired, but not sleepy. Perhaps the bed was too comfortable. Anyway, Molly lay wide awake in the dark, wondering what was going to happen to her. This trip to Flagerstown might be a calamity for her. But she must have it. She must enjoy every moment of it, no matter what discontent it might engender. The hounds bayed the wolves and made her shudder. Wolves and coyotes seldom ranged down in the brakes of the Cibeque. Bears and lions were plentiful, but Molly had never feared them. Wolves had such a mournful, blood-curdling howl. And when the hounds answered it they imitated that note, or else imparted to it something of hunger for the free life their wild brothers enjoyed. When at last Molly fell asleep it seemed only a moment until she was rudely awakened. Mrs. See was up, dressing by lamplight. A gray darkness showed outside the open window, and the air that blew in on Molly was cold enough for early fall, down on the West Fork. But the great day was at hand. She found her voice, and even had a friendly word for the boy Harry, who certainly made the most of it. When she came out from breakfast, a clear cold morning, with rosy flush in the east, greeted her triumphantly, as if to impart that it had some magic in store. &ldquo;Hope so,&rdquo; replied Molly. Then they were off behind fresh horses and soon into the cedars. Jack rabbits bounded away, with their ridiculously long ears bobbing erect; lean gray coyotes watched them roll along; deer trotted out of sight into thick clumps of brush. Soon they came to the open top of a ridge and Molly saw a gray, dim, speckled world of range, so immense as to dwarf her sight. The scent from that vast gulf was intoxicating. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s the sweet smell?&rdquo; she asked. &ldquo;Sage, you Cibeque Valley backwoods girl,&rdquo; replied Mrs. See. &ldquo;Anyone would think you&rsquo;d never been out of the timber.&rdquo; &ldquo;I haven&rsquo;t, much,&rdquo; laughed Molly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve seen an&rsquo; smelled sage, but it&rsquo;s so long ago I&rsquo;d forgotten. Reckon I&rsquo;d better be pretty careful up at Flag, Auntie See?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore you had. But what aboot?&rdquo; &ldquo;Talkin&rsquo;. I&rsquo;m so ignorant,&rdquo; sighed Molly. &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t need to be dumb. You just think before you speak. You&rsquo;re such a pretty little mouse that it&rsquo;ll become you. I don&rsquo;t care for gabby girls, myself. An&rsquo; I never seen a man who did, if he was in earnest.&rdquo; Molly was silent enough for the next long stretch. She watched a sunrise that made her think how beautiful the world was and how little she had seen, hidden down there in the green brakes. But she reproved herself for that. From her porch she could see the sun set in the great valley when the Diamond sheered abruptly down into the Cibeque, and nothing could have excelled that. And what could be better than the wooded canyons, deep and gray and green, with their rushing streams? But this open range took her breath. Here was the cattle country&mdash;what Mr. See had called the free range, and which riders like her brother Arch and Seth Haverly regarded as their own. Yet was it not a shame to fence that magnificent rolling land of green? For a moment Molly understood what it meant to be a range-rider, to have been born on a horse. She sympathized with Arch and Seth. A barbed-wire fence, no matter how far away, spoiled the freedom of that cedared grassy land. &ldquo;Wal, lass, thar&rsquo;s the smoke of Flag,&rdquo; said Mrs. See. &ldquo;Way down in the corner. Long ways yet. But we&rsquo;re shore gettin&rsquo; there.&rdquo; &ldquo;Smoke,&rdquo; said Molly, dreamily. &ldquo;Are they burnin&rsquo; brush?&rdquo; &ldquo;Haw! Haw! That smoke comes from the railroad an&rsquo; the sawmill.&rdquo; From there on the miles were long, yet interesting, almost every one of them, with herds of cattle wearing different brands, with ranches along the road, with the country appearing to spread and grow less cedared. Ten miles out of Flagerstown Mr. See pointed to a distant ridgetop, across which a new fence strung, startlingly clear against the sky. It gave Molly a pang. &ldquo;Traft&rsquo;s drift fence, I reckon,&rdquo; said See. &ldquo;An&rsquo; I&rsquo;d almost rather have this a sheep range!&rdquo; For all her poor memory, Molly remembered Flagerstown&mdash;the black timbered mountain above it, the sawmill with its pile of yellow lumber, the gray cottages on the outskirts, and at last the thrilling long main street, with buildings that looked wonderful to her. Mr. See remarked with satisfaction that the time was not much past four o&rsquo;clock. He drove straight down this busy thoroughfare. Molly was all eyes. &ldquo;Hyar we are,&rdquo; said Mr. See, halting before a pretentious brick building. &ldquo;This is the new hotel, Molly. Now, wife, make the best of our good trip in. Take Molly in the stores. I&rsquo;ll look after the horses, get our rooms, an&rsquo; meet you hyar at six o&rsquo;clock.&rdquo; Molly leaped out of the buckboard with a grim yet happy realization that she would not need much longer to be ashamed of her shoes and stockings. Three hours later, Molly, radiant and laden with bundles, tagged into the hotel behind Mrs. See, likewise laden, to be greeted vociferously by Mr. See. &ldquo;For the land&rsquo;s sake! Have you robbed a store or been to a fire?&mdash;An&rsquo; hyar me waitin&rsquo; for supper!&rdquo; &ldquo;Caleb, it happens seldom in a lifetime,&rdquo; replied his beaming wife. &ldquo;Help us pack this outfit to our rooms. Then we&rsquo;ll have supper.&rdquo; Molly had a room of her own. She had never even seen one like it. Loath to leave her precious purchases, she lingered until they called her from the hall. It struck her again how warmly these old people looked at her. Molly guessed she was a circus and ruefully admitted reason for it. The dining-room might have been only &ldquo;fair to middlin&rsquo;,&rdquo; as Mr. See put it, but it was the most sumptuous place Molly had ever entered. Sight of it added to the excitement of the few hours&rsquo; shopping effectually robbed her of appetite. &ldquo;Wal, I reckon Molly wants a biscuit an&rsquo; a hunk of venison,&rdquo; remarked Mr. See. Molly did not know quite how to take that remark. She became aware, too, of being noticed by two young men at a near-by table. They were certainly not cowboys or timber-rangers. Molly was glad to get out and upstairs to the privacy of her room. There she unpacked the numerous bundles and parcels, and laid out her newly acquired possessions upon the bed. How quickly her little hoard of money had vanished! Still, it had gone farther than she had anticipated. Mrs. See had been incredibly generous. A blue print dress, a white dress with slippers and stockings to match, the prettiest little hat Molly had ever seen in her life, ribbons and gloves and what not&mdash;these had been the expansion of the good woman&rsquo;s promise. But not only the pleasure of looking and buying had Molly to think of. She had met more people than she had ever met before. She had been asked to serve in one of the booths at the fair. One of the storekeepers had offered her a position as clerk in his dry-goods department. And altogether the summing up of this day left Molly staggered with happiness. &ldquo;Oh, dear!&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;If it&rsquo;s true it&rsquo;ll spoil me.&rdquo; And she cried a little before she went to sleep. Another morning probed deeper into Molly&rsquo;s faculties for enjoyment and wonder. Mrs. See had relatives and friends in Flagerstown, and they made much of Molly. Not the least of that morning&rsquo;s interest was a look at Jim Traft, cattle king of the range. It was in the bank, where Molly and Mrs. See had visited with Mr. See. &ldquo;Thar&rsquo;s the old reprobate,&rdquo; whispered See to Molly. &ldquo;Jim Traft, who&rsquo;s fencin&rsquo; off West Fork from the range!&rdquo; Molly stared. She saw a big man in his shirt sleeves and dusty top boots. He had a shrewd weather-beaten face, hard round the mouth and chin, but softened somewhat by bright blue eyes that certainly did not miss Molly. If he had not been Jim Traft it would have been quite possible to like him. As they turned to go out he hailed See. &ldquo;Hey, don&rsquo;t I know you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, I reckon I know you, Traft,&rdquo; returned See, not over-civilly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m Caleb See.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. I never forget faces. You live down in the Cibeque. Glad to meet you again. If you&rsquo;re not in a hurry I&rsquo;d like to ask you some questions about your neck of the woods.&rdquo; &ldquo;Glad to accommodate you, Traft,&rdquo; returned See, and then he indicated his companions. &ldquo;Meet my wife.... An&rsquo; this is our little friend, Molly Dunn. Her first visit to Flag since she was a kid.&rdquo; Traft shook hands with Mrs. See, and likewise Molly. He was quaint and genial, and his keen eyes approved of Molly. &ldquo;Wal, wal! I&rsquo;m shore glad to meet you, young lady,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Molly Dunn of the Cibeque. I think I used to know your father. An&rsquo; this is your first visit to Flag in a long time?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, sir. It seems a whole lifetime,&rdquo; replied Molly. When Molly got outside again she exclaimed, breathlessly: &ldquo;Oh, Mrs. See, he looked right through me! ... I don&rsquo;t want his pity.... But I&rsquo;m afraid the Dunns of the Cibeque have a bad name.&rdquo; &ldquo;Reckon they have, Molly dear,&rdquo; rejoined Mrs. See, practically. &ldquo;But so far as you are concerned it can be lived down.&rdquo; &ldquo;But, Mrs. See&mdash;I&rsquo;d have to stick to dad an&rsquo; Arch,&rdquo; said Molly, suddenly confronted with a lamentable fact. &ldquo;Shore. In a way you&rsquo;ve got to. I wouldn&rsquo;t think much of anyone who couldn&rsquo;t stand by her own kin.&rdquo; Not until afternoon on the ride out to the fairgrounds did Molly quite forget Jim Traft&rsquo;s look and the ignominy of the Dunns. But once arrived there she quite lost her own identity. This girl in blue at whom everybody stared was some other person. Crowds of people, girls in gay apparel, cowboys in full regalia, Indians in picturesque attire, horses, horses, horses, and prize cattle, and every kind of a vehicle Molly had ever heard of, appeared to move before her eyes. Quite by magic, it seemed, she found herself separated from the smiling Mrs. See and conducted to a gayly decorated booth. There she was introduced to a girl about her own age, with whom she was to share the fascinating work of serving the public with sandwiches and coffee. Fortunately for Molly, her partner was nice and friendly, and certainly gave no indication that she had ever heard of the Dunns of West Fork. Under her amiable instruction Molly, who was nothing if not expert at waiting at table, acquitted herself creditably. But she could not get used to the marvelous gown she had on, and was in a panic for fear she might get a stain upon it. She did not, however, have so much work that she could not see what was going on, and presently she was having a perfectly wonderful time. Once she served three cowboys. They were hardly a new species to Molly. Nevertheless, she had not seen such brilliant scarves and fancy belts. She noticed, too, that these young men, like Arch and Seth, packed guns in their belts, a custom she had hardly expected to find at a fair. One of them made eyes at Molly. After a while they came back, when Molly&rsquo;s partner had left, and if ever Molly had seen the devil in the eyes of a youth she saw it in one of these customers. Still, he was not bad-looking and Molly could not help liking him. &ldquo;Miss&mdash;Miss&mdash;What&rsquo;d you say your name was?&rdquo; he asked as he straddled the bench before the counter. &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t say,&rdquo; replied Molly. &ldquo;No,&rdquo; replied Molly. &ldquo;Or ginger ale?&rdquo; Molly shook her head. &ldquo;Not any pink lemonade?&rdquo; &ldquo;Only coffee an&rsquo; sandwiches an&rsquo; cake.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cake? Well, give us cake an&rsquo; coffee,&rdquo; ordered the cowboy. She served them swiftly and discreetly, deftly avoiding the bold hand that sought to include her fingers as she passed a cup. &ldquo;Do you live here?&rdquo; he asked, presently. &ldquo;You know quite well I&rsquo;m a stranger in Flag, else you wouldn&rsquo;t be so impertinent,&rdquo; returned Molly, severely. &ldquo;Aw!&rdquo; He subsided with that exclamation. And his comrades proceeded to enjoy themselves at his expense. Molly&rsquo;s keen ears lost nothing of the banter. They were just brimful of fun. Evidently the bold one enjoyed something of a reputation as a lady-killer, and had at last met defeat. Presently, as he could not get Molly to notice him, and grew tired of listening to his friends, he threw some silver on the counter and said, loftily, &ldquo;Keep the change, Little Snowflake.&rdquo; Then he strode away, and after a few moments the others followed. From this time Molly was kept busy, and only gradually did it dawn upon her that a string of cowboys kept coming and going, for the very obvious reason of getting a look at her. More than once she heard the name Snowflake. Still, none of them were rude. Manifestly they had taken her for a guest of some prominent family in town, and a lady of quality. Molly enjoyed it hugely, though she had more than one melancholy reservation that it might have been different if they had guessed she was only one of the Dunns of the Cibeque. Soon she was relieved by the young lady, Miss Price, who shared the booth with her. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ve got the boys guessing,&rdquo; said this smiling worthy. &ldquo;They&rsquo;ve nagged me to death. I don&rsquo;t know them all, though. Just keep it up.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&mdash;I don&rsquo;t do anythin&rsquo; but wait on them,&rdquo; gasped Molly. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s it. Guess they think you&rsquo;re cold when you&rsquo;re only shy,&rdquo; went on Miss Price. &ldquo;But you can have a heap of fun. Keep on freezing them. Tomorrow night you&rsquo;ll have the time of your life.&rdquo; &ldquo;Tomorrow night?&rdquo; faltered Molly. &ldquo;I&mdash;I hadn&rsquo;t heard. It&rsquo;s terrible kind of you. But I really couldn&rsquo;t go. I&rsquo;m such a stranger. An&rsquo; if they&mdash;they think&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;You dear kid! You are going. Mrs. See promised mother.&rdquo; Molly thrillingly resigned herself to the unknown. The afternoon ended all too soon, and she rode back to town, babbling to the pleased Mrs. See about the adventure she was having. That night they were out to dine with relatives of Mrs. See. No other young person was present and Molly had the relief of being comparatively unnoticed. These serious older people talked about the affairs of the town and the range, all of which found lodgment in Molly&rsquo;s mind. A young man, in overalls and heavy boots, got off a dusty horse and approached the booth. He asked for something to eat and drink. Apparently he took no notice of Molly. His face wore a troubled look. Of all the young men Molly had waited upon in two days, this was the first one who had not looked at her twice, and the only one who had not appeared gay or bold or pleasant. Molly felt a little pique and secondly more than a little curiosity. He might have been twenty-two or -three years old and evidently was not a cowboy. Molly judged that he would have been fair-skinned if he had not been so sunburnt. His nose had begun to peel, but these demerits did not exactly keep him from being handsome. Presently he laid his sombrero on the counter, which act disclosed light wavy hair, and a broad brow marred by deep furrows. He struck Molly just about right, and considering her vast experience during these two days, she imagined she was a connoisseur in young men. He slowly drank the last of his coffee, and looking up, met Molly&rsquo;s glance. Then she knew he had not seen her before. He had gray eyes full of shadows. &ldquo;What&rsquo;d you do if you were just about licked?&rdquo; he queried, suddenly. &ldquo;Sir?&rdquo; exclaimed Molly. He repeated the question, this time more deliberately, as if now he weighed it. &ldquo;I&mdash;I&rsquo;d get up an&rsquo; fight some more,&rdquo; declared Molly, surprised into genuine sincerity. He smiled. Then something beside surprise happened to Molly. &ldquo;You would? Suppose then you got licked sure?&rdquo; &ldquo;It wouldn&rsquo;t make no difference,&rdquo; replied Molly, at last forgetting to watch her speech. And she smiled back at him. He saw her really then as a girl, and not as any individual who might propound a personal point of view. Leaning his elbows on the counter, he regarded her with interest verging upon admiration. His words were severe, his purpose almost grim, yet Molly realized the best compliment she ever had received was being paid her. &ldquo;I never saw you before,&rdquo; he went on. &ldquo;That isn&rsquo;t my fault,&rdquo; replied Molly, demurely, with level gaze on him. What a nice face he had! &ldquo;But you don&rsquo;t live in Flag,&rdquo; he protested. &ldquo;No indeed.&rdquo; &ldquo;Where then?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m from the Cibeque.&rdquo; &ldquo;Cibeque. Is that a town or a ranch or what?&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a valley.&rdquo; &ldquo;Never heard of it. How far?&rdquo; &ldquo;Two days&rsquo; ride.&rdquo; &ldquo;Just here on a visit?&rdquo; continued the young man, and it was manifest that every word carried him farther into interest. &ldquo;Yes. We leave in the mawnin&rsquo;,&rdquo; said Molly, and sighed. Would she ever come back to Flagerstown? And if so, could it ever be so wonderful? &ldquo;That&rsquo;s a long while yet,&rdquo; he returned, and smiled again, with a meaning which made Molly&rsquo;s heart jump. &ldquo;I haven&rsquo;t heard, but of course there&rsquo;ll be a dance tonight. And you&rsquo;ll be going?&rdquo; Molly nodded. She had begun to be conscious of confusing sensations. &ldquo;Not quite.&rdquo; He gave her a long gaze that began in doubt and ended with trust. Molly felt that he knew every last thing in the world about her and she wanted the earth to open and swallow her. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t care for these town dances,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;But I&rsquo;m going to this one&mdash;if it&rsquo;s true you&rsquo;re not engaged for every dance.&rdquo; &ldquo;To tell the truth I&mdash;I haven&rsquo;t one single dance yet,&rdquo; she replied. &ldquo;Well! Then your best fellow isn&rsquo;t here?&rdquo; &ldquo;He doesn&rsquo;t happen to exist,&rdquo; said Molly, wistfully. Like all the others, he had taken her for somebody, and if he knew she was only Molly Dunn of West Fork he would not be so nice. &ldquo;Listen. This is a serious matter,&rdquo; he rejoined, gravely. &ldquo;Young ladies aren&rsquo;t always to be believed.&rdquo; &ldquo;I wouldn&rsquo;t lie to anyone,&rdquo; retorted Molly. &ldquo;Honest! You haven&rsquo;t a best fellow?&rdquo; &ldquo;Your age hadn&rsquo;t occurred to me. But I&rsquo;d have taken you for eighteen, anyhow. It really doesn&rsquo;t matter.... Have you been in Flag lately?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not for years. I was a little girl.&rdquo; &ldquo;Will you dance with me tonight?&rdquo; he asked, without any pretense. &ldquo;Yes,&rdquo; replied Molly, equally sincerely. &ldquo;How many times?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&mdash;I don&rsquo;t know about that. You see, I&rsquo;m not used to city dances.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;d not be terribly disagreeable to me,&rdquo; said Molly, archly, and after a roguish glance she looked away. But he responded to that differently from what she might have expected. &ldquo;Thank you,&rdquo; he rejoined, and stood up, with his gray eyes alight. &ldquo;Save some dances for me. Good-by, Miss Cibeque.&rdquo; He strode away and led his horse in the direction of the corrals. Molly stood there tingling, to be disturbed by the arrival of Mrs. See. &ldquo;I had one last customer,&rdquo; said Molly, as if apologizing for the delay. Then came exhibition trick riding by experts of the range. Molly could ride a horse herself and she knew what good horsemanship included, but this riding went far beyond anything she could have imagined. One rider, bareback, rode at full speed, and he slipped all over his horse, even underneath. But that appeared less wonderful than the splendid rider who rode two race-horses, standing with one foot on the bare back of each. Molly thrilled to her toes at that performance. The roping of calves was not new to her, though she had never believed such swift time possible. The roping of two-year-olds was a sterner game. Next after that came the riding of bucking bronchos. In any horse country there are bound to be some mean horses, and Molly imagined she had seen a few. But she had not even known what a mean horse was like. There was a black devil of a mustang with rolling white eyes that simply made the cold chills run over Molly. Buck! He went six feet into the air, doubled up, and came down stiff-legged. And he threw three successive riders. Yet how these lean, supple, round-limbed, small-hipped cowboys could ride! They stuck on like burrs. But it was dangerous sport. Many were thrown. One red-headed fellow had a horse fall back on him. Another rolled clear over with his horse and still came up in the saddle. At this the crowd roared. Molly saw another boy carried off the field, but she had not observed what had happened to him. When that whirling, dusty, snorting, and yelling mêlée ended it was none too soon for Molly. &ldquo;Bulldoggin&rsquo; steers next,&rdquo; said Mr. See, consulting his program. &ldquo;Goodness! Do they chase them with bulldogs?&rdquo; ejaculated Molly, in amaze. See laughed heartily. &ldquo;Wal, thet&rsquo;s a good one.&rdquo; Molly was soon to learn more. A wicked wide-horned steer was let loose, and a cowboy, superbly mounted, came tearing down the field, to drive the steer furiously, catch up with it, and then to dive out of his saddle. He alighted on the neck of the steer, and swinging down by the horns he tumbled it head over heels, and rolled over with it. Molly screamed. But the cowboy came out of the dust unhurt and victorious, for there he sat on the head of the steer, holding it down. &ldquo;There! What do you think of that for a cowboy?&rdquo; exclaimed See, turning to Molly. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s wonderful. But he&rsquo;s crazy. Who ever heard of such a thing?&rdquo; returned Molly, feelingly. &ldquo;Molly&rsquo;s right,&rdquo; agreed Mrs. See. &ldquo;I think this bulldoggin&rsquo; sport is brutal. Where&rsquo;s the sense in it?&rdquo; &ldquo;Ain&rsquo;t none. But shore takes a slick rider to do it,&rdquo; said her husband. &ldquo;A cowboy once told me he didn&rsquo;t have an unbroken bone in his body. Now I can see why,&rdquo; commented Molly. Nevertheless, though this style of riding, and downing a steer like a bulldog, made Molly cold and sick, she could not help watching it through. She would certainly have something to tell Arch Dunn. Fortunately, none of the riders were seriously hurt during this most perilous test of horsemanship and hardihood. And the rest of the program allowed Molly to recover. &ldquo;Wal, lass, an&rsquo; how&rsquo;d you like it?&rdquo; asked Mr. See. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve had the most wonderful time in all my life,&rdquo; dreamily replied Molly. &ldquo;With the best yet to come,&rdquo; added Mrs. See. &ldquo;This is all very well for the men, but it&rsquo;s the dance where a girl shines.&rdquo; With that remark there came flashing back to Molly the strange thoughts and sensations roused by her last customer at the booth. Ought she not tell Mrs. See about this young man? Molly was inherently honest and she knew she should, but she was also in conflict with feelings new to her, and most confusing. She would wait. What would Mrs. See and Mrs. Price think of her if they knew she had promised dances to a stranger who had not even told her his name? That omission had not even occurred to Molly until now. All during the service at the booth she had been most careful of her tongue, and then the very last young man had made her forget herself and what was due her hostesses. Molly could not understand it. He had sort of carried her away. Still, he had not been bold like some of the cowboys, or flirtatious like others. At least she did not think so. But then she might have been wrong. The trouble was he had surprised her into liking him. No doubt of that! Who was he and what had troubled him and how had he been about licked, as he called it? Would it be possible for any young fellow to be so clever and so deceitful as to make up all that? Molly was startled. She had known boys to do queer tricks. But she loyally defended this one who had found her weak. He was as honest as he was nice. Here Molly got down to the point where he had become so undisguisedly interested in the fact that she did not have a best fellow. There had been a soft, almost mischievous light in his gray eyes. Could he have meant that he would like to be her best fellow? Molly burned within and without. The tumultuous fact was that he could be her only fellow, if he wanted to be. And she was ashamed and shocked to confess it. What had good Mrs. See brought upon her? This perturbing state of mind got side-tracked again at the hotel, from which there was a general exodus of ranchers who lived near town. Molly was thrown in contact with women whom the Sees knew. And at dinner they sat with friends, so that Molly was not able to think for herself, until she got to her room. There anticipation and delight assailed her again, into which crept a dread of what she knew not. Then she spread the white gown and accessories out on the bed, to revel in them, and forget the proximity of catastrophe. The following hour was one of exultation and dismay combined. She fixed her hair this way and that, never satisfied. It was an apparition which stared wide-eyed at her from the mirror. Who was this girl? Her arms and shoulders were bare. She could not get over the feeling of being still undressed. When Mrs. See came in, to exclaim in raptures over her, then Molly&rsquo;s last vestige of sense went into eclipse. She needed to be scolded and reminded that she was only Molly Dunn of the Cibeque, instead of being lauded to the skies, and told not one of the society girls at St. Louis, where Mrs. See had once been, could hold a candle to her. &ldquo;Upon your haid be it!&rdquo; murmured Molly, tragically. And so they went out to the dance, which was held in the town-hall a few blocks from the hotel. They walked, and Molly trod on air. Never in her life had she felt anything like her sensations when she walked through the crowd before the hall, between lines of men and women, children, Indians, and then at the entrance, before a phalanx of polished-faced cowboys. Whatever happened, she would have that to remember. Inside there was a goodly assembly, among whom were Mrs. Price, her daughter Ellen, and an awkward son about Molly&rsquo;s age. They at once appropriated Molly, and this perhaps was the time of greatest strain for her. It was scarcely necessary, however, for Molly to remember Mrs. See&rsquo;s injunctions. She scarcely had a voice at all. Fortunately, she was too little and dainty to be clumsy, and shyness only added to her charm. There was a good deal of standing around, talking and waiting, with introductions in order, while slowly the hall filled comfortably. Then the music began and it was not a fiddle sawed by a backwoodsman, but real music, and Molly could have danced in leaden boots. Waltzes and square dances, with the former in large proportion, were to be the program. Molly&rsquo;s first partner was the Price boy, who could not dance very well, but that did not spoil it for her. It developed that intermissions were frequent, but brief. The young people clamored to dance. They would let the old folk sit and look on and talk. Molly had the next dance with a friend of Ellen&rsquo;s, a young man of the town, very pleasant and attentive, and a good dancer. And the third with Mr. Price, himself, who declared he would allow nothing to prevent him having a dance with Molly. She enjoyed it, too, for he was light on his feet and full of fun. All this while, which seemed interminable despite her enjoyment, Molly knew there was something amiss. The dance had really begun and she was there. Everywhere she turned she met smiles and admiring glances. They bewildered her. Yet she was keen to note that this was no wild, continuous, stamping log-cabin dance. Hilarity prevailed, but not boisterousness. She wondered if there would be any drinking and fights. A dance without these would be new to Molly. Then, after the fourth, when she was standing with a group near the entrance of the hall, she saw Him. She felt herself tremble. He wore black and looked tall and slim. His eager eyes, dark with excitement, swept the hall. Molly knew what they were searching for. She had never wanted anything so badly as for him to see her, yet she was afraid. He did see her. His smile and bow came before he had time to look her over. Then Molly&rsquo;s cup grew perilously full. But what would he do? She was standing with Ellen Price and her friends. Suppose this stranger would present himself! Molly thought she might pretend to have forgotten his name; then if he had any sense he could save the situation for her. To her relief, however, he did not approach. The music burst forth again and another partner claimed Molly. But even in the whirling throng she did not lose sight of this young man whom she had promised dances. Now and then as she turned she saw him leaning against the wall. Met his dark glance! It followed her everywhere. He did not dance. He did not mingle with the crowd. During the next intermission she saw that he was noticed by girls, who whispered to one another, and by cowboys who gave him rather contemptuous looks. Both actions struck Molly singularly. In some sense he seemed an outsider or else he did not choose to make himself agreeable. The meaning of feminine glances sent his way was not lost upon Molly. And when she, too, dared to glance his way, to find him watching her, she would quickly avert her eyes. She realized that he hopefully and reasonably expected her to give him an opportunity. At the end of the next number, which Molly had with young Price, she claimed to be a little tired and wanted to sit down. They found a vacant place, where they conversed for the brief interval. &ldquo;Shall I take you across to mother?&rdquo; asked young Price. &ldquo;No, thanks. I&rsquo;ll wait here for my next partner,&rdquo; replied Molly, graciously. &ldquo;Excuse me then,&rdquo; he returned, and left her. Molly hoped her stranger would be quick. But she had scarcely prepared herself for his sudden arrival. &ldquo;Is this mine?&rdquo; he asked, bending over her eagerly. &ldquo;Yes,&rdquo; murmured Molly, rising. Then he whirled her into the throng. His presence did not quite make her oblivious to his strong arm, his light step, his perfect time. But instantly Molly realized she did not need to help this young man learn his steps. &ldquo;I was afraid you&rsquo;d forgotten me,&rdquo; he said, pressing her hand. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t talk,&rdquo; returned Molly. He laughed and obeyed her. Molly&rsquo;s head came about to his shoulder and she just escaped contact with it. Not that she wished to! She felt that her face must be burning and she would have liked to hide it there. She did not seem to be making any effort to dance. Yet she was whirling, swaying, gliding around among dancers who looked vague and dim. All the threatened feelings accumulated during the last two days took possession of her now. The music ceased when they were at the farther end of the hall. &ldquo;Come. Let&rsquo;s get out before some of them grab you,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;I must talk to you.&rdquo; &ldquo;You look perfectly lovely,&rdquo; he said, expelling a deep breath, as if in relief. &ldquo;I just didn&rsquo;t know you.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&mdash;you don&rsquo;t know me, anyhow,&rdquo; returned Molly, not knowing what to say. &ldquo;Nor you me. But at first I thought you did. It sure was jolly. To think I almost didn&rsquo;t ride to town!&rdquo; &ldquo;That would have been terrible, wouldn&rsquo;t it?&rdquo; murmured Molly. She could not remain silent. He seemed to draw expression from her. &ldquo;It sure would. But I don&rsquo;t want to tell you about myself, now. I want to talk about you.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; I&rsquo;d rather not.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aren&rsquo;t we mysterious?&rdquo; He took her hand and held it. Molly did not have the desire to withdraw it, nor the strength. But she managed to look up. How pale and eager he was! His eyes devoured her. And his face wore an ineffable smile. Molly&rsquo;s presagement that something was going to happen to her was near its fulfillment. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s a strange thing about you&mdash;a lack I can&rsquo;t understand,&rdquo; he went on. &ldquo;What&mdash;do you mean?&rdquo; faltered Molly. Had he found her out already? &ldquo;Oh, it&rsquo;s the most wonderful thing for me. I mean about the&mdash;the lack of that best fellow you said you didn&rsquo;t have. How does it happen? Sure you&rsquo;re only sixteen. But that&rsquo;s quite grown up in this country. Have you a fierce father or brother?&rdquo; &ldquo;You bet I have. Both!&rdquo; replied Molly, with a little laugh. She was sure of her ground here. &ldquo;Then that accounts. I&rsquo;m glad. I was afraid of something else. ... Very well, I make application for the vacant place.&rdquo; &ldquo;Place?&rdquo; echoed Molly, weakly. &ldquo;Yes. I most earnestly apply for the job of being your best fellow.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, you&mdash;you can&rsquo;t be serious!&rsquo; exclaimed Molly, in confusion. &ldquo;Serious? I&rsquo;m afraid it is,&rdquo; he said, running a hand through his hair. &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t know me. And I haven&rsquo;t any recommendations. But I&rsquo;d like you to take me on without these.&rdquo; &ldquo;It isn&rsquo;t customary,&rdquo; returned Molly, trying to be light when she feared he might hear the outrageous beating of her heart. &ldquo;I know that. But I&rsquo;ve a weakness to be trusted with responsibility. If I hadn&rsquo;t I&rsquo;d never gotten into the trouble I&rsquo;m bucking now.... You&rsquo;ll have to take me on faith&mdash;or not at all.&rdquo; &ldquo;But doesn&rsquo;t it&mdash;it apply to me&mdash;that way?&rdquo; asked Molly, tremulously. &ldquo;No. I&rsquo;m a man. And you&rsquo;re a girl.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. I&rsquo;m beginnin&rsquo; to find that out.&rdquo; He laughed as if her reply was encouragement and possessed himself of her other hand. Then undoubtedly he began to draw her a little toward him, but to do him justice Molly imagined he did not realize it. She did, however, to the imminent danger of rout of reserve and self-control. Molly felt irresistibly drawn to the edge of an abyss. Here was an opportunity quite beyond even her dreams. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re&mdash;you&rsquo;re&mdash;&rdquo; Molly did not know just what he was, besides being very careless and foolish. He had her almost leaning on his shoulder now. She had not made the slightest resistance. She was as unstable as water. Still, she tried to think in spite of his nearness and her dawning emotions. &ldquo;If I said&mdash;yes&mdash;an&rsquo;&mdash;an&rsquo; afterwards you went back on me!&rdquo; &ldquo;Good Heavens! ... Little girl, you had better say &lsquo;yes&rsquo; pretty quick or I&rsquo;ll&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; He choked at the end of that passionate utterance. Molly knew what he meant. For the moment it paralyzed her. And then it was too late. He had her in his arms&mdash;tight against his breast. Molly closed her eyes. She did not realize her state beyond the exquisite contrast to what her backwoods admirers had roused in her. And suddenly that thought ended in a singular revulsion. She stiffened. She repulsed him with a stinging slap which, blindly delivered, struck him across the lips. He uttered an inarticulate cry of surprise and regret. His hand went to his mouth, and then he applied a handkerchief there. The force of the blow had cut his lip. &ldquo;I apologize,&rdquo; he said, constrainedly. &ldquo;Sure lost my head&mdash;but I didn&rsquo;t mean to insult you.&rdquo; Molly, with as unaccountable an impulse as the other placed a tender trembling hand on his lips. &ldquo;I&mdash;I&rsquo;m sorry,&rdquo; she whispered, wildly. &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t mean that&mdash;at all.&rdquo; And she followed the touch of her hand with a shy swift kiss. Then she gasped at her utter effrontery. &ldquo;Well!&mdash;You make sweet amends,&rdquo; he said, haltingly, as if she were beyond him. &ldquo;By that did you mean &lsquo;Yes&rsquo;?&rdquo; Molly dropped her head and covered her face with her hands. The tight, hot constriction in her breast eased its grip. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know what I meant, only it wasn&rsquo;t &lsquo;Yes.&rsquo;&rdquo; &ldquo;In that case you&rsquo;d better explain.&rdquo; Molly looked up, impelled by his tone. His eyes burned doubtfully down upon her. His face shone pale in the moonlight. &ldquo;You wanted me to believe you were what?&rdquo; he asked, sharply, bending over her. &ldquo;Like Miss Price an&rsquo; her friends.&rdquo; &ldquo;I think they might do well by being more like you,&rdquo; he returned. &ldquo;I asked to be your best fellow. I sure never asked it of any of them.&rdquo; &ldquo;But you don&rsquo;t know me!&rdquo; cried Molly, distracted. &ldquo;I can see and think, cain&rsquo;t I? You&rsquo;re the sweetest, loveliest little girl I ever met.&rdquo; Molly was brutally torn between the ecstasy of that and the mercilessness of her honesty. &ldquo;Fine feathers make fine birds,&rdquo; she replied, bitterly. &ldquo;You poor kid! ... There&rsquo;s something queer here, but I swear it&rsquo;s not in you. I&rsquo;m taking your kiss for &lsquo;Yes!&rsquo;&mdash;Heavens! what else could a kiss mean?&rdquo; &ldquo;No, no. It meant nothin&rsquo;,&rdquo; said Molly. &ldquo;Are your kisses so common, then?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m very proud of that. Well, then, what else could it mean except &lsquo;Yes&rsquo;?&rdquo; &ldquo;I was beside myself. I told you.... I was ashamed&mdash;sick because I hit you. But I wasn&rsquo;t dishonest when I kissed you.&rdquo; &ldquo;You said you wanted me to think you like Miss Price and her friends. That puzzles me. I do think they can&rsquo;t compare with you.&rdquo; &ldquo;But you&rsquo;re only fooled,&rdquo; she said, despairingly. &ldquo;By what?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know. This pretty dress, an&rsquo; the place&mdash;an&rsquo; everythin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why, it wouldn&rsquo;t make any difference to me what you wore or where you were,&rdquo; he protested, tenderly. &ldquo;Oh yes, it would!&rdquo; &ldquo;But, you child, didn&rsquo;t I fall in love with you at the booth?&rdquo; This protestation was almost too beautiful and poignant for Molly to bear. It came in the nature of a revelation of her own beset state. In another instant she knew she would surrender and fall into his arms. &ldquo;But you don&rsquo;t know who I am!&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re my sweetheart!&rdquo; he returned, triumphantly. Molly suffered during one instant of glorious exaltation. &ldquo;I am Molly Dunn, of the Cibeque,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;Molly Dunn. What a pretty name! ... Cibeque? Oh, that&rsquo;s the valley you told me about.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. They call it the brakes of the Cibeque.&rdquo; &ldquo;Dunn, I&rsquo;ve heard that name, too. Oh yes, I got into an argument with a fellow named Dunn. Slinger Dunn, they called him. But sure you couldn&rsquo;t be any relation to him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why couldn&rsquo;t I?&rdquo; she queried, in a curious calm. &ldquo;Heavens! He&rsquo;s a desperado! Wonderful-looking chap. They call him &lsquo;Slinger&rsquo; because of his habit of throwing a gun. He has killed several men. The sheriff here is scared to death of him. I happened to cross his trail, unfortunately, and gave him a piece of my mind. If I ever saw lightning in a man&rsquo;s eyes I saw it then. Whew! ... Well, his companion, as tough-looking fellow as he was, dragged him away. Saved me a scare if not more.&rdquo; &ldquo;His right name is Arch,&rdquo; replied Molly, and rose to her feet. &ldquo;Of course, living around here you&rsquo;d have heard of him. It must be disgusting to have a criminal like that roaming around with the name of Dunn. People might think he is related to you.&rdquo; &ldquo;He is.&rdquo; The young man rose slowly, in consternation, and made an appealing gesture. &ldquo;Impossible, Miss Dunn.... Perhaps a very distant relation?&rdquo; &ldquo;He is my brother.&rdquo; &ldquo;Good God! Your brother? You lovely, dainty, sweet little lady! ... Why, I saw him again, drunk and dirty, hobnobbing with Mexicans and Indians. If he&rsquo;s a&mdash;one of your family, he surely must be an outcast.&rdquo; &ldquo;No. He was home the day I left to come here.&rdquo; He appeared suddenly staggered, not only by the truth, but by the nature of his transgression. &ldquo;Oh, I&rsquo;m sorry!&rdquo; he began, hurriedly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve hurt you. I never dreamed&mdash;please forgive me.... After all, it was natural enough. Another of my damned tenderfoot blunders! But who would ever think that you&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Ah! Now you&rsquo;ve said it,&rdquo; she interrupted, passionately. &ldquo;Miss Dunn, I was only going to say who would ever think a wonderful girl like you could have such a rotten brother.&mdash;Well, it makes no difference to me, I assure you of that,&rdquo; he said, bravely, essaying a fine effort to keep under restraint. He was regarding her fearfully and again he had turned pale. The bitterness of reality had steeled Molly, yet she shook inwardly as he stood there, erect and earnest, doing her honor. &ldquo;Yet it does, or you couldn&rsquo;t have talked so,&rdquo; she replied, shaking her head gravely. &ldquo;I say it doesn&rsquo;t,&rdquo; he retorted. &ldquo;And I certainly shall hold you to your word.&rdquo; &ldquo;Word? I didn&rsquo;t give any.&rdquo; &ldquo;You kissed me. Of your own sweet will! You can&rsquo;t get over that.&rdquo; &ldquo;No, I cain&rsquo;t.... But it wasn&rsquo;t a promise.&rdquo; &ldquo;It certainly was. More than a promise. Unless you lied to me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lie? I wouldn&rsquo;t lie to you,&rdquo; she declared. &ldquo;Then I hold you to it.... Come, let&rsquo;s forget the&mdash;the thing. You see, Molly, we fell in love before we got acquainted. Didn&rsquo;t we? Isn&rsquo;t that great?&rdquo; &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t say we&mdash;I fell in love,&rdquo; returned Molly, pondering over the significance of the words. &ldquo;Not in words&mdash;yet.&rdquo; Again Molly felt the imminence of a precipice. She could not resist this young man, stranger though he was, and presently she would not care to try. &ldquo;I kissed you because I wanted to be square,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;With myself, same as with you. I sure wasn&rsquo;t when I hit you. That&rsquo;s all.&rdquo; &ldquo;How can you say that? Kissing me proved your honesty. And for such a girl as I hold you to be, a kiss means a good deal. It just about means everything.&rdquo; Molly was mournfully becoming cognizant of that very fact. Desperately she cried out again that she was only Molly Dunn of the Cibeque. At this he seized her in his arms, masterfully, yet guardedly. &ldquo;Stop harping on that,&rdquo; he demanded. &ldquo;What do I care who you are? You might be Sally Jones of the Missouri. I don&rsquo;t care any more than you&rsquo;d care if my name was Bud Applegate instead of Jim Traft.&rdquo; &ldquo;Instead&mdash;of&mdash;what?&rdquo; faltered Molly, slipping out of his arms. &ldquo;Why, James Traft! Jim, for short.&rdquo; &ldquo;Traft? But that&rsquo;s the name of&mdash;of the cattle king.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sure. I&rsquo;m his nephew. My Dad is his brother. I was named after Uncle Jim.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re the new foreman of the Traft outfit?&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re the fellow who&rsquo;s buildin&rsquo; the drift fence?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes&mdash;I am,&rdquo; he replied unsteadily. &ldquo;Did you know that fence is a slap in the face to every person down in the Cibeque?&rdquo; &ldquo;No, I didn&rsquo;t. Uncle Jim said it would rile a lot of no-good homesteaders.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m the daughter of one of them. An&rsquo; sister to another,&rdquo; rejoined Molly, in tragic finality, and with a flash of eyes she left him there. Then had come an interval of four years during which Jim Traft did not visit Missouri. His vast interests had grown so complicated that he could not leave them. During this time James had been at loose ends, trying farming, clerking, and odd jobs without any indication that he might set the world on fire. At last a letter from the West at least changed the world for James. Some passages in this blunt letter to his father were hard for James to swallow.&mdash;In the natural course of events all of Jim Traft&rsquo;s property would go to his nephew James. But that was something aside from James ever making good use of it. If he were a strong, resourceful boy with guts he might become a rancher. The cattle industry was growing. The days of the great rustler barons were gone, though cattle-stealing still represented altogether a big loss to the range. And so on. The implication seemed to be that James would get all his uncle&rsquo;s money without having worked for it, and that there was a question whether or not he was big enough for the West. At first James had been humiliated and furious, and would hear nothing of going to Arizona. Nevertheless, his father prevailed in the end. Old Jim was caustic and crude; he had grown up in the stern school of the ranges, but he was the very salt of the earth and had genuine affection for James. He would be terribly hurt if James refused and he would never understand. &ldquo;It scares me a little, Jimmy,&rdquo; his father had said. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ve got to have the real stuff in you out there. I believe you have and I want you to go. Show Jim you&rsquo;re a Traft!&rdquo; Persuaded and made to realize his opportunity, for which he should sacrifice anything and strive with all his heart, James started West. His first acquaintance with the Great Plains had come from the window of a train, and long before he saw the vast gray slopes of Colorado and the white-peaked Rockies, the latent spirit of adventure stirred thrillingly in him. Then the wild timbered uplands of New Mexico and the red-walled canyons of Arizona won him to the West, long before he stepped off the train at Flagerstown. He had telegraphed his uncle as to his arrival, but there was no one to meet him. What a funny, slow, sleepy, wide-streeted town! Every other building, all high-boarded and weathered, appeared to house a saloon. He knew his uncle lived out of town, though not far. James finally found a livery-stable, where he engaged a loquacious negro to drive him, bag and baggage, out into the country. What he learned from this citizen of Flagerstown, in that short drive, was certainly not reassuring. But James liked the pine forest and the gray levels along the road, and the black mountains rising in the distance. And he had a fine view of Jim Traft&rsquo;s ranch home. It was nothing at all like he had pictured. Uncle Jim had been long on cattle deals and short on description, so far as talk was concerned. Across one of the wide grassy flats the long, low white house stood on a pine-timbered knoll, and below it clustered a bewildering array of corrals, barns, and sheds. Cattle dotted the wide valley, and on the fenced meadows horses and colts grazed, too numerous to count. The road wound along the edge of the timber, from which James had ample opportunity to see the ranch at different angles, and by the time he reached the house was wild with enthusiasm about his future home. A low-roofed comfortable porch fronted the house. Here James deposited his baggage, and paying the driver, he knocked. Nobody answered, however, so he went around to the back. A wide courtyard led out to the corrals. He espied men out there and directed his footsteps in that direction. Soon he came upon three cowboys around a horse, and then his uncle, who stood with another man, watching them. &ldquo;Hello, Uncle Jim!&rdquo; he yelled, and his rapid strides soon fetched him up. &ldquo;Howdy, Jim!&rdquo; replied the rancher, as if he had seen his nephew only yesterday, and extended his hand. &ldquo;Got your telegram, but forgot to meet you.... By gum! you&rsquo;ve sprung up like a weed.&rdquo; Traft had not changed. His garb, however, was new to Jim, and consisted of high boots, corduroys tucked in them, an old leather belt with an empty gun-sheath on it, gray soft shirt, and a vest that had been new years ago. He was a stalwart figure of a man, nearing seventy, but still erect and rugged, with a lined hard face expressive of his life on the frontier. &ldquo;Shake hands with Ring Locke,&rdquo; said Traft, indicating his companion, a tall, lean, sandy-complexioned Westerner whose narrow eyes were almost hidden under an old black sombrero. Jim was cordial and prompt in his greeting. &ldquo;How do!&rdquo; drawled Locke, whose accent proclaimed him a Texan. &ldquo;I shore am glad to meet you, sah.&rdquo; &ldquo;This is the nephew I told you about, Ring,&rdquo; went on Traft. &ldquo;He has come West to run the Diamond outfit.&rdquo; Jim tried to bear well the scrutiny given him by this range boss of his uncle&rsquo;s, a right-hand man who had been with him twenty years. Perhaps his earnestness favorably impressed Locke, for he smiled and replied, dryly, &ldquo;Wal, it ain&rsquo;t bad to start when you&rsquo;re a tenderfoot, just so long as you know it.&rdquo; &ldquo;You bet I know it,&rdquo; continued Jim, hastening to follow that up. &ldquo;When Uncle&rsquo;s letter came I was sure up a tree. It sounded wonderful. But I had listened to Uncle Jim&rsquo;s stories about gunmen and bad cowboys, wild steers and bucking bronchos, stampedes and rustling. It wasn&rsquo;t easy to decide.... But here I am. And I can take a licking.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, reckon you&rsquo;re likely to get it,&rdquo; rejoined Locke. &ldquo;But in this heah country a lickin&rsquo; ain&rsquo;t nothin&rsquo;, so long&rsquo;s it&rsquo;s not for keeps.&rdquo; Jim took almost instantly to the lean Texan. But the three cowboys standing by, apparently like hitching-posts, yet with still eyes and faces, gave him an uncomfortable sensation. To be sure, they heard every word. What clean-cut, lithe-limbed young men! The one holding the horse had a gun hanging low from his belt. Jim faced this triangle of judges, for so they seemed, expecting to be introduced. But his uncle apparently neglected or avoided it. &ldquo;We&rsquo;ll go back to the house,&rdquo; he said, and led Jim away. &ldquo;Have a good trip out?&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; how&rsquo;s that?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know yet. Maybe the great red walls&mdash;the canyons.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh.... Sorry I didn&rsquo;t meet you at the train. I reckoned I would. How&rsquo;s your mother?&rdquo; &ldquo;Fine and well. Uncle, she was crazy to have me come, but scared stiff.&rdquo; &ldquo;Good! An&rsquo; how&rsquo;s that storekeeper brother of mine, your dad?&rdquo; &ldquo;He hasn&rsquo;t been so well lately, but I guess it&rsquo;s nothing much. He sent a letter and some things which I have for you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Did he kick about your comin&rsquo; out?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. All he kicked about was my making good. He gave me a stiff talk, you bet.&rdquo; They reached the house, where Jim was conducted to a large light room, with walls and floor of clean yellow pine. A few deerskin rugs, a wood-burning stove, a table with lamp, an old bureau and mirror, and spare blanketed bed, constituted the contents, the simplicity of which pleased Jim. &ldquo;Come out on the porch and we&rsquo;ll talk,&rdquo; his uncle had said. And Jim, after securing the letter and parcels he had mentioned, hurried out to deliver them. &ldquo;Thanks. I&rsquo;ll look at them later.... Wal, Jim, you&rsquo;ve growed. You&rsquo;re a pretty husky chap. Too heavy, mebbe, but ridin&rsquo; the range will soon change that. By the way, have you been ridin&rsquo; much since I saw you last? You used to take to hosses.&rdquo; &ldquo;Had two years of riding every day. You know I tried farming.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, your dad mentioned it. How&rsquo;d you make out?&rdquo; &ldquo;I fell down, Uncle,&rdquo; replied Jim, regretfully. &ldquo;I just couldn&rsquo;t do it.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; why not?&rdquo; asked Traft, as if he already knew. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know, unless it was too tame. Every day the same! I thought I&rsquo;d die. But I stuck two years. Then dad sold the farm, which was lucky for me.&rdquo; &ldquo;What else you been doin&rsquo; these four years since I seen you?&rdquo; &ldquo;I was still in school for a year after you last visited us. Then the two years on dad&rsquo;s farm. And the last year I tried several jobs, only one of which I was any good at.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; what was that?&rdquo; asked Traft, kindly. &ldquo;Reckon it wasn&rsquo;t clerkin&rsquo; in the store?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. I&rsquo;m almost ashamed to tell you, Uncle. It was on my own hook, though. I got an idea some shade trees would look fine round our place. So I drove out to the river and dug up cottonwoods and planted them. Dad laughed at me. Then our neighbor hired me to do the same for his place. Through that I got other jobs, and I was making good money when your letter came.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ll be dog-goned!&rdquo; ejaculated the rancher. &ldquo;Plantin&rsquo; trees, an&rsquo; cottonwoods at that. Son, it was a darn good idee.&rdquo; Jim thanked his lucky stars he had confided something he had been afraid his uncle would think trivial. &ldquo;Wal, so much for Missouri,&rdquo; went on Traft. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re here in Arizona now. Reckon I might have wrote you all about what I want and hope. But it wouldn&rsquo;t have been fair to you or me. Fact is I couldn&rsquo;t have said all I need to in a letter. Your dad would have throwed a fit. I reckoned it&rsquo;d be better to get face to face an&rsquo; have it out. Don&rsquo;t you figger that way, too?&rdquo; &ldquo;I certainly do, Uncle, especially if it&rsquo;s as big and hard a job as I imagine. And if it&rsquo;s really true that you have made me your heir.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, naturally, all I have would go to your dad an&rsquo; you. But that&rsquo;s not the question.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a serious part of it for me,&rdquo; declared Jim, bravely. &ldquo;I appreciate your kindness, Uncle Jim, but if I can&rsquo;t make good as a rancher&mdash;well, I don&rsquo;t want the property.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh, I see. Wal, reckon your dad never guessed that.&rdquo; Jim felt the piercing intensity of eyes like a pale blue gleam, yet not lacking understanding. &ldquo;However, what becomes of my property ain&rsquo;t the main issue with me. Blood is thicker than water. An&rsquo; under any circumstances I&rsquo;d want my only kin to have what I left.&rdquo; &ldquo;Then, Uncle, what is the main issue?&rdquo; queried Jim, anxiously. &ldquo;Wal, I reckon it&rsquo;s I want you to be as near a son to me as possible.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s easy, Uncle, if it depends on sincerity and affection and obedience.&rdquo; &ldquo;They&rsquo;ll help, but it depends most on what I said in my letter. Guts!&rdquo; &ldquo;I remember, and that worried me. But I hope I have some.&rdquo; &ldquo;Jim, the job I want you to take is the hardest in the West.&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t care. The harder the better,&rdquo; declared Jim, answering the stimulation of doubt. &ldquo;I always told dad that I needed responsibility. He never gave me any. The fact that you will put responsibility on my shoulders is half of the battle right now.&rdquo; &ldquo;Son, that&rsquo;s straight talk,&rdquo; returned his uncle, nodding his head thoughtfully. &ldquo;An&rsquo; I liked the way you spoke up to Ring Locke. If he took a shine to you it&rsquo;d help a lot.... But, Jim, the hell of it is no rancher who knows the West ought ever to give a tenderfoot from the East such a job.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why not?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I reckon because of natural human feelin&rsquo;s. But I&rsquo;m just bull-headed enough to want a Traft an&rsquo; nobody else to take my place.&rdquo; &ldquo;If you were a young man, Uncle, could you take care of this job?&rdquo; asked Jim, curiously. &ldquo;Yes. An&rsquo; I reckon I could do it yet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Fine! I like your spirit, son,&rdquo; exclaimed Traft, warmly, and a smile transformed his hard lined face. &ldquo;Now listen. I&rsquo;m runnin&rsquo; about eight thousand head of cattle, mebbe more. But we can never get a count. That&rsquo;s a lot of stock, Jim. Figger out the value at forty dollars a head, which is a low estimate. Wal, I lose from a thousand head up every year. Most of this loss can be laid to cattle thieves. It has gradually growed worse an&rsquo; has begun to rile me. I used to laugh at this two-bit rustlin&rsquo;. But it&rsquo;s no good deceivin&rsquo; myself any longer. The thing is serious. I&rsquo;ve reason to believe Ring Locke knows it&rsquo;s worse than he&rsquo;ll tell me. Anyway, he&rsquo;s the best-posted cowman on the range.&mdash;Blodgett runs a big lot of cattle. So does Hep Babbitt. They&rsquo;re all losin&rsquo; stock, too.&rdquo; &ldquo;Uncle Jim, this is bad,&rdquo; declared Jim, in surprise. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s almost like the rustler stories you told me when I was a kid.&rdquo; &ldquo;Son, if I don&rsquo;t miss my guess you&rsquo;ll shore live one of them stories,&rdquo; responded Traft, with a grim laugh. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re being robbed, but you don&rsquo;t know where the cattle go?&rdquo; queried Jim, ignoring the start his uncle&rsquo;s statement gave him. &ldquo;Humph! We know darn well where they&rsquo;re goin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Where?&rdquo; &ldquo;South of here, in the brakes under the Diamond. An&rsquo; the Diamond, I should explain, is high country south of here. On three sides it sheers straight down an&rsquo; cattle can&rsquo;t get off. But on the west, for forty miles or so, it slopes off into the roughest canyon country in Arizona. Thicker than the Tonto. These canyons head up high in the timber an&rsquo; run down deep an&rsquo; rough. All of them have fine grass an&rsquo; water. Lots of deer, bear an&rsquo; turkey, too, if you like to hunt. Wal, a good deal of stock, especially cows with unbranded calves, drift into these draws an&rsquo; work down into the brakes. There the cows are killed an&rsquo; the calves stolen.
