It used to be these thieves would take the meat an&rsquo; bury or burn head an&rsquo; hide. But lately they kill too many. They just down the cows in a thicket or drag them into one, an&rsquo; leave them there for the varmints. Locke&rsquo;s last report shore riled me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Then, Uncle, this tough job you&rsquo;re giving me has to do with the thieves,&rdquo; asserted Jim. &ldquo;Wal, I should sort of smile it does,&rdquo; drawled Traft. &ldquo;But why not intrust it to an experienced Westerner, like Locke?&rdquo; &ldquo;Locke can&rsquo;t bother with it, an&rsquo; wouldn&rsquo;t if he could, at least the way I want to stop it. An&rsquo; as I told you I want a Traft to do this. Son, it&rsquo;ll be a big thing for the range, if we succeed. I don&rsquo;t want one of these gun-packin&rsquo; cowboys to have the credit, when I can throw it to you.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re very kind, Uncle,&rdquo; said Jim, with a dry humor not lost upon Traft. &ldquo;Are you sure anyone but a fool tenderfoot would tackle the job?&rdquo; Traft laughed. He was growing more at ease with Jim. &ldquo;Some of my boys are achin&rsquo; to get the job. Jim, my Diamond outfit is the damnest bunch of cowpunchers in Arizona. An&rsquo; it&rsquo;s this Diamond outfit you&rsquo;re to take charge of.&rdquo; &ldquo;Damnest bunch! Doesn&rsquo;t sound very good, Uncle. Just what do you mean?&rdquo; returned Jim, dubiously. &ldquo;Huh! I&rsquo;ll leave that for you to find out.... Now to come out with my plan. I want a drift fence built from my ranch here clear down across the Diamond to where it jumps off. A hundred-mile fence!&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;s a drift fence?&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s just a fence along which cattle will drift south as far as they can go, then drift back. It&rsquo;d have several good uses, but the main one is to keep the cows an&rsquo; calves from driftin&rsquo; down into the brakes.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, the building of a fence even a hundred miles long oughtn&rsquo;t be so difficult.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore it won&rsquo;t. But keepin&rsquo; it up after it&rsquo;s built is where the hell will come in.&rdquo; Jim grasped subtly that here was the crux of the whole matter. &ldquo;There&rsquo;ll be opposition? Down on the Diamond,&rdquo; he rejoined. &ldquo;Shore will. An&rsquo; for that matter all over the range. Even Blodgett is oncertain about fencin&rsquo; the range. You see, a barbed-wire fence in this country is nothin&rsquo; short of murder. An&rsquo; these nesters an&rsquo; homesteaders an&rsquo; backwoodsmen will lose by it. An&rsquo; as for cowboys, Lord! how they hate any kind of a fence! I reckon I used to. But I&rsquo;m ahead of my day. I can see what is needed an&rsquo; what is comin&rsquo;. So, Jim, you can trust me so far as the benefit to ranchers is concerned.&rdquo; &ldquo;Uncle, have you an actual right to fence the range?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore, but it&rsquo;s open to question. If it ever went to law I&rsquo;d have to prove my contention. An&rsquo; whoever sued me would have to show why he was bein&rsquo; hurt. No honest cattleman will ever take such action. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m shore doubtful about any of the homesteaders goin&rsquo; so far.&rdquo; &ldquo;Could you prove your contention, which of course is that you are being robbed?&rdquo; queried Jim, earnestly. &ldquo;Wal, I could, if Ring Locke an&rsquo; some of my cowboys would testify. But they hate the idea like sixty. I&rsquo;ll expect you to find out what they know, an&rsquo; then add evidence of your own.&rdquo; &ldquo;I see. So far so good. How about your moral right?&rdquo; &ldquo;How do you know? Prove it to me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you can see how us big cattlemen will profit by it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, that&rsquo;s easy.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, the little cattlemen will have to stop stealin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;If there&rsquo;s no more to it, you&rsquo;re absolutely right. But won&rsquo;t you close the range or fence off part of it from them?&rdquo; &ldquo;In some sense, yes. But only those draws an&rsquo; canyons I told you about. There&rsquo;s shore water an&rsquo; grass enough in them for ten times the stock they own. Any one of them will admit that. These honest ranchers will just stop appropriatin&rsquo; calves they&rsquo;re not shore they own.&rdquo; &ldquo;The really honest fellows do that now?&rdquo; queried Jim, in surprise. &ldquo;Shore they do, an&rsquo; don&rsquo;t hardly think they&rsquo;re stealin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; the other kind?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, we&rsquo;ll put them up a stump. Some of them will see the handwritin&rsquo; on the wall an&rsquo; quit. An&rsquo; others will cut our fence an&rsquo; go right ahead stealin&rsquo;. That&rsquo;s where our trouble will come in. Mebbe we can stop it in a few years, mebbe not. Some of the ranchers here think it&rsquo;ll start a long hard fight. An&rsquo; that&rsquo;s why they&rsquo;re leavin&rsquo; it to me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nothin&rsquo; much compared to what I&rsquo;ve been through. But I reckon some fence-cuttin&rsquo; an&rsquo; hard ridin&rsquo; an&rsquo; shootin&rsquo; will seem a whole lot to you. Ha! Ha!&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s not very funny,&rdquo; said Jim, soberly. &ldquo;Wal, son, your face was. Don&rsquo;t let my Diamond outfit see you look like that.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m to hide my feelings from them?&rdquo; queried Jim. &ldquo;Nope. You&rsquo;re not to have any, except gettin&rsquo; mad, an&rsquo; when you do that you want to let them see it damn pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh! ... Uncle, I can see this is going to be a lovely easy job.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, it&rsquo;d shore please me if you&rsquo;d find it that.... Jim, I&rsquo;m puttin&rsquo; a lot on you. An&rsquo; I reckon in a way it&rsquo;s selfish.&rdquo; &ldquo;No. Nothing of the kind,&rdquo; replied Jim, hastily. &ldquo;I believe implicitly in you, Uncle Jim. I&rsquo;ll make your ideals and motives mine.... Otherwise, I&rsquo;m scared stiff, but I believe I like the tough job you&rsquo;ve given me&mdash;if I can only make a go of it!&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, the best encouragement I can give you is that I like the way you face it,&rdquo; returned Traft. &ldquo;Shore it&rsquo;s more than I expected, first off.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks, Uncle. That&rsquo;ll help a lot,&rdquo; declared Jim, feelingly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;d like some advice, too.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I never was much on givin&rsquo; advice. Nobody ever follows it.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;d try. But at least you can tell me what you&rsquo;d do, if you had all your knowledge of the range, yet were only my age.&rdquo; &ldquo;Haw! Haw! That&rsquo;s a stumper. Jim, I reckon I&rsquo;m goin&rsquo; to like you, outside of blood relationship. What&rsquo;d I do? Wal, let&rsquo;s see.... First off I&rsquo;d go to town an&rsquo; buy the best cowboy outfit I could get, an&rsquo; that means saddle, bridle, spurs, chaps, sombrero, gun, boots, an&rsquo; so on. That would be for special occasions. Then I&rsquo;d wear most the time just plain overalls. I&rsquo;d pack the gun an&rsquo; begin to learn to shoot it. I&rsquo;d have a little straight talk with the boys who was to work under me an&rsquo; I&rsquo;d let them know I was to be boss. I&rsquo;d always do my share of any an&rsquo; all kinds of work. I&rsquo;d show a disposition not to give any boy a job I wouldn&rsquo;t try to tackle myself. I wouldn&rsquo;t be too nice to take a drink, on occasions where it might be wise, but I&rsquo;d leave drink alone. Also&mdash;an&rsquo; I hardly need to tell you this&mdash;I&rsquo;d leave the town slatterns alone. I&rsquo;d lend my money free, but never my hoss or saddle or spurs. I&rsquo;d always stand the brunt of any trouble directed against my outfit. That&rsquo;ll be hard, for you&rsquo;ll find each an&rsquo; every one of your cowboys keen to do that same thing. Last, an&rsquo; I reckon most particular an&rsquo; hard, I&rsquo;d stand up under the hell the Westerners will make for a tenderfoot. I&rsquo;d run the gauntlet. I&rsquo;d make all the decent fellows like me&mdash;an&rsquo; most of them are decent&mdash;an&rsquo; I&rsquo;d make the others respect me.&rdquo; Jim had the good humor and the nerve to laugh in his uncle&rsquo;s face. &ldquo;Wal,&rdquo; declared Traft, gruffly, with a dubious look at his nephew, &ldquo;I was a-goin&rsquo; to add somethin&rsquo; that I never could do, myself.&rdquo; &ldquo;And what is that?&rdquo; asked Jim, suddenly. &ldquo;Get married pronto.&rdquo; Jim whistled. &ldquo;Heavens! I hope you haven&rsquo;t picked out a Western girl for me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nope. But I&rsquo;m hopin&rsquo; you didn&rsquo;t leave no Eastern girl behind.&rdquo; &ldquo;Luckily I didn&rsquo;t, Uncle.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, that&rsquo;s somethin&rsquo;. You won&rsquo;t find these Western girls special sweet on a tenderfoot. But you&rsquo;re not bad-lookin&rsquo;. You shore have prospects, an&rsquo; if you try you might win one of them.&rdquo; &ldquo;Whew!&mdash;Uncle, that&rsquo;s a sticker. I&rsquo;m afraid I&rsquo;ll have to jump the traces on that one.&rdquo; &ldquo;Jim, I&rsquo;m onreasonable,&rdquo; said Traft, wistfully. &ldquo;But I&rsquo;d shore like to hear the laughter of children round this ranch-house before I die.&rdquo; Jim made the most of that reprieve. He was up at dawn and did not go to bed till late. He went at Ring Locke like a youngster who could not swim in a swift current and was going to hold on or die. But Locke seemed only kind and aloof. He answered some questions; he never vouchsafed any range lore. Jim was sharp enough to find out, however, that Locke had a keen eye for him, and this gave him some grain of comfort. Apparently his uncle never saw him unless he bumped right into him. Jim refused to take all or any of the wonderful horses that were his to own and ride. He rode or tried to ride all the mustangs and bronchos about the ranch. At first he kept account of the times he got thrown, but he gave this up. He certainly did know, however, that he had many bruises and sprains and bumps. Moreover, he grew so saddle-sore that it was agony for him to struggle up on a horse. During this wait he learned every nook and cranny of the ranch, and there were a thousand acres and more, including the timber. He could not avoid coming into occasional contact with other of his uncle&rsquo;s cowboy outfits. And these instances were painful to Jim. He found also, on numerous trips into town, where it was impossible to keep from meeting people, that he was an object of very great interest to everybody, especially the girls and young women. Jim, remembering his uncle&rsquo;s wishes, and being far from a hater of the opposite sex, at the outset made himself most agreeable. Presently he confined himself merely to politeness. The interest he had observed did not extend as far as personal propinquity. One morning he had returned from a disastrous determination to stick on the back of a mustang, and had again taken up a dirty job at the barn, when a farmhand approached him with a message: &ldquo;Boss says the Diamond outfit is in waitin&rsquo; fer you.&rdquo; Jim let them wait awhile, until he got himself thoroughly dirty and tired and cross. Then he limped round to the bunkhouses. His uncle did not appear to be among the bunch of cowboys at the last house. No doubt he had beat a hasty retreat. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll bet the old devil is snickering,&rdquo; muttered Jim. He approached the young men, and before he got even close he saw they constituted a remarkable group. They had a singular similarity, and yet upon near scrutiny they were not at all alike. &ldquo;How do, boys!&rdquo; he said, bluntly, as he halted before them. &ldquo;So you&rsquo;re the Diamond outfit I&rsquo;m to boss? ... Well, I&rsquo;m not a damn bit gladder to meet you than you are to meet me.&rdquo; Most of them greeted him with a word or nod. Jim found that he had not exactly spoken the truth, for he certainly sustained thrills when he looked these cowboys over. There did not appear to be one as old as he was, nor, for that matter, as big, though several were as tall. Lithe-bodied, long of limb and bow-legged, with small round hips and wide shoulders, lean and sharp of face, bronzed and sunburnt, with expressionless eyes like gimlets they certainly belonged to a striking and unique class. &ldquo;Who was your last foreman?&rdquo; asked Jim. After quite a long silence one of them replied, &ldquo;Jud Blue.&rdquo; &ldquo;Is he here with you now?&rdquo; &ldquo;Reckon no one has noticed him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Where is he?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, if he&rsquo;s where he ought to be he&rsquo;s in hell,&rdquo; came the laconic reply. &ldquo;How so?&rdquo; flashed Jim. &ldquo;Jud was shot last month down on the Diamond.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shot! ... Was it an accident?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore was, for him. But whoever did it was lookin&rsquo; pretty straight.&rdquo; Jim did not betray the shock this intelligence gave him, but he certainly made note of another circumstance his uncle had not imparted. &ldquo;Which one of you has been longest with my uncle?&rdquo; he questioned. &ldquo;Hump Stevens, heah, was in the first Diamond outfit. Six years ago, wasn&rsquo;t it, Hump?&rdquo; &ldquo;Round aboot thet,&rdquo; drawled a tall tawny cowboy who was stoop-shouldered. &ldquo;Stevens, then, ought to be foreman of this outfit,&rdquo; returned Jim. &ldquo;And after him every one of you according to your service. Well, let&rsquo;s understand each other right here. I certainly am not stuck on the job and think I&rsquo;m the last fellow on earth to tackle it. But my uncle has put it on me. He wants to leave his property to me. And I won&rsquo;t have it unless I can deserve it. And that means make good at ranching from the ground up.&rdquo; Blank, still faces baffled Jim. It was impossible to tell whether or not these cowboys were in the least impressed. They certainly thought he was a liar. &ldquo;You can lay off till Monday morning,&rdquo; added Jim, curtly. And before he started to limp away he gathered that his first order to them had been received with a pleasant surprise. Perhaps the ensuing hour was the most profoundly thoughtful of any since he had decided to embark upon this adventure. What an unknown quantity the Diamond outfit! He had needed only one look at these devil-may-care boys to realize it. Cowboys were not wholly strangers to him. These, however, were the dyed-in-the-wool range product. They were potential chain lightning and firebrand. He was conscious of admiration, dread, and an acute desire to make friends with them. After meeting them he realized he could not expect any material help from Ring Locke or his uncle. The matter was personal. Wherefore he carefully kept out of their way for a day and a half. On Saturday afternoon he went to town, and he had not been there long before he heard that the Diamond outfit was painting some very vivid red. Jim laughed. After a while, however, it grew monotonous. And when he happened to encounter Miss Blodgett in one of the stores and have her subtly refer to his cowboys he became irritated. Must the whole town take up the situation which his uncle had precipitated? A second look into Miss Blodgett&rsquo;s hazel eyes confirmed the suspicion. She was the nicest of the girls he had met so far, a tall, rangy girl who looked like she could ride a horse. She had freckles and brown curls and was rather pretty. &ldquo;I met Curly Prentiss in the post-office,&rdquo; she announced, after he had greeted her. &ldquo;Who&rsquo;s he?&rdquo; asked Jim, though he had an inkling. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t you know Curly yet?&rdquo; she rejoined, merrily. &ldquo;Well, he&rsquo;s one of your Diamonds.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, I see! Fact is I don&rsquo;t know any of them. Was there anything particular about your meeting him today?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not so very&mdash;for Curly. He used to ride for us. Finest cowboy in the world. But when he drinks&mdash;well, his tongue wags.&rdquo; &ldquo;Reckon it wagged about me on this occasion?&rdquo; &ldquo;It sure did.... Mr. Jim, have you anyone to write home to your mother and sister?&rdquo; Jim eyed her with misgivings. These Western girls were as deep as the cowboys. Jim conceived an idea, however, that Miss Blodgett was friendly, or she would never have made that remark. &ldquo;You mean about the disposition of my remains?&rdquo; rejoined Jim, dryly. &ldquo;Thanks, but I&rsquo;m going to see that will not be necessary. I&rsquo;ve fallen in love with my new job. In fact, I like the West&mdash;and everybody out here. Good afternoon, Miss Blodgett.&rdquo; And Jim went on, muttering to himself: &ldquo;Dog-gone it! They&rsquo;re all after my scalp, even the girls. I hope I&rsquo;m not going to get sore.&rdquo; Presently he went into a pool-room to buy a smoke. The place was fairly well crowded, and at the very first table he saw a cowboy he recognized as one of his Diamond outfit. He was in the act of making a shot. But he straightened up. His fine tanned young face was flushed and there were other indications that he had been drinking. &ldquo;Boss, I ain&rsquo;t doin&rsquo; nothin&rsquo;,&rdquo; he said, slowly. &ldquo;Who said you were?&rdquo; returned Jim, realizing that he must have looked sharply at the boy. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re lookin&rsquo; for Curly?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes,&rdquo; answered Jim, suddenly inspired. &ldquo;Sheriff Bray just collared Curly. Honest, boss, Curly was behavin&rsquo; himself strick proper. But Bray has got it in for us Diamond fellars. An&rsquo; Curly&rdquo;&mdash;here the cowboy came round the table to be closer to Jim&mdash;&ldquo;Curly was pretty drunk an&rsquo; noisy. It was a chance for Bray, who&rsquo;d never had nerve enough any other time. This sheriff is a four-flusher, boss. He&rsquo;s never had one of us in jail yet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Which way did they go?&rdquo; asked Jim. &ldquo;Down the street. The jail&rsquo;s across the tracks.&rdquo; Jim hurried out and in the direction advised, not certain of his position in the matter. Still, he did not take kindly to the idea of one of his cowboys going to jail. Moreover, he had met Sheriff Bray and had not been greatly impressed in that individual&rsquo;s favor. Jim, while crossing the tracks, espied Bray dragging a reluctant and protesting cowboy along the station platform, followed by a small crowd. Running ahead, Jim intercepted them. This cowboy he also recognized, a tall handsome fellow with curly yellow hair, just now very red in the face, but not so drunk as Jim had been led to suppose. &ldquo;Hold on, Sheriff!&rdquo; called Jim, as he confronted them. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ve got one of my boys. What&rsquo;s the charge?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hullo!&rdquo; gruffly returned Bray. He was a burly man, thick-featured, with a bluish cast of countenance, and he wore his sheriff&rsquo;s badge and gun rather prominently. &ldquo;Oh, it&rsquo;s young Mr. Traft. I didn&rsquo;t know you.... Wal, this boy was gettin&rsquo; a little too obstreperous to suit me. So I&rsquo;m runnin&rsquo; him in.&rdquo; &ldquo;Obstreperous! What do you mean by that?&rdquo; demanded Jim, arriving at his decision. &ldquo;Wal, thet&rsquo;s what I call it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Curly, what&rsquo;d you do?&rdquo; inquired Jim, of the red-faced, blue-eyed boy. &ldquo;Boss, I was singin&rsquo;,&rdquo; asserted Curly. &ldquo;This heah one-hoss occifer sings in&mdash;choir an&rsquo; he thinks he&rsquo;s&mdash;only singer.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you can sing in jail,&rdquo; declared the sheriff, with a gleam in his eye. &ldquo;Bray, I reckon you&rsquo;d better not run Curly in,&rdquo; said Jim, coolly. &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s walk along across and get away from this crowd. I&rsquo;ll take Curly around the block.&rdquo; &ldquo;Say, for a tenderfoot you&rsquo;re startin&rsquo; right in to play a high hand,&rdquo; sneered Bray. All the same he had his doubts, which Jim was quick to observe. &ldquo;Correct, Bray,&rdquo; rejoined Jim, as he took Curly&rsquo;s arm. Between them they walked him away from the curious onlookers, and round a corner to the entrance of the jail. Here Curly&rsquo;s face was a study. Manifestly before Jim&rsquo;s arrival he had surrendered to the majesty of the law, but this amazing champion in the shape of his boss had galvanized him. &ldquo;Bray, there ain&rsquo;t none of Diamond outfit ever been&mdash;in jail. It&rsquo;s disgrash,&rdquo; he asserted, belligerently. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll vouch for him, Sheriff,&rdquo; added Jim. &ldquo;Prentiss, you come along,&rdquo; ordered Bray, roughly. By this time Jim&rsquo;s blood had grown a little hot. He had recalled what his uncle had said about Bray and thought he might just as well face the issue. He jerked Curly free from the sheriff, and interposed himself between them. &ldquo;If you had any charge against Curly I wouldn&rsquo;t interfere. But you haven&rsquo;t. Why, he isn&rsquo;t half drunk.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll arrest you both for resistin&rsquo; an officer,&rdquo; threatened Bray, his hand going to his hip. Jim saw the action, followed it with his eye. &ldquo;So you&rsquo;d throw your gun on us,&rdquo; he said, with derision. &ldquo;Boss, let go an&rsquo; stand aside. This heah ain&rsquo;t funny no more,&rdquo; spoke up Curly. The change in him, the ring in his voice, made Jim jump, but he did not release the cowboy. &ldquo;No, Curly, I&rsquo;m responsible here,&rdquo; he replied. Bray had subtly altered, which fact Jim grasped to have to do with Curly&rsquo;s sudden menace. &ldquo;Wal, Traft, I&rsquo;ll let him go in your care,&rdquo; he growled. &ldquo;But I&rsquo;m givin&rsquo; you a hunch. Prentiss said somethin&rsquo;. This Diamond outfit ain&rsquo;t funny no more.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks, Bray. I consider that a compliment to me. Come on, Curly.&rdquo; Jim walked the cowboy down the block and up a side street until they got out of the center of the town. Neither he nor Curly broke silence during this walk. Finally Jim halted on a corner. &ldquo;Curly, will you go back to the ranch? I&rsquo;d go with you, but I&rsquo;ve errands to do.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, are you orderin&rsquo; me?&rdquo; queried the cowboy. &ldquo;No, I&rsquo;m asking you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Then I ain&rsquo;t a-goin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Very well, then. I&rsquo;ll have to make it an order. Will you go now?&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckon. The Diamond ain&rsquo;t disobeyin&rsquo; orders. But what&rsquo;d you want to go do this heah trick for?&rdquo; &ldquo;Trick? I&rsquo;ve only kept you out of jail.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. An&rsquo; the outfit will be sore at me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Curly, I don&rsquo;t understand you,&rdquo; protested Jim. &ldquo;They ain&rsquo;t a-goin&rsquo; to stand for me bein&rsquo; friends with a tenderfoot boss.&rdquo; Jim began to get a glimmering. The tall cowboy seemed pained over this little service. He looked most disapprovingly at Jim. &ldquo;Curly, you needn&rsquo;t let that embarrass you.&rdquo; Apparently Curly could not help being embarrassed. He wheeled and strode away. Half across the street he turned. &ldquo;Boss, I forgot. I&rsquo;m in an orful fix,&rdquo; he said, and strode back. &ldquo;My gurl&rsquo;s in town. I haven&rsquo;t laid eyes on her for two months. Shore it won&rsquo;t be safe to let it go longer.&rdquo; &ldquo;Curly, are you asking me to explain to your girl or to allow you to come back to town?&rdquo; queried Jim. &ldquo;Reckon I was just tellin&rsquo; you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, I dare say you are in a fix. What do you want of me?&rdquo; rejoined Jim, who divined that the cowboy did not like to ask a favor. &ldquo;It&rsquo;d never do for you to see Nancy. I lost one gurl that way.&rdquo; &ldquo;Curly, I&rsquo;ll help you out. Promise you&rsquo;ll not take another drink today. Then walk out to the ranch and walk back tonight. That&rsquo;ll sober you. And you can see your girl.&rdquo; Curly swore. He bent a strange blue gaze upon Jim. &ldquo;I reckon there ain&rsquo;t no help for it,&rdquo; he muttered, as if declaring an inevitable fact to himself. Then he strode away. Jim scarcely knew how to take this last declaration and he went back uptown, pondering over it. These cowboys were certainly going to be problems. They were like children. But he had had a most pleasing reaction from his first encounter with one of the Diamond outfit. Jim returned to his errands, which took him up and down the main thoroughfare of Flagerstown, and therefore past the saloons and pool-halls. It struck him that the town was growing rather lively as evening approached. All the hitching-rails were crowded with saddle-horses, many of which took Jim&rsquo;s appreciative eye. Jim was entering the hotel, where he expected to meet his uncle and ride home with him, when he was detained by another member of the Diamond, who barred his way obviously if not rudely. Two other cowboys drew back. &ldquo;Excuse me, Mister Traft. I&rsquo;m Hack Jocelyn, an&rsquo; I&rsquo;m wantin&rsquo; a word with you.&rdquo; He was cool, insolent, and something else Jim could not name. &ldquo;Aren&rsquo;t you one of my cowboys?&rdquo; asked Jim. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve been ridin&rsquo; with the Diamond, if thet&rsquo;s what you mean. But I ain&rsquo;t shore I&rsquo;m stayin&rsquo; with the outfit.&rdquo; Jim had been told by no less an authority than Ring Locke that horses and men could not separate the Diamond outfit. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re not, eh? Well, you want to be pretty sure, or you won&rsquo;t be riding for it. What do you want?&rdquo; Jocelyn appeared to be gauging Jim. &ldquo;I was in Babbitt&rsquo;s an&rsquo; they told me they&rsquo;d sent out a wagon-load of barbed wire to the ranch. Fer the Diamond outfit! An&rsquo; I calls him a liar.&rdquo; &ldquo;Then you&rsquo;ll have to apologize. It was for the Diamond. And there&rsquo;s a carload more ordered.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell you say!&rdquo; ejaculated Jocelyn, in amaze and gathering anger. &ldquo;An&rsquo; what&rsquo;s it fer?&rdquo; &ldquo;None of your business, Jocelyn,&rdquo; retorted Jim. &ldquo;If you&rsquo;d asked me civilly I&rsquo;d have told you.&rdquo; &ldquo;But barbed wire is most used fer fences!&rdquo; exclaimed the cowboy. His two comrades edged closer until they were beside him, watchful, hiding their feelings, if they had any. &ldquo;An&rsquo; nobody in Gawd&rsquo;s world would reckon the Diamond&rsquo;d have anythin&rsquo; to do with thet!&rdquo; &ldquo;The Diamond is in for some new experience, Jocelyn. And you may as well know fence-building is one of them.&rdquo; &ldquo;Haw! Haw! It shore is. A tenderfoot dude foreman! Then barbed-wire fence! My Gawd! what&rsquo;s the range comin&rsquo; to?&rdquo; Jocelyn had turned to his companions, to whom, in fact, his exclamation had been directed. Jim shot out a hand and spun him around like a top. &ldquo;Did you call me a tenderfoot dude foreman?&rdquo; he queried, and despite his temper he was quick-witted enough to ascertain that Jocelyn was not armed. Otherwise he would wisely have restrained himself altogether. &ldquo;Wal, Mister Traft, I reckon I did,&rdquo; he drawled. He expressed the usual cowboy nonchalance, but there was also a vindictive quality in his words, if not their content. Jim knocked him flat. He had not calculated consequences. In accepting his uncle&rsquo;s job he had burned his bridges behind him. But when Jim saw Jocelyn lying there, then slowly rising, his hand to his face, which was black as a thundercloud, he awoke to another sensation. He would not, however, have recalled the blow. &ldquo;Jocelyn, you&rsquo;re fired,&rdquo; he said, as coolly as the cowboy had spoken. &ldquo;But if you come out to the ranch and apologize to me I&rsquo;ll take you on again.&rdquo; &ldquo;You better be packin&rsquo; a gun,&rdquo; declared Jocelyn, darkly. &ldquo;Aw, shut up, if you can&rsquo;t talk sense,&rdquo; returned Jim, in disgust. &ldquo;You insulted me. And if you&rsquo;re not man enough to own up to it you can bet there&rsquo;s no place on the Diamond for you.&rdquo; Jocelyn&rsquo;s two friends laid hold of him and drew him away. Whereupon Jim turned to enter the hotel, where among several persons who had been spectators of the little byplay were his uncle and Ring Locke. &ldquo;Hello! Say, I&rsquo;m sorry you happened to see that,&rdquo; said Jim, regretfully. &ldquo;But, Uncle, he just made me boil.&rdquo; &ldquo;Come inside,&rdquo; rejoined Traft, and when the three of them were out of earshot of bystanders he turned to Locke. &ldquo;Ring, he called Hack&rsquo;s bluff. An&rsquo; mebbe he didn&rsquo;t poke that puncher&rsquo;s snoot! I damn near choked myself to keep from yellin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Uncle!&rdquo; exclaimed Jim, as surprised at this speech as at Traft&rsquo;s glee. &ldquo;Son, the only fault I can locate in you so far is you talk too quick an&rsquo; too much. It&rsquo;ll get you in serious trouble.&rdquo; &ldquo;But I nearly burst at that,&rdquo; expostulated Jim. Ring Locke shook his lean hawklike head forebodingly. &ldquo;Wal, it&rsquo;s six fer me an&rsquo; half a dozen fer the other,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s bad an&rsquo; good. More good, I&rsquo;d say. If the new foreman of the Diamond had stood fer that&mdash;wal, he couldn&rsquo;t have had a chance. Mebbe the boys put Hack up to it. If so he&rsquo;ll be as nice as pie an&rsquo; come back to square himself. If not&mdash;&rdquo; Here Locke shook his head gravely. &ldquo;Ring, I&rsquo;ll bet you four bits he&rsquo;ll be out here tomorrow,&rdquo; said Traft. &ldquo;But suppose he doesn&rsquo;t come?&rdquo; &ldquo;Can&rsquo;t we fill his place?&rdquo; asked Jim, anxiously. &ldquo;Nope. We can&rsquo;t fill the place of any of thet outfit,&rdquo; rejoined Locke. &ldquo;But I was thinkin&rsquo; of what it&rsquo;d mean... . Young man, this same Hack Jocelyn has shot fellars fer less.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s true, son,&rdquo; corroborated Traft, somberly. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s the worst of it. This gang of yours has made way with nine men since they rode for me. Six years! Shore some of them were damn good riddance.&rdquo; &ldquo;More&rsquo;n nine, boss,&rdquo; corrected Locke. &ldquo;Lonestar Holliday got drove out of Texas fer a shootin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;What!&rdquo; gasped Jim. &ldquo;Those fine clean boys murderers?&rdquo; &ldquo;Jim, you&rsquo;re out West now,&rdquo; said his uncle, testily. &ldquo;When two men get into an argument or quarrel an&rsquo; draw&mdash;it ain&rsquo;t murder if one is killed. We couldn&rsquo;t run the range without cowboys an&rsquo; they&rsquo;re shore a tough crowd of young roosters.... Ring, fetch round the buckboard an&rsquo; we&rsquo;ll go home.&rdquo; On the way out Traft dilated on the serious and uncertain side of range life. Jim realized that his education on the West had but just begun. He had not been ignorant of facts, but they seemed vastly significant and perturbing at close hand. During the ride out and at supper he maintained silence. Later, when he had recovered from the effect of this first clash, he could not feel that he would have desired to have met it in any other way. But neither his uncle nor Ring Locke could understand his feelings. Jim himself found them rather complicated. He had been furious, then frightened, then cold. And now, instead of wanting to go home to Missouri, he surrendered still more to Arizona. &ldquo;Bud Chalfack,&rdquo; anounced Traft, with a chuckle. &ldquo;Who&rsquo;s Bud?&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll see pronto. He&rsquo;s the peacemaker of the outfit. Bud&rsquo;s a diplomat, the slickest, coolest hand on the range. Whenever anyone is in wrong, Bud is elected to set him right.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh,&rdquo; returned Jim, dubiously. He watched the cowboy stroll up the lane and down the path to the porch. Bud appeared to be a little fellow, but sturdy, bow-legged, and otherwise suggestive of long association with horses. He was smoking a cigarette, which he threw away as he reached the steps. He had an open, guileless countenance and he reminded Jim of a rosy-faced cherub. &ldquo;Good evenin&rsquo;, Mr. Traft, an&rsquo; howdy, boss!&rdquo; he said, cheerfully. &ldquo;May I set down?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore, Bud,&rdquo; replied Traft, genially. &ldquo;Reckon you look sort of weak. What ails you? Too much town?&rdquo; &ldquo;Weak? I couldn&rsquo;t fork a bench at a feast, let alone a hoss. We been wrastlin&rsquo; all day with Hack. But you ought to see the other fellars. They&rsquo;re laid out.&rdquo; &ldquo;Is Hack here?&rdquo; asked Jim, quietly. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s down at the bunkhouse. I been sent fer him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why didn&rsquo;t he come himself?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, fust off he wouldn&rsquo;t come an&rsquo; I reckon now he ain&rsquo;t able.&rdquo; Bud&rsquo;s grave infantile face was averted for a moment, during which Jim shot a quick glance at his uncle, to be rewarded by a wink. Matters were progressing favorably. Jim drew a breath of relief and gave in to the fun of the situation. &ldquo;What ails him? Did he get drunk last night? Sick this morning?&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, I reckon both. But it ain&rsquo;t them. We was sorta put out with Hack. Shore we didn&rsquo;t mean to let him bust up the Diamond. An&rsquo; he was daid set on thet. So after arguyin&rsquo; all day we got tired, an&rsquo;&mdash;wal, Hack&rsquo;s in bed.&rdquo; &ldquo;I see. Then he can&rsquo;t come over to apologize and get his job back. Or did he want to do it?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Hack shore wanted the job back all right, but he hated the terms. He said, &lsquo;Jest tell the boss I&rsquo;ll apologize if he&rsquo;ll swear I won&rsquo;t have to dig no holes fer fence-posts.&rsquo;&rdquo; Jim had difficulty in restraining a shout. &ldquo;Bud, I&rsquo;m afraid Hack will have to dig holes along with the rest of us. I sure expect to,&rdquo; replied Jim, gravely. The cowboy looked incredulously to Mr. Traft for corroboration of that statement. &ldquo;Bud, it&rsquo;s up to Jim. You&rsquo;ll have to fight it out together. You can tell the boys, though, that Jim will not give them anythin&rsquo; he won&rsquo;t tackle himself.&rdquo; &ldquo;All right, sir. Thet&rsquo;s shore fairer&rsquo;n any foreman I ever rode under.... But Hack won&rsquo;t never dig no fence post-holes. We had it out with him an&rsquo; so I may as well tell you. He yells at us, &lsquo;Hell! if I gotta dig holes, let &rsquo;em be graves fer thet Cibeque bunch of calf thieves.&rsquo;&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal!&rdquo; the old rancher exclaimed, and glanced from Bud to see how his nephew would take that. Jim did not feel like shouting with laughter over it. Nevertheless, he concealed his consternation. &ldquo;Bud, wasn&rsquo;t Hack just raving?&rdquo; he asked. &ldquo;Shore he was ravin&rsquo;. An&rsquo; he&rsquo;d reason, too. Boss, shore your uncle has told you what this drift fence will do?&rdquo; &ldquo;He hasn&rsquo;t told me anything,&rdquo; replied Jim. &ldquo;Wal, some of us will have to, then,&rdquo; said Bud. &ldquo;I ain&rsquo;t quite agreed with Hack an&rsquo; the other boys of the Diamond. But I&rsquo;ve only been ridin&rsquo; hyar fer a couple of years. Hack swears we&rsquo;ll have to fight. He knows Seth Haverly an&rsquo; his outfit of the Cibeque. Bad hombres, he called them. An&rsquo; Slinger Dunn don&rsquo;t need no introduction &rsquo;round Flag. You can see his trade-mark in more&rsquo;n one place.... Some of the boys agree with Hack. We&rsquo;ll have hell buildin&rsquo; thet fence, an&rsquo; wuss&rsquo;n hell when we get it done.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bud, is the Diamond game to build that drift fence?&rdquo; queried Jim, with sarcasm. &ldquo;You bet your life it is,&rdquo; flashed Bud. &ldquo;Are you going to stick together?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, we reckon nothin&rsquo; but death can bust the outfit.&rdquo; &ldquo;If I left it to a vote, how many of you would be for building the fence?&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, we&rsquo;ve already voted. This mawnin&rsquo;. An&rsquo; Hack was the only fellar to drop a black mark in the hat. Leastways, we think it was Hack.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m glad to hear that. Now how do you stand on the moral issue?&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, I don&rsquo;t just savy.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re all cattlemen in the making. Is it right or wrong?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, fact is, we&rsquo;re not all shore. But the most of us believe that Mr. Traft knows more an&rsquo; sees farther, an&rsquo; wouldn&rsquo;t never do no homesteader or little cattleman a low-down trick. We&rsquo;re goin&rsquo; to believe he&rsquo;s right an&rsquo; stand by him.&rdquo; &ldquo;But you all think he shouldn&rsquo;t have saddled this job on to a tenderfoot nephew?&rdquo; queried Jim, penetratingly. &ldquo;Wal, I&mdash;I reckon we do,&rdquo; replied Bud, growing red in the face. &ldquo;There!&rdquo; cried Jim, triumphantly, to his amused uncle. &ldquo;See what you&rsquo;ve done! ... Come, Bud, I&rsquo;ll walk down to the bunkhouse with you.&rdquo; He found half a dozen of the boys there, but missed Curly Prentiss. Hack Jocelyn lay on a bunk under a window, the light of which showed him rather badly bruised up. He had one black eye, which he endeavored to hide. &ldquo;Hack, you get your job back, but it was a half-hearted apology,&rdquo; said Jim. &ldquo;Boss, I&rsquo;d never give in but fer this low-down lousy outfit,&rdquo; replied Jocelyn. &ldquo;An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m tellin&rsquo; you straight I won&rsquo;t dig no fence-post holes. I&rsquo;ll cut an&rsquo; haul posts. I&rsquo;ll cook an&rsquo; wash, an&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll pack water an&rsquo; run errands.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hack, you don&rsquo;t look like you were sorry you insulted me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, I don&rsquo;t reckon it no insult. I was only bein&rsquo; funny. But you shore do wear nice store clothes, don&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Can&rsquo;t I wear overalls all week and put on clean shirt and pants without being a dude?&rdquo; inquired Jim. &ldquo;Wal, it depends on the pants an&rsquo; the shirt.&rdquo; &ldquo;Matter of taste, eh? Well, I&rsquo;ll wear out my St. Louis clothes pronto.&rdquo; Jocelyn peered hard out of his unclosed eye, at this new specimen of range foreman, and then gave up with a disgusted grunt. Next morning before sunrise the Diamond rode out upon their momentous adventure. Thirty saddle and pack horses, one four-horse wagon hauling wire and tools, and the chuck-wagon, made quite a cavalcade. Ring Locke saw them off, but Traft did not show up. Locke&rsquo;s last word was one of commendation at Jim&rsquo;s wise move to pitch the first camp five miles out of town. Jim intended to drop bales of wire all along the way, then work back from camp. &ldquo;But I&rsquo;m tellin&rsquo; you,&rdquo; protested Curly, at length. &ldquo;He&rsquo;ll eat out of your hand till he gets a chance to kick your brains out. Thet ain&rsquo;t no hoss fer the boss of the Diamond. It&rsquo;s an orful disgrace.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll have to put up with a lot, Curly,&rdquo; said Jim, patiently. &ldquo;I can ride this nag.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ride him! There ain&rsquo;t no cowpuncher in this outfit who can do it.&rdquo; &ldquo;But, Curly, I have ridden him.&rdquo; &ldquo;He&rsquo;s only foolin&rsquo;. Boss, he&rsquo;ll pile you up aboot tomorrow.&rdquo; Jim started the work just after the noon hour and it beat any circus he ever attended. He dug the first hole himself, with his cowboys gaping around. &ldquo;There!&rdquo; he exclaimed, in satisfaction. His fellow cowboys whooped so wildly in reply that Jim felt constrained to believe Jocelyn&rsquo;s pessimistic augury. Then, under Jim&rsquo;s orders, they set to work, and they yelled and swore, and kept up a constant harangue with one another. Jim had three of them dragging in long poles, which were cut into seven-foot lengths, five feet of which were to stand above ground. Jim had thought out his plan and it bade fair to work. Bud Chalback, Lonestar Holliday, and Hump Stevens had volunteered to handle the wire, which, next to post-hole digging, seemed to be the most obnoxious to these aristocrats of the range. Fortunately for Jim, it was not necessary to build the drift fence in a straight line. A general direction to the south, keeping to levels and the easiest way, was the rule. Jim marked the line and the holes for a certain distance, then went back to help in all details of the labor. It had happened that not so many months ago he had built a barbed-wire fence round his father&rsquo;s farm in Missouri. He had not forgotten that. His father had not only been an exacting employer, but he knew how to go about fence-building. So Jim had a distinct advantage over his cowboys, who certainly had never had any share in such work. All the same, they found fault with every single detail of Jim&rsquo;s plan and execution, sometimes so guilelessly and with such apparent sincerity that he knew they had begun their mischief. He took especial care, however, in every instance to explain and prove to them the fallacy of their criticisms. Here was too good a chance to miss. The first day ended, and a dirty, sweaty, hungry string of cowboys walked back to camp. &ldquo;Who&rsquo;d a thunk it? Hoofin&rsquo; it to camp! As if barbed-wire-fence buildin&rsquo; wasn&rsquo;t enough. Boys, we&rsquo;d be better off in the pen at Yuma,&rdquo; declared Cherry Winters, throwing his sombrero. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a helluva good thing none of us has to cook on this job,&rdquo; said Uphill Frost. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ve all got to thank the boss fer thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Up,&rdquo; replied some one, &ldquo;we&rsquo;d liefer you was the cook, &rsquo;cause then we&rsquo;d soon be daid.&rdquo; &ldquo;Look aheah, Jackson Way,&rdquo; retorted Frost. &ldquo;You hungry-lookin&rsquo; jack rabbit! I kin beat you makin&rsquo; sourdough biscuits any day.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore you can. I ain&rsquo;t no cook.&rdquo; And so the badinage went on. Jim shut his ears when he was a little way off, to avoid hearing their facetious remarks about him, but on occasions he caught some of it. The amazing day ended with Jim&rsquo;s adding a lame back and blistered hands to his other ills. They had camped in the open field, where a few straggling pines had escaped the lumbermen, and the site was far from pretty. Jim unrolled his tarpaulin under one of them. He had never slept out in the open in his life. The cowboys would have laughed if they had seen him in his room at the ranch-house, struggling over the rolling and roping of that bed. The cook had been highly recommended to Jim, by no less a person than himself, and that, too, in writing. He claimed to hear fairly well, but he was dumb. Shot in the throat once, by a vicious cowboy! &ldquo;Say, when is thet cadiverous galoot a-goin&rsquo; to yell, &lsquo;Come an&rsquo; get it&rsquo;?&rdquo; demanded Hack. &ldquo;Anybody know what his handle is?&rdquo; asked another. &ldquo;Boys, our cook&rsquo;s name is Jeff Davis,&rdquo; announced Jim, importantly. &ldquo;He hails from Alabama. He can&rsquo;t talk, but he wrote he could hear fairly well.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why cain&rsquo;t he talk?&rdquo; asked Hack. &ldquo;A dumb cook! Holy Jupiter! We&rsquo;re Jonahed fer keeps!&rdquo; &ldquo;Fine. He cain&rsquo;t cuss the daylights out of us.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, if he can cook&mdash;O-Kay!&rdquo; &ldquo;Thar&rsquo;s enough rebels in this heah outfit now without havin&rsquo; a rebel cook,&rdquo; growled another. &ldquo;Boys,&rdquo; added Jim, by way of answer to all these remarks, &ldquo;Jeff claims to have been shot in the throat by a vicious cowboy. Made dumb forever. Think of it!&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, he might have been one of these fellars who talk too much,&rdquo; declared Hack, significantly. The sudden and violent beating of a tin pan appeared to be Jeff&rsquo;s call to supper. Uphill Frost, who had fallen into a doze, leaped up with a yell, &ldquo;Injuns!&rdquo; He was the last to reach the chuck-wagon, perhaps by the fraction of a second. The things they said to the grave-faced cook, as he filled their plates and cups, were enough, Jim thought, to make a dumb man swear. Probably he alone caught a curious little gleam in Jeff&rsquo;s deep-set eyes. That gave Jim food for thought. Then the members of the Diamond stood around, or sat cross-legged like Indians on the ground. The ensuing silence fell like a mantle. It seemed so beneficent and wonderful that Jim imagined he had been suddenly transported to another world. After supper they had a camp fire around which they sat and smoked. Jim enjoyed that hour. The infinite and various moods of the cowboys seemed to have flagged. Then one by one they, some without removing their boots, rolled in their tarpaulins. Jim took off some of his clothes, and when he stretched out in his bed with a groan he felt that he would never move again. How delicious that bed! He burned and ached all over, and tired as he was could not soon go to sleep. The canopy of white stars seemed so wonderful and strange. The air, which had turned cold with the night wind down off the mountain, blew over his face. Jim had heard his first coyote chorus at the ranch, so he was in a way prepared for another at close range. Evidently this visiting bunch sat round in a half-circle, just behind his bed, and barked, yelped, whined their wild concatenations. He enjoyed the music for a while, but he conceived that he might have a murderous instinct develop. He endeavored to enumerate the especial happenings and remarks of the day. Impossible! It had been his intention to keep a diary. He did not think he could do the opportunity justice, though he would try. Besides, it would never do to record many of the speeches of these range-riders. Suddenly he felt something tugging at the back of his bed, at the blankets under him. It made him start violently and instinctively frightened him. A coyote! Did the scavengering beggars steal that close? He yelled with much meaning but poor articulation. &ldquo;GIOUT!&rdquo; Then he popped up. He could see a few feet back of his bed. Nothing there! Perhaps it might have been a gopher or a rattlesnake about which the boys had remarked after supper. With a boot he beat around back of his coat, which he had folded up for a pillow. Then with a still shaking hand he felt back there. And it came in contact with a rope. It had been tied to an end of the blanket over him, and this had been drawn half off. &ldquo;Well, by gosh!&rdquo; he muttered. From all around strange sounds arose. He sat there amazed until he grasped the situation. The cowboys had played their first trick on him and were now trying to hold in a fiendish and bursting glee. Presently from way over near the chuck-wagon broke out a raucous: &ldquo;Haw! Haw! Haw!&rdquo; Jim untied the rope and flung the end as far away as he could. Sons-of-guns, he called them to himself! But nevertheless, he was tickled. They would make life miserable for him. Nevertheless, he took this as an augury of good luck. His uncle had assured him that if they played tricks on him there was hope. If they suffered him in silence and let him severely alone the case was hopeless. Jim lay back happy, despite his chagrin at being scared half out of his wits, and went to sleep. Next morning he hobbled around cheerfully without mentioning the incident and went to work. That turned out to be a trying day, particularly in keeping at it. Upon returning to camp he washed his blistered hands, and then thinking to rest a little before supper, he sought his bed. But it was gone. At first he imagined he had mistaken the place, but he soon reassured himself that he was right about the location. After a few moments he discovered his bed-roll high up in the pine tree, swinging by the rope. Jim swore under his breath. If he had to climb that tree, crippled as he was, it would afford these devilish cowboys a treat. He just would not attempt it. They stood and sat around, waiting for call to supper, and obviously they were aware of his predicament &ldquo;Oh, were I a little bir-r-rd,&rdquo; sang one of them. Jim decided he would make one gigantic bluff. He had been pretty lucky so far. Why not play that to the limit? Whereupon he went to his pack, and taking up his Colt, he aimed steadily at the rope above his head and shot. He cut the rope. As he gasped, the bed-roll fell with a heavy thump. Jim flipped the gun in a way he had learned through a week&rsquo;s practice, and carelessly tossed it over to his pack. Then approaching the cowboys he said, seriously, &ldquo;I sure hope none of you hombres make me throw a gun on you.&rdquo; They seemed to be a wide-eyed and stricken group. &ldquo;Boss, I shore ain&rsquo;t goin&rsquo; to do thet little thing,&rdquo; spoke up Curly. So they had to score another for the tenderfoot. That night around the camp fire they were thoughtful, and whispered behind Jim&rsquo;s back. He went to bed knowing the war was on, and that he was in for utter rout. Ring Locke had said cowboys when pressed would do anything under the sun. Jim had thought it all out, and he could only regulate his conduct according to theirs. The main issue for him was to earn their respect. Nothing happened that night nor the next day, but on the following night he was awakened by a terrible crash and jar. He lay there shaking. The stars above showed more numerously. The pine tree must have been struck by lightning, for its branches no longer sheltered him. It had fallen. But there could not have been a stroke of lightning, because the sky gave no sign at all of storm. The cowboys had provided the lightning. Probably they had sawed the tree nearly through during the day, when he was absent, and at night they had pulled it down. The tips of one of the branches lay across Jim&rsquo;s feet, which he moved after some effort. Nice gentle cowboys! Not for a long while did Jim go to sleep, after that. By the end of the week they had finished the drift fence townward to where it joined Traft&rsquo;s ranch. They had also about finished Jim. Saturday night he spent alone in camp, except for the cook, who plainly showed a solicitude for Jim. Sunday he was to have gone in to see Uncle Jim, but he never left the camp. Late afternoon the cowboys began to straggle back in twos and threes, some of them still pretty drunk. Jim resented that. He watched them come, careful to see who were the sober ones. Bud returned, holding Hack Jocelyn in his saddle; and Curly performed a like office for Cherry Winters. Drink manifestly was Hack&rsquo;s besetting sin, for he presented a somber and ugly figure. On the other hand, Cherry was funny. Curly handled him roughly, even to tripping him up twice; still in spite of it Cherry succeeded in getting to Jim. &ldquo;Bosh, ish all ri&rsquo;,&rdquo; he said, waving a deprecatory hand. &ldquo;Be&rsquo;n lookin&rsquo; at redeyes, but I&rsquo;m sober&rsquo;s jedge. An&rsquo; I wanna tell you. Lash night&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; Curly dragged him away. At sunset Jim took a long walk, which in a way mitigated his mood. The supper gong recalled him. As he neared camp his keen ears caught Curly saying: &ldquo;Wal, it&rsquo;s shore eatin&rsquo; into my gizzard. I reckon he&rsquo;s pretty decent, considerin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Curly, somethin&rsquo; shore is eatin&rsquo; you,&rdquo; returned Hack Jocelyn, sarcastically. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s got no kick comin&rsquo;. Ain&rsquo;t we buildin&rsquo; this drift fence? What the hell!&rdquo; Jim wondered if this talk referred to him or his uncle, and after a moment&rsquo;s consideration decided it was about himself. Curly, then, as Jim had grasped before, was leaning a little toward championing him. It was the one bright spot in a gloomy week-end. On Monday at dawn they broke camp and drove south to the edge of the timber, perhaps another five miles. Here the camp site delighted Jim. It was on a grassy bench just where the pines began, and near a beautiful spring. The bare despoiled land they had traversed was no longer visible. This drive, of course, included the dropping of bales of wire along the way; and that, with the unpacking and making camp, constituted the day&rsquo;s work. At this camp matters came to a pass where Jim grew beside himself with rage. And the incident that capped the climax was where Curly Prentiss, after gradually and perceptibly leaning toward friendship for Jim, suddenly became alienated. Jim laid this to influence of a clique of four in the Diamond outfit, the ringleader being Hack Jocelyn. And it happened that Jim had begun to need some little evidence of reward for his unswerving patience and endurance. &ldquo;Curly,&rdquo; he said, &ldquo;you&rsquo;ve got a saffron streak as wide as the road.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, is thet thar relatin&rsquo; to my work on this heah drift fence?&rdquo; queried Prentiss, with keen glance on two of the cowboys who certainly heard Jim. &ldquo;No. I&rsquo;m bound to admit you do more than your share,&rdquo; rejoined Jim. &ldquo;Good Heavens, Curly!&rdquo; ejaculated Jim, wearily. &ldquo;You wouldn&rsquo;t make it a matter of guns?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, in this heah country a man has to protect himself.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. And how in the hell am I to protect myself from most of this devilish bunch without a friend?&rdquo; queried Jim, bitterly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve been banking on you right along. You have helped me a good deal, though you may not know it. I like you. And I swear you were beginning to like me. Then all of a sudden you turn cold and mean.&rdquo; &ldquo;So that&rsquo;s why you called me saffron?&rdquo; asked Prentiss, with curious little flecks of light in his eyes. &ldquo;Yes, it is.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, the outfit put it up to me this heah way. They accused me of bein&rsquo; friends with you. An&rsquo; they made me cut the deck. You or the Diamond.&rdquo; &ldquo;So that is it,&rdquo; replied Jim. &ldquo;I never thought of that. Naturally you&rsquo;d stick to them.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m demandin&rsquo; an apology before the outfit,&rdquo; said Curly, with the air of one who held a whip hand. It was on Jim&rsquo;s lips, of course, to beg Curly&rsquo;s pardon, when something perverse prompted him. &ldquo;Suppose I won&rsquo;t?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, then, as you cain&rsquo;t stand fer a little gun-play, I&rsquo;ll have to lick you,&rdquo; drawled Curly. &ldquo;Shore will.&rdquo; &ldquo;Curly, you couldn&rsquo;t do that if you tried it every day we are building this fence.&rdquo; This retort, as amazing to Jim as to Curly, came out of the past days of Jim&rsquo;s turmoil. &ldquo;Mister Traft, I&rsquo;m shore differin&rsquo; with you. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll show you pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, let&rsquo;s get the apology over first,&rdquo; replied Jim, dryly. It happened to be just before supper-time and all the boys were there. Jim did not require to be told that they had heard about what he had called Curly. &ldquo;Fellows, I&rsquo;ve a word to say,&rdquo; he began, and the slow sly glances he drew made him want to swear at them. &ldquo;I want to apologize to Curly before you all. I told him he had a streak of saffron. Well, I take it back. And instead of Curly I&rsquo;ll apply it to those of you who made Curly turn me down. I don&rsquo;t know who they are, but you know. It is pretty low down. This rotten job I&rsquo;ve tackled for my uncle has got on my nerves. There&rsquo;s so much I don&rsquo;t know. Curly or any one of you could help me. I think he was leaning that way. But you made him quit.&rdquo; Then Jim turned to Curly. Curly&rsquo;s face was more of a study than that of his allies, which certainly presented a good deal in puzzles. But presently at the prospect of a fight long deferred they began to beam. &ldquo;Boss, I reckon your uncle will&mdash;&rdquo; began Bud, conscience-stricken. &ldquo;Shet-up, you durn little crow!&rdquo; bawled somebody, presumably Hack. Lonestar Holliday burst into the circle. &ldquo;Fellars, heah comes thet Slinger Dunn,&rdquo; he whispered, warningly. The rider looked like an Indian, yet his skin was dark bronze instead of red. His features were shadowed by the brim of an old black sombrero, yet piercing eyes shone out of that obscurity. He wore a buckskin shirt, much the worse for long usage. The rest of his apparel corresponded with any cowboy&rsquo;s. The second stranger was a lean-faced, hard-lipped young man, with eyes that appeared to oscillate like a compass needle. The foremost of these two riders did not greet the Diamond, individually or collectively. And Jim, quick to note this and that none of his men spoke, waived any sort of greeting himself. But he stepped out in front with an air of authority. This placed him somewhat closer to the rider, in a better light, and he looked up into a handsome, still, strange face. &ldquo;You the new boss of this heah Diamond outfit, hey?&rdquo; inquired the rider, in cool hostility. &ldquo;Yes. I&rsquo;m supposed to be,&rdquo; replied Jim, just as coolly. &ldquo;Young Traft, from Mizzourie?&rdquo; &ldquo;James Traft,&rdquo; corrected Jim. &ldquo;Howdy, Mister Traft! I shore am glad to meet you,&rdquo; drawled the rider, without any intimation that he intended to reveal his own name. Presently Jim replied. &ldquo;I can&rsquo;t return the compliment if you don&rsquo;t say who you are.&rdquo; Holliday took a couple of slow steps, which placed him beside Jim, an action that somehow thrilled Jim, though he might have misunderstood its significance. &ldquo;Boss, it&rsquo;s Slinger Dunn an&rsquo; his pard, Seth Haverly, from the Cibeque,&rdquo; said Holliday, crisply. &ldquo;Howdy, Lonestar,&rdquo; returned Dunn. It was noticeable that Holliday had no more to say. But Jim greeted the visitors and asked them to get down and stay for supper. Dunn might not have heard, for all the sign he gave. &ldquo;Say, ain&rsquo;t you buildin&rsquo; sort of a long fence?&rdquo; he queried. &ldquo;Yes. It&rsquo;s getting pretty long,&rdquo; replied Jim, with a laugh. &ldquo;How long&rsquo;s it goin&rsquo; to be?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hundred miles, the cowboys say.&rdquo; &ldquo;They don&rsquo;t say! ... Thet&rsquo;s purty long. An&rsquo; barb-wire, too. What kind of a fence is this heah goin&rsquo; to be?&rdquo; &ldquo;A drift fence to hold cattle, Mr. Dunn,&rdquo; returned Jim. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s the idee&mdash;a drift fence?&rdquo; &ldquo;What do you suppose?&rdquo; counter-queried Jim, just as curtly. Then he gazed up into burning eyes that positively gave him a shock. The second rider came up. &ldquo;Arch, let&rsquo;s be goin&rsquo;,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Wal, I suppose a lot. But I&rsquo;m askin&rsquo; questions,&rdquo; went on Dunn. &ldquo;This heah fence is ole Jim Traft&rsquo;s idee?&rdquo; &ldquo;Certainly it is.&rdquo; &ldquo;Does he own the land?&rdquo; &ldquo;No.&rdquo; &ldquo;Is he aimin&rsquo; to fence free range?&rdquo; &ldquo;It will divide the range, that&rsquo;s sure.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, along heah it won&rsquo;t make no difference to nobody. But when it gets down in the Diamond it&rsquo;s goin&rsquo; to be a bad deal for cattlemen in the brakes.&rdquo; &ldquo;So I&rsquo;ve been told, Mr. Dunn,&rdquo; rejoined Jim. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m just beginning to learn the cattle business, and can&rsquo;t say one way or the other.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I reckon you won&rsquo;t go far,&rdquo; replied the other, meaningly, and he rolled a cigarette without ever taking his gleaming eyes off Jim. &ldquo;Arch, I&rsquo;m sayin&rsquo; let&rsquo;s rustle out of this,&rdquo; interposed Haverly, reaching over to give Dunn a jerk. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m going to the end of the Diamond with this drift fence,&rdquo; averred Jim. &ldquo;And after it is finished my outfit will see that it stays up.&rdquo; &ldquo;Your outfit?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, my outfit.&rdquo; &ldquo;Say, mister, you cain&rsquo;t have it in your haid thet your Diamond outfit will stick to you.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve got that notion.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you&rsquo;re plumb off the trail. Even down in the brakes we heah what Jocelyn thinks of you. An&rsquo; Cherry Winters. An&rsquo; Uphill Frost.&rdquo; Dunn poised his cigarette, arrested by a speech that evidently struck him. &ldquo;Traft, no drift fence will ever stay up in Cibeque County,&rdquo; he returned, presently, with grim passion. &ldquo;That remains to be seen. Thanks for giving me a hunch. And now, since you&rsquo;re neither polite nor agreeable, will you please mozey on out of my camp?&rdquo; Dunn&rsquo;s face showed the darker for a wave of blood. His companion roughly seized him, nearly unseating him from the saddle. &ldquo;Slinger, I shore ain&rsquo;t waitin&rsquo; to be ordered out of no tenderfoot&rsquo;s camp,&rdquo; he said, gruffly, dragging at Dunn. The instant Jim had delivered this contemptuous order he realized he had done something terrible. Not only Dunn&rsquo;s aspect, but Jim&rsquo;s men appeared to freeze. Jim had never seen such a blazing hell in human eyes as he encountered in Dunn&rsquo;s. Haverly reached down, and grasping the bit of Dunn&rsquo;s horse wheeled it and the rider away. Dunn could be heard cursing his comrade and getting roundly cursed in return. They were watched out of sight into the woods. Jim turned to his men. As he did so he observed Curly Prentiss move his right hand, containing a cocked gun, from behind his back. This revealed to Jim more of the nature of this encounter with the two riders from the Cibeque, but it in no wise mitigated his temper. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t think much of you men,&rdquo; he said, with a ring in his voice. &ldquo;If I told my uncle about this he&rsquo;d fire every last one of you. You may be the great Diamond outfit, but to me you&rsquo;re a lot of four-flushers. What the hell do I know about Slinger Dunn and his kind? Some of you might have chipped in and stood by me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, I reckon we stood by you without you knowin&rsquo; it,&rdquo; declared Curly Prentiss. &ldquo;You were doin&rsquo; the talkin&rsquo;. An&rsquo; if we&rsquo;d chipped in there would have been gun-play.&rdquo; &ldquo;Curly&rsquo;s right, boss,&rdquo; added Lonestar Holliday. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know if Dunn seen Curly slip his gun out. But I reckon he did, as he was shore civil for him. This Slinger Dunn has a bad rep. He&rsquo;s killed several men an&rsquo; shot up more&rsquo;n you could count.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, I&rsquo;ll chip in a word,&rdquo; said Hack Jocelyn. &ldquo;It shore was nervy of you to fire Dunn out of our camp. If you hadn&rsquo;t been a tenderfoot you&rsquo;d never have done it. You want to look out for Slinger Dunn.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, I&rsquo;m not afraid of him, even if he is a gun-slinger,&rdquo; declared Jim, passionately. &ldquo;How do I know it wasn&rsquo;t another trick, hatched by some of you? You may think you&rsquo;re having fun at my expense. Perhaps you are. But there ought to be a limit. You&rsquo;re such awful liars I can&rsquo;t believe one word you say.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, are you callin&rsquo; us liars?&rdquo; inquired Curly, in a queer tone. &ldquo;I reckon I am. Damn liars! You&rsquo;re more. You&rsquo;re a flock of swell-headed, cross-grained, cantankerous cowboys. Now put that in your cigarettes and smoke it!&rdquo; At least for once Jim silenced them. He had no idea of the magnitude of his offense and he did not care. He had been frightened, and if anger had not come to his rescue he would have betrayed it. That would be the finish. He had vowed he would never let any of this famed Diamond outfit see him scared. At that juncture Jeff called them to supper, but Jim stalked off into the woods and walked under the dark pines. He recovered from his fit of temper, but not from the sense of disaster that had slowly accumulated. This task he had undertaken seemed well nigh impossible. At length, gloomy and troubled, he stalked back to camp and to bed. Next day the stretching of wire fence went on, and now with a remarkable celerity, compared with that done in the open country. The strands of wire were nailed upon trees, and seldom had a post-hole to be dug. Perhaps this had a cheering effect upon the members of the Diamond, but Jim regarded their attempts at approaching him, their sudden solicitude, their amazing amiability, with suspicion. He was right, too, for that night, when he opened his bed-roll he found the blankets soaked. Some one had poured a bucketful of cold spring water into one end of the roll. Jim pondered over this mean trick. Oh, he would get even, but what ought he do on the moment? He could strip a blanket off every one of his men and by so doing catch the guilty party. But he decided against that course of procedure. Instead he built a roaring fire, so hot that he almost roasted the boys alive before they could awake and move back in the woods. Such profanity! Jim had heard a little on the docks at St. Louis, but it could not hold a candle to this. He took half the night to dry his blankets, knowing, of course, that a huge fire would keep his men awake. Then he went to bed, and in the darkness before dawn he crawled out to yell words he had heard them often use: &ldquo;THE DAY&rsquo;S BUSTED! ROLL OUT!&rdquo; That day and another passed. The camp was moved ten miles down in the woods, into a wide pleasant draw where a creek ran, and wild turkeys gave Jim a thrill. The wagon had to go into Flagerstown for more wire, and the driver returned with a note from Traft, asking Jim why he had not been in to the ranch to report. Jim never answered it, nor did he ride in. Another week-end went by, including Sunday, when Jim was left in peace. He was nearing the end of his rope now. As the days passed and the drift fence lengthened, these incomprehensible cowboys of the Diamond outfit were driving Jim to distraction. They meant to break him. They were going to. No tenderfoot out of Missouri could ever run the Diamond! So Jim had overheard. They were bewildering in the infinite variety of their attacks, and he was almost helpless because he knew so little of the West, and horses, and of the nature of cowboys. To him they seemed inhuman&mdash;cool, still-faced or smiling devils, hiding their sincerity, if they had any, possessed of a fiendish desire to nag him, worry him, inconvenience him, make him acknowledge defeat. On Monday the wire-wagon went on ahead, along a line Jim had blazed on Sunday, and dropped its load, then went back to town for another, necessitating now a trip of three days. Jim, driving the men hard that week, reached the end of the blazed line before the wagon returned. There was a wide deep ravine that had to be crossed. The country was growing rougher. &ldquo;Boss,&rdquo; said Bud, that morning, &ldquo;there&rsquo;s a lot of bales of wire been rolled down in the draw.&rdquo; The rest of the outfit whooped, but Jim could not tell whether it was from resentment or satisfaction. &ldquo;Who rolled them?&rdquo; he roared. &ldquo;How&rsquo;n&rsquo;ll do I know?&rdquo; retorted Bud. &ldquo;Another funny trick!&rdquo; ejaculated Jim. And he walked on along the line to the ravine. There far down at the bottom he espied a dozen or more bales lying scattered about. &ldquo;Go down and pack them up,&rdquo; ordered Jim to his men, who had followed him. &ldquo;Up thet hill on foot?&rdquo; One and all they sat down in the shade, to begin rolling cigarettes. When Jim swore at them they smiled in the slow, cool way that always infuriated him. &ldquo;All right,&rdquo; he fumed. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll pack them up myself.&rdquo; He strode down the slope, which he found steeper and longer than it had appeared at first glance. Like a toy he handled a bale of wire. Jim was powerful. He could throw a sack of grain or a barrel up into a wagon. However, by the time he had packed that bale up the hill he knew what a heavy load was. He had bitten off more than he could chew. But with those intent eyes on him so watchfully, he could not quit. He went down again, and this time was careful to zigzag up the steep slope. It took him two hours of the most trying toil to finish the job. Then, hot as fire, and wringing wet with sweat, he panted at the silent cowboys. &ldquo;Now&mdash;if you&rsquo;re rested&mdash;we&rsquo;ll go on&mdash;with the fence.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, we was just waitin&rsquo; to see how soon you&rsquo;d think of gettin&rsquo; a pack-hoss fer thet job,&rdquo; observed Hump Stephens, amiably. For Jim to realize how again, for the thousandth time, he had showed how utterly unfit he was to be foreman of the Diamond, did not improve his mood. He worked off the mood at length, as he invariably succeeded in doing, just as if he did not know another would soon be imposed upon him. It was Curly Prentiss who discovered horse tracks along the line, and he showed them to Jim, no doubt in the interest of himself and companions. &ldquo;Fresh tracks, an&rsquo; unshod horses at thet,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Reckon we can lay it to some of the Cibeque outfit.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sorry I accused you fellows,&rdquo; he replied, regretfully. No matter what he did or said now, it was wrong. Friday, at the lunch hour, Bud Chalfack approached Jim. &ldquo;Boss, I&rsquo;m talkin&rsquo; fer the outfit,&rdquo; he announced. &ldquo;We figger thet we&rsquo;re aboot up on this week&rsquo;s work, an&rsquo; we want this afternoon an&rsquo; tomorrow off.&rdquo; &ldquo;What for?&rdquo; queried Jim, in surprise. Jim actually could not decide whether this was insubordination or the legitimate claim of a cowboy. He felt helpless. If he refused they would go, anyhow. If he gave his consent it might well be that they had him &ldquo;buffaloed,&rdquo; as Curly had been overheard to say. &ldquo;Very well, you go on your own hook,&rdquo; he said to Bud. After they had gone, Jim strode off toward the woods. He was astounded to hear Jeff Davis call. &ldquo;Boss, you oughtn&rsquo;t go out alone. But if you must go, take a rifle.&rdquo; Jim did not answer, though he was grateful to Jeff. The cook, then, had perpetrated a hoax on the Diamond. He was not dumb. And it was a sure bet that neither was he slightly deaf, as he had claimed. Here would be one on the cowboys, presently. Jim assured himself that he would not betray Jeff. He did not, however, take the latter&rsquo;s advice, but went on into the forest alone and unarmed. He was beginning to feel a strange solace or help or something in the deep solitude of the woodland. It was early summer now. The ferns and wild flowers were springing up along the brown aisles. His step made not the slightest noise. He saw squirrels and birds, and once, gray vanishing forms that might have been deer. Huge cliffs festooned with moss and vines, from which dripped water, arrested him in his walk. There was a pleasant low sough of wind in the tree tops. If anything could have spurred his flagging spirits, the sweetness and loneliness of this forest would have done so. But he guessed he was about beaten. And that confession, gaining audible admission into his consciousness, could not be dislodged. The afternoon passed, and he returned to camp, there to eat supper in silence, without appearing to remember that the cook had spoken to him. He slept well, but in the morning the old bitterness and hopelessness assailed him again. An impulse to ride to town seized him, and straightway he acted upon it. He did not quite acknowledge to himself that this was a signal of defeat, but it would probably come out when he faced his uncle. He reached home about noon, to ascertain that his uncle was at the fair. Jim rode out there, and walked his horse. What a vacillating jackass he was! It would hurt Uncle Jim to find him a quitter. Ought he not to try again? Weary and distressed, he entered the fairground; and espying a gayly decorated booth advertising lunch, he dismounted and approached the counter. He gave an order to a girl behind. When he was about through he happened to look up, to see a pair of gold-brown eyes upon him. And they wrenched out of him the query, &ldquo;What would you do if you were about licked?&rdquo; It was when the girl had finally said, &ldquo;I&rsquo;d get up an&rsquo; fight some more!&rdquo; that Jim really looked at her with seeing eyes. If her spirited reply had stirred him, what more did the toss of her pretty dark head, with its glints of gold. She was a little girl, very young, and she wore a dainty blue dress. Still, there were contours under it that betrayed womanhood. She had a small, oval, almost dusky face. Her hands were small and brown, yet they struck him as strong and capable. She was Western, of course, but she was a little lady of quality. He kept talking, scarcely with any idea of what he was saying. And that suddenly betrayed to him a swift and remarkable interest in her. After that he knew what he was saying to her, and every word added to a realization of charm. By the time he had found out she did not have a best fellow he seemed far on a strange new adventure; and when he rode away toward the stands, with her shy half-promise of a dance that night, he changed his mind about his mission to Flagerstown. &ldquo;I&rsquo;d get up an&rsquo; fight some more!&rdquo; She had looked it, too. What a sweet and fiery little girl! Evidently she had taken it for granted that he had been knocked down. Well, he had been. But he had gotten up. And he would act precisely and indomitably upon her advice. Fight! He would whip each and every one of that Diamond outfit. He gave his horse to a boy at the stalls, and went round to the stand, where he soon found his uncle, and was welcomed in a way that made him ashamed. It pleased his uncle that he had come to town in his work clothes. He sat there gripping his seat, yelling when the crowd yelled. And he saw Curly Prentiss ride wild bronchos that threw all the other riders; he saw Hack Jocelyn break the record for roping two-year-olds; he saw Bud Chalfack climb all over a horse, racing at breakneck speed; he saw Jackson Way ride two beautiful horses, standing with one foot on the back of each, and beat his opponents two full lengths; and lastly he saw Lonestar Holliday win the money for the perilous feat of bulldogging steers. Out of sixteen events the Diamond outfit took nine first prizes. Jim scarcely could contain himself. That was his band of cowboys. He might have hated them before today, but now he loved them. He gathered vaguely that something tremendous had happened to him. He rode out to the ranch-house in a trance, divided between pride for his cowboys and the momentous dance near at hand. He shaved and washed and changed his clothes, aware of an undue regard for his appearance. His uncle had guests, to whom Jim was presented as the boss of the Diamond. Jim acquitted himself creditably, and kept his miserable secret, strangely growing less miserable. Then he was off to the dance, eager, with palpitating heart, amused at himself, amazed and glad. He now had opportunity to show some of these Flagerstown girls that if he was a tenderfoot he could interest and perhaps win a prettier girl than any of them. But he had not prepared himself for a vision in white&mdash;his girl of the booth&mdash;stunningly transformed by a gown, a brown-armed, brown-faced beauty with haunting dark eyes. She saw Jim at once. She bowed. And whatever had not already happened to his heart surely happened then. Jim did not approach anyone; he saw only her. And he was in a torment waiting for her to give him the opportunity he longed for. If she did not he must go to her, and introducing himself ask for the dance. But she divined his predicament. How the dark eyes met his across the hall, through the whirling throng! Soon she sought a seat with her young partner, and obviously dismissed him. Jim made haste to reach her, to bend over her. &ldquo;My dance!&rdquo; he whispered. She rose, her face like pearl, her eyes downcast, and gave herself to his embrace. All the other egregious tenderfoot blunders he had made could not in the aggregate sum up the magnitude of alienating Molly Dunn. He writhed under that. And always he would protest&mdash;who could have imagined that sweet dainty girl sister to a desperado, a gun-thrower, a killer, a rustler of the very brakes it was Jim&rsquo;s task to fence off from the range? Then, just as poignantly he would add&mdash;but what difference did it really make who was her brother and why had he been so asinine as to distress and shame her with the connection? He found excuse for himself for everything but that. It had struck him almost dumb at first to realize that he had lost his head and seized her, hugged her atrociously, surely would have devoured her with kisses had she not brought him to his senses with a cutting blow across his mouth. That act, at once under the astounding dominance of her voluntary kiss&mdash;a strange sweet little touch of cool lips&mdash;had lost its heinousness. In the light of the present hour, when it came to him that there had been genuine sincerity, the wild impetus of first love, he realized he had not much to be sorry for there. But she had not known that. How would she ever know? Not that knowing would change her! Her eyes haunted him. What a magnificent blaze! In them had been repeated the fire of Slinger Dunn&rsquo;s. Why had Molly Dunn kissed him? He recalled her pitiful little confession, and that hurt Jim more than anything else. She was not what he had thought her and she yearned to be. Jim stoutly fought against the fading and receding of something beautiful. The dream, the glory of new vibrant emotion, had to pale before reality. Yet Jim could not renounce, nor could he resign himself to another bitter blow this West had given him. It was sudden, complicated, and disastrous. But was it irremediable? A grain of comfort lay in the fact that he had time to think, to go over it, to puzzle it out, to find what had actually happened to him, to conceive who and what Molly Dunn was, and understand her reaction to their meeting. Most vivid of the things he recalled was the bitter reproach in her big eyes. Why had she reproached him? Not because he had unwittingly intimated of a wide class distinction between Jim Traft and Molly Dunn. She knew she was Molly Dunn of the Cibeque. The reproach had come from a deep and terrible hurt. Jim divined suddenly that maybe she, too, had fallen in love at first sight. The ecstasy of that was short-lived. It could not be true. His own state for weeks had been one of excitement, strain, growing to morbidness. Then a swift change to romance and sentiment had thrown him off his balance. He sank as deep into the depths as he had been lifted to the skies. All of which seemed only to add to the mystery and fascination and fatality of Molly Dunn. He discovered himself vainly trying to do battle against her own estimate of herself. Just a little backwoods girl to whom a white gown had added charm! But he soon scouted that. Molly Dunn had innate charm. Through the pine trees he saw some of the cowboys returning from Flagerstown, and the considerable distance between them did not drown sounds of their hilarity. Jim plumped down under a pine, overcome by an entirely new and unforeseen probability. Some of the members of the Diamond had been at that dance last night. Beyond the bounds of hope and reason was it that they might not have seen him there with Molly Dunn. Those hawk-eyed cowboys could see through walls. They had an uncanny genius for finding out everything. But they needed only to know a little to drive Jim to distraction. He lingered there in the woods, not actually afraid yet, but certainly loath to confront them. Molly Dunn&rsquo;s terse statement that she would get up and fight some more, which he had appropriated as a slogan, in this vacillating hour lost its grip. What a pity if he could not be actuated by that spirit again! Perhaps it would revive, if he got sufficient cue for anger.
