Long before the supper hour, which Jeff had early on Sunday, the cowboys rode into camp. So presently Jim felt the imperative necessity of showing himself, because longer absence would only aggravate any suspicions concerning him. He dragged himself up and to the edge of the forest, only there bracing himself for the ordeal. Somebody saw him coming. Did they pursue their usual demeanor and pay no attention to him? They did not. To his horror they lined up on each side of the camp fire, and faced him like a lot of grim judges. Jim divined that whatever he had dreaded was about to be perpetrated. And some last shred of courage came to his aid as he ran the gauntlet of the merciless Diamond. Not a murmur! He bent over to move a couple of billets on the newly started camp fire, solely to hide his face. Then he sauntered on toward the tree where he kept his pack and bed. &ldquo;Wal, boss, we heahed ya walked off with a prize yourself,&rdquo; called Bud Chalfack. &ldquo;Some little blue-ribbon boy from Mizzourie!&rdquo; That sally of Bud&rsquo;s was greeted by uproarious mirth. Like a giant hand it pulled Jim round. His cowboys presented a group of nine young men in varying stages of convulsions. The devil himself emanated from them. Jim choked back a sharp retort. Then Cherry Winters, still quite drunk, staggered over to Jim, his red face a whole grin of impish delight, and he essayed to deliver what must have been intended for an important speech. But its manner of delivery and incoherence spurred Jim to a blunt, &ldquo;Get out!&rdquo; and a hard shove. Cherry kept on staggering, this time backward, and he kept on until he met with an obstacle in shape of Jeff&rsquo;s woodpile. He fell on this, toppled it over upon him, and he did not arise. The gang whooped. They had all been drinking, and Bud looked pretty wabbly on his feet. &ldquo;Tenderfoot hell!&rdquo; he bawled. &ldquo;You got us&mdash;skinned to frazzle.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks, Bud, but I don&rsquo;t quite savvy,&rdquo; returned Jim. &ldquo;Well, anybody not blind could have done that,&rdquo; snapped Jim, trying to pierce the secret intensity of Bud&rsquo;s rapture. &ldquo;But I ben&mdash;savin&rsquo; it up,&rdquo; crowed Bud, slapping his knees with his hands. &ldquo;You better keep saving it,&rdquo; warned Jim. &ldquo;Aw, boss, I couldn&rsquo;t think of thet. It&rsquo;s too orful good. I gotta tell the Diamond aboot it.&rdquo; If Jim had any dignity left it was that of the gathering might of fury. What did the little fool know? Jim resisted a fierce impulse to slap the gaping mouth. The cowboys clamored to hear. They sensed the long-hoped-for corralling of their tenderfoot foreman. Jim grimly gazed from face to face. Most of them were only in fun, and Bud was drunk. Curly, too, appeared a little under the influence of the bottle. But Hack Jocelyn was keen, cold, leering. Jim did not miss anything. &ldquo;Boss, I seen you on the porch,&rdquo; shouted Bud, triumphantly. Jim gasped at that revelation. Oh, misery! How exceedingly worse than he had dreaded! &ldquo;Fellars, I seen our fastidinous boss from Mizzourie,&rdquo; went on Bud, swelling with his dénouement, &ldquo;I seen him huggin&rsquo; thet little hussy&mdash;Molly Dunn!&rdquo; Before Jim realized what he was doing he had leaped at Bud, to knock him over the pack-saddles. The blow had been solid, and at least for the moment another of the Diamond was down and out. Curly Prentiss jumped out from the circle, slamming his sombrero down with a smack. &ldquo;You two-faced Mizzourie gent!&rdquo; he yelled. &ldquo;Bud&rsquo;s my pard. I&rsquo;m shore gonna lick you for thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bah! You bow-legged cowpuncher!&rdquo; blazed Jim. &ldquo;If you can&rsquo;t fight any better than you dance&mdash;you&rsquo;ll never lick me.&rdquo; That hurt Curly more than the blow to his friend. He threw his scarf one way, his vest another, his gun-belt at Jackson Way, and he almost sprawled on the ground ridding himself of chaps and spurs. The delay gave Jim time to collect his wits. But even that and some semblance of coolness did not mitigate his welcome of a battle with any or all of this confounded Diamond outfit. The incentive he needed had been miraculously forthcoming. Curly gave a capital imitation of a bull about to charge when he was detained by Uphill Frost. &ldquo;Hold on a minnit,&rdquo; yelled that individual, impressively. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t corral me. I&rsquo;m gonna pulverize this gurl-huggin&rsquo; foreman,&rdquo; replied Curly, swinging his arms. &ldquo;Shore, an&rsquo; my money&rsquo;s on you, Curly, old buckaroo,&rdquo; said Frost, holding up a hand stuffed with greenbacks of small denominations. There was a noisy scramble then among the cowboys to get money up on Curly. But it developed that not a single backer of Jim came forward. If Jim had not been so wretched about Molly and so furious at these pig-headed cowboys he would have howled with mirth. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll take every bet,&rdquo; he declared. &ldquo;Whoopee!&rdquo; yelled somebody, with an eye to fortune. &ldquo;Give any odds, boss?&rdquo; &ldquo;Two to one,&rdquo; answered Jim. &ldquo;But hurry. I want to get this over and have my supper.&rdquo; &ldquo;Atta boy, Curly!&rdquo; &ldquo;Larrup him unmerciful!&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re fightin&rsquo; fer the Diamond!&rdquo; When Curly came lumbering in Jim stepped aside and hit him in the ribs. The blow gave forth a hollow sound. Curly whirled around and plunged, swinging. He delivered wide sweeping blows that, if any of them had connected with Jim, would have fulfilled the hopes of the yelling Diamond. But they expended their strength on the empty air. In a couple of moments Jim became aware that Curly could not hit him. The cowboy was a wonder in a saddle, but on the ground he was lost. He got his feet tangled up and he appeared the farthest remove from nimble. After hitting Curly a few times here and there Jim discovered a vulnerable spot in Curly&rsquo;s big handsome nose. Wherefore Jim began to concentrate on that. He whacked it with his left. Soon the blood began to flow and with it Curly&rsquo;s wild exuberance. But this did not improve his fighting. Then Jim landed his right and that upset Curly. He sat down quite suddenly. This also upset Curly&rsquo;s backers. &ldquo;Git up, you big cheese!&rdquo; yelled Hack Jocelyn, and he did not mean to be funny. &ldquo;Our money&rsquo;s on you.&rdquo; Curly responded to that trenchant call. And now to his amazement was added fury. In the ensuing few moments he showed that his spirit was willing but his flesh weak. The blows he landed upon Jim were inconsequential. On the other hand Jim gave him a severe drubbing. After the third knock-down Curly merely sat up. It chanced that this time he was near Bud, who was also sitting up. &ldquo;My Gawd! pard, hev we come to this?&rdquo; cried Bud, evidently sober enough to realize. &ldquo;What&rsquo;d he hit me with?&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckon it was a hoss-shoe,&rdquo; said Curly, feeling his swollen and bloody nose. Jim stepped closer and peered down at Curly. &ldquo;Well, Prentiss, have you got enough?&rdquo; he asked, fiercely. Curly looked up without the least trace of resentment and nodded. &ldquo;Boss, I cain&rsquo;t say how it come aboot, but I&rsquo;m shore licked,&rdquo; he said, and he grinned. &ldquo;Did I pick this fight?&rdquo; went on Jim, quick to grasp the situation. &ldquo;You shore didn&rsquo;t.&rdquo; &ldquo;All right. Are you going to threaten me with that damn nonsense about gun-play or are you going to shake hands?&rdquo; Curly laboriously got to his feet. Something had pierced the armor of his stupidity. &ldquo;I reckon I&rsquo;ll shake,&rdquo; he rejoined, and he took Jim&rsquo;s proffered hand. &ldquo;Aw, you&rsquo;re drunk,&rdquo; growled Hack Jocelyn, in disgust, with a hard eye on Curly. &ldquo;An&rsquo; we had our money on you!&rdquo; With that he strode surlily away. Bud Chalfack essayed to rise, a task no means easy, owing to the billets of wood that surrounded his feet. &ldquo;Bud, you better stay there, because if you get up I&rsquo;ll only have to knock you down again,&rdquo; said Jim, menacingly. &ldquo;But I wasn&rsquo;t fightin&rsquo; you. Thet was Curly,&rdquo; protested Bud. &ldquo;No matter. You&rsquo;re a little skunk, and I&rsquo;ll have to do it, unless you have another pard who&rsquo;ll take it for you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh.&rdquo; Bud looked around, and it was evident that at the moment not one of the cowboys seemed eager to take Jim&rsquo;s hint. &ldquo;Boss, so you&rsquo;re one of them fellars thet&rsquo;s never satisfied once he gets a-goin&rsquo;?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m afraid I am.&rdquo; &ldquo;But you&rsquo;re bigger&rsquo;n me an&rsquo; I didn&rsquo;t do nothin&rsquo; except give you away. An&rsquo; if you hadn&rsquo;t soaked me I&rsquo;d hev told the fellars&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;If you tell any more I&rsquo;ll beat you into jelly. And I&rsquo;ll do that anyhow unless you apologize.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw! Fer callin&rsquo; Molly Dunn a little hussy?&rdquo; Jim looked a grim affirmative. &ldquo;All right, boss. I crawl. But I didn&rsquo;t mean any insult.&rdquo; &ldquo;You didn&rsquo;t?&mdash;Say, you little dumb-head! Didn&rsquo;t you lie when you said you saw me hug her?&rdquo; Jim faced the silent puzzled cowboys. He had extracted all the humor for them from the situation, and he believed for once he had the upper hand. &ldquo;Boys, Bud was drunk last night. He has heaped disgrace on me and insulted a fine little girl.&rdquo; Bud took violent exception to this, as was manifest from his face and actions, without the speech that followed. &ldquo;Boss, it ain&rsquo;t no disgrace to hug a gurl in Arizonie. An&rsquo; if it happened to be Molly Dunn you&rsquo;d shore make this outfit proud. &rsquo;Cause Molly Dunn has handed the mitt to me an&rsquo; Lonestar. An&rsquo; she shore set Hack Jocelyn down cold an&rsquo; hard. Hack is plumb crazy over her.&rdquo; &ldquo;But you called her hussy!&rdquo; thundered Jim, trying to hide his rapture. &ldquo;But, boss, I didn&rsquo;t mean thet in no insultin&rsquo; way,&rdquo; protested Bud. &ldquo;Molly&rsquo;s a little wildcat. She&rsquo;s a devil. She has shore made this Diamond outfit sick. Why, me an&rsquo; Lonestar was flirtin&rsquo; with her down at West Fork, an&rsquo; we never knowed she was Slinger Dunn&rsquo;s sister! ... Boss, I take thet hussy back.&rdquo; &ldquo;Very well, in that case I&rsquo;m sorry I misunderstood you,&rdquo; said Jim, and offered his hand to Bud. Jeff&rsquo;s noisy banging call to supper ended the incident. Jim imagined an almost imperceptible transformation in the air of the cowboys, except in Jocelyn and Winters, who were surly and uncommunicative. Jim sought his bed early. There was no singing round the camp fire that night. Scarcely had darkness set in when the fire flickered and went out. The coyotes had uninterrupted possession of the silence. But Jim did not find slumber coming easily. He believed he had stumbled on a way to get the best of the Diamond outfit. He was not sure that his hope was not father to the conviction, but the more he pondered over his achievement the more elated he became. These simple elemental boys respected only achievement. They did not really in the least care who a man was. It was what he could do! And Jim believed he could whip every last one of them. Hack Jocelyn would be a mean customer. Jim had liked Hack less and less all the time, and Bud&rsquo;s allusion to Hack&rsquo;s interest in Molly Dunn aggravated the feeling. Jocelyn would be dangerous in a fight and not to be trusted in relation to a woman. Jim could have laughed aloud at this deduction. Already he seemed to be anticipating rivals! But so far as Molly Dunn was concerned he certainly could not trust himself. Jim reflected. It had amazed him&mdash;the ease with which he had bested Curly, and he tried to reason out why. Curly was a big, lithe, strong fellow, who ought to have put up a very aggressive battle. But he had been born on a horse and had lived in a saddle. On the ground he did not know what to do with himself. Slow, awkward, uncertain, he had been at Jim&rsquo;s mercy. Whereupon Jim took stock of himself. He weighed around one hundred and seventy. He had a long reach, a fist like a mallet; he was quick as a cat and fast on his feet. Only a couple of years back he had been pretty clever with his hands. And this had come about naturally enough. When he was about fourteen years old he was friends with a boy who had a cousin come visiting from the East. And this cousin taught Jim and his friend something of the manly art of self-defense. It began to look as if Jim had another asset which he had not counted on at all. The fence-building on a Missouri farm had been the first; now the second was this playful and friendly boxing habit. These cowboys had long since given him reason to resort to violence. But Jim knew he had been backward and perhaps afraid. From some source had come the courage of a lion. He chuckled to himself. No fear that these cowboys would haul in! By their very nature&mdash;their pugnacity and curiosity&mdash;they must grow worse. Each and every one of them would feel it his bounden duty to wipe up the camp-ground with the tenderfoot foreman of the Diamond. Jim reveled in a situation that a few days back had been well-nigh intolerable. No wonder they had been a pondering lot of young men! A gleam of light they refused to see had begun to penetrate their craniums. But as for Molly Dunn! When Jim let clamoring thoughts of her dominate his consciousness his exultant glee died and his heart sank like lead in his breast. So some of these tough cowboys knew her! Had dared to approach her! The idea made Jim&rsquo;s blood run hot and cold. Still, Molly could not have helped that, if these cowboys had discovered where she lived or any place she frequented. West Fork! He had not heard of that place. But he would very soon see it for himself. Stone walls could not keep these riders of the Diamond out. How much less then could a girl do it? Molly had spunk, though, Jim thought. It was no use to let pride and distrust have sway over him. As he lay there, his face upward to the dark canopy of pines, through which starlight filtered, he realized he was infatuated. That was as far as he grasped truth. Molly was an enigma, but only that because of the allusions of Bud Chalfack. That confounded cowboy had been witness to the scene on the porch in Flagerstown. He had seen Molly&rsquo;s incredible response to Jim&rsquo;s embrace. Jim felt it as a sacrilege. How could he dream and ponder over something that had been profaned by the keen eyes of a vulgar cowboy? Jim writhed over this aspect of the situation. Then when Jim was abject a staggering thought struck him. Bud Chalfack had lied about the interpretation of the word hussy. Bud was a smooth-tongued, crafty cowboy. Jim had observed a hundred instances of his diplomacy with his comrades. He was the brainiest of the lot. The thought of Bud&rsquo;s trenchant remark was sickening. If these cowboys actually knew Molly Dunn, if they had ever been in her willing presence&mdash;especially that hard-lipped, veiled-eyed Jocelyn&mdash;it would be for Jim no less than a calamity. Because he would have at once to turn his back on romance. Then suddenly he recalled Molly&rsquo;s shy swift kiss&mdash;timid if there had ever been a timid kiss&mdash;and later her sharp retort that he had been the first ever to have that from her, and loyalty and love leaped to her defense. There, then, the battle raged in Jim&rsquo;s mind&mdash;between uncertainty and gossip, between romance and realism, between faith and unfaith. She had been home from Flagerstown ten days&mdash;two weeks&mdash;exactly fifteen days, and they seemed years. All had changed. Her very identity was not the same. If she had ever had any capacity for happiness, it had fled. She looked at the old wooden bucket She had carried it as long as she could remember, and that was as far back as when she had been so little she could scarcely lift it. And the spring&mdash;all her life she remembered that. Once it had been an unfailing source of wonder and gladness. It was the biggest and finest spring of granite water of all the springs in the Cibeque Valley. It roared out from under a mossy cliff at the head of a shady glen, as large as a room and as deep as a well. Molly could see the pink-sided trout lying along the rocky sides; she could see the golden gleams deep down where the bubbles sparkled out of the shadow. Maple Spring was deep enough to drown her, and Molly wished she had the courage to fall in and sink. But she had accidentally fallen in there often, and she could swim like a fish. It would be of no use. The glen, too, had been one of her perennial joys. The spring lent music and movement as it boiled and eddied round the great hole and then burst over a little fall to begin its melodious way down the winding gully. Many rocks and drooping ferns lined the banks; maples and sycamores leaned over the amber water, letting only gleams of sunshine through; huge pines and spruces rose from the bank above to tower high. Above the spring on a level bench were grass and flowers, and clean, flat, gray stones where Molly had played alone all her childhood and dreamed hours of her girlhood. It had been a hiding-place, too, for it was isolated and not close to the cabin or trail. The squirrels and jays shared this secret with Molly. Only in the fall when the wild turkeys came down, and the hunters followed, were the sanctity and sweetness of this glen disrupted. But Molly gazed about with eyes hopelessly disillusioned or else blinded by the trouble that had come to her. All because of the wonderful ride to Flagerstown&mdash;a white gown, a dance&mdash;and Him! Molly felt she could never be again what she had been before that visit. She would be what he had mistaken her for or she would die. Every day since her return home had seen a struggle. At first she had been hot and resentful in her humiliation. She had hated Jim Traft. She would be a worthy sister to Slinger Dunn. She would carry on so boldly with the riders that her name would become a byword on the range for&mdash;for&mdash;Molly did not know what, but she would find out. She would encourage that nice little cowboy of the Diamond&mdash;Bud Chalfack, whose name she had never known until the night of the dance. And she would flirt outrageously with Curly Prentiss. Indeed, she would no longer repel the advances of Hack Jocelyn, though she disliked and feared him. She would show Jim Traft that she could win his vain and stuck-up cowboys and cast them aside, for Seth Haverly and even for Andy Stoneham. But opportunities for such conduct had multiplied and she had not availed herself of them. Seth had been often at her home, with her brother Arch, but she knew why he came. And always Andy waylaid her when she went on errands down to the village. On Sunday she had espied Hack Jocelyn riding up the trail from West Fork, and she had dodged into the brush. She resented her vacillation and was long in understanding. But as the days went by she had finally realized that she was terribly, miserably in love. Her amaze and scorn, her fury and contempt, her pride and her shame, her wretched attempts to sink under this malady and make of herself what Jim Traft must think her&mdash;all were of no avail and only added to her burden. Lately Molly had begun to soften toward Jim, and that had been the forerunner of dreams and remembrances and longings. Once she had surrendered to this sweet fancy she was doomed. Before she knew it she could no longer fight herself or the thought of Jim. So that a few lonely afternoons in the glen, a few nights lying wide awake in the dark loft of the cabin, had been her undoing. The shame of it made her more furious than ever, but she was helpless. In the daylight she had some semblance of character. At least she could throw off yearnings and what she deemed idiotic enchantments, but at night she became a weak girl, with aching heart and mad tumultuous emotions. Each morning for a week when she had come to the spring for water she had set the bucket down, to indulge in pondering, brooding thoughts. This morning they prevailed longer than usual. But at length she picked up the heavy bucket and took the trail home. She left the glen behind, and perhaps the most bearable of her thoughts. The trail led to the clearing, to the forty acres of hideous slash in the forest that Dunn had homesteaded twenty years before. The rest of the hundred and sixty acres consisted of timber land. Dunn had never &ldquo;proved up&rdquo; on his claim, and therefore had no patent for it. Arch Dunn plowed the red field and planted the beans and corn and sorghum and potatoes. He had spells when he stayed home and worked about the farm. But the last year or two he had left the harvesting of the crop to Molly and her mother. As Molly passed along the trail, under the cedars and piñons and an occasional pine, she gazed out at the red furrows, now green-ridged with beans and corn; she saw that she would soon have to get in there with a hoe. It had never occurred to her before, but she certainly hated that beanfield. Here and there stood dead pines and cedars that had been &ldquo;ringed&rdquo; by an ax and left standing to become bleached and ghastly; and everywhere black stumps stuck up, surrounded by a patch of weeds. Farmers in the Cibeque let the stumps rot out. The cabin, at the end of the clearing, fitted the scene. It consisted of two one-room log buildings, with a porch between, and one rough-shingled roof over all. The shingles were moss-green in places and the logs were bleached. In the chinks between, sections of clay had broken out. The fences were down, the sheds built of uneven clapboards. Altogether Molly saw the homestead with gloomier eyes than ever. Yet it could boast of the finest view in all the Cibeque. To north and east the vast gray-cliffed and canyon-notched wall of the Diamond towered, a mountain face of a thousand features and never the same any two hours of the day. Southward opened up the valley of the Cibeque, all its whole length from this high prospect. But its endless promontories, and long ridges running down, its red walls and gray crags, its winding bright ribbon of water through the green valley, and in the far distance the dim blue ranges, had little charm to intrigue Molly Dunn this unhappy day. She carried the bucket into the kitchen end of the cabin&mdash;a dark room, only in winter a place of comfort. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ve been gone long enough to dig a well,&rdquo; remarked her mother, with sarcasm. Mrs. Dunn was still young and good-looking enough to be jealous of Molly. She had come from a station considerably above John Dunn&rsquo;s, and she was a bitter, unhappy woman. Since Molly had grown old enough to attract the attention of men, what accord that had existed between mother and daughter had gradually been broken. &ldquo;That bucket is heavy an&rsquo; it&rsquo;s a long ways,&rdquo; replied Molly. &ldquo;You used to fetch it in a jiffy. You met some cowboy on the trail.&rdquo; &ldquo;No. It&rsquo;s funny, but I didn&rsquo;t this mawnin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, you must have fallen in the spring, then.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wisht I had,&rdquo; returned Molly, flippantly. She went out and across the porch. Her father had gotten up and was slouching in his crippled way to the big blanketed chair where he passed his wakeful hours. Of late Molly had seemed to see him differently, with pity and tolerance. These feelings, too, applied to her mother. Dunn was not an old man in years, but he was in all else. His dark hair, shading gray, hung down over his knotted pale brow. He was a broken man, living in the past, ruined by some secret association with an infamous war between sheepmen and cattlemen, and devoured by passions that had outlived his enemies. Molly had affection for her father, despite the impatience and disgust which grew with the years. And of late, since her own trouble, she had begun to have some little understanding of her mother&mdash;of what it must have meant to be faithful to Dunn all these years. There flashed over Molly a consciousness that she could follow Jim Traft to the end of the world and slave her fingers to the bone for him. Then she laughed at her imbecility. For her to aspire to be the wife of Mr. James Traft, gentleman tenderfoot from Missouri, and the sole heir to old Jim Traft&rsquo;s land and stock&mdash;that was rather far-fetched and ridiculous. Besides, she did not do anything of the kind. But she could not control vagrant and perverse thoughts. Here flashed another. This same Jim Traft had importuned her to let him be her best fellow. How pale and earnest he had looked! It was impossible to repudiate his sincerity. To be his sweetheart! Molly had a stronger, sweeter, more suffocating rush of emotion. What would become of her if this thing grew? She must throw herself at Andy or Seth, or both of them, and kill this affliction. But could those louts kill it? Molly divined they could not kill anything except her self-respect. And that, strangely, had become a pronounced thing. She would now have it to contend with. Her pride&mdash;her vanity! All because she had been hugged by a handsome young fellow from Missouri! Molly let out a peal of ringing laughter that made even her father take notice. &ldquo;Lass, you&rsquo;ve been queer since you come back from Flag,&rdquo; he remarked, in his quavering voice. &ldquo;I feel queer, dad,&rdquo; she replied. &ldquo;I waked up from my backwoods sleep.&rdquo; &ldquo;Backwoods! You mean this heah homestead?&rdquo; &ldquo;Never mind what I meant, dad.&rdquo; He could not understand, and if she confessed the truth to her mother it would only add fuel to that strange animosity which had developed between them. Molly plunged into one of her working spells. It could never have been said of her, at any time, that she was lazy, but she took streaks in which she was busy from dawn to sundown. Small as she was, Molly had strength and endurance. Her little tanned hands were pretty to look at, on the back; her palms, however, and the insides of her fingers were hard and cut and calloused. They occasioned Molly pangs of regret, though she had never felt ashamed of the labor that had coarsened them. It was just that the vanity inborn in her rebelled. She was vain of her little feet and trim ankles; and that was why she never went barefoot and barelegged any more. She would brush her hair until it crackled and shone gold glints in its dusky splendor. But she could work like a beaver, and she did this day, preoccupied and sad most of the time, yet cheerful during intervals. Toward sunset, while Molly was at the wood-pile&mdash;one of her many accomplishments being skill with an ax&mdash;her brother Arch approached. This was an unprecedented action for him. Years before she and Arch had been like sister and brother, playmates, but that seemed long before he had taken to evil ways of the woods and had earned the sobriquet of Slinger Dunn. &ldquo;Molly, cain&rsquo;t you leave a little work around heah for me?&rdquo; he complained. She dropped the fagot she had picked up, and she stared at him. What a handsome, shiftless, backwoods beggar! He had the build of an Indian. Horses had not warped his legs nor broken any bones. His soiled buckskin garments somehow suited him. For some amazing reason he had shaved the golden down from chin and lips, and his dark, lean face shone clean and smooth. Arch&rsquo;s eyes usually lost their piercing quality when they beheld Molly. Just now they were bearable. &ldquo;Arch, you shore ain&rsquo;t drunk,&rdquo; she said, thoughtfully regarding him. &ldquo;No. An&rsquo; I ain&rsquo;t sick, nuther,&rdquo; he replied. &ldquo;Never you mind aboot me. But cain&rsquo;t I be civil without you gettin&rsquo; sassy?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore I hope so. An&rsquo; now I know somethin&rsquo; ails you, Arch.&rdquo; &ldquo;Set down. A fellar cain&rsquo;t talk in there,&rdquo; he said, jerking a sinewy brown hand toward the cabin. &ldquo;Dad is out of his haid. An&rsquo; mother&mdash;wal, poor mother raves if you so much as open your mouth.&rdquo; &ldquo;Poor mother? Yes, an&rsquo; poor dad, too.... Arch, you said a lot.&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly, what chanct have we ever had to help make them different?&rdquo; he queried, swallowing hard. &ldquo;I never had no schoolin&rsquo; atall. An&rsquo; you&mdash;only a couple of years. What do we know? ... An&rsquo; before I was sixteen I was Slinger Dunn!&rdquo; Molly was so amazed she could not keep track of flying thoughts. One held, however, and it was that if awakening consciousness of heretofore vague things could come to her it surely was possible for them to come to Arch. &ldquo;Arch, is it too late?&rdquo; she whispered. &ldquo;Hell! yes, for me. But for you, Molly&mdash;wal, mebbe it&rsquo;s not. I hope to Gawd it ain&rsquo;t.&rdquo; &ldquo;Arch, if you want to know, I&rsquo;m as down in the mouth as you,&rdquo; said Molly. &ldquo;You have been queer lately, since you come home.... Mol, did anythin&rsquo; happen at Flag?&rdquo; &ldquo;A whole lot.&rdquo; &ldquo;Your feelin&rsquo;s get hurt?&rdquo; Molly gave a solemn little nod. &ldquo;Wal, tell me aboot it,&rdquo; he went on, with that wonderful flash of eyes. Molly laughed. &ldquo;So you can shoot somebody up?&mdash;No, Arch, you cain&rsquo;t ever help me that way.&rdquo; &ldquo;Is thet so? Wal, you never know. But all the same; tell me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Arch, it was awful for me,&rdquo; she replied, unable to resist sympathy. &ldquo;Mrs. See took me, as you know. She was lovely. Bought me clothes&mdash;an&rsquo; oh! such a beautiful dress. All white&mdash;what there was of it! Arch, I actually felt naked. She has fine friends in Flag. She introduced me. I was asked to help in a booth at the fair.... Well, it&rsquo;s like a dream now. A dream you cain&rsquo;t believe. I&mdash;I was the belle at that dance, Arch. Can you believe it? I was crazy with joy&mdash;at bein&rsquo; taken for a&mdash;a lady. I must have looked one, Arch.... Then somethin&rsquo; happened an&rsquo; my heart broke.&rdquo; &ldquo;They found you out, Mol?&rdquo; he queried, shrewdly. &ldquo;Thet you lived down in the brakes? Thet your dad was John Dunn? An&rsquo; your brother Slinger Dunn of the Cibeque?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, Arch, I was found out,&rdquo; she replied: &ldquo;But I didn&rsquo;t go back on you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore you&rsquo;d never do thet,&rdquo; he said, somberly. &ldquo;You oughtn&rsquo;t to have gone to Flag!&rdquo; &ldquo;Must I stick here buried in the backwoods all my life?&rdquo; she asked, passionately. &ldquo;There is, Arch. That&rsquo;s the trouble.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; it&rsquo;s with dad an&rsquo; me? An&rsquo; with mother, too, &rsquo;cause she&rsquo;s stuck to him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Arch, I am a Dunn, too.&rdquo; &ldquo;Mol, I come out heah to ask you a fool question. I see thet now. But I promised Seth an&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll keep it. He wants to know if you&rsquo;ll marry him?&rdquo; &ldquo;Seth Haverly? Heavens!&mdash;No!&rdquo; she cried, aghast. &ldquo;I reckoned you liked Seth.&rdquo; &ldquo;Perhaps I did, once, a little.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, marryin&rsquo; Seth wouldn&rsquo;t help none. Now you make me think, Molly, I&rsquo;d be ag&rsquo;in&rsquo; thet. But you needn&rsquo;t never tell him.... Andy Stoneham is a decent fellar, though. An&rsquo;, Mol, he&rsquo;s turrible sweet on you.&rdquo; &ldquo;That tobacco-chewin&rsquo; lout!&rdquo; exclaimed Molly. &ldquo;Who you gonna marry, then&mdash;to git you out of this heah hole?&rdquo; &ldquo;No one, Arch. You see that marryin&rsquo; one of these Cibeque boys would only nail me heah.... An&rsquo; the&mdash;the kind of a boy I&mdash;I&mdash;who could save me&mdash;wouldn&rsquo;t have me.&rdquo; &ldquo;He&rsquo;d be a damn poor sort, then,&rdquo; flashed Arch, with passion. &ldquo;If I was sich a fellar as you mean I could swaller a lot for you, Molly. Don&rsquo;t fool yourself. You&rsquo;re good enough for any fellar.&rdquo; His earnestness, his sincerity, touched Molly. She went to the wood-pile and sat down beside him, meaning to thank him, to yield to this surprising sympathy in him, but all she did was to burst into tears. &ldquo;Wal, don&rsquo;t cry,&rdquo; he said, almost roughly. &ldquo;Molly, I heah this Diamond cowboy, Hack Jocelyn, was seen in West Fork last Sunday.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal, thet lets you out, if you&rsquo;re not lyin&rsquo;.&rdquo; Molly dried her useless tears and presently faced him. &ldquo;Arch, I used to lie to you, about the boys an&rsquo; everythin&rsquo;. But I wouldn&rsquo;t no more.&rdquo; &ldquo;Reckon Jocelyn hasn&rsquo;t rid down heah so often the last year for nothin&rsquo;. Molly, is it you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, Arch, it&rsquo;s me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t you like him?&rdquo; &ldquo;I did at first. But not now. He&rsquo;s older. He&rsquo;s daid in earnest.&rdquo; &ldquo;Humph! You mean aboot you? In love with you? Wants to marry you, same as Seth?&rdquo; &ldquo;Arch, he&rsquo;s in love, all right, but he never said nothin&rsquo; about marryin&rsquo; me. An&rsquo; of course I wouldn&rsquo;t have him if he had.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I should smile not!&rdquo; ejaculated Arch. &ldquo;Marry one of thet Diamond outfit? My Gawd! ... But at thet they&rsquo;re some pretty decent boys compared to Jocelyn.... Mol, he an&rsquo; me are aboot ready to meet up.&rdquo; &ldquo;Arch, you can&rsquo;t help me by killin&rsquo; any more fellars,&rdquo; cried Molly, shocked at the significance of his words. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s one thing against me&mdash;your bloody record. An&rsquo;, Arch, if you keep on&mdash;if you should happen to kill one of that Diamond outfit&mdash;well, I&rsquo;ll just go to the bad!&rdquo; He took that darkly, without a protest, as if consciousness was being driven home to a man inevitably lost. &ldquo;Wal, I reckon I want to tell you suthin&rsquo;,&rdquo; he spoke up. &ldquo;All this talk aboot marryin&rsquo;.... I up an&rsquo; asked Lil Haverly to marry me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Arch! You did? Lil Haverly&mdash;that&mdash;that&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Never mind what. Anyway, I did, an&rsquo; I told her I&rsquo;d turn over a new leaf an&rsquo; go to ranchin&rsquo; if she&rsquo;d have me. An&rsquo; she laughed in my face! ... She&rsquo;d let me be sweet on her, too, Molly, an&rsquo; thet fooled me.&rdquo; &ldquo;She&rsquo;s no good!&rdquo; retorted Molly, fiercely, suddenly and astoundingly conscious of her brother&rsquo;s pain. &ldquo;Wal, not much, mebbe. Anyway, it doesn&rsquo;t matter,&rdquo; he replied, sitting up. &ldquo;Come. I&rsquo;ll help you pack in this wood.&rdquo; Molly slaved and brooded the days away. She could make life bearable by getting so tired during the day that sleep made short shift of the long, dark, silent nights. On Sunday she accepted the calls of Andy Stoneham and other village boys. Her wild intention to flirt viciously, to play fast and loose with them, seemed an utter impossibility, once they were in her presence. One Sunday afternoon, while walking the trail with Andy from the cabin to the creek, he took her hand in his, as had been his wont in the past&mdash;a preliminary to his by no means timid love-making. Molly let her hand lie passively in his a moment, which encouraged Andy to squeeze it. She wondered about this. She had absolutely no interest whatever in the action, except as something further to study about herself. Coming to the shade of a piñon tree, Andy gave her a little pull, accompanying the move by one of his speeches. Molly freed herself. &ldquo;Andy, don&rsquo;t do that again,&rdquo; she said, quietly, with a glance, and walked on. Presently he caught up with her, and falling into step with her went on beside her for a goodly distance, without speaking. It was some time after this incident that Molly realized he had never attempted another liberty with her. The other boys, however, were hopeless in this regard, and the only way Molly could circumvent them was to entertain them on the porch. Two of these Sunday afternoons were rendered exciting by the fact of cowboys from the Diamond riding up and down the trail. Hack Jocelyn was one of them on both occasions. The other cowboys Molly did not know, though she had certainly seen the youngest one, frank-faced, and as rosy as a girl. &ldquo;Molly,&rdquo; said Andy, on the second of these visits, &ldquo;you know thet cowboy Jocelyn belongs to the Diamond?&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore I know,&rdquo; replied Molly. &ldquo;Is that a crime?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, reckon &rsquo;tis, leastways since thet outfit&rsquo;s been buildin&rsquo; the drift fence.... Jocelyn has an eye fer you, gurl.&rdquo; &ldquo;He&rsquo;s very handsome an&rsquo; attractive. &rsquo;Most all the Diamond boys are.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Why doesn&rsquo;t Jocelyn brace in hyar then?&rdquo; &ldquo;He aims to catch me alone, Andy. An&rsquo; it&rsquo;s just as well you&rsquo;re heah.&rdquo; &ldquo;Much obliged. Glad I&rsquo;m good fer suthin&rsquo;,&rdquo; replied Andy, with sour humor. &ldquo;But jokin&rsquo; out of it, this fellar Jocelyn is up to tricks. Last night I seen him in Mace&rsquo;s saloon. He had money an&rsquo; was spendin&rsquo; it. Sort of stumped me to see Seth Haverly drinkin&rsquo; with him.&rdquo; &ldquo;No, Andy!&rdquo; exclaimed Molly, aroused. &ldquo;Wal, I jest did. Honest Injun. An&rsquo; you ain&rsquo;t the only one surprised.&rdquo; &ldquo;Was Arch there?&rdquo; &ldquo;Reckon not. Arch hasn&rsquo;t been around lately. Now what&rsquo;s he a-goin&rsquo; to say to thet?&rdquo; &ldquo;Say! He won&rsquo;t say anythin&rsquo;. But he&rsquo;s liable to do somethin&rsquo;. Seth an&rsquo; Arch are like brothers.&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly, around hyar Arch is considered a dangerous fellar, but one you can trust. Seth ain&rsquo;t so dangerous, they say, but he cain&rsquo;t be trusted. Now we know thet Arch an&rsquo; Seth, the other Haverlys, an&rsquo; Hart Merriwell, an&rsquo; Boyd Flick, an&rsquo; some others make up the Cibeque outfit. One or more of them, on an&rsquo; off, rode fer the Coffee Pot, the Hash Knife, the Bar O an&rsquo; other outfits in the valley. But so far as I know not one of them is ridin&rsquo; fer any outfit now&mdash;except their own.&rdquo; Molly echoed his last words and she caught the significance of his lowered voice and roving look around. &ldquo;It jest strikes me funny thet Jocelyn of the Diamond seems to me makin&rsquo; up to Seth Haverly of the Cibeque. How does it strike you, Molly?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not funny, you can bet,&rdquo; replied Molly, with a short laugh. &ldquo;Andy, there&rsquo;s somethin&rsquo; in the wind. I&rsquo;m thankin&rsquo; you for givin&rsquo; me a hunch. Arch is strange an&rsquo; sore lately. He&rsquo;s that touchy aboot the Diamond boys an&rsquo; their work I cain&rsquo;t get a word out of him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal, Molly, you can shore get them out of me,&rdquo; rejoined Andy, solidly, as one who had hit upon a happy medium. &ldquo;I heard folks talk aboot Jocelyn. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll tell you what. If he keeps on ridin&rsquo; down to West Fork it&rsquo;s gonna be plumb bad fer him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh&mdash;Andy!&rdquo; faltered Molly. &ldquo;Andy, I know he won&rsquo;t,&rdquo; whispered Molly. &ldquo;But I&rsquo;ve told Arch aboot it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly, air you shore you ain&rsquo;t interested in Jocelyn atall?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, I&rsquo;m shore. I was at first. Like a ground squirrel with a snake! But now&mdash;Jiminy, I&rsquo;ll run like a scared rabbit.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s good if it&rsquo;s true. Will Slinger believe you? Molly, excuse me, but thar&rsquo;s folks who say you&rsquo;re meetin&rsquo; Jocelyn on the sly, along the trail somewheres.&rdquo; Again Molly suffered a hint of her status in West Fork. She passed by Andy&rsquo;s covert suspicion. What could he know of a girl&rsquo;s honesty? But she told him rather forcibly that at least her brother trusted and believed her. &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal, I&rsquo;m glad. You know what a place West Fork is fer talk. Wuss&rsquo;n a beehive. An&rsquo; the drift fence has shore stirred up hell. Molly, do you know thet son-of-a-gun tenderfoot Traft is &rsquo;most clear to the brakes with his damned fence?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&mdash;I&mdash;hadn&rsquo;t heahed,&rdquo; replied Molly, thrilling. &ldquo;Wal, he is. Most clear on the Diamond! Ole Enoch Summers says, &lsquo;Tenderfoot hell! Thet boy is Jim Traft over again, an&rsquo; you two-bit calf-chasers hev gotta look out.&rsquo;&rdquo; &ldquo;Did Mr. Summers&mdash;say that?&rdquo; gasped Molly. &ldquo;He shore did, an&rsquo; to a whole storeful of fellars.&rdquo; From that hour Molly was kinder to Andy. She relied upon him to fetch her news&mdash;the facts as well as gossip. And Andy, evidently sensing some change in her, accepted the part gratefully. Molly went to one of the Saturday-night dances with him. It was an ordeal. She was made much of, though some of the boys were rude, and some of the girls catty with their remarks. Where was the grand white dress with which Molly Dunn had killed off the Diamond outfit, including young Mr. James Traft? Molly did not stay long, especially after she had espied Hack Jocelyn enter the hall. She was quick enough, and Andy helped her, to get out before Jocelyn saw her. &ldquo;Take me home, Andy,&rdquo; she said, nervously holding his arm. &ldquo;Wal, it&rsquo;s a huggin&rsquo;-bee inside an&rsquo; a fightin&rsquo;-bee outside,&rdquo; replied Andy, sardonically. &ldquo;West Fork as she is an&rsquo; always will be, I reckon.&rdquo; Saturday night at West Fork always had been exciting, and Molly could count on her fingers the times she had seen it. But no longer was it diverting. There were groups of men and boys outside, noisy and rough. Sounds of hilarity came from the saloons. Indians passed her with soft tread. She heard the jingle of spurs on all sides. Shadows passed in front of the dimly lighted windows. The air was cool and sweet after the smoky hall. The dark bulk of the Diamond promontory stood up against the star-studded sky line. Molly was glad to turn off the street into the lane, and she held Andy&rsquo;s arm tighter. &ldquo;Molly, I ain&rsquo;t had a chanct like this in a coon&rsquo;s age,&rdquo; said Andy, as if stolidly realizing the opportunity. He did not reply at once. Evidently the facts were hard to assimilate by his slow mind. &ldquo;Wal, now you tax me aboot it, I reckon I don&rsquo;t.&rdquo; Molly regarded that as more than an answer to her question. Andy, too, like Arch, had unplumbed depths in the darkness of which there must be good. Things that concerned the life of West Fork revolved uppermost in her mind. The drift fence, so dominant a factor now, was well out on the Diamond. In the eyes of the Cibeque folk young Jim Traft was a perfect hurricane for work. Conflicting stories about how he worked and his fights with his cowboys were rife about the village. More cattle had drifted down into the brakes this summer than ever before. Vague hints that they all did not drift there of their own accord filtered to Molly&rsquo;s ears. Her father was perceptibly failing now, and her mother talked of going back to Illinois. Molly wondered what was to become of her, and she did not care much. Arch had been home steadily for a month, more morose than she had ever seen him. But he had driven Molly out of the beanfield and had harvested the beans himself. She tried not to see that he and the Haverly gang had cattle deals frequently. They drove cattle in from the brakes and down the Cibeque, small bunches and short drives that had a sinister significance to Molly. Resolutely she refused to think the matter out clearly. She went back home through the woods, watchful along the trail, sure that she was going to hear any rider or see anyone before he discovered her. A ten-acre pasture appeared to have more young cattle than it could very well graze. But they had not been there the day before. Along the eastern side of the cabin a line of sunflowers blazed gold in the sun. Molly&rsquo;s mother had planted sunflowers every spring as long as Molly could remember. It had always seemed funny to Molly, but she guessed nothing was that any more. Her father drooped in his chair on the porch; her mother puttered around in the kitchen; some ragged saddled horses stood bridles down out at the edge of the timber. Molly knew that Arch would be out there. The still hot summer air seemed charged with a suspense. Molly&rsquo;s mother sent her on an errand to the village. She was always glad when a trip of this kind could be made in the daytime. Her mother had a habit of forgetting until just before the evening meal. Molly did not start out toward the horses. She hurried out back of the barn and slipped into the woods to cut across to the trail, sure that none of the riders with Arch had observed her. The dusty village street was asleep. So was old Enoch Summers in his store. Molly made the necessary purchase, and not tarrying to listen to his gossip, though his query about Arch made her curious, she made haste homeward. When she reached the shady trail she went slower to regain her breath. At a thickly overgrown point, where the trail turned, a brown hand shot out from the green and grasped her arm. Molly was not the screaming kind. But she struggled with all her fierce young strength. It availed nothing, however, and she was dragged through the brush a step or two into a little clearing beside a huge fallen pine. Molly was frightened but not surprised to look up into the dark face of Hack Jocelyn. &ldquo;Howdy, sweetheart!&rdquo; he drawled. &ldquo;I bin layin&rsquo; fer you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Let go,&rdquo; said Molly, and she jerked so hard that she freed her arm. &ldquo;What you want? ... Did you have to drag me in heah?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, there&rsquo;s some folks I&rsquo;d rather not see me,&rdquo; replied Jocelyn. &ldquo;My brother Arch, for one, I reckon.&rdquo; &ldquo;No. I&rsquo;m standin&rsquo; tolerable with Slinger. I was thinkin&rsquo; of thet sneak, Andy Stoneham. He&rsquo;s got eyes an&rsquo; he thinks too much.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, what you want? Mother sent me on an errand She&rsquo;ll be waitin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Let her wait. Reckon I&rsquo;ve waited often an&rsquo; long enough. I had to see you.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve had reasons, Molly. But I&rsquo;m glad to tell you one of them ain&rsquo;t pressin&rsquo; no more. I&rsquo;ve quit the Diamond.&rdquo; This had decided interest and surprise for Molly, though she did not voice it. She peered, however, up under the big sombrero, at the cowboy&rsquo;s handsome, wicked face. It bore recent signs of battle. &ldquo;I couldn&rsquo;t stand Jim Traft no longer,&rdquo; went on Jocelyn. &ldquo;I bin layin&rsquo; fer a chance to quit a month an&rsquo; more. We had some words. An&rsquo; it ended in a fist fight.&rdquo; &ldquo;Did he lick you?&rdquo; asked Molly, unable to resist her swift curiosity. &ldquo;I reckon he did,&rdquo; returned Jocelyn, ruefully. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s a slugger with his fists, an&rsquo; thet kind of fightin&rsquo; ain&rsquo;t mine, I couldn&rsquo;t make him draw a gun while I was ridin&rsquo; with the Diamond, so I jest quit.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. An&rsquo; I reckon thet sounds good to you?&rdquo; &ldquo;It means nothin&rsquo; to me,&rdquo; replied Molly, lying with tight lips. &ldquo;What was this heah talk aboot Bud Chalfack seein&rsquo; Traft insult you at the fair dance in Flag?&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s news to me. Was there such talk?&rdquo; rejoined Molly, flippant to hide her start. &ldquo;There shore was, an&rsquo; it&rsquo;s goin&rsquo; the round like brush fire. Any reason fer it?&rdquo; &ldquo;I cain&rsquo;t stop waggin&rsquo; tongues.&rdquo; &ldquo;You danced with Jim Traft an&rsquo; went out with him?&rdquo; &ldquo;Mr. Jocelyn, if I did, that&rsquo;s my business an&rsquo; none of yours.&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly Dunn, I&rsquo;m makin&rsquo; it mine,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;I reckon you&rsquo;d lie even if there was anythin&rsquo;. But you ought to have sense enough to know Jim Traft couldn&rsquo;t be serious aboot you.&rdquo; Molly looked away into the forest, conscious of deep-seated pain. &ldquo;Good day, Mr. Jocelyn. I&rsquo;ll be goin&rsquo;,&rdquo; she said, and moved away. Like a panther he leaped at her, cursing under his breath. She was a leaf in his grasp and she realized it. He lifted her high, held her close, and it was only by contortions of her supple body that she escaped hot fierce kisses on her lips. These burned face and neck. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re stayin&rsquo; heah&mdash;till I get through&mdash;with you,&rdquo; he panted. &ldquo;Let me&mdash;down. I&mdash;I&rsquo;ll stay,&rdquo; choked Molly. He let her slide till her feet touched the ground, but did not at once release her. Molly burned with more than shame. She could have killed him if she had not been so scared. All cowboys were violent and rude at times, but this one was a man with a cruel hand as masterful and relentless as if she were a horse. She must use a woman&rsquo;s wit or she might have infinitely more to regret. The place was secluded, lonely, so far from any cabin that screams would be useless. &ldquo;I reckon you&rsquo;d better,&rdquo; he said, giving her a shake. &ldquo;Molly Dunn, you made up to me when I met you. An&rsquo; now you&rsquo;re goin&rsquo; to pay fer it. I&rsquo;m through with the Diamond an&rsquo; I&rsquo;m throwin&rsquo; in with the Cibeque. If you fell sweet fer Jim Traft, like them moon-eyed Flag girls, you can jest forget it.&rsquo;Cause I&rsquo;m crazy in love with you an&rsquo; I&rsquo;m goin&rsquo; to have you. An&rsquo; you&rsquo;ll marry me as soon as I make the stake we&mdash;I&rsquo;m figgerin&rsquo; on.&rdquo; &ldquo;Marry you!&mdash;Mister Jocelyn, you&rsquo;re shore takin&rsquo; a lot on your haid,&rdquo; said Molly, aghast and astounded at his effrontery. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m thankin&rsquo; you because a man cain&rsquo;t do any more than ask that of a girl, but I must say no.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; why must you?&rdquo; he queried. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t love you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, if I know gurls, you&rsquo;ll change your tune soon as I make my stake.&rdquo; &ldquo;Stake. What you mean?&rdquo; &ldquo;Never you mind now. I want to talk to you aboot your brother. Accordin&rsquo; to the Haverlys he ain&rsquo;t got any use fer me. No reason except I was on the Diamond an&rsquo; thet I&rsquo;m supposed to have been driftin&rsquo; round heah on your trail. An&rsquo; thet ain&rsquo;t no lie! Wal, you&rsquo;re goin&rsquo; to set me straight with Slinger.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, I am!&rdquo; quoth Molly. Her fear held her in abeyance, but she could not wholly suppress her leaping spirit. &ldquo;If I know Arch Dunn that little job wouldn&rsquo;t be easy.&rdquo; &ldquo;We&mdash;I&mdash;I&rsquo;ve figgered it out,&rdquo; declared Jocelyn. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s been goin&rsquo; the rounds thet Jim Traft made a show of you at the dance. An&rsquo; we&rsquo;ll see it gets to Slinger&rsquo;s ears pronto. Wal, you&rsquo;re goin&rsquo; to admit it&rsquo;s true, an&rsquo; tell him I quit the Diamond because of it. Savvy, sweetheart?&rdquo; &ldquo;I savvy that you are a deep an&rsquo; cunnin&rsquo; hombre, Mister Jocelyn,&rdquo; replied Molly, sarcastically. Not to have saved her another of his onslaughts could she have restrained from that retort. But Jocelyn&rsquo;s instant grin proved that he took it for a compliment. &ldquo;You can jest bet your sweet life I am.... An&rsquo; now soon as you swear you&rsquo;ll tell this to Slinger&mdash;an&rsquo;, wal, give me a kiss&mdash;you can run home to your mom.&rdquo; Molly could not look back on any experience with men like Jocelyn. He lacked something most cowboys had. But she divined that with whatever sort of girls he had consorted he had won them to his will. It seemed rather a force of character than conceit. Where Molly had disliked him, had been vaguely repelled, now she thoroughly hated and feared him. &ldquo;I cain&rsquo;t let you go on thet, Molly,&rdquo; he said, cheerfully. The idiot actually felt sure of her. And he began to roll a cigarette, a shadow of a smile on his dark smooth face. Molly averted her eyes, lest he read in hers the thought that was forming. Once out of reach of his long arm she could get away. The cowboy did not live who could catch her on foot. Molly calculated distance and moment, while concentrating all her energy. Suddenly like a flash she ducked and bounded at once, under the brush into the trail. Jocelyn shouted and crashed through. But Molly darted away. She could run like a deer. Her moccasined feet pattered on the hard trail. He thudded heavily after her, soon losing ground. Molly looked over her shoulder to see that he might as well have been roped, for all the hope he had of catching her. She screeched a laugh back at him and sped on out of his sight, never slacking her swift pace until she reached the place she had cut across to avoid the riders. Once in the clearing, she went slowly so as to regain her breath. The fool! The hard-lipped cowpuncher&mdash;to think he could bully her into knuckling to him! But otherwise she welcomed the encounter. Hack Jocelyn had quit the Diamond. It was a stunning fact against all precedent. He was making up to the Cibeque, and especially to Arch Dunn. Some deviltry afoot! Molly divined that she was not the whole contention. She recalled Jocelyn&rsquo;s looks and words&mdash;his actions. How grimly satisfied with himself! He had built some big cowboy trick. What a clever ruse to win Slinger Dunn&rsquo;s friendship! If anything could have placated the lone timber wolf of the Cibeque, that crafty lie might. Molly sat down on a log to rest. And the gist of this complex situation burst upon her in realization of menace to Jim Traft. That agitated Molly even more than Jocelyn&rsquo;s attack on her. Hackmore Jocelyn would kill Jim, and if he did not Slinger Dunn or the Haverlys would. Molly divined it, shocked that it had not dawned upon her before, and her warm pulsing blood turned to ice. Jocelyn had proved traitor to the Diamond. For that he would be welcomed by the Cibeque, though she doubted Arch&rsquo;s complaisance. An infernal scheme had been hatched and Hack Jocelyn was the prime mover in it. And what did he mean by a stake? Molly understood the word to imply what riders called food or money that they hoped to get by some lucky or clever break. Jocelyn had certainly meant the latter and no small amount. Molly racked her brains for a solution, which was not forthcoming. Then her mother, espying her on the log, called, loudly. Molly hurried to the cabin. &ldquo;I declare, but you take long to do anythin&rsquo;,&rdquo; said Mrs. Dunn, harshly. &ldquo;Well, so would anyone if she got waylaid by a bully of a cowpuncher,&rdquo; retorted Molly. &ldquo;Who?&rdquo; demanded the mother, with a keen look at Molly&rsquo;s heated face. Instead of answering Molly ran like a squirrel up the ladder to the loft that opened over the porch. It had two compartments, one over each cabin. That over the kitchen was a storeroom, and the other, over the living-room, was Molly&rsquo;s. It had a window at the far end, a rude contraption that had been put in by Arch. The V-shaped roof was high enough only in the middle for Molly to stand erect. Since Molly had been a child this had been the only room she had ever had, but she preferred it to the living-room, where her parents slept and quarreled. Arch Dunn used to sleep on the porch below, and then Molly had felt safe, but for years now, most of the nights, only the pines and spruces had sheltered him. What furniture there was in Molly&rsquo;s boudoir was home-made, and some of it by her own hands. Yet until Mrs. See had taken her to Flagerstown she had been contented in this loft. But then she had been a good many things before that lamentable visit. At any rate, Molly kept her cubbyhole, as she called it, clean and neat, and fragrant with the scent of pine and spruce boughs, upon which she spread her blankets. She lay down there now and tried to puzzle a way through the maze of circumstances which seemed to have involved her. Following her mother&rsquo;s call to supper, Molly heard Arch&rsquo;s slow, clinking footfall. When he wore moccasins he could not be heard at all. &ldquo;Where&rsquo;s Molly?&rdquo; he asked. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know. She got huffy because I asked her a question an&rsquo; flounced out somewhere,&rdquo; replied the mother. &ldquo;Some of your questions are shore irritatin&rsquo;,&rdquo; said Arch. &ldquo;What was thet particular one?&rdquo; &ldquo;She came home red in the face and panting, and when I told her it had taken her a long time to go to the store, she said she&rsquo;d been waylaid by a bully of a cowpuncher. Then I asked her &lsquo;who?&rsquo;&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. I reckon the gurl&rsquo;s gettin&rsquo; too big an&rsquo; pretty to be alone on the trails,&rdquo; returned the son, ponderingly. &ldquo;Big! She&rsquo;s only a mite, an&rsquo; not so awful pretty.&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly is no kid any more. You can bet I&rsquo;m findin&rsquo; thet out. An&rsquo; as fer bein&rsquo; pretty&mdash;where&rsquo;s your eyes, ma? I heah she beat them Flag gurls all hollow at the fair dance. I heah a lot.&rdquo; &ldquo;I never hear anything,&rdquo; complained Mrs. Dunn. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m stuck in this cabin from sunup till dark. I want to get away before I die on my feet.&rdquo; Molly had heard such ranting of her mother&rsquo;s for years, but she was stunned to hear Arch reply that maybe soon they would be able to move away from the Cibeque. &ldquo;Arch! What&rsquo;ve you done now?&rdquo; queried the mother. &ldquo;Nothin&rsquo; jest yet. But I reckon to make a stake soon.&rdquo; &ldquo;Humph!&mdash;Call the girl to supper.&rdquo; Arch stepped to the end of the porch and called, &ldquo;Hey, Mol.&rdquo; Molly thought best to answer sleepily: &ldquo;Yes, Arch. Heah I am. What you want?&rdquo; &ldquo;Supper, you wood-mouse,&rdquo; he replied, and he waited by the ladder to give her a slap and then lift her down. &ldquo;No kid no more! Mol, you&rsquo;re as heavy as a sack of beans.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore, an&rsquo; worth aboot as much,&rdquo; retorted Molly. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re plumb interested in me lately, Arch.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yep. I am, more&rsquo;n you&rsquo;d guess,&rdquo; he replied. &ldquo;You an&rsquo; me are goin&rsquo; to have a little confab after supper.&rdquo; Molly looked straight into his piercing eyes, though she felt her face burn and her nerves quiver. However she did not reply. She carried her father&rsquo;s meal to him and placed the board across the arms of his chair. &ldquo;Lass, what&rsquo;s Arch jawin&rsquo; you aboot?&rdquo; he asked. &ldquo;I haven&rsquo;t any idea, dad. He&rsquo;s got one of his spells.&rdquo; &ldquo;There&rsquo;s too much goin&rsquo; on heah,&rdquo; declared Dunn. &ldquo;They all think I&rsquo;m blind, settin&rsquo; on this porch. But my eyes are good yet for cattle-rustlers.&rdquo; Molly saw Arch lift his lean head with the action of an eagle, and give his father a dark look. She felt perturbed herself at her father&rsquo;s pointed remark, though now and then he would make some caustic allusion to the movements of cattlemen. He hated them, and made little distinction between ranchers and rustlers. His day had been one when sheep held the range. Molly ate her supper in silence, which, in fact, was what they all did. Molly did not need to look at him again. She knew there was something in the wind, but she felt only half frightened. Arch was a puzzle lately. She walked beside him as he led her along the edge of the field toward the creek. The heat of the day was gone; the sun had sunk, yet the top of the Diamond blazed gold and red; there were deer in the pasture with the cows; somewhere a burro brayed shrilly. When Arch got to the head of the trail where it led down into the glen toward the spring he halted and said: &ldquo;Reckon this is far enough. No one can heah you if you do squawk.&rdquo; &ldquo;Arch, you&rsquo;re not a bit funny,&rdquo; replied Molly. &ldquo;An&rsquo; you cain&rsquo;t make me squawk.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who was the bully of a cowboy thet waylaid you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Hackamore Jocelyn.&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckoned so. On your way home from the store? I seen you go out the back trail.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. He must have seen me, too, for he waited along the trail.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, what&rsquo;d he do, Mol?&rdquo; went on Arch. He was hard to penetrate, yet so far Molly had no great misgivings. &ldquo;Reckon you don&rsquo;t, to be fair aboot it. But you ought to. Poor ole dad is done, an&rsquo;, wal, I reckon ma&rsquo;s no good. I&rsquo;m no good, either, fer thet matter. But I&rsquo;m your brother, Molly.&rdquo; She had never heard him speak like that before. &ldquo;Arch, you&mdash;you don&rsquo;t trust me,&rdquo; she faltered. &ldquo;Hell! It&rsquo;s only the last few days thet I seen you was old enough to mistrust.&rdquo; &ldquo;Then&mdash;how can I confide in you?&rdquo; she asked, simply. &ldquo;Molly, are you goin&rsquo; to make me choke thin&rsquo;s out of you?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m not anxious aboot it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, then, are you sweet on this Diamond cowboy?&rdquo; &ldquo;Which one?&rdquo; rejoined Molly, with a titter. He laughed, too. &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s one on me. But fer the moment I mean this heah Jocelyn.&rdquo; Molly told him bluntly just what she thought of Jocelyn. &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ll be dog-goned. I been given to believe you was sweet on him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who told you, Arch?&rdquo; &ldquo;It come round aboot.&rdquo; &ldquo;Somebody lied. I haven&rsquo;t any use for Jocelyn. If you want to know, I&rsquo;ll give you the straight goods aboot him&mdash;so far as I am concerned&mdash;but, Arch, I&rsquo;d a good deal rather not tell you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why&mdash;if you&rsquo;re willin&rsquo;?&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, it&rsquo;ll make you mad, an&rsquo; probably run you into another fight.&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly, it&rsquo;s no shore bet thet Jocelyn an&rsquo; I won&rsquo;t fight, anyhow. Seth has been talkin&rsquo; too much for Jocelyn. He&rsquo;s too anxious. There&rsquo;s somethin&rsquo; up. It looks to me like Jocelyn wants to double-cross the Diamond an&rsquo; somehow throw in with the Cibeque. I&rsquo;m not shore. But I don&rsquo;t take to the puncher. Mebbe I&rsquo;m cross-grained. Mebbe it&rsquo;s because he&rsquo;s throwed a gun heah an&rsquo; there. All the same, if he&rsquo;d double-cross Traft he&rsquo;d shore do the same by us.&rdquo; Long before Arch had concluded that speech, Molly had made her decision to be honest&mdash;to hold back nothing, though at the suspicion he might presently ask pertinently about Jim Traft her heart came into her throat. &ldquo;Arch, you&rsquo;re on the track,&rdquo; she replied, swiftly. &ldquo;Jocelyn has quit the Diamond. Had a fight with&mdash;Jim Traft, he said, an&rsquo; jumped at a chance to quit. He had a black eye, a cut lip, an&rsquo; some other fist marks. An&rsquo;&mdash;an&rsquo;&mdash;&rdquo; Here Molly paused to relieve the oppression in her breast, and failed to do it. &ldquo;Jocelyn tried to bully me into lyin&rsquo; to you. Wanted me to tell you he&rsquo;d quit the Diamond because&mdash;because Jim Traft had insulted me. Then he could throw a gun on Traft!&rdquo; Slinger Dunn whistled long and low, and afterward muttered a deep curse. His brown hand shot out like the strike of a snake and snapped a dead twig of cedar. &ldquo;Molly, you&rsquo;ve shore cleared up part of this heah deal,&rdquo; he said, gratefully. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll bet a hundred Jocelyn aimed to throw in with the Cibeque.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s what he said, Arch,&rdquo; rejoined Molly, and impelled by released emotion, she began at the point when Jocelyn waylaid her on the trail and recalled every word and action of that encounter. &ldquo;So much fer Hackamore Jocelyn,&rdquo; muttered Dunn. &ldquo;I wonder now&mdash;I jest wonder what cairds Seth holds&mdash;or if it is his deal.&rdquo; &ldquo;I wouldn&rsquo;t trust Seth Haverly with his grandma&rsquo;s spectacles,&rdquo; said Molly. &ldquo;Wal, Molly, mebbe I&rsquo;d better take your hunch. But I cain&rsquo;t believe Seth would double-cross me, leastways with a puncher from the Diamond. An&rsquo; thet brings us to real talk.&rdquo; Molly had only to look up to realize that herself. Dunn fastened a hand in her blouse, so close and tight as to pinch her neck, and with a slow pull he drew her up so that her eyes were scarcely a foot from his. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s this I heah aboot Jim Traft insultin&rsquo; you?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&mdash;I don&rsquo;t know what you heahed, Arch&mdash;but it&rsquo;s a lie.&rdquo; &ldquo;Did you meet him?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, at the fair.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; went to thet dance with him?&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh no. I went with Mrs. See.... But Jim Traft came&mdash;an&rsquo; I&mdash;I danced with him once.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; thet&rsquo;s all? You wasn&rsquo;t out in the moonlight with him, on the porch?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes&mdash;we walked out a&mdash;a little.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; he grabbed you an&rsquo; hugged you?&rdquo; demanded Dunn, leaning down. &ldquo;Y-yes, Arch&mdash;he did,&rdquo; whispered Molly. &ldquo;You let him?&rdquo; &ldquo;He&rsquo;s big an&rsquo; strong.... What could I&mdash;I do? But at that I slapped him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Which was proof you felt insulted. So how&rsquo;s it come you say you wasn&rsquo;t?&rdquo; Molly reached the subtlety of a woman in her instinct to protect Jim Traft. And she seemed to divine that her brother might not know more. &ldquo;Took to him somethin&rsquo; outrageous!&rdquo; ejaculated Arch, incredulously. &ldquo;Molly Dunn!&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, Molly Dunn,&rdquo; retorted Molly, gaining courage with resentment. &ldquo;Even if I do belong to the Dunns of the Cibeque I&rsquo;ve got a heart.&rdquo; &ldquo;I ain&rsquo;t blamin&rsquo; you, Mol,&rdquo; he returned, as if realizing the inevitableness of the fact, and he let go of her. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re only a kid. An&rsquo; he&rsquo;s shore a good-lookin&rsquo; fellar. An&rsquo; he can talk. But, Molly, the thing is he&rsquo;s old Jim Traft&rsquo;s kin. He&rsquo;s worth a million. An&rsquo; he couldn&rsquo;t have no honest intent toward you.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m no fool, Arch, if I am a kid,&rdquo; she rejoined. &ldquo;I know when a man means bad by me. Lord knows I&rsquo;ve had reason to. I shore knew today when Jocelyn grabbed me.... But Jim Traft didn&rsquo;t mean bad. I swear it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly, he must have talked you out of your haid,&rdquo; said Dunn, amazed. &ldquo;No. He didn&rsquo;t waste any time talkin&rsquo;. I&rsquo;d hardly got out in the moonlight. It was that white dress, Arch.&rdquo; &ldquo;What white dress?&rdquo; &ldquo;The one Mrs. See bought for me. It&rsquo;s lovely. He&mdash;they&mdash;everybody there said I looked&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;You poor kid! Mebbe they was all to blame. Damn thet See woman, anyway.&rdquo; &ldquo;Arch, let me put on the dress for you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly, I don&rsquo;t need any white dress to know how pretty you are.... An&rsquo; I reckon I hold ag&rsquo;in&rsquo; Traft your defendin&rsquo; him.&rdquo; &ldquo;But that&rsquo;s not fair, Arch.&rdquo; He shook his shaggy head doggedly. &ldquo;You cain&rsquo;t tell me honest thet Jim Traft didn&rsquo;t make you feel you was Molly Dunn of the Cibeque.&rdquo; &ldquo;No, Arch, I&mdash;I can&rsquo;t. He did. He was shore surprised. An&rsquo; he let out what he thought. Afterward he tried to&mdash;to soften it. But&mdash;but then my heart was broke.&rdquo; Dunn made a swifter and more expressive movement of his hand, passionate and vindictive. &ldquo;Hurtin&rsquo; your feelin&rsquo;s thet way is a wuss insult than huggin&rsquo; you,&rdquo; he declared, with a note of pathos in his anger. &ldquo;It hurt like sixty, Arch. But he didn&rsquo;t mean it. There is a difference between Molly Dunn of the Cibeque an&rsquo; Jim Traft from the East&mdash;rich, educated, with family name.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. You said it, Molly. Family name. You&rsquo;re daughter of old John Dunn. An&rsquo; sister to Slinger Dunn!&rdquo; &ldquo;I told him that.&rdquo; &ldquo;Before or after?&rdquo; &ldquo;It was after, of course.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore it was. No, Mol, there ain&rsquo;t no overlookin&rsquo; thet. He was only playin&rsquo; with you. Shore, in some safer place he&rsquo;d gone farther. Made a hussy of you!&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; you&rsquo;re givin&rsquo; it away thet you&rsquo;d let him go as far as he liked.&rdquo; He shook his head gloomily, and averted his blazing eyes, to hide their thought from her. Then he strode away, leaving her there trembling and stricken. Arch remained in one of his brooding moods. He worked in the fields as if to make up for lost time. And he did not go to the village in the evenings. The Haverlys rode over every day or so and engaged him in long talks, which left Arch more taciturn than ever. Then one morning when Molly came down she saw Arch&rsquo;s horse saddled and carrying a small pack. &ldquo;Where are you goin&rsquo;, Arch?&rdquo; she asked, at breakfast. &ldquo;Up on the Diamond, an&rsquo; I reckon you better stick around home,&rdquo; he replied, gruffly. &ldquo;What&rsquo;re you&mdash;goin&rsquo; for?&rdquo; she dared ask. &ldquo;Wal, fer one thing, to see if this outfit is lyin&rsquo; to me. I didn&rsquo;t tell you thet Seth an&rsquo; his new pard swear they posted a notice up north of the Diamond. They laid down the law fer thet drift fence. The Cibeque wouldn&rsquo;t stand fer the fence goin&rsquo; farther than the haid of East Fork. Thet&rsquo;s in the saddle just under the Diamond. An&rsquo; if it was built farther it&rsquo;d be laid down pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;What else you goin&rsquo; for?&rdquo; added Molly, anxiously. &ldquo;Wal, I reckon you ought to know,&rdquo; he replied, with a dark glance on her. Molly in trepidation followed him out to his horse, where her mother would not hear. She laid a trembling hand on him, as he was about to mount. She did not know what to say. But terror of something possessed her. &ldquo;Arch, if you really love me you&mdash;you won&rsquo;t&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; He stared down at her, arrested by her agitation if not her words. &ldquo;Who ever said anythin&rsquo; aboot me lovin&rsquo; you?&rdquo; he asked. &ldquo;You seem to&rsquo;ve been tolerable healthy all along,&rdquo; he drawled. Nevertheless, he evaded the question. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve only you to help me, Arch,&rdquo; she went on, swiftly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m tryin&rsquo; hard not to run true to what they expect of Molly Dunn. An&rsquo; I can be drove too far. I&rsquo;ll hate you if&mdash;if you&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; But he was flint. Molly gathered that her emotion somehow augmented his suspicion of her. &ldquo;You have run true to the Dunns, I reckon,&rdquo; he replied, bitterly, and rode away. Molly hid up in the loft, tormented beyond endurance. When she could stand it up there no longer she climbed down and went out to look for her horse. She had not done her errands by horseback for a year or more, for the very good reason that she had no saddle and would not be seen in the village without one. The dignity of sixteen years had been embarrassing in many ways. Molly had ridden bareback since she was big enough to get on a horse. She found the pony in the unfenced north end of the clearing. He was wild and she did not easily corner him. But finally, when she had him and was patting his neck she wondered what she had wanted him for. And she seemed struck with the fact that it was not beyond her to ride up on the Diamond to warn Jim Traft his life might be in danger. But worried as Molly was, she did not think that so dire a thing would threaten until the drift fence actually crossed the Diamond. Down in the valley the opinion was general that the barbed wire would never go so far. Moreover, Molly remembered more than once when Slinger Dunn had ridden off with intent to fight, and this morning he had not been like that. Three days later, while Molly was in the village with her mother, she heard that her brother was there, drunk and quarrelsome. At the store she met Andy Stoneham, who was a clerk for Enoch Summers. While waiting upon Molly, he contrived to whisper: &ldquo;Heaps goin&rsquo; on, an&rsquo; hadn&rsquo;t I better come out tonight?&rdquo; Molly nodded, and joined her mother, conscious of a sinking sensation in her breast. Old Enoch was full of talk. &ldquo;High jinks goin&rsquo; on around West Fork. Hain&rsquo;t been so busy since last fall round-up.&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;s goin&rsquo; on?&rdquo; queried Mrs. Dunn. &ldquo;Cattle movin&rsquo;. Riders comin&rsquo; through.&rdquo; &ldquo;This time of year! Well, that&rsquo;s strange.&rdquo; &ldquo;So it is, Mrs. Dunn. An&rsquo; we can lay it to thet drift fence. Did you heah the latest aboot Jim Traft?&rdquo; &ldquo;Where&rsquo;d I ever hear any news?&rdquo; Molly felt the blood tingle in her cheeks. &ldquo;Wal, it&rsquo;s shore news,&rdquo; replied the storekeeper. &ldquo;Young Traft has had notices put up thet he&rsquo;d make no claim to cattle already drifted down into Sycamore Canyon. His drift fence has crossed the head of Sycamore an&rsquo; is now out on the Diamond.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, is that all the news?&rdquo; declared Mrs. Dunn, indifferently. &ldquo;Wal, most folks is agreed thet Jim Traft was shore more than square to the Cibeque when he done thet,&rdquo; went on Summers. &ldquo;Mebbe there&rsquo;s more&rsquo;n a thousand haid of steers, let alone cows an&rsquo; calves, in the brakes of Sycamore. Seth Haverly an&rsquo; his outfit sold near two hundred two-year-olds hyar yesterday. Forty-eight dollars a head! An&rsquo; they&rsquo;ve gone back fer another drive. Reckon they&rsquo;ll have most of thet stock, before the other boys get wind of it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Forty-eight a head!&rdquo; ejaculated Mrs. Dunn, who appeared to be conjuring with figures. &ldquo;Arch was in on that?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, it seems not. He&rsquo;s had a fall-out with the Cibeque, or somethin&rsquo;. Anyway, he was in town last night. It was Slinger who busted in here with news thet the drift fence had reached Tobe&rsquo;s Well. It shore upset all reckonin&rsquo;s around West Fork. Tobe&rsquo;s Well is way out on the Diamond.&rdquo; While her mother completed her purchases, Molly waited in the grip of conflicting feelings. She could not help a thrill to hear Jim Traft kindly spoken of, nor a start at the information he had passed Sycamore Canyon with his fence. This was the dead line drawn by the riders of the brakes. Traft had disregarded their notice. Molly calculated that he was then actually within ten miles of West Fork, and not more than forty, to the east end of the Diamond. She had ridden up and down the brakes of Beaver Canyon and knew the trail to Tobe&rsquo;s Well. She had also been over the Derrick Trail once, but had no confidence that she could remember how to find it. After supper that evening she waited at the gate for Andy, something she had never before done, and she knew he would make stock of it. &ldquo;Shucks!&rdquo; he ejaculated, when he arrived to beam upon her with his homely face. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s different when you want suthin&rsquo; of somebody, ain&rsquo;t it, Molly?&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckon so, Andy. You see what bein&rsquo; friendly does. I&rsquo;m doin&rsquo; a heap of worryin&rsquo; an&rsquo; I&rsquo;m just low-down enough to want your help.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you&rsquo;ve cause to worry, Molly Dunn,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s get out of sight somewhere. I&rsquo;m plumb scared of thet Hackamore Jocelyn.&rdquo; Molly led him back of the cabin along the edge of the clearing to the creek and halted beside a huge fallen pine. The amber creek babbled over the rocks below; the squirrels and jays were noisy; the woods was full of the golden glow of sunset. &ldquo;Molly, last night I happened on suthin&rsquo; thet&rsquo;s got me scared stiff,&rdquo; he began. &ldquo;After work hours I went up the crick to ketch a mess of trout. An&rsquo; as I was comin&rsquo; back along the crick trail I seen Seth Haverly an&rsquo; some riders. I ducked into the brush an&rsquo; squatted down. I reckoned they&rsquo;d pass. But I&rsquo;m a son-of-a-gun if they didn&rsquo;t stop in a little glade not forty steps from where I hid. With Seth was his brother Sam, an&rsquo; Hack Jocelyn. The Haverlys stayed in their saddles, but Jocelyn got off. He acted oneasy. An&rsquo; he walked up an&rsquo; down the trail. They was waitin&rsquo; fer some one.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll bet it was Slinger,&rdquo; said Molly, who sometimes in excitement used her brother&rsquo;s sobriquet. &ldquo;It shore was. Wal, fust off, Jocelyn swore Slinger wouldn&rsquo;t come, an&rsquo; Seth said he would.... Molly, they stayed there till dark an&rsquo; I could repeat every blame word they said. But heah short an&rsquo; sweet is what it all means.... Hack Jocelyn has got in with the Cibeque outfit, an&rsquo; they&rsquo;re aimin&rsquo; to double-cross Slinger. They&rsquo;re goin&rsquo; to cut the drift fence an&rsquo; lay the blame on to Slinger. They&rsquo;re goin&rsquo; to kidnap Jim Traft an&rsquo; squeeze a big ransom out of his uncle. Thet&rsquo;s the stake Jocelyn is playin&rsquo; fer. But I seen, if Seth couldn&rsquo;t, thet Hack is playin&rsquo; a deeper game than they savvy. Mebbe Slinger is on to him.&rsquo;Cause Slinger wouldn&rsquo;t heah of Jocelyn throwin&rsquo; in the Cibeque. He wouldn&rsquo;t go in the cattle drive they&rsquo;re plannin&rsquo;. Thet all came out in their talk before Slinger got there.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh&mdash;did he come?&rdquo; cried Molly, breathlessly. &ldquo;Yep. Jest before dark. Hart Merriwell an&rsquo; Boyd Flick was with him. &lsquo;Seth,&rsquo; says Slinger, cool-like, &lsquo;I&rsquo;m splittin&rsquo; with the Cibeque. No hard feelin&rsquo;s. You&rsquo;ve got mebbe a better man than me in Jocelyn. Anyway, I cain&rsquo;t take the share of this cattle money you sent me. Much obliged, Seth. An&rsquo; heah it is.&rsquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; then Seth says,&rdquo; went on Andy, after a pause. &lsquo;All right, Slinger. You&rsquo;re shore your own boss. An&rsquo; if there&rsquo;s no hard feelin&rsquo;s on your side there&rsquo;s shore none on mine. But you ought to come in on thet kidnappin&rsquo; deal. Why, it&rsquo;s a gold-mine. Old Jim Traft won&rsquo;t blink an eye at a hundred thousand.&rsquo; &ldquo;Slinger owned it was a pretty slick deal an&rsquo; easy money, but he jest couldn&rsquo;t see it thet way. &ldquo;&lsquo;An&rsquo; why in hell not?&rsquo; asked Seth, sore as a pup. &ldquo;&lsquo;Wal,&rsquo; says Slinger, &lsquo;I reckon there&rsquo;s more&rsquo;n one reason. But particular with me is thet I happen to be goin&rsquo; to shoot Jim Traft.&rsquo; &ldquo;&lsquo;So we figgered,&rsquo; says Seth, &lsquo;but cain&rsquo;t you wait till we&rsquo;ve got thet ransom money?&rsquo; &ldquo;&lsquo;Nope,&rsquo; replies Slinger. &lsquo;I don&rsquo;t like the deal. An&rsquo; if I happen to run into any of you Cibeque gents up on the Diamond, I&rsquo;ll take particular offense. Savvy thet?&rsquo; &ldquo;&lsquo;We ain&rsquo;t deaf, Slinger. But I, fer one, am shore plumb surprised,&rsquo; answers Seth, slick-like. &ldquo;&lsquo;Wal,&rsquo; went on Slinger, &lsquo;I&rsquo;ve been sort of surprised myself, lately.&rsquo; ...
