An&rsquo; he shore said it full of meanin&rsquo;. Slinger rode away then, an&rsquo;&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;You heahed all this last night?&rdquo; interrupted Molly, poignantly. &ldquo;Yes, before sundown.&rdquo; &ldquo;But we heahed Slinger was drunk!&rdquo; &ldquo;Nary drunk. Somebody lied to you. He was as cool an&rsquo; hard as ice. Those fellars was scared of him, Molly. I haven&rsquo;t seen him since. Now to go on with what happened. You never heerd such cussin&rsquo;. Some from Seth an&rsquo; most from Jocelyn. Then they got their haids together again. It was all right with them aboot Slinger goin&rsquo; to kill young Traft. But they didn&rsquo;t want that to spoil their stake. An&rsquo; they reckoned they&rsquo;d have to give up the ransom idee. Hack Jocelyn busts in then with another. An&rsquo; it was that if Slinger did queer their kidnap deal by killin&rsquo; Traft, they&rsquo;d have a chanct to rustle a big bunch of stock an&rsquo; lay thet on to Slinger also. This struck Seth an&rsquo; his brother all right. An&rsquo; they won over the other two fellars. Then they started off. One of the horses must have smelled me fer he shied an&rsquo; didn&rsquo;t come far from jumpin&rsquo; on me. &ldquo;&lsquo;Somethin&rsquo; in thet brush,&rsquo; said Jocelyn. &ldquo;By this time I was pretty scared an&rsquo; I up an&rsquo; tore through the brush. Mebbe Jocelyn didn&rsquo;t let out a roar. An&rsquo; he shot three times after me. I don&rsquo;t want no bullets closer to me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, Andy, they&rsquo;ll find you out,&rdquo; cried Molly, fearfully. &ldquo;Wal, mebbe, but I don&rsquo;t see how they can. I&rsquo;d hate to have thet Hack Jocelyn meet up with me very soon, an&rsquo; you bet I&rsquo;ll see him fust.&rdquo; &ldquo;But Jim Traft will never see Slinger first,&rdquo; wailed Molly. &ldquo;I reckon not. Molly, ain&rsquo;t you a little over-concerned aboot Traft?&rdquo; asked Andy. &ldquo;No more than I am about Slinger,&rdquo; retorted Molly. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s an awful mess. Oh, Andy, what can I do?&rdquo; &ldquo;Do? Good Heavens! gurl, you cain&rsquo;t do nothin&rsquo;,&rdquo; he declared. And his idea of the enormity of such a supposition made Molly conscious of her helplessness. Yet could she not ride up to Tobe&rsquo;s Well, find the Diamond camp, and save Jim Traft from both her brother and the Haverlys with their new and treacherous ally? Tobe&rsquo;s Well was only ten miles. She could ride there and back in less than half a day. If she once eluded Slinger! But she would have to wait until he left, for he might come home any time, and he would find her trail and run her down. It was not conceivable that he would ride directly into the Diamond camp bent on his deadly intent. Slinger was slow to act upon any project, secretive and wary. Like an Indian he would watch the trails. Andy, roused to a sense of his importance, repeated parts of his story, and then guardedly leaned toward the possibility of telling Slinger of the plot against him. &ldquo;Molly, you might let on you heerd all this yourself,&rdquo; continued Andy. &ldquo;I reckon Slinger would believe you. Shore he would. An&rsquo; thet&rsquo;d let me out. I&rsquo;m plumb afraid of Hackamore Jocelyn. He&rsquo;s got it in fer me because of you. Aw, I figgered thet. He only had to look at me onct.&rdquo; &ldquo;Andy, I&rsquo;ll not give you away,&rdquo; said Molly, earnestly. &ldquo;An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m shore owin&rsquo; you a good deal.... Yes, I ought to tell Slinger. Oh, I must think.... Andy, I&rsquo;ll go back now. I don&rsquo;t want to risk missin&rsquo; him.&rdquo; They walked through the woods in the gloaming, and Molly did not repulse Andy when he timidly took her hand. He deserved some recompense for his interest in her brother&rsquo;s fortunes and his loyalty to her. She went as far as the gate with him. &ldquo;Andy, you&rsquo;ve been good to me lately,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;An&rsquo; I like you for it. I haven&rsquo;t a friend in all this valley, unless it&rsquo;s you. I wish I could be more to you. But I&mdash;I cain&rsquo;t.&rdquo; Bidding him good night, she ran back to the cabin. It was quite dark under the porch. No lamp had been lighted. &ldquo;You better come a-runnin&rsquo;,&rdquo; came in slow, cool voice from the gloom. &ldquo;Who was thet with you? Looked like Andy.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh&mdash;Arch&mdash;&rdquo; panted Molly, feeling her way to the porch. &ldquo;Yes, it was Andy.&rdquo; &ldquo;You like thet fellar?&rdquo; &ldquo;Why, yes&mdash;more than I used to&mdash;which wasn&rsquo;t much.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Andy ain&rsquo;t as no-good as some of them. He works. An&rsquo; he doesn&rsquo;t guzzle drink all the time.&rdquo; &ldquo;Arch, are you goin&rsquo; to be home a while now?&rdquo; asked Molly, sitting down beside him. She bumped against his gun, which contact gave her an icy little shudder. &ldquo;Pa had one of his sinkin&rsquo; spells tonight,&rdquo; said Arch, ignoring her query. &ldquo;He looked bad today,&rdquo; rejoined Molly, sighing. &ldquo;Where&rsquo;s ma?&rdquo; &ldquo;She got him to bed an&rsquo; I reckon she&rsquo;s in there.... I been sittin&rsquo; heah thinkin&rsquo;, Molly.&rdquo; &ldquo;What aboot?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, fer once not aboot my own damn self. Pa&rsquo;s failin&rsquo;, an&rsquo; ma&rsquo;s thet contrary. Course I&rsquo;m home so little it don&rsquo;t matter. But it&rsquo;s tough on you, Molly. What&rsquo;re you goin&rsquo; to do soon&mdash;when we bust up?&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh&mdash;Arch!&rdquo; faltered Molly, and she felt for his hand, slipping hers into it. &ldquo;Might as well face it,&rdquo; he went on. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m goin&rsquo; to get killed or have to leave the Cibeque country. Pa won&rsquo;t last long. An&rsquo; ma will go back to Illinois. Will you go with her?&rdquo; Molly pondered a long moment. &ldquo;I wouldn&rsquo;t if she didn&rsquo;t make me. I&rsquo;d hate to leave heah.... Arch, I can get work in Flag.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. An&rsquo; it ain&rsquo;t a bad idee. But don&rsquo;t get any moonshine idees in your haid.&rdquo; &ldquo;What you mean, Arch?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, aboot Jim Traft. Because you&rsquo;ll be in fer a heap more hurt than you&rsquo;ve had.&rdquo; Molly had fortified herself for the very thing hinted in his slow drawl, nevertheless it made her blood run cold. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m shore in for more hurt any way you look at it,&rdquo; said Molly, sadly. Molly thought the moment propitious. &ldquo;Arch, the Cibeque outfit is goin&rsquo; to double-cross you. Hack Jocelyn is at the bottom of it. But Seth Haverly is as treacherous. They want you to throw in with them an&rsquo; kidnap Jim Traft. Hold him for ransom. Course you know this, because you refused to be in it. But there&rsquo;s a lot you don&rsquo;t know.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you darned little wood-mouse!&rdquo; he ejaculated. &ldquo;You was there last night?&rdquo; &ldquo;Arch, I heahed more&rsquo;n you,&rdquo; she replied, evasively. &ldquo;Ahuh. What was thet shootin&rsquo; aboot, after I left?&rsquo;&rdquo; &ldquo;Hack Jocelyn heahed a noise in the brush an&rsquo; shot three times.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal! I come darn near ridin&rsquo; back.... Molly, what did you heah?&rdquo; Briefly then she told him what had happened along the trail, as related by Andy; and though the supposition was that she had been there she actually did not lie. &ldquo;&mdash;&mdash;!&rdquo; muttered Slinger Dunn, and he held her hand tight. &ldquo;I had a hunch. I didn&rsquo;t trust Jocelyn. But thet Seth could be so low-down! My Gawd!&rdquo; &ldquo;Arch, it couldn&rsquo;t be no worse,&rdquo; went on Molly, in agitation. &ldquo;They mean to cut the drift fence an&rsquo; lay it on to you. An&rsquo; if the ransom deal falls through&mdash;owin&rsquo; to you killin&rsquo; Jim Traft&mdash;they&rsquo;ll rustle a big bunch of cattle. An&rsquo; it&rsquo;ll all be laid on you!&rdquo; She felt him freeze, and he was silent so long that she could not restrain an appeal for him to abandon an idea of revenge and to go away somewhere. &ldquo;An&rsquo; leave it easy fer them?&rdquo; he queried, harshly. &ldquo;I know their deal now.... I&rsquo;ll settle with them afterward.&rdquo; He slipped off the porch. His moccasined feet made no sound and his dark form vanished in the gloom. Molly clapped a hand over her mouth. Of what avail to call out! Shaken and wretched, she climbed to her loft and crawled to her bed. Through the hours of distress that followed, one growing resolve at last found permanence, and then she slept. In the gray of dawn she was peering down from the loft, watching Slinger Dunn&mdash;when on the first time for long he rolled out of his bed on the porch and pulled on his boots and buckled on his spurs and gun-belt&mdash;when he slipped off in the gloom to return with his horse&mdash;when he packed blankets and biscuit and meat, and then rode away on his mission. Molly sat there, thinking out her plan. She would let Slinger get some miles ahead, then she would follow, climb the Diamond, and when some distance from Tobe&rsquo;s Well she would make a detour and hit the trail beyond Traft&rsquo;s camp, and ride back to it. She could hardly get lost, going only a short way from the trail. The peril lay in being discovered by Slinger. He would probably half kill her. But if she accomplished her purpose and saved Jim Traft from his enemies, she did not care what Slinger might do. In fact, that would be the end of Molly Dunn in the Cibeque. She would be hated from one end of the valley to the other. Traitress! A little fool who betrayed her brother and her kind for love of a man who had only played with her! An object of scorn she would be, and so far as Seth Haverly and Hack Jocelyn were concerned, she would be marked for life. Molly knew the Cibeque. Seth Haverly would choke the very life out of her, and Hack Jocelyn would do worse. These convictions added to Molly&rsquo;s terror, yet in no wise changed her mind. Before her mother was up, Molly put on her buckskins and slipped down out of the loft with a blanket, which she carried out to the barn and strapped on her pony. She fed him grain. Then she coiled a lasso and tied it to the end of his halter. The sun was rising like a rose in the cloudless sky. Dark and wild loomed the Diamond. It seemed to call to Molly. Every moment added to her courage. She saw clearer the beauty and the good of her action, if it were successful. In time even her own people would see that. &ldquo;What you up early for?&rdquo; demanded her mother. &ldquo;Didn&rsquo;t you heah Slinger?&rdquo; asked Molly, who knew how to divert attention from herself. &ldquo;No, I didn&rsquo;t, and you quit calling him that name,&rdquo; returned Mrs. Dunn, garrulously. &ldquo;Haven&rsquo;t I told you enough.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ma, if I guess right he&rsquo;s aimin&rsquo; to add more to what gave him that name.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, Lord help us!&rdquo; cried the mother, and she launched into a tirade against her son and ended against her husband and Molly and the low-down life in the brakes. That likewise strengthened Molly. She made another resolve. If she got out of this adventure alive, she would ride to Keech&rsquo;s and hire the boy there to drive her to Flagerstown. There she would go to work. Molly roused to a glamour over this enterprise. After breakfast, the first moment that offered, she ran to the barn, and leading Jigger out she mounted him and rode into the woods. Soon she came to the trail, in the dust of which she espied the tracks of Slinger&rsquo;s horse, and turning into it she rode north. Jigger was not shod and his hoofs padded softly. He could not be heard very far, and Molly vowed that she would see any rider before he saw her. It was still and cool in the forest, and she soon reached the zone where the Diamond cut off the morning sunlight. Here the dark shade was almost like twilight. The trail followed the creek and often crossed it. The amber water foamed around the boulders. Great gray rocks lined the banks, and above stood up the maples and oaks, and the lofty pines and spruces. At every turn of the trail Molly would peep around before going on. Always, too, she had a keen eye for Slinger&rsquo;s tracks. This was easy riding and there were miles of the gradual ascent before the trail entered one of the brakes, which was merely a local name for canyon. Open forest, with scarcely any brush, afforded vistas on all sides. Molly saw deer, but no cattle. She was still down out of the cattle zone. In a few miles the forest changed and thickened. The maples and oaks had been left behind. Clumps of young pine obstructed vision. The silver spruces began to lord it over the yellow pines. Manzanita appeared, and tufts of grass in open spots. Molly had difficulty in keeping the charm of the forest from breaking her vigilance. The wall of brown-streaked green rose on both sides. Through openings in the foliage she saw the lofty gray crags of the Diamond promontories. Soon she would be climbing in earnest, and then she must exercise extreme caution. Presently she missed Slinger&rsquo;s tracks in the dust of the trail. Molly rode back, bending in anxious scrutiny. She had been careless. Soon she found where he had turned out of the trail. Molly slipped off her horse. What could this mean? She dared not go on for fear she might get ahead of him. Perhaps he was going to climb the Diamond by a trail known only to himself. Molly stood in dismay, pondering the problem. Suddenly a rifle-shot clear and sharp up the trail gave her a violent start. A wild cry followed. Molly stood transfixed. Then came the clatter of rapid hoof beats. She drew her horse behind a thicket of pines. &ldquo;Nope. You cain&rsquo;t deal no more cairds to me,&rdquo; Curly Prentiss was saying to some one. &ldquo;I won&rsquo;t take a hand in any more deals against the boss.&rdquo; &ldquo;But, Curly, we all reckon you wasn&rsquo;t at your best when he licked you,&rdquo; rejoined Uphill Frost. &ldquo;How so?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you&rsquo;d been drunk. An&rsquo; you know how weak likker makes you. An&rsquo; you was sort of in fun. You didn&rsquo;t get mad.&rdquo; A silence betrayed that no doubt this eloquence and persuasion were balm to Curly&rsquo;s wounded vanity. &ldquo;Mebbe so. Dog-gone! ... But he hit me on the nose an&rsquo; seen how it hurt. Then he kept pokin&rsquo; me again right there. An&rsquo; thet&rsquo;s what he&rsquo;ll do to you, Up. He&rsquo;ll find out how anythin&rsquo; ag&rsquo;in&rsquo; your belly hurts you. Aw, you know you cain&rsquo;t even stand a tight belt. You&rsquo;ve et so many million sour-dough biscuits thet your insides is gone. An&rsquo; you, Jack Way, if you pick a fight with our boss, Gawd help you! Because you&rsquo;re all weak spots. You&rsquo;ve not got a well bone in your body. An&rsquo; ev&rsquo;ry time he cracks you with one of them big fists, you&rsquo;ll yell. I&rsquo;ll bet a month&rsquo;s pay.&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t you believe sech talk!&rdquo; declared Curly. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re doin&rsquo; a big stunt with this fence. Boys, the boss is a chip of the old block.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hack Jocelyn says so an&rsquo; Hack&rsquo;s an old-timer heah.&rdquo; &ldquo;Natural you&rsquo;d like to see Hack get licked proper,&rdquo; growled Frost. &ldquo;You can shore bet on thet,&rdquo; said Curly, emphatically. &ldquo;Wal, you an&rsquo; Bud have double-crossed us,&rdquo; complained Jackson. &ldquo;Bud is clean out with it. He says: &lsquo;You can all go to hell. I&rsquo;m sidin&rsquo; with the boss.&rsquo;&rdquo; &ldquo;Boys, you cain&rsquo;t blame Bud much. The boss kept me out of jail, but thet ain&rsquo;t a marker to what he done fer Bud. An&rsquo; if you look back hasn&rsquo;t this heah Jim Traft done somethin&rsquo; fer each of us, &rsquo;ceptin&rsquo; mebbe, Hack? Now I ask you?&rdquo; Both cowboys were significantly silent. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m gonna pick a fight with him, anyway,&rdquo; concluded Up Frost. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s sore at me an&rsquo; it&rsquo;ll be easy.&rdquo; &ldquo;When you aim to pick it?&rdquo; &ldquo;I had my mind made up more&rsquo;n onct. But when I get near the son-of-a-gun, I don&rsquo;t stick to it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. You jest cain&rsquo;t help likin&rsquo; Jim Traft. If Hack Jocelyn wasn&rsquo;t in the outfit we&rsquo;d all be fer the boss pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;Up, if you tackle him, I&rsquo;ll do it, too, soon&rsquo;s he licks you,&rdquo; put in Jackson Way. &ldquo;An&rsquo; mebbe we can get Cherry to try ag&rsquo;in, if he licks me.&rdquo; &ldquo;If? Haw! Haw! Say, you couldn&rsquo;t hire Cherry to stand up ag&rsquo;in under them sledge-hammer fists. Fer fact is he was sittin&rsquo; down all the time.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, we can get Hump to tackle it.&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know. He an&rsquo; Hump&rsquo;s been tolerable civil lately. It&rsquo;s workin&rsquo; on Hump.&rdquo; &ldquo;Curly, air you helpin&rsquo; to keep this outfit from bustin&rsquo;?&rdquo; queried Frost, scornfully. &ldquo;I reckon I am,&rdquo; replied Prentiss, in colder knife-edged tone. &ldquo;An&rsquo; you can expect trouble between me an&rsquo; Jocelyn any day.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell you say!&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Jack, I told you.... Let&rsquo;s go an&rsquo; try to talk Hump into suthin&rsquo;.&rdquo; The cowboys strolled away into camp, arguing and complaining, while Jim Traft lay there amused and touched and singularly pleased. This incident occurred at a camp on Pine Creek in the forest on what was called the Saddle. It lay half way between Black Butte and the Diamond. Not for weeks had Jim seen or heard anything so encouraging to him. He could not contain his delight. The drift fence was going on. He had not been in Flagerstown for weeks, but letters from his uncle kept him informed of the news at that end of the fence. He had long since worked and ridden out the soreness of muscle and bone, that had made him suffer so excruciatingly. He was now strong and hard. He could stick in a saddle. He could dig fence-post holes all day. In truth he seemed vastly changed and improved, and almost happy, despite periodic spells of depression. The months did not pale Molly Dunn&rsquo;s charm. The call to supper disturbed Jim&rsquo;s reverie. He got up, and keeping the thick, low-spreading spruce tree between him and camp he walked off into the woods, and circling, came back to camp on the other side. Meantime he had evolved a plan to help Uphill Frost and Jackson Way to realize their ambitions in regard to a physical clash with him. The prospect simply filled him with glee. He found the boys at supper, and a sudden cessation of voices proved he had interrupted something unusual, for cowboys seldom talked during their brief mealtime. &ldquo;Sorry to spoil your talk, boys,&rdquo; he said, with sarcasm and putting on a severe face. &ldquo;But I can&rsquo;t go without my meals to let you gab about me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, you might flatter yourself,&rdquo; replied Bud Chalfack. &ldquo;Sure. But not this time,&rdquo; said Jim, as he surveyed the mask-like faces. &ldquo;Hack looks mighty innocent and indifferent. The rest of you, though, strike me bad.&rdquo; &ldquo;We was only talkin&rsquo; aboot the fence,&rdquo; put in Frost. &ldquo;Me an&rsquo; Jack stand by our argyment of this mawnin&rsquo;. You&rsquo;re runnin&rsquo; thet line wrong lately, keepin&rsquo; to this thick woods hyar.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, tomorrow we&rsquo;ll run off a way, then, into the open draws. And I&rsquo;ll give you and Jack the job of digging post-holes for a few days.&rdquo; A long howl of mirth made the welkin ring. After that had subsided Uphill and Jackson rendered the air blue around camp. Jim stood it a while and then, though it was not out of the ordinary, he pretended to take exception to such profanity. &ldquo;Shut your dirty mouths,&rdquo; he ordered. &ldquo;If you want to rave you can do it without such cuss words.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wha-at!&rdquo; bawled Uphill. &ldquo;Ain&rsquo;t this hyar a free range? Cain&rsquo;t I open my mouth?&rdquo; &ldquo;Why don&rsquo;t you eat with it? This is supper time. And the rest of us are hungry.&rdquo; &ldquo;You heered thet, Jack,&rdquo; roared Up, in real or assumed wrath. &ldquo;I shore did,&rdquo; yelled Jack. Whereupon the two of them burst out into another long string of profanity. Jim got up and deliberately punched Uphill in the mouth and then Jack. That certainly and effectually stilled the profanity, as well as all other vociferations. &ldquo;After supper I&rsquo;ll oblige you boys with all the satisfaction you want,&rdquo; said Jim, sitting down again. Uphill&rsquo;s face was brick red and he appeared about to explode. &ldquo;You bet&mdash;you will&mdash;boss,&rdquo; he choked. Jackson Way bent thoughtfully over his supper, with the somber air of a boy who had been let into something unawares. Another silence fell. This time Hackamore Jocelyn broke it. &ldquo;So the Diamond&rsquo;s come to this!&rdquo; he exclaimed, in derision. &ldquo;My Gawd! no wonder we&rsquo;re the talk of the range.&rdquo; Jim bent a clear straight gaze upon his one real enemy. &ldquo;Jocelyn, the Diamond is doing fine, according to reports from Flag,&rdquo; he said, deliberately. &ldquo;And I can see how it could do better.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. So can I,&rdquo; replied Jocelyn, with far less subtlety than Jim&rsquo;s. &ldquo;Aw, hell,&rdquo; interposed Bud Chalfack, &ldquo;there ain&rsquo;t no outfit on the range thet could run this drift fence.&rdquo; &ldquo;All stale talk, Hack,&rdquo; spoke up Jim. &ldquo;Can&rsquo;t you think of something new? I sure get tired of it. You&rsquo;ve got a reputation for hatching up tricks and deals. Why can&rsquo;t you be original in your gab? It&rsquo;d amuse us, anyhow.&rdquo; Jim had spoken cheerfully, yet with scintillating pointedness. And this remark fell like a blanket upon the company. Most of the cowboys took a bold look at Jocelyn, to see what effect this strong talk from the boss might have. Jocelyn&rsquo;s lean face paled and he bent over his plate. The tension relaxed, but Jim felt that he had inserted a wedge into the breach. Uphill Frost finished his meal in remarkably quick time for him. And he arose to make most elaborate preparations for the fray. &ldquo;Boss, tell me where you want your carcass planted,&rdquo; spoke up Bud, quizzically. &ldquo;An&rsquo; give me word to send home.&rdquo; That resurrected the humor of the cowboys, except in several instances. &ldquo;Any money to bet, Up?&rdquo; inquired Curly. &ldquo;This ain&rsquo;t goin&rsquo; to be fun,&rdquo; quoth Frost. &ldquo;I should smile not. Lay you ten to one you cain&rsquo;t lick Jim.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why, our boss, you damn fule,&rdquo; replied Curly, innocently. Jim got up with alacrity, and threw off his vest. &ldquo;Bud, you be my second,&rdquo; he said, cheerily. &ldquo;Fetch those soft gloves of mine.&rdquo; Bud complied and Jim pulled them on. These were heavy, soft, woolen gloves that Jim wore occasionally. &ldquo;Lemme see? What&rsquo;s the idee of gloves?&rdquo; asked Uphill, suspiciously. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t want to hurt you badly, Up. These gloves are like pads.&rdquo; &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t say. Wal, you might have a hoss-shoe or nail hid in them. Lemme see.... Ahuh, I&rsquo;ll take my chanct with your bare fists.&rdquo; &ldquo;Come on. Let&rsquo;s get it over,&rdquo; said Jim, business like. &ldquo;We&rsquo;ve fence to build, you know.&rdquo; After a few passes at each other Uphill swung in like a snorting bull, and Jim making a feint with his right came up hard with his left. It connected. Bam! Up got rid of breath he could not retain. &ldquo;Wow!&rdquo; yelled Curly. &ldquo;Got Up&rsquo;s bread-basket fust off,&rdquo; added Bud, merrily. &ldquo;Fellars, we&rsquo;d better move back,&rdquo; suggested Cherry. &ldquo;Fer if the boss soaks him thar ag&rsquo;in we&rsquo;ll be dodgin&rsquo; biscuits.&rdquo; Hack Jocelyn stood in the background, smoking a cigarette, disdainful and aloof. The other cowboys, including Jackson Way, gave vent to their riotous feelings. Jim took the fight in hand then. That last one, a solid blow over Up&rsquo;s rather stout middle, elicited an awful groan from him. He sagged, and bent forward like a jack-knife. &ldquo;Fer Gawd&rsquo;s sake!&rdquo; gasped Up, his face convulsed. &ldquo;Boss, don&rsquo;t hit me&mdash;there&mdash;no more.&rdquo; Jim lowered his fists and ceased prancing around the bewildered cowboy. &ldquo;Very well. Have you got enough?&rdquo; &ldquo;Aplenty,&rdquo; groaned Up. &ldquo;All right, then, provided you call it square for keeps and shake hands. Otherwise&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss,&rdquo; interrupted Frost, hastily, &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve ben ag&rsquo;in you, jest natural an&rsquo; cussed. But there was no sense in it. An&rsquo; hyar you are!&rdquo; Uphill showed the true manliness back of his contrary, cowboy, cantankerous spirit. &ldquo;Now, Jack, come on. It&rsquo;s the only way for us to settle the difficulty.&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckon so, boss,&rdquo; returned Jack, ruefully. &ldquo;But it ain&rsquo;t fair.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why not? Sure, I didn&rsquo;t start this. If you want to apologize&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Nope. I ain&rsquo;t takin&rsquo; nothin&rsquo; back. But my pore body has too many sprains an&rsquo; dislocations an&rsquo; cracks to stand your hammerin&rsquo;. All the same, I&rsquo;ll take my medicine.&rdquo; And he did take it, putting up a better fight than Frost&rsquo;s, though of obvious pain to himself and less fun for the cowboys. He brought the blood from Jim&rsquo;s nose, an inconsequential blow, yet one loudly applauded by his companions. Shortly after that Jim caught him in the ribs. Jack went down in a heap, rolled over, and laboriously sat up, his face ashen. &ldquo;Jim&mdash;it was&mdash;bad enough&mdash;hittin&rsquo; all my other&mdash;places,&rdquo; he panted. &ldquo;But them busted&mdash;ribs! I knowed you&rsquo;d find them.... Would you mind&mdash;callin&rsquo; it off fer keeps?&rdquo; &ldquo;If you want,&rdquo; replied Jim. &ldquo;But you&rsquo;ve bloodied my nose! And I reckon I&rsquo;d like to soak you some more.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw no! Take it out on Hackamore,&rdquo; replied Jack, beseechingly, and he got up to offer his hand to Jim. His suggestion instantly charged the atmosphere with something more compelling. &ldquo;Strikes me,&rdquo; said Jim, quickly. &ldquo;How about it, Jocelyn? You&rsquo;ve a sore head. You&rsquo;ve been mean and full of poison talk. And I haven&rsquo;t been as decent as I might have been. Let&rsquo;s have a whack at each other. It can&rsquo;t do any harm and it might do good.&rdquo; &ldquo;I wouldn&rsquo;t soil my gloves on you, Mister Traft,&rdquo; drawled Jocelyn, with undisguised malignity. &ldquo;Oh, wouldn&rsquo;t you? I reckon you&rsquo;re thinking more of your handsome face.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yep, I ben told it&rsquo;s handsome lately, an&rsquo; I&rsquo;m shore keepin&rsquo; it so,&rdquo; returned Jocelyn, with caustic significance. No one there could have misinterpreted him. Jim bit his tongue. It was no time for him to say more or make a move. Jocelyn packed his gun and had the look of a man who would strain any chance to use it. That incident marked the definite break between Jim and Jocelyn. There was no hope of a better state of feeling. Jim hoped this disgruntled cowboy would keep to his threat to quit the Diamond. Both Bud and Curly had warned Jim to let Hack alone to take his own time. They assured Jim that Hack would go presently, but they could not quite figure just what he was up to. Jim was glad of their advice and this late evidence of friendship. It seemed, indeed, that Jocelyn was now the only thorn in the flesh of the Diamond. Day by day he became more alienated from Cherry Winters and Hump Stevens, who had been the last of his stand-bys. Another Saturday came. Some of the boys went to Flagerstown. Hack had disappeared before daylight, and according to Bud and Curly his horse tracks led south. &ldquo;Boss, he&rsquo;s gone to West Fork,&rdquo; claimed Bud, with a wise look. &ldquo;Shore as you&rsquo;re born,&rdquo; agreed Curly, wagging his bright head. &ldquo;Curly, let&rsquo;s ride down there, too,&rdquo; suggested Bud. The idea found favor with Curly, who looked at Jim for approval. &ldquo;It ain&rsquo;t a bad idee, boss. We&rsquo;ll hit the Sycamore trail at Tobe&rsquo;s Well an&rsquo; go off the mountain there. Thet&rsquo;s aboot at the haid of the brakes. We&rsquo;ll get a line on driftin&rsquo; cattle, an&rsquo; mebbe other thin&rsquo;s, too.&rdquo; &ldquo;Go, by all means,&rdquo; replied Jim, and he found himself fighting an almost irresistible longing to go with them. That was the longest week-end Jim had put in since his advent in Arizona. He was on pins and needles until Bud and Curly returned, late Sunday night. To his disappointment, they seemed uncommunicative and brought no news of any moment. They had not seen Hackamore Jocelyn. They did not mention either Slinger or Molly Dunn. They reported considerably more cattle than they had expected to run across. Numerous dead cows, and old carcasses, neither of which had been killed by wolves or lions, lying in the open along the trails, attested to a changed and startling condition of affairs since the drift fence had been started. Jim was too sick at heart to go into the subject of stolen stock any deeper that night. What had he expected of the boys? When he lay alone in the dark, his eyes peering through the canopy of foliage at the stars, he confessed to himself that he had yearned for even a word of Molly Dunn. Next morning at an opportune moment he asked Bud. &ldquo;Shore I seen Molly,&rdquo; replied the cowboy, frankly. &ldquo;At the village. But she didn&rsquo;t see me. Looked sweeter&rsquo;n a peach to me. Changed a little, though, somehow. Curly says so.&rdquo; &ldquo;Did you hear anything about Hack?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yep. An&rsquo; it ain&rsquo;t good news,&rdquo; replied Bud, soberly. &ldquo;Curly reckoned we&rsquo;d better not tell you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why not?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you might ride off down there.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bud, tell me if there&rsquo;s anything to worry you.&rdquo; &ldquo;It ain&rsquo;t worryin&rsquo; us exzactly,&rdquo; rejoined Bud, scratching his head. &ldquo;I wanted to tell you, an&rsquo; Curly talked ag&rsquo;in&rsquo; it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Jocelyn went to see Molly Dunn,&rdquo; asserted Jim, quietly. &ldquo;He shore did, which ain&rsquo;t none of our bizness, since thet little devil has no use for us. But, boss, this is damn queer. Hack is makin&rsquo; up to the Haverlys. An&rsquo; thet means the Cibeque outfit!&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, what of it? I wish he&rsquo;d quit us, as he&rsquo;s hinted.&rdquo; &ldquo;What of it? Gosh! I don&rsquo;t know. But if Hack quits us I&rsquo;ll shore tell you what Curly an&rsquo; I think.&rdquo; Hackamore Jocelyn did not ride into camp until late Monday night. This was a break against rules, and Jim saw that the cowboys were keen to get his reaction to it. &ldquo;Where&rsquo;ve you been, Jocelyn?&rdquo; he asked. &ldquo;Down in the brakes to see my gurl,&rdquo; replied the cowboy, almost gayly. He had not been drinking. There seemed to be a singular elation or buoyancy about him. &ldquo;Why didn&rsquo;t you get back Sunday night, same as Curly and Bud? They went down to West Fork.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hell you say!&rdquo; In a twinkling Jocelyn was his old self. &ldquo;Wal, if you want to know I was havin&rsquo; too sweet a time with Molly.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m not inquiring into your private affairs,&rdquo; said Jim, coldly. &ldquo;But if you take another day off without permission, I&rsquo;ll fire you.... Do you understand?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I ain&rsquo;t hard of hearin&rsquo;, Mister Traft,&rdquo; replied Jocelyn, softly. No more was said. Jocelyn ate his supper alone. The cowboys sat silent. Jim sought his bed presently, and tried to find ease from his pain in slumber. The drift fence went on mile after mile, and one camp followed another. Three week-ends in succession Jocelyn rode down to West Fork, but on each occasion he returned some time late on Sunday night. The other cowboys ceased riding to Flagerstown. Curly and Bud rode off alone the second week-end, evidently bent on a mission of their own, which Jim guessed to be the movement of cattle, as well as of Hackamore Jocelyn. Early in August, Jim Traft, acting solely upon his own judgment and responsibility, celebrated his fencing off the many heads of Sycamore Canyon from the brakes of the Cibeque, by posting notices that the Diamond would lay no further claim to stock in and north of Sycamore, and west of the drift fence. It was a strategic move on Jim&rsquo;s part. It caught the fancy of every cowboy in his outfit, excepting Jocelyn, who, nevertheless, was staggered by it. Jim had just wanted to be generous to the riders down in the brakes. He had authority to do what he thought best. What did a few herds of cattle, more or less, matter to his uncle? But a singular reaction to this proclamation was the effect upon his cowboys and the several homesteaders living in the proscribed limits. Jim won his cowboys by that act, and further alienated Jocelyn. The homesteaders called at his camp, unmistakably friendly and grateful. They all profited by it. One of them told Bud that he could now make a good start at ranching. &ldquo;He was stealin&rsquo; a few cattle right along,&rdquo; vowed Bud. &ldquo;An&rsquo; he admitted it. Any honest cattleman will admit he&rsquo;s run a few haid not strictly his.... Dog-gone it, boys, Jim Traft has hit one plumb center.&rdquo; But so far as Jim was concerned this splendid news, which he knew would travel like wild-fire all over the range, was more than offset by Jocelyn&rsquo;s talk. The shrewd cowboy had divined how his talk about West Fork and Molly Dunn hurt Jim, and after that he kept everlastingly at it at meal hours or round the camp fire. At first it was just conceited comments upon his girl and her attachment to him. Gradually, however, it developed into vulgar parade of conquest, at which times Jim would make himself scarce. It threatened worse, and Jim vowed if he heard any more derogatory to Molly Dunn that he would force the issue with Jocelyn, come what might, and dismiss him from the Diamond. In mid-August the drift fence, now up on the great triangular promontory, received its first backset from enemies. Half a dozen stretches, where it crossed the heads of Rocky Canyon, had been laid low, with wire cut and hopelessly tangled, and posts broken down. Two days of hard labor were required to repair the damage. Jim had his consolation in the slow anger of the cowboys. It frightened him, too, for he now saw indications of his uncle&rsquo;s prophecy coming true. &ldquo;Boss, let me trail them hoss tracks,&rdquo; begged Curly Prentiss. &ldquo;I can find out in two days who cut our wire.&rdquo; &ldquo;Suppose you do, Curly. What good will that do?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, we can stop it from happenin&rsquo; ag&rsquo;in. An&rsquo;, Jim, shore as Gawd made little apples it will happen, onless we hit thet trail.&rdquo; &ldquo;How do you propose to stop it?&rdquo; queried Jim, aghast at the flashing-eyed cowboy. &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s fer you to say, after we ketch these hombres.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, let&rsquo;s wait a little. A few miles of wire, a few days&rsquo; extra work&mdash;what&rsquo;s that to us? I&rsquo;ll admit, though, if they keep on we&rsquo;ll be up a stump.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. We cain&rsquo;t help matters by waitin&rsquo;. Bud an&rsquo; I agree it&rsquo;s thet Cibeque outfit, though we cain&rsquo;t prove it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Prentiss,&rdquo; spoke up Jocelyn, bitingly, &ldquo;I reckon you know it&rsquo;s onhealthy to speak names when you cain&rsquo;t prove nothin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hack, I can shore prove one thing damn pronto,&rdquo; snapped Curly. &ldquo;An&rsquo; what&rsquo;s thet?&rdquo; &ldquo;I take offense at your speakin&rsquo; up fer the Cibeque.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. But what does thet prove?&rdquo; &ldquo;It proves you can git my game any minnit.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, if thet&rsquo;s so there ain&rsquo;t any helluva rush. I&rsquo;ve a date with my gurl, Molly, on Sunday, an&rsquo; I shore wouldn&rsquo;t want to miss thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, you&rsquo;re a liar, Hack!&rdquo; retorted Curly, passionately. Jim got between the two, and by backing Curly away from the camp fire he prevented more trouble for the present. &ldquo;Thick!&rdquo; echoed Jim. &ldquo;Shore. He claims he&rsquo;s thick with Molly. Haven&rsquo;t you heahed him?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. What do you mean by thick?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, it&rsquo;s pretty low-down fer a gurl as sweet an&rsquo; young as Molly.&rdquo; &ldquo;But you&mdash;It might be true?&rdquo; &ldquo;How about you, Curly?&rdquo; asked Jim, in strangely level voice. &ldquo;I would, too. In a minnit,&rdquo; replied Curly, with his fair head lifting in a way to thrill Jim. &ldquo;What chance has thet pore kid had? Her father&rsquo;s no good. An&rsquo; they say her mother will cock her eye at a cowboy. An&rsquo; Slinger Dunn! He&rsquo;d be enough to ruin any gurl.&rdquo; Jim made no immediate reply. He sat on his bed and pulled the petals off a tiny aster, which he had absentmindedly plucked. Twilight was stealing down through the forest, melancholy and tranquil. The heat of the day was dissipating. A bell on one of the hobbled horses tinkled musically. The vast forest sighed with a breath of breeze, moving down from the heights. &ldquo;Curly, I wish I were as much of a man as you are,&rdquo; said Jim, presently. &ldquo;Aw, boss, thet&rsquo;s nonsense! You shore are an&rsquo; more,&rdquo; burst out Curly, nonplussed yet pleased. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s bin a drill fer you&mdash;this Diamond job. But the outfit&rsquo;s with you. Honest, Jim, an&rsquo; heah&rsquo;s my hand on it. We cain&rsquo;t count Jocelyn. But to hell with him! He&rsquo;s bin a disturber always, an&rsquo; he&rsquo;s growed wuss. I reckon you&rsquo;d better fire him pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;Curly, I couldn&rsquo;t answer for my temper&mdash;if I faced him again tonight,&rdquo; replied Jim, unsteadily. &ldquo;Perhaps tomorrow or soon.&rdquo; &ldquo;Jim, ain&rsquo;t you takin&rsquo; this hombre too serious? After all, you&rsquo;re boss of the Diamond. An&rsquo; who&rsquo;s Hack Jocelyn?&rdquo; &ldquo;Claims! You mean aboot Molly?&rdquo; asked Curly, incredulously. &ldquo;Yes. Only I take it harder than you, Curly.&rdquo; &ldquo;Fust Bud, an&rsquo; then me, an&rsquo; now you! Aw! ... Who&rsquo;d ever thunk it? Thet black-eyed little devil.&rdquo; &ldquo;Curly, I&rsquo;d bet my life she&rsquo;s decent,&rdquo; declared Jim, with emotion. &ldquo;So would I. But it&rsquo;s a long shot, an&rsquo; we&rsquo;re takin&rsquo; odds!&rdquo; It was from the western promontory under which the Cibeque curled like a winding snake. The rest was endless green, relieved by bare spots and gray specks, which were the homesteads of the inhabitants. West Fork lay almost under the rim, a few cabins and fields, a gray line of road between some houses. Far to the south the dense forest began to lose its grip and showed bare grass flats and ridges. Westward the slopes ran up in long slants, like the ribs of a washboard, ending in a craggy mountain range. Standing above the forest to look back through the wilderness he had built the drift fence, Jim gazed down over a gradual descent to the open cattle country, rolling and vast and dotted, ending in deep purple distance. The splendid panorama transported Jim. He gazed long, and ever his eyes traveled back to the picturesque little homestead in the bend of the Cibeque, where he imagined Molly Dunn lived. He could see a log cabin, a ragged clearing in the woods, and tiny specks that must have been cattle or horses. He lingered there a long while. This, his first sight of Arizona land from a lofty prospect, had staggered him with its vastness, its magnificence, its tremendous note of solitude and the wild. For weeks a subtle happiness in his surroundings had been almost imperceptibly stealing over Jim. He had grown to love the forest and life in the open. He could not hate this beautiful wilderness, because through it he had received the cruelest hurt he had ever suffered. And when at length he clambered over the rocks, up to a level and to his horse, he realized that Arizona had claimed him. On the way back to camp, while riding across one of the grassy draws which headed on the western side of the Diamond, Jim found another placard nailed to a tree. It was identical with one Hackamore Jocelyn had brought in from the head of Sycamore, and its crude misspelled message read the same. This one, however, had a round black spot in the center. Upon tearing the placard loose Jim saw that the spot was a bullet hole. It stirred a curious heat in his veins. And he was reminded of Curly&rsquo;s admonition, not to ride around alone through the forest. &ldquo;To be honest aboot it, boss, I&rsquo;m more afeared Hack Jocelyn will take a shot at you than one of these Cibeque hombres,&rdquo; said Curly. &ldquo;Curly! I can&rsquo;t believe that of Jocelyn,&rdquo; expostulated Jim. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s not hard to believe it of Slinger Dunn. But one of my own men. No!&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, heah you are&mdash;the same old tenderfoot! You&rsquo;re daid wrong, Jim. Jocelyn would do thet little thin&rsquo; if he had a chance. Mebbe it&rsquo;s on his mind an&rsquo; thet&rsquo;s why he hangs on heah. But Slinger Dunn would never shoot you or no other man in the back.&rdquo; &ldquo;You rate Slinger Dunn above Jocelyn as a man?&rdquo; queried Jim, in surprise. &ldquo;Lord! yes. An&rsquo; it&rsquo;s hard to explain, boss. But you&rsquo;ll get it some day.... Please take another hunch from me. An&rsquo; from this next camp we&rsquo;re makin&rsquo; I&rsquo;m advisin&rsquo; you to have one man whose job is to keep back off the fence line an&rsquo; watch.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s not a bad idea, Curly,&rdquo; replied Jim. &ldquo;You can take turns, one man a day. But it&rsquo;ll slow us up.&rdquo; &ldquo;Listen, Jim. This heah drift fence won&rsquo;t be done this fall, an&rsquo; what with patchin&rsquo; it up an&rsquo; savin&rsquo; our hides, we&rsquo;ll shore be next year on the job, an&rsquo; then some more.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gosh! Curly, but you&rsquo;re a pessimistic cuss.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I don&rsquo;t know jest what thet is, but I reckon it&rsquo;s a compliment.&rdquo; Next day Jim moved camp to Quaking Asp and inaugurated the scout duty for one man. It was hailed with satisfaction, except in case of Jocelyn, who ridiculed it as another &ldquo;new-fangled idee from Missourie.&rdquo; &ldquo;That lets you out, Jocelyn,&rdquo; retorted Jim, like a flash. &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know as I would have trusted you, anyhow, with a job so important as that.&rdquo; This was throwing down the gauntlet with a vengeance, and certainly against the advice of Curly and Bud. &ldquo;Mister Traft, you&rsquo;re gettin&rsquo; mighty pert these days,&rdquo; returned Jocelyn, the ugly expression changing his handsome face. &ldquo;Yes. I&rsquo;m finding out what little good it does to be civil to some people.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Wal, your wise joke was more&rsquo;n oncivil, I take it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Jocelyn, you can take that crack of mine any way you like.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shucks! We&rsquo;re shore bustin&rsquo; out brave now.&rdquo; &ldquo;Keeping my mouth shut in the past wasn&rsquo;t proof I was afraid of you, Hack Jocelyn. I&rsquo;ve politely invited you to fight&mdash;twice. Beware of the third time.&rdquo; Bud slouched in between the belligerents, sloe-eyed, and hard of face for him, but he did not speak. &ldquo;Shore,&rdquo; drawled Jocelyn, with that peculiarly mean note. &ldquo;I know you invited me to a fist fight. An&rsquo; I couldn&rsquo;t take you on. My sweetheart won&rsquo;t have me all bunged up.&rdquo; Jim felt the cold tightening of his skin, which heralded the receding of blood from his face. Either this crafty cowboy had guessed his secret or he had learned something from Molly Dunn. And in the passion of the moment Jim inclined to the latter suspicion. If he replied to Jocelyn, or even glanced at him, there would be no more possibility of restraint. Yet Jim quivered in his eagerness to get his hands on Jocelyn. Curly saved the situation for him. &ldquo;Hack, you ain&rsquo;t showin&rsquo; much respect fer our boss,&rdquo; he said, and interposed his long frame before Jocelyn. That worthy let out a guffaw. &ldquo;It ain&rsquo;t so long thet you did the same. Now you&rsquo;re eatin&rsquo; out of his hand. Wal, Curly Prentiss, you lay to this. Heah&rsquo;s one who&rsquo;ll never do it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Reckon none of us figgered you would. Hack, you may have the edge on me an&rsquo; Hump, an&rsquo; mebbe Lonestar, when it comes to years on the range. But we ain&rsquo;t quite looney.&rdquo; This droll speech, delivered in apparent good humor, effectually silenced Jocelyn, who without more ado left the group. &ldquo;Boss, I&rsquo;d reckon he&rsquo;ll last heah till one more pay day,&rdquo; ended Curly. But several of the boys, outspoken and less humorous, took issue with him on that score. Another Saturday arrived, and at noonday, when the cowboys knocked off for the week, Jocelyn, with more than usual of his donning his best and gaudiest, made a parade of his start for West Fork. Jeff Davis, the dumb cook, electrified Jim and prostrated the cowboys. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m a quiet man an&rsquo; I like peace,&rdquo; he began, in a perfectly clear and resonant voice. &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s why I never talk. But that &mdash;&mdash; &mdash;&mdash; &mdash;&mdash; Jocelyn has got me riled. An&rsquo; if some of you &mdash;&mdash; &mdash;&mdash; cowpunchers don&rsquo;t shet him up, I&rsquo;ll slip some coyote poison in the &mdash;&mdash; meat!&rdquo; Such profanity had never before been heard in the Diamond camp, at least since Jim had taken charge. The boys gave the cook wide-eyed and gaping stares, then after their usual manner when enraptured, they whooped like Indians and rolled on the ground. Upon partial recovery they delivered themselves with characteristic remarks. &ldquo;Our tongue-tied cook!&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; mebbe he cain&rsquo;t cuss!&rdquo; No other of the Diamond outfit left camp that week-end. They had a lazy, jolly time of it, interspersed with some thoughtful conferences about the obstacles ahead. Jim could not feel that they had accepted him as one of them yet, but they had changed so materially that their humor had lost its sting. At Quaking Asp a stream of cold water ran down into the canyon, and half a mile below the rim there were pools full of fine trout. Bud took Jim fishing. Now it chanced that fishing had always been Jim&rsquo;s favorite pastime, but he had never seen a rainbow trout. The tackle was not much, Bud averred; merely hooks and lines which he dug out of his kit. They cut poles and caught grasshoppers for bait. Jim had the most thrilling few hours of his Western experience, always putting aside those fatal hours with Molly Dunn. He caught a number of nice trout, and Bud caught a string as heavy as he wanted to pack uphill. When they arrived at camp with fresh fish for supper they were received with vociferous acclaim. On Sunday they assayed to go again, and Curly begged to be taken. He was put to catching grasshoppers, and the spectacle of that long-legged, awkward-running cowboy, whooping wildly and batting grasshoppers with his huge sombrero, was something to see. They had a great day on the brook, the climax of which was when Curly slipped off a rock and fell in to his neck. It wanted an hour to sunset when they arrived in camp. To Jim&rsquo;s amaze Hack Jocelyn had just ridden in and had kicked his chaps off. Jim&rsquo;s quick eyes noted the cowboy&rsquo;s gun belt hanging on the pommel of his saddle. The sight seemed to make every fiber in Jim leap. Jocelyn presented a marked contrast to the debonair braggart of a courtier who had left for West Fork only the day before. His garb looked as if he had slept in a hay-loft. His face was haggard, dark, and sullen. Not many hours past he had been under the influence of liquor. If Jocelyn saw the approach of Jim and his companions, he gave no evidence of it, but went on in forceful harangue to Cherry Winters and Lonestar. Jim caught the tail end of a speech that drew him up, strung and sharp. He dropped his string of trout. In a few strides he was upon Jocelyn, and with hard hand jerked him round. &ldquo;Were you speaking of Molly Dunn?&rdquo; he demanded. &ldquo;Wal, if it&rsquo;s any of your &mdash;&mdash; mix, yes, I was,&rdquo; returned Jocelyn, deliberately, and the way he squared himself and dropped his right hand to his side was not to be overlooked. Only he had forgotten that his gun hung in his belt over the pommel of his saddle. &ldquo;Jocelyn, you&rsquo;re fired,&rdquo; rang out Jim. &ldquo;Nope. Beat you to it, Traft. I quit,&rdquo; replied Jocelyn. &ldquo;When?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, if I recollect, it was yestiddy.&rdquo; &ldquo;All right. Get out of my camp. You can&rsquo;t even eat here.&rdquo; &ldquo;Traft, thet ain&rsquo;t Western&mdash;to send a man out hungry,&rdquo; said Jocelyn, darkly. &ldquo;Is it Western to speak vilely of a girl?&rdquo; flashed Jim, hotly. &ldquo;Depends on thet gurl, Mister Traft.&rdquo; Then Jim lunged out with all the fury of might and grief. His big fist covered one of Jocelyn&rsquo;s malignant eyes. The cowboy fell over the bench, knocking Winters down. Jim leaped after them, and plunged upon Jocelyn, to kick him fiercely, and then fall on him like a battering-ram. Curly was the first to leap and grasp Jim. But he could do nothing. &ldquo;Help, somebody!&rdquo; he yelled. &ldquo;Bulldog him, Bud! ... There! ... Why, boss, shore you&rsquo;d killed the fellar. Come now.... You shore lost your temper.&rdquo; Between them they dragged Jim off the bloody-faced Jocelyn, who sat up groggily. &ldquo;Some of you&mdash;lead him out of&mdash;camp,&rdquo; panted Jim, struggling for breath. &ldquo;Then come back for his horse and pack.... And his wages, too.&rdquo; Jim turned away unsteadily, with Bud still holding his arm. &ldquo;Let go!&rdquo; growled Jim, roughly. &ldquo;Or I&rsquo;ll biff you one.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, I ain&rsquo;t fit to face my Maker jest yet,&rdquo; drawled Bud, complying with this command. They crossed to where Jim had his bed and pack. &ldquo;Fetch my bag, the one I keep my money in,&rdquo; said Jim, as he sat down. He was visibly shaking and the sweat dropped from his face. Bud brought out the little bag and opened it. &ldquo;Wal, I don&rsquo;t see how we could hev avoided thet, boss,&rdquo; he said, resignedly, shaking his head. &ldquo;Avoid hell!&mdash;Ought to have jumped him long ago.&rdquo; Jim looked at his big, dirty, red-stained hands. &ldquo;Get out the money, Bud.... Let&rsquo;s see. I&rsquo;ll pay him the month out.&rdquo; &ldquo;Pay him nuthin&rsquo; you don&rsquo;t owe,&rdquo; declared Bud, carefully calculating and counting out the amount due. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll give it to Cherry an&rsquo; let him hand it over.&rdquo; Jim did not look up from the ground until he had recovered his balance. Jocelyn had evidently been led off into the woods, for Cherry appeared to be following with his horse and Hump Stevens with his pack. They disappeared. Whereupon Curly and Bud returned to Jim. &ldquo;Best job fer the Diamond in six years,&rdquo; said Bud. &ldquo;Ahuh. But bad fer the boss, Bud,&rdquo; added Curly, gravely. &ldquo;Boys, I couldn&rsquo;t stand him any longer. I couldn&rsquo;t,&rdquo; broke out Jim, spreading wide his hands. &ldquo;Shore you couldn&rsquo;t. But Jocelyn will never get over thet. I don&rsquo;t mean the lickin&rsquo;, though you near busted him. It&rsquo;s what you called him an&rsquo; orderin&rsquo; him out of camp. Thet&rsquo;ll go over the range. It&rsquo;ll aboot make Hack Jocelyn an outlaw.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m not sorry. I suppose he&rsquo;ll kill me, but I couldn&rsquo;t have done otherwise.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, will you promise to do thet little trick?&rdquo; asked Bud, just as earnestly as Curly had spoken. &ldquo;Yes, boys, I promise,&rdquo; replied Jim. &ldquo;Fine an&rsquo; dandy,&rdquo; declared Curly. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re no slouch with a gun an&rsquo; Hack ain&rsquo;t so much. It&rsquo;d been plumb murder fer me to draw on him. But the chances are slim you&rsquo;ll ever meet up with Jocelyn alone. If he gets in the Cibeque an&rsquo; Slinger Dunn stands fer it&mdash;which I&rsquo;ll gamble he won&rsquo;t&mdash;he&rsquo;ll be jumpin&rsquo; out of the fryin&rsquo;-pan into the fire. Jocelyn is only another cowpuncher thet&rsquo;s gone to the bad. I&rsquo;ve seen a lot of them. The Cibeque will be the wuss off with Jocelyn. They&rsquo;ll cut our fence an&rsquo; rustle our stock. An&rsquo;, boss, if you don&rsquo;t hold the Diamond back, every damn one of thet two-bit outfit will be daid or in jail before the snow flies.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss,&rdquo; added Bud, impressively, &ldquo;Curly an&rsquo; I know right now thet the Haverlys are cuttin&rsquo; our fence an&rsquo; rustlin&rsquo; our cattle. We didn&rsquo;t ride down to West Fork fer nothin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why didn&rsquo;t you tell me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you had trouble enough. An&rsquo; we reckoned we&rsquo;d keep it as long as we could.&rdquo; &ldquo;If you give me proofs I&mdash;I&rsquo;ll not hold the Diamond back,&rdquo; declared Jim, and probably that decision marked the West claiming him for its own. &ldquo;Wal, we cain&rsquo;t lay a hand on proof jest this minnit, but it&rsquo;ll shore come,&rdquo; replied Bud. The Diamond operated out of that camp until wagon loads of fresh supplies and more wire arrived from Flagerstown. Then they pushed on to Tobe&rsquo;s Well, which was the end of the road. Thereafter all transports had to be packed on horseback. Each succeeding camp site had added something to Jim&rsquo;s appreciation of Arizona; and Tobe&rsquo;s Well outdid them all. Tobe, an obscure bear-hunter, had long ago built a log shack there, after which the canyon and the bubbling well of crystal spring water had taken his name. The situation was wholly different from the other camp settings. The canyon headed on the west slope of the Diamond, in a deep bottle-necked, gray-cliffed gorge. The floor consisted of several acres of cleared land and as much more in pine and spruce. The well was a huge spring at the upper end of the clearing and the wonderful flow of water boiled and bubbled up in the center. Under a magnificent spruce, the dead top of which tipped the rim-wall above, stood the old log shack in ruins. There were several outlets to this miniature lake, little rivulets that wound away under grass and fern-bordered banks to merge into one stream in the center of the gorge. Soon it gathered velocity and plunged with low roar through the canyon neck, thereafter to fall precipitously down to the Cibeque. Giant pines and spruces stood far apart around the well, too lordly and self-contained to mingle branches or shade. White and purple asters, gold daisies, and blue flags waved in the summer wind. Birds and squirrels appeared tame and friendly. A herd of deer, does and fawns, stood with ears erect to watch the wagons roll clatteringly in at the rocky gateway. &ldquo;Boss, this heah is one waterhole you cain&rsquo;t fence off the Diamond,&rdquo; declared Curly, with a chuckle. And indeed it seemed imperative to run the drift fence considerably below the gateway. Tobe&rsquo;s Canyon was one of the brakes of the Cibeque, not so large and open as the Sycamore, or Deer or Long Horn Canyons. It was, however, a big country in itself, extremely wild and rugged up on the slope of the Diamond, and a noted place for bear and lion. The cattle that drifted down Tobe&rsquo;s Canyon had never been recovered by their owners. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m going to have my fall hunt right here,&rdquo; declared Jim. &ldquo;Wal, heah&rsquo;s a gent who&rsquo;d like to chop wood an&rsquo; pack water fer you,&rdquo; said Curly. &ldquo;An&rsquo;, boss, if I do say it myself, I can cook rings around Jeff,&rdquo; added Bud, eagerly. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re both on,&rdquo; replied Jim, gladly. &ldquo;But, Lord! we&rsquo;re forgettin&rsquo;,&rdquo; went on Curly. &ldquo;We won&rsquo;t hev the fence up by October, an&rsquo; if we do get it up it&rsquo;ll only go down ag&rsquo;in.... An&rsquo; then we&rsquo;ll be huntin&rsquo; wire-cutters an&rsquo; cattle thieves.&rdquo; On the second day at Tobe&rsquo;s Well the drift fence was run below and around this famous waterhole and back up on the Diamond. Jim had a rather uneasy conscience. Tobe&rsquo;s Well lay below the rim, and there was no other water close either to the north or south. Of course all the water from the well ran on down the mountain and cattle west of the fence could find it. But somehow the thing did look as if he was fencing off a waterhole. And he ventured to ask Curly what he thought the West Forkers would say about it. &ldquo;Aw, hell, let &rsquo;em drink whisky,&rdquo; declared Curly. &ldquo;Boss, you&rsquo;re too turrible conscientious. Our side will say we&rsquo;re savin&rsquo; the drift of cattle. Their side will say we&rsquo;re fencin&rsquo; off grass an&rsquo; water. It&rsquo;s nip an&rsquo; tuck. But wait till you heah some bullets hissin&rsquo; past your ear!&rdquo; The sixth day from that fell to Jim as his turn for the scout duty he had initiated. He had saddled and mounted while yet the others were at breakfast. &ldquo;Boss, how fur do you aim to ride back along the line?&rdquo; asked Bud. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll make for Sycamore, strike the fence, and work this way,&rdquo; replied Jim. &ldquo;Wal, keep your eye peeled,&rdquo; said Curly, gruffly. Jim rode out of the canyon and up to the rim above, from which he gazed down with a stinging sensation of pleasure at the wild camp site, the does and fawns grazing with the horses, the blue column of smoke curling upward, and at the circle of cowboys round the fire. Lonestar&rsquo;s mellow voice floated up: &ldquo;Oh, I am a Texas cowboy, jest off the Texas plains. My trade is cinchin&rsquo; saddles&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; Jim wheeled his horse off into the pines, conscious that his full heart could not quite attain the joy of realization. He loved the West; he was proving his mettle to his uncle and his father; he had begun to fit in with these elemental, dare-devil cowboys; he liked life in the open and the wild. But he could not rid himself of the pang and oppression in his breast, of the persistence of a bitter sickening doubt and regret. Long weeks, even months, had not killed his love for Molly Dunn. Time and thought, long hours in the lonely nights and bitter reflection, had proved to Jim that the one constant, ever-growing thing had been love. Some day before long he would ride down to West Fork and see Molly Dunn. See for himself what she was and if there was not reason for his blind faith! Arriving at Sycamore, he rode west a short distance until he came to the fence, when he turned south to follow it. He walked his horse, keeping back a few rods, and he peered ahead with keen eyes. He covered several miles in this cautious manner and came out on the bank of a swampy swale, from which water drained down into Rocky Canyon. Aspen thickets, clumps of new spruce and pine, filled the head of this canyon. Cattle were drifting down. Jim found the fence down in a section clear across this gateway to the brakes. He really had not expected to find a cut, and hard on his surprise and dismay roused his slumbering wrath. He proceeded, though less cautiously, with his rifle across the pommel. And along toward noon he rode down the wooded slope under Tobe&rsquo;s Well. Cattle were working down here, too. Not drifting! Jim prepared himself for another surprise. The bottle-neck width of Tobe&rsquo;s Canyon had been sheared of its wire fence. A clean-cut job it was, every wire strand having been cut on each side of trees and posts. Jim descried fresh hoof tracks in the dim, seldom-used trail. Such was his wrath now that he followed them, keeping on the grass and under the pines where his horse walked quietly. Once the animal neighed, much to Jim&rsquo;s annoyance. That worried Jim, but did not deter him. And as he worked down, keeping intense vigilance on all sides, he could scarcely fail of impressions as to the wildness of Tobe&rsquo;s Canyon. His intent was to trail this fence-cutter clear to his hole, wherever that was; and Jim&rsquo;s excitement kept him from realizing the miles he descended, until he got down on comparative level, in the midst of a most beautiful forest of spruce and pine. Few indeed were the open glades and aisles. He heard cattle or elk breaking sticks back of the trail; the black squirrels and blue jays noisily resented his presence; he saw a squirrel-hawk flash down through an opening and crash into a tree, to flop violently and fly off, clutching a furry victim in his claws. That was a near bit of cruel nature to Jim. Suddenly his horse elevated his ears and stopped. The trail ran straight for twenty paces, and turned at a huge brown pine. A man stepped out, silent as a shadow. Jim recognized the lithe buckskin-clad form, the dark face, the eyes like holes that exposed fire behind. He leveled a gun, took deliberate aim, while Jim froze in his saddle. He saw red belch from the gun. A sharp impact as from a violent gust of wind struck him. Then a terrific shock, a tearing rend of flesh, a thundering crash! Jim&rsquo;s horse plunged to throw him. He forced his weighted eyelids open, to see bright sunshine and spread of foliage over him. He lay half sitting up against a log. Beside him knelt a girl. She uttered a broken cry. &ldquo;Oh&mdash;Mister Traft&mdash;you&rsquo;ve come to!&rdquo; she exclaimed, huskily. &ldquo;Molly Dunn!&rdquo; Jim&rsquo;s voice was not very strong. She straightened up a little so the light struck her face. It appeared pale and convulsed, with big dark eyes dilated in horror. Both her little hands were red with blood. This fully clarified Jim&rsquo;s stunned faculties. &ldquo;Am I bad hurt?&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh&mdash;I don&rsquo;t know! I&mdash;I&rsquo;m afraid!&rdquo; she cried. &ldquo;Show me where I&rsquo;m shot.&rdquo; &ldquo;Your breast ... heah!&rdquo; Jim&rsquo;s shirt was open. Even with the girl&rsquo;s help it took nerve to feel for the wound. His whole breast burned. A wet scarf lay pressed tight over his heart. Warm slippery blood! It had saturated his clothes. It was flowing freely. Then his fingers located a hole in his flesh that seemed on fire. Despite the shock to his wits Jim knew he had not been shot through or near the heart. The bullet hole ranged upwards. Had it missed his lungs? The moment was fraught with terror. And as he felt of it he was gazing up into the strained eyes of the girl. He localized the sharpest pain higher up. The bullet had come out on top of his left shoulder. From here the blood poured. Something cold and ghastly released its clutch upon Jim. &ldquo;Bullet&mdash;glanced,&rdquo; he muttered. Then he asked if there was any other wound. &ldquo;You hit on your haid,&rdquo; replied the girl. &ldquo;Molly, I&rsquo;ll not die,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Thank God! I&mdash;I was so terribly frightened. What can I do?&rdquo; &ldquo;You had water here?&rdquo; &ldquo;I filled your hat at the brook.&rdquo; &ldquo;Tear this scarf in strips,&rdquo; directed Jim. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll find a clean one in my pocket.... Inside.... Now tear that&mdash;and fold into pads. You&rsquo;ve only to wash off the blood&mdash;and bind the pads on tight.&rdquo; She complied with his instructions, without hesitation, though her hands shook. &ldquo;But this top hole! You&rsquo;re bleedin&rsquo; so! Fast as I wipe the blood off&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Put one of the pads over it. Press tight. Now I&rsquo;ll hold it there.... Knot the strips.... Slip under my arm&mdash;over my shoulder. Now the other pad ... and tie tight.&rdquo; The difficult task was at length accomplished. Molly sat back on her heels, plainly weak. Tiny drops like dew stood out upon her forehead. Her hair was wet. She made a move to brush it back from her face, then, aware of her bloody hands, she essayed to do it with her elbow, holding them out so as to avoid contact. &ldquo;Molly Dunn, you&rsquo;ve got my blood on your hands&mdash;all right,&rdquo; said Jim, with significance. But it escaped her. &ldquo;I shore have&mdash;and it sickens me,&rdquo; she whispered. Then pouring water from his sombrero she washed them, and wiped them upon the grass. Jim felt faint, and he was suffering severe pain, both of which in the thrilling agitation of the moment seemed nothing to be concerned about. &ldquo;I must get you back off the trail heah,&rdquo; she spoke up, suddenly. &ldquo;Yes. If you help me I can manage,&rdquo; he replied. With her assistance he labored to his feet and walked, leaning on her. For a little girl she certainly was strong. They seemed to go far. Jim grew dizzy. He heard running water, but could not see it. Blurred spruce trees stood up all around. His head swam, and when the girl stopped to let him down it was none too soon for Jim. He spoke, but apparently she did not hear. Then his sight cleared. She was coming with water in his sombrero. &ldquo;You aboot fainted that time, Mister Jim,&rdquo; she said, lifting his head so he could drink. &ldquo;I&mdash;should&mdash;smile,&rdquo; whispered Jim, as he lay back. Presently he had recovered again, not only to the mounting pains, but to a thrilling sense of this extraordinary situation. &ldquo;What&mdash;happened?&rdquo; he asked. &ldquo;Shore a lot,&rdquo; she replied, mournfully. &ldquo;Do you know who shot me?&rdquo; he went on. She hung her head. &ldquo;You needn&rsquo;t tell me. I know. It was your brother, Slinger Dunn.... He stepped out from behind a tree ... didn&rsquo;t seem in any hurry. But I was paralyzed. I forgot I had a gun.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;s beyond me&mdash;that you&rsquo;re alive. Oh, I&rsquo;m thankful.... But what&rsquo;ll we do?&rdquo; Jim felt the lump over his breast where the lower pad had been bound. It was right under his shirt pocket over his heart. His finger located the bullet hole in his shirt and at the same instant his watch. &ldquo;See here, Molly,&rdquo; he said, curiously. &ldquo;Take my watch out.&rdquo; She complied. The watch was of heavy silver and had been long in the Traft family. Evidence was not wanting that it had served its day. The bullet had struck the case, making a heavy dent and then had glanced upward. &ldquo;That accounts,&rdquo; whispered Molly. &ldquo;I wondered aboot it. Slinger doesn&rsquo;t often miss what he shoots at.&rdquo; &ldquo;Saved my life!&mdash;Well, I&rsquo;ll be jiggered!&rdquo; exclaimed Jim, fondling the watch. &ldquo;And I came awful close not fetching it!&rdquo; &ldquo;Do? I haven&rsquo;t the slightest idea. I couldn&rsquo;t get on a horse.... I&rsquo;ll probably be laid out. My, but it throbs and burns!&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll have fever. An&rsquo; maybe blood-poisonin&rsquo;. We got to have medicine,&rdquo; she rejoined, tragically. &ldquo;It won&rsquo;t do to send you up to my camp.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why not? My horse is heah, tied over there.&rdquo; &ldquo;The boys would know your brother shot me. I don&rsquo;t want that.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore they&rsquo;ll find it out, anyhow.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, I suppose. But if I&rsquo;m on my feet I could keep them from&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Mister Jim, you needn&rsquo;t let Slinger off on my account,&rdquo; she interrupted. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll have something to say to him when I&rsquo;m all right again,&rdquo; declared Jim, grimly. &ldquo;What&rsquo;d the&mdash;Why did he shoot me? On account of this drift fence?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. Reckon it was aboot me.&rdquo; &ldquo;You!&rdquo; &ldquo;Bud Chalfack&mdash;the devil!&rdquo; ejaculated Jim, stirringly aware of Molly&rsquo;s scarlet blush. &ldquo;He told it! ... Oh, I&rsquo;m sorry, Molly! That was such a&mdash;a crazy thing for me to do.&rdquo; &ldquo;What does he think, Molly?&rdquo; &ldquo;It couldn&rsquo;t be no worse, Mister Jim.&rdquo; &ldquo;The damn blockhead! Some of these Arizona fellows make me see red. ... I&rsquo;ll make it my business to hunt Mister Slinger up.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, don&rsquo;t do that! I beg you!&rdquo; she implored. &ldquo;Suppose I busted in on him somewhere. At your home or in the saloon at West Fork&mdash;before other people. He wouldn&rsquo;t murder me outright, would he?&rdquo; &ldquo;No. But he&rsquo;d make you draw. Arch is the most awfulest man when he&rsquo;s mad.&rdquo; &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t know whether he was mad or not, but he sure looked awful,&rdquo; replied Jim, forcing a laugh. &ldquo;Molly Dunn, how&rsquo;d you happen to come along here and find me?&rdquo; &ldquo;Slinger just the same as told me he&rsquo;d kill you,&rdquo; she replied, grave, dark eyes on him. &ldquo;I waited this mawnin&rsquo; till he was gone. Then I trailed him. My plan was to keep behind him till we were up on top, then I&rsquo;d ride off the trail an&rsquo; go round to your camp.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well! What for?&rdquo; &ldquo;I wanted to warn you aboot Slinger. An&rsquo; that you was in &rsquo;most as much danger from the Cibeque outfit.&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly Dunn!&rdquo; he cried, incredulously. &ldquo;Then it wasn&rsquo;t an accident? You actually came to save me?&rdquo; The distance to the little brown hand on her knee was not far, and his quickly bridged it. She seemed neither shy nor cold. &ldquo;It shore wasn&rsquo;t no accident, Mister Jim,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;You must have risked a good deal, Molly.&rdquo; &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t think aboot it. But if they find it out down in the brakes&mdash;well, the Cibeque wouldn&rsquo;t be no place for me. An&rsquo; Slinger will aboot kill me.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, I&rsquo;m sorry! And grateful! Molly Dunn, I&rsquo;ve got to think up some way to prove it.... But you must not let Slinger or any one see you. How far is it to your home?&rdquo; &ldquo;Reckon aboot five miles, down through the woods. I can keep off the trail.&rdquo; &ldquo;How&rsquo;d you happen to find me?&rdquo; &ldquo;I trailed Slinger to where his tracks went off into the woods. Then I stopped. Pretty soon I heahed a shot&mdash;then a cry&mdash;then a horse comin&rsquo; my way fast. I hid behind a spruce. An&rsquo; I seen Slinger ride by. I didn&rsquo;t know what had happened. But I tied my horse an&rsquo; slipped up the trail. First I saw your horse. He was makin&rsquo; off, draggin&rsquo; his bridle. Then I found you lyin&rsquo; by a tree. You&rsquo;d fallen off an&rsquo; hit your haid.&rdquo; She released her hand from his and gently touched Jim&rsquo;s head above his temple, where even a slight pressure caused him pain. &ldquo;I&rsquo;d better fetch your horse,&rdquo; she added, rising. &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t go far or stay long,&rdquo; said Jim. The slight buckskin-clad form vanished in the green. And only then did Jim take note of his surroundings. He lay in a sort of lean-to made of spruce boughs, dead and brown. He could see one small bit of blue sky; all the rest was green. He heard a low murmur of running water. The place was secluded, and redolent of dry sweet pine needles. But the effort to sit up caused him so much pain that he was glad indeed to lie back. He must not forget that he had been shot. What an adventure! His thoughts simply whirled, and he had to force them to a logical plan. He absolutely must send the girl home soon. Could he dare let her risk coming back the next day? The idea overcame him. Sooner or later the cowboys would find him, though the last place they would look would be down the trail from Tobe&rsquo;s Well. Soft thuds on the ground made Jim aware that Molly was returning with his horse. They ceased. Then he heard a rustling sound, and then the soft flop of leather. Next she appeared with his saddle blankets and his coat. &ldquo;Mister Jim, didn&rsquo;t you have a gun?&rdquo; she asked. &ldquo;So I did. A rifle.... I had it across the front of my saddle when Slinger stepped out from behind that tree.&rdquo; She disappeared again. And he lay there, subject to renewed emotion. Presently she returned with the rifle, to deposit it by his side. How pretty, slim, and graceful she looked in buckskin! He told her so. She just gazed at him with great, reproachful, melancholy eyes. &ldquo;Have you worn the lovely white dress since?&rdquo; he added. &ldquo;No.... I&rsquo;m savin&rsquo; that to be buried in.&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly! ... You should have said married. How sweet&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Mister Jim, you&rsquo;re shore talkin&rsquo; too much&mdash;not to say too wild. Your face is hot. An&rsquo; time is flyin&rsquo;. What&rsquo;ll we do? I&rsquo;ve hobbled your horse.&rdquo; &ldquo;You must go home at once. Don&rsquo;t let anybody see you.... Will you come back tomorrow?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes,&rdquo; she replied, with gravity, as if only she knew what that promise implied. &ldquo;Thank you. Better fetch some medicine. I&rsquo;m going to be pretty sick.... And some clean linen to bandage my bullet holes.&rdquo; &ldquo;There&rsquo;s a canteen on your saddle,&rdquo; she said, and stepped outside the shack to procure it. Upon returning she placed it beside his rifle. Then on her knees she scraped up a soft pile of pine needles, and spread his saddle blankets upon them, and folded his coat for a pillow. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s a bed for you. I reckon you won&rsquo;t get cold. If you do, use one of the blankets.... An&rsquo; now I&rsquo;ll go.&rdquo; Jim gazed up at her as she knelt there. &ldquo;Molly ... kiss me,&rdquo; he importuned, quite beside himself. &ldquo;Mister Jim!&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll be here alone, unable to move.... Slinger might come back and kill me.... Kiss me, Molly.&rdquo; &ldquo;No. He won&rsquo;t come back.&rdquo; &ldquo;I might bleed to death or die of fever,&rdquo; he went on, wildly. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re out of your haid.&rdquo; &ldquo;Of course I am. But I know what I&rsquo;m asking. Please kiss me.&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t care who or what you are.&rdquo; Twice he reached for her, but she was just a little too far away. &ldquo;You mustn&rsquo;t move,&rdquo; she said, in alarm, edging close on her knees. Then he could reach her. How large and dark her eyes appeared in her pale face! &ldquo;Kiss me, and go.&rdquo; She bent over his forehead as if impelled, yet earnestly. &ldquo;No!&rdquo; he cried, seeing her intent, and moving impatiently. Then blushing duskily scarlet she bent lower, closing her eyes, and laid cool sweet lips on his. Before Jim could catch his breath to speak again she had fled. &ldquo;Low down of me to take advantage of her that way,&rdquo; he muttered. &ldquo;But, after all, was it? ... Curly would say, &lsquo;Thet shore aboot spills me.&rsquo;&rdquo; Whereupon Jim called Molly every adorable name that he had ever heard or could make up, and in some measure satisfied himself of sincerity. After which he lay for long in a dream, the pleasure of which made him almost unconscious of pain. From that he fell into a fitful slumber. When he awoke the light low down had shaded and rays of gold slanted through the forest. He was uncomfortably hot and thirsty, but the pain had somewhat dulled. It hurt him, however, to move over on to the bed of boughs, and he was glad to stretch out. Common sense told him that his situation was pretty serious, if his condition was not, but he could hardly dwell on anything else save Molly Dunn&rsquo;s return the next day. What endless hours to wait! Suppose she did not come? But that girl would come. Jim knew it positively. Nevertheless, he tried to consider his predicament. The boys would scarcely be apt to search for him until next day. He had often been absent from camp until late. Then they would ride north to Sycamore, and the chances were favorable that they would not find him until the following day. Jim hoped they would not. Slinger Dunn, though, might come back to see his victim. After pondering this, however, Jim inclined to Molly&rsquo;s conviction that Slinger would not come. There was nothing else to speculate upon then but the girl. Jim suddenly felt glad for the solitude of the forest and the long night hours that he must pass wakefully. He watched the gold fade from the tips of the spruces. The sky turned gray. Twilight enveloped the green surroundings in obscurity. Soon all was black except a small space of sky overhead where pale stars blinked, and grew clear and white. The stillness was something that Jim soon felt he could not endure. But it did not last. A sigh breathed through the tree tops. The night wind had begun its dominance of the forest. Soon from all around came rustlings and stealthy sounds. From time to time Jim had to take a swallow of water from the canteen. These movements caused him acute pain, but when it subsided he suffered only the dull throbbing ache in head and breast. He could stand it. He did not mind only when it interfered with the pondering of his problem. Some late hour in the night relief from his mental struggle came, if not for the physical distress under which he labored. He had made up his mind about Molly Dunn, and a sweetness, a gladness, pervaded his soul. Then he fell asleep. Jim awoke at what he imagined must be the dark hour before dawn. He lay flat on his back, and it took a moment or more for him to realize he was not in camp, but alone in the deep forest, wounded, and parched by thirst. This he satisfied, and had begun to mill over his predicament again when his heavy eyelids closed as if weighted with sleep. When again they unclosed not only had dawn come, but the sun had arisen; and squirrels and jays were holding forth noisily. Jim&rsquo;s throbbing headache had departed, and aside from the painful itching and contracting of his wounds he seemed to be doing pretty well indeed. At any rate, he told himself so. He sat up, finding that only the use of his left arm and shoulder hurt him severely. About this hour, he reflected, and with considerable gratification, there would be consternation in the Diamond camp. Curly and Bud, on the rampage, would scatter those cowboys like a bunch of quail; and lonesome riders encountered in the woods were going to have to give strict account of their presence. Sooner or later some one would find him, and if no one did he might be able in a couple of days to reach camp. Perhaps he was not very practical this morning, for he almost felt grateful to Slinger Dunn. Young Jim Traft, head of the Diamond outfit, shot by a fence-cutter in the Cibeque&mdash;how that would fly over the range! And how would his uncle take it? And Ring Locke? Jim realized that he would have risen in their estimation. All the same, he did not intend to betray Molly Dunn&rsquo;s brother. Jim had the nucleus of a plan hazily forming in his mind. Waiting for Molly Dunn was not in the least wearisome, though the moments multiplied into hours. Jim had too much to thrill over&mdash;to conjecture about. How would she take him? He tried to imagine the expression of her face&mdash;of her wonderful eyes at the critical moment. Thus dreamily engaged, he was not prepared for low clatter of hoofs down the trail. He sat up with such a start that he wrenched his shoulder and had to pay a severe penalty. The forest was very still, except for birds and squirrels. The rhythmic beat of hoofs subsided, and likewise Jim&rsquo;s mounting heart. Then the sound came clear again. The hoofbeats were soft, and the horse that made them was neither heavy nor shod.
