They swelled clearer for a little, slowed down, and then ceased. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s Molly,&rdquo; said Jim, softly, and yet he endeavored to fortify himself against mistake. The moments dragged, and he was becoming nervous and discouraged, when a rustle of brush and soft footfalls just behind the lean-to brought him up transfixed and thrilling. Suddenly the slight gray form he had in his mind&rsquo;s eye appeared before him. She was bareheaded and the sunlight glinted from her disheveled hair. She carried a bundle. &ldquo;Oh, there you&mdash;are! I was&mdash;shore scared!&rdquo; she panted. Her eyes appeared unnaturally large and black in her small pale face. &ldquo;Morning, Molly! I reckoned you&rsquo;d never come,&rdquo; he said, gladly. &ldquo;Are you&mdash;all right?&rdquo; &ldquo;I had an awful night. And I was bad until just now,&rdquo; he replied. She sat down, and depositing her bundle, she put a hand to her bosom as if to still its heaving. She averted her eyes. It was plain she could not look at him. She appeared worn and troubled, as if she had passed a harassed, sleepless night. She seemed such a wistful, pathetic little figure. Jim&rsquo;s heart filled with tenderness and he could scarce contain his tremendous secret. &ldquo;Molly, did you get home safe?&rdquo; he asked, anxiously. &ldquo;Yes. Slinger didn&rsquo;t come, an&rsquo; ma never heahed me.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s fine. I&rsquo;m sure glad. I&rsquo;ve worried a lot.&rdquo; Then, noticing that she wore a blood-stained bandage around her left hand, he asked solicitously if she had not injured herself. &ldquo;No. I just put this on to fool ma, so I could go to the store,&rdquo; she explained in a matter-of-fact tone. &ldquo;An&rsquo; I told Mr. Summers I&rsquo;d cut myself an&rsquo; was scared of blood poison. I bought this medicine, an&rsquo; I&rsquo;ve fetched some clean linen, a towel an&rsquo; soap, an&rsquo; somethin&rsquo; for you to eat.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;re just wonderful, Molly!&rdquo; said Jim, and meant it in a multiplicity of ways. She gave him a fleeting flash of eyes. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll fetch some water,&rdquo; she returned. Jim noted, as she glided away with the canteen, how cautiously she peeped around the spruce tree before venturing farther. She was gone some moments, that dragged for the eager Jim. Then presently she reappeared as noiseless as a shadow, to set the dripping canteen down beside him, and knelt to undo a buckskin thong around the bundle. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s goin&rsquo; to hurt right smart,&rdquo; she said, practically. &ldquo;Molly, I could stand to be carved into mincemeat by you.&rdquo; Whereupon she laid a cool hand on his forehead and temples, and then slipped it inside his open shirt to feel of the bandages. &ldquo;Wal, you don&rsquo;t&rsquo;pear to have fever, but you shore look an&rsquo; talk flighty,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;Will you hold still an&rsquo; keep quiet? This&rsquo;s goin&rsquo; to hurt like sixty. I&rsquo;ve done it more&rsquo;n once for Arch.&rdquo; Jim nodded his acquiescence, and thought it would be safer and facilitate the operation if he closed his eyes. She cut the strips of scarf that had served as a bandage and then she essayed to remove the pads. They had evidently crusted with blood and they stuck tight. She saturated them with water and pulled gently at them until it appeared that more force must be used. Jim made the mental reservation that it hurt twice times sixty. But once the pads had been removed, how soothing to his hot skin and the irritated wounds were the touch of her cool hand and the feel of water! &ldquo;Mister Jim, from what little I know aboot gun-shot wounds, I&rsquo;d say you&rsquo;re not so bad off this mawnin&rsquo;,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;I dare say my&mdash;my favorable condition can be laid to my beautiful and splendid nurse,&rdquo; he replied, without opening his eyes. He thought he caught a suppressed titter, but could not be sure. Then he attended to her applying a stinging solution to his wounds and a deft and thorough bandaging of them. &ldquo;There. I cain&rsquo;t do no more for you,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;I reckon these bullet holes are healin&rsquo; clean, but it&rsquo;d be sense to have your men get you to Flag, pronto.... An&rsquo; then stay there, Mister Jim.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks, Molly. I&rsquo;m very grateful to you,&rdquo; he rejoined, opening his eyes to smile up at her. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll go to Flag, of course, if it&rsquo;s necessary. But I&rsquo;ll come back.... Molly, is it safe for you to stay with me awhile?&rdquo; &ldquo;Run? From what?&rdquo; &ldquo;From you, Mister Jim.&rdquo; &ldquo;Please leave off the Mister. We&rsquo;ve known each other a long time, now. It&rsquo;d be perfectly proper.&rdquo; She laughed, and her mirth had just a touch of bitter mockery. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve been with you only twice an&rsquo; you made a fool of me both times,&rdquo; she replied, resentfully. &ldquo;I never did,&rdquo; he protested. If he could stir her, even to anger, he might overcome the aloofness he sensed in her this morning. But failing in this, he thought he knew a way. &ldquo;You shore did. That night on the porch at Flag! You grabbed me just like Hack Jocelyn an&rsquo; other cowpunchers I know. An&rsquo; yesterday&mdash;that was worse. Your persuadin&rsquo; me!&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly, consider. There was no excuse for me at the dance. But yesterday there was.&rdquo; &ldquo;Maybe for you. But not for me. I shore wonder what you think of me.&rdquo; Jim saw the delight of playing upon her simplicity must be forsworn. She seemed to be smarting under shame. The moment had come, far swifter than he had anticipated, but he welcomed it, hastened to end forever her doubts of him. &ldquo;Molly, if you&rsquo;ll look around I&rsquo;ll tell you what I think of you,&rdquo; he said, in the coolest, easiest tone he could muster. The dark head whirled. Her spirited action, the fire of her eyes, the receding of hot color, implied a doubt of him and her resentment at it. Yet there seemed something else in her expression, not conscious hope, but a pathos due to the dream she only half divined. &ldquo;Will you marry me?&rdquo; he asked, simply, but with deep emotion. Perhaps nothing else could have so altered her. At length she burst out: &ldquo;Mister Jim!&rdquo; &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s dispense with the Mister. Call me plain Jim, and say &lsquo;yes.&rsquo;&rdquo; &ldquo;Boy, now I know what ails you,&rdquo; she said, in self-reproach. &ldquo;No, you don&rsquo;t. I haven&rsquo;t come to that.&rdquo; &ldquo;If you&rsquo;re not out of your haid, what on earth are you?&rdquo; she cried, wildly. &ldquo;In love, Molly.&rdquo; He got hold of her hand, to find it trembling. &ldquo;Listen, child,&rdquo; he began, determined to convince her. &ldquo;You remember our meeting. Well, I think I fell in love with you then. If not then, at the dance surely. And last night I made up my mind to ask you to marry me. I&rsquo;ve been shot, I know, Molly. And I&rsquo;m excited. But I am absolutely in possession of all my wits. I love you very dearly. And I want to make you my wife.&rdquo; &ldquo;Me? Molly Dunn?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, you, Molly Dunn.&rdquo; Then Jim Traft devoutly thanked the god of love or faith, or both, whatever it had been that had prompted him to broach this most impelling and sacred of all propositions to a woman. For though Molly Dunn was wholly unconscious of it, the instant he had convinced her was one of a singular transformation. He had convinced her of so marvelous a thing that it worked a like change in her. &ldquo;Oh! ... I cain&rsquo;t believe it,&rdquo; she exclaimed, incredulous with the amazing truth. &ldquo;My God! What would Slinger say to that? An&rsquo; ma an&rsquo; pa an&rsquo; Andy&mdash;an&rsquo; that grinnin&rsquo; Jocelyn?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Mister Jim&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore I heah you ... Jim,&rdquo; she replied, sweetly. &ldquo;It sounds very nice. Now, what do you say, Molly?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m shore upset. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll be so&mdash;so proud all my life&mdash;an&rsquo; happy that you love me an&rsquo; want me.... But, Jim, I cain&rsquo;t marry you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly! Do you love some one else?&rdquo; he queried, sharply. &ldquo;No.&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t you&mdash;couldn&rsquo;t you care for me?&rdquo; he implored. She gave him an enigmatical little smile, as much mournful as derisive&mdash;something dedicated to the stupidity of man. &ldquo;Jim, heah you have lost your haid,&rdquo; she went on. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m Molly Dunn of the Cibeque. They always called me that. My brother is Slinger Dunn. An&rsquo; this last shootin&rsquo; will make him an outlaw.&rdquo; &ldquo;It needn&rsquo;t, Molly. No one need ever know. I&rsquo;ll not tell. And I&rsquo;ll find a way to change Slinger.&rdquo; &ldquo;But that&rsquo;s only one reason,&rdquo; she protested. &ldquo;My father has lived for years under a cloud. No one can prove he belonged to that murderous outfit. But it&rsquo;s behind.... My mother isn&rsquo;t much good, either. There&rsquo;s talk aboot her an&rsquo; this an&rsquo; that cowboy.&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly, I wouldn&rsquo;t be marrying your family,&rdquo; replied Jim, sagely. &ldquo;Jim, I may be pretty, like a wood-mouse, as Arch calls me, but I&rsquo;m nobody,&rdquo; said Molly, mercilessly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m no&mdash;no fit girl for you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Why not?&rdquo; he demanded, stabbed again by that fierce, jealous doubt. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ve sure got good looks. You&rsquo;ve the face, the hands&mdash;the instincts of a lady. There must be good blood in your family somewhere.&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes. My mother came from a Southern family. She brags aboot it to this day. It&rsquo;s a lot to do with her fittin&rsquo; so poor in a log cabin.&rdquo; Jim did not press this point any further, for her demeanor, the way she clung to his hand, the tremendous agitation that shook her, the traitorous eyes which she did not realize, were facts that moved him with tumultuous joy. &ldquo;But, Jim, I reckon you&rsquo;ve heahed talk aboot me,&rdquo; she went on. &ldquo;An&rsquo; you&rsquo;re shore the one man I&rsquo;d tell everythin&rsquo;. Don&rsquo;t you let any lies aboot me stick in your haid.&rdquo; &ldquo;I didn&rsquo;t, Molly,&rdquo; said Jim, which he feared was not wholly truth. But on the other hand, under circumstances of extreme exasperation, had he not clung to some strange championship of her? Then he told her of Hack Jocelyn&rsquo;s vain boastings, of his innuendoes, and at last his open, vile claim. &ldquo;Slinger will kill him for that,&rdquo; she replied, furiously. &ldquo;I&rsquo;d have done it myself,&rdquo; declared Jim. &ldquo;The boys had to pull me off him.&rdquo; &ldquo;I saw his face an&rsquo; taunted him with it,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;Hack admitted you licked him.... Jim, he scared me bad that time. He lay in wait for me along the trail home. An&rsquo; he jerked me into the brush. That&rsquo;s when I learned he was quittin&rsquo; the Diamond an&rsquo; makin&rsquo; up to the Cibeque. He was playin&rsquo; for a big stake. My brother had no use for Hack. An&rsquo; Hack aimed to win him over by makin&rsquo; me tell him he&rsquo;d quit the Diamond because you insulted me. When I didn&rsquo;t take kind to this deal, he grabbed me up in his arms an&rsquo; near broke every rib I own. An&rsquo; he kissed me, Jim&mdash;all over except my mouth.... You bet I was scared an&rsquo; I changed my tune. He let me down then, an&rsquo; pretty quick I broke an&rsquo; ran.&rdquo; &ldquo;Darling!&rdquo; cried Jim. That and the stealing of Jim&rsquo;s arm round her waist, drawing her down, would have been Molly&rsquo;s undoing. But he heard a soft step. He felt Molly freeze under his arm. A shadow crossed the light above him. &ldquo;So heah you are, you wood-louse,&rdquo; grated Slinger. &ldquo;Arch, I found him&mdash;on the trail&mdash;bleedin&rsquo; to death,&rdquo; cried Molly. &ldquo;When&mdash;an&rsquo; what you doin&rsquo; up heah?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&mdash;I went ridin&rsquo;, Arch. Yesterday. I heahed a shot. Then you came by like mad. An&rsquo; I found him.&rdquo; &ldquo;You was meetin&rsquo; him heah.&rdquo; &ldquo;No. No. Honest to God&mdash;I wasn&rsquo;t.&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly Dunn, I&rsquo;ve had my doubts aboot you lately. An&rsquo; now I&rsquo;m shore.&rdquo; Jim managed to move enough to get his eyes from under Molly&rsquo;s disheveled hair. He needed only one look up at Slinger Dunn&rsquo;s face to understand Molly&rsquo;s terror. &ldquo;Dunn, she&rsquo;s not a liar,&rdquo; he said, hurriedly. &ldquo;She couldn&rsquo;t have known I was on the trail. I was trailing the man who cut the drift fence.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, she&rsquo;d lie an&rsquo; you&rsquo;d swear to it.&rdquo; His piercing gaze rested curiously on Jim. &ldquo;How&rsquo;n hell do you happen to be alive?&rdquo; &ldquo;Your bullet hit my watch.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, heah&rsquo;s another,&rdquo; said Slinger, with strange intensity, and he brought the gun up to align it with Jim&rsquo;s head. But quick as a flash Molly covered Jim. &ldquo;For God&rsquo;s sake, Slinger, don&rsquo;t kill him,&rdquo; she begged. &ldquo;I shore will. An&rsquo; I jest aboot as lief bore you, too.... Git up.&rdquo; He kicked Molly, and his boot came partly in contact with Jim&rsquo;s thigh. The brutality of it, after the deadly speech, seemed to liberate Jim from a cold paralysis. &ldquo;Let go of me, Molly,&rdquo; ordered Jim. &ldquo;If he&rsquo;s such a skunk as that, he might kill you. And if he means to murder me you can&rsquo;t stop him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Now you&rsquo;re talkin&rsquo;, Mister Traft,&rdquo; returned Dunn, harshly. Molly not only refused to move, but she got her arms around Jim, and clung to him, shielding his vital parts with her quivering body. &ldquo;Arch, you cain&rsquo;t shoot him&mdash;when he&rsquo;s down&mdash;crippled&mdash;defenseless,&rdquo; she burst out, passionately. &ldquo;It wouldn&rsquo;t be human. It wouldn&rsquo;t be like Slinger Dunn... . If it&rsquo;s on my account, you&rsquo;re wrong&mdash;terribly wrong. He never meant&mdash;bad by me.&rdquo; &ldquo;You cold-hearted devil!&rdquo; she exclaimed. &ldquo;How could you have a decent thought? ... Kill me, too!&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. You damn little hussy! ... But bein&rsquo; daid wouldn&rsquo;t hide your shame, Molly Dunn.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, Arch, there&rsquo;s no shame.... There&rsquo;s been nothin&rsquo; but thoughts an&rsquo; feelin&rsquo;s that have changed me... . I love him. Cain&rsquo;t you see it? I love him!&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I ain&rsquo;t blind,&rdquo; replied Slinger, and reaching down he laid hold of Molly and tried to pull her off. But she clung like a leech. Then with a curse he shifted his hold to her hair. Even then he could only budge her by savage force, and he had to step on Jim&rsquo;s shoulder to keep her from lifting him with her. Suddenly Molly, fierce as a wildcat, flung herself upon Slinger, and fought him for the gun, which he held out in his right hand. The girl&rsquo;s courage, following her declaration of love, roused the lion in Jim. With all his might he kicked Dunn&rsquo;s hand, knocking the gun out of it. &ldquo;Hold still!&mdash;Somebody comin&rsquo;!&rdquo; whispered Dunn, hoarsely, stiffening. Molly slipped out of his grasp, down to the ground. Jim listened, but could hear nothing save the pounding of his heart. Dunn stood strung like a listening deer, gradually relaxing his intensity. He might have been mistaken. But during this short interval he had removed his eyes from Molly, who suddenly snatched up the gun. She held it low, with both hands tight, pointing up at Dunn&rsquo;s body. Jim could not bear the terrible intent expressed in her face and eyes. He grasped the gun, turned it aside. &ldquo;Molly, you mustn&rsquo;t shed your brother&rsquo;s blood&mdash;even to save my life,&rdquo; he said, very low. And at his words she relinquished the gun. Slinger Dunn seemed to be calculating desperate chances. That Jim would not instantly turn the gun on him never flashed into his consciousness. Jim grasped this and thought he might turn it to good account. The last thing he could be forced to do would be to kill Molly&rsquo;s brother. Then the brush crashed. Dunn&rsquo;s back was turned. Swiftly he elevated his arms high above his head. Jim moved to see that Curly Prentiss had appeared round the corner of the lean-to. He held a cocked gun. &ldquo;Come round heah, Bud,&rdquo; he called. And with more crashing in the brush Bud Chalfack followed his extended rifle into view. Both cowboys stepped closer, eyes quick and hard. Jim slipped Dunn&rsquo;s gun out of sight&mdash;an act as impulsively swift as the thought that prompted it. &ldquo;Face around heah,&rdquo; ordered Curly, and as Dunn pivoted he showed no surprise, but an alert, cold suspicion. &ldquo;By Gawd!&rdquo; shouted Bud. &ldquo;Look, Curly! If there ain&rsquo;t the boss ... an&rsquo; Molly Dunn!&rdquo; Jim seized avidly upon his opportunity, though his wit and spirit far exceeded his physical strength. &ldquo;Hello&mdash;boys!&rdquo; he began, huskily. &ldquo;So you found me&mdash;at last.... Put up your guns.&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;s wrong heah?&rdquo; demanded Curly, and Jim&rsquo;s heart felt a rush of warm gladness and thrill at the significance of the cowboys&rsquo; mien. &ldquo;Nothing wrong, Curly&mdash;now&mdash;you&rsquo;ve found me,&rdquo; replied Jim, cheerfully, though he realized his appearance must have given the lie to his words. &ldquo;Boss, you&rsquo;re pretty white&mdash;an&rsquo; there&rsquo;s blood on your shirt,&rdquo; returned Curly, sharply. &ldquo;You bet. But put down your gun&mdash;and you, too, Bud. I&rsquo;m nervous.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you look it,&rdquo; growled Curly, complying with Jim&rsquo;s order. Bud likewise lowered his weapon. &ldquo;I reckon you&rsquo;d better talk fast.&rdquo; &ldquo;Curly,&rdquo; burst out Jim, in relief, &ldquo;yesterday I found a cut in the drift fence. And horse tracks on the trail. I followed them down here.... Somebody shot me, knocked me galley-west off my horse.... When I came to, Molly Dunn was bathing my face. Pretty lucky for me she happened to ride along. She was on her way up to camp&mdash;to warn me that Jocelyn and the Cibeque outfit had planned to kidnap me&mdash;for ransom. She heard the shot and found me.... Well, it turned out the bullet had glanced off my watch and cut up through my shoulder. We tied it up. I wasn&rsquo;t able to get on my horse. Molly wanted to ride up to camp and fetch you, but I thought it&rsquo;d be best for her to go home. You fellows would probably trail me, and if you didn&rsquo;t she could ride up today.... Well, Molly came today, and it seems that her brother trailed her. Found us here. And, well&mdash;the truth is, he thinks I&rsquo;m a bad egg&mdash;and had evil intentions toward Molly. That upset me&mdash;and made Molly sore. We were having hell when you came up.... And, I reckon&mdash;that&rsquo;s all.&rdquo; Slinger eyed them while they delivered their separate speeches, and then looked strangely down upon Molly, who had rallied somewhat from the ravages of emotion, and lastly at Jim. There was little to be made of his impassive face, strong and hard as brown stone. Then he strode out to disappear in the brush. The situation lost its suspense. &ldquo;You dog-gone old tenderfoot!&rdquo; said Curly, sitting down beside Jim, to place a hand on him. &ldquo;Gone an&rsquo; got yourself shot fer the Diamond! I shore hope it ain&rsquo;t bad.&rdquo; Bud crowded in beside Molly. &ldquo;Boss, I wuz orful scared fust off. &rsquo;Course we didn&rsquo;t see who it wuz.... Gosh! I never wuz so glad aboot nothin&rsquo;.... Did thet bullet go deep?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not very. I bled a good deal. And I&rsquo;m sore. But I should think you could hold me on my horse and get me up to camp.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; so little Molly found you!&rdquo; ejaculated Curly, tossing his sombrero and shaking his curly head, while he bent bright knowing eyes upon the confused girl. &ldquo;My Gawd! the luck uv some fellars! ... How air you, Miss Molly?&rdquo; &ldquo;Not so well, this minute, Mr. Prentiss,&rdquo; replied Molly, with a wan smile. &ldquo;But I&rsquo;m shore glad&mdash;the way it&rsquo;s turned out.&rdquo; &ldquo;Howdy, Molly!&rdquo; drawled Bud. &ldquo;You look orful purty this minnit. So you saved our boss? Wal, I reckon now the Diamond will belong to you.&rdquo; The cowboys looked bewildered volumes and were speechless, which loss of function absolutely testified to a state so rare in them. And Molly was hopelessly stricken and confused. She had no strength left, even if rebellion was in her. &ldquo;You boys take yourselves off in the woods for ten minutes,&rdquo; ordered Jim, audaciously. Curly got up awkwardly, after the manner of cowboys, and Bud followed suit. It was just as well, thought Jim, that he had flabbergasted them and then had not allowed them time to recover. Signs were forthcoming of potential and scintillating cowboy wit. &ldquo;But say good-by to Molly,&rdquo; added Jim, relenting. Curly had dignity and selflessness. &ldquo;Miss Molly, you shore hev my thanks. Jim has won over the Diamond, an&rsquo; if, as he hints, he&rsquo;s won you&mdash;wal, I don&rsquo;t wonder, an&rsquo; I reckon him the luckiest fellar on the range. Good-by an&rsquo; good luck.&rdquo; They slumped away into the spruce, leaving a pleasant jingle of spurs. &ldquo;Oh, they&rsquo;re terrible&mdash;an&rsquo; lovable, too,&rdquo; burst out Molly, lifting a flushed face. &ldquo;You bet they are. But that tickled me, Molly,&rdquo; declared Jim. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ve had a time with them. And of all the tables turned on them, this with you is the best!&rdquo; &ldquo;But, Jim, it cain&rsquo;t ever be true,&rdquo; she murmured, sorrowfully. &ldquo;Forget your trouble now, Molly dear,&rdquo; replied Jim. &ldquo;You must hurry home. I&rsquo;ll find some way to let you hear from me. A letter&mdash;or I&rsquo;ll come to West Fork.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, you mustn&rsquo;t! It wouldn&rsquo;t be safe!&rdquo; she cried. &ldquo;What else could I do?&rdquo; wailed Molly. &ldquo;But, Heavens, tell me you didn&rsquo;t lie!&rdquo; She turned away her face. &ldquo;No, Jim.&rdquo; &ldquo;You do love me?&rdquo; he implored, drawing her close. &ldquo;I cain&rsquo;t help it.... But don&rsquo;t ask me&mdash;never no more&mdash;what you did.&rdquo; &ldquo;I shan&rsquo;t, if you&rsquo;re going to be distressed. But, Molly Dunn, that offer stands!&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t!&mdash;What do you think I&rsquo;m made of, anyhow? ... But, Jim, the wonderfulest thing I ever lived was when you hatched that lie to save Slinger.... That Prentiss boy is a real hombre. He&rsquo;d have shot Slinger in the wink of an eye. An&rsquo; my brother knew it.... Jim, if I hadn&rsquo;t loved you before that I&rsquo;d have done it after.... Perhaps it&rsquo;s all for some good. You shore have changed me. Who knows? Maybe even Slinger Dunn might be made to see.&rdquo; &ldquo;I had the same thought, Molly,&rdquo; said Jim, earnestly. &ldquo;And some day I&rsquo;ll follow it up.... Now you must go, Molly.&rdquo; Jim lay back spent, elated, conscious of recurring pangs in his wounds, overcome by his feelings. So that sooner or later, when the cowboys returned of their own accord, it was no wonder they were concerned. &ldquo;Lemme see where he got plugged?&rdquo; growled Curly, and with Bud assisting, he untied the bandages. &ldquo;Wal, Bud,&rdquo; he continued, presently, &ldquo;it&rsquo;s only a groove, an&rsquo; we could do no better by it.... Reckon it&rsquo;s the gurl thet took the sap out of him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Anyone could see he was light-haided,&rdquo; replied Bud, plaintively. &ldquo;Ain&rsquo;t it funny what a lovely female can do to a fellar? But, my Gawd! if I&rsquo;d been Jim&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Idiots!&rdquo; burst out Jim, opening his eyes and sitting up. &ldquo;Shut up and get me to camp.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ex-coose us, boss,&rdquo; said Curly, hastily. &ldquo;You see, we was comin&rsquo; back&mdash;reckonin&rsquo; your&mdash;the little lady had gone&mdash;but jest then she pounced on you, an&rsquo;&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Curly, you needn&rsquo;t explain,&rdquo; interrupted Jim. &ldquo;You are a couple of two-faced demons from Hades. Find my horse. He&rsquo;s hobbled here somewhere.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, boss, I understand you,&rdquo; drawled Curly, as he got up. &ldquo;My conscience has stung me more&rsquo;n onct fer mistrustin&rsquo; my friends on account uv a gurl.&rdquo; They ambled away into the woods, snickering and talking low, and presently Curly&rsquo;s mellow laugh rolled out. Jim had to love them, realizing they were secretly delighted with his conquest of Molly Dunn. But what would his uncle Jim say? Jim wisely decided to withhold the matter until sometime when he could contrive to have the old bachelor cattleman see Molly in that white dress. Then the rest would be easy. Just here, however, he happened to remember Molly had refused him and importuned him not to ask her &ldquo;never no more.&rdquo; The poor harassed, conscientious, adorable child! Presently Jim&rsquo;s reverie was disrupted by the advent of the cowboys with his horse and their own. Bud found his saddle, and then came into the lean-to for the blankets. &ldquo;Come on, you boss of the Diamond,&rdquo; he said, making no effort to help Jim up. Jim slowly crawled to a post of the lean-to and using that as a support he labored to a standing position. For a moment he felt dizzy. Then it passed. &ldquo;Fetch this medicine and towel,&rdquo; he said, as he walked out. Bud was singing one of his Texas ditties: &ldquo;When a boy falls in love with a pretty turtle dove, He will linger all around her under jaw&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; They made no move to help Jim on his horse, and he certainly gave no hint that their aid would have been most helpful. This cowboy school was a hard one. Jim remembered something about the Spartans, and concluded that Curly&rsquo;s and Bud&rsquo;s mothers had been Spartans. He swung up into the saddle to fierce shooting pangs through his shoulder. They rode off, with Bud leading the way, and Curly beside Jim. &ldquo;Boss,&rdquo; he said, abruptly, &ldquo;are you shore Slinger Dunn didn&rsquo;t shoot you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Say! Whatever gave you that idea?&rdquo; queried Jim, who did not need to feign surprise. &ldquo;Wal, when he turned round he shore expected to be bored. An&rsquo; fact is, I come damn near borin&rsquo; him.&rdquo; &ldquo;You&rsquo;d have made a terrible mistake, Curly,&rdquo; rejoined Jim, seriously. Jim managed fairly well in the saddle on the level ground, and even up the first gradual ascent of the trail, but when they reached the steep and rough part he had to hold on to the pommel. And he had never before suffered such sickening weakness and pain. But he stuck it out. He realized that he could do it, else these cowboys would never have let him tackle it alone. Nevertheless, he ground his teeth to keep from calling out. If anything in the world could have kept Jim Traft from falling out of his saddle, upon arriving in camp, it would have been the warwhoop the cowboys let out when they saw him. But even that could not do it. Good luck facilitated an otherwise insupportable situation. Her parents never learned she had been away from home nearly the whole of two days. Slinger had arrived at the cabin before Molly, and Molly had a suspicion it had been through him, somehow, that their mother had not become aware of her absence. She felt Slinger&rsquo;s eyes upon her, at times when she was not looking at him, but he never spoke to her, never gave any indication that he heard her. More significant of his mental chaos were the hours he spent sitting idle. Sometimes he would go into the field and work, careful to take a rifle along. Molly would catch a glimpse of him, leaning motionless on a spade, looking off into the forest. &ldquo;Whatever ails your brother?&rdquo; inquired Mrs. Dunn. &ldquo;I hear him walkin&rsquo; the porch at nights.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ma, I reckon he&rsquo;s in love,&rdquo; replied Molly, mischievously, &ldquo;Folks get queer when they&rsquo;re that way.&rdquo; Molly nursed a calamitous conviction that she did. And she became grief-stricken. She fell into a state in which there were no moments of relaxation, or dream, or relief from misery. Her heart was broken and she wanted to die. She thought of drowning herself in the great Maple Spring, and pictured herself lying at the edge, down in the clear water, her face white and her hair waving, and her eyes wide open, staring up at the cruel world which she had chosen to relinquish. There was some consolation in this picture. Arch would find her and suffer sorrow that he had been partly the cause of her demise. The thought of a conscience-stricken mother was not unpleasant. And then Jim might be the first to discover her lying cold and dead in the spring. Where he had driven her! The aching ecstasy of this thought vanished in sudden pain and self-reproach. Why should she make Jim suffer? He had done nothing but fall in love with her&mdash;nearly lose his life through her. He had lifted her out of obscurity. He had exalted her. No! She could not make him any unhappier than he was already. She had to forswear thought of suicide. She could not do her work, which involved her in trouble with her mother. She moped all day, most of it in some corner; and she chose the dark and bare and squalid places, that seemed to be in harmony with her moods. At dusk she sat out in the clearing, on a stump, and watched the bats and the night-hawks. She did not sleep well. Many hours she crouched at her little window in the loft, and during those when the gloom and silence of dawn seemed like her life, she suffered most. A second Sunday came. Would she date all the rest of her life from that last day in the woods with Jim Traft? What would become of her? When Andy called she begged him to excuse her. &ldquo;Why, gurl, you&rsquo;re sick! You look turrible!&rdquo; he said, anxiously. Then in the afternoon young Keech came on horseback. He was very mysterious, and Molly felt she would presently be rude, but he whispered to her that he had brought something for her which he had left outside by the fence. He gave no explanation and departed even more mysteriously than he had come, even winking at her. &ldquo;Darn fool!&rdquo; muttered Molly, who had no patience these days. &ldquo;Whatever is he up to?&rdquo; Nevertheless, curiosity consumed her and she went out by the gate and searched in the bushes. There she stumbled upon a large package, neatly tied in paper, the like of which she had seen in the big Flagerstown store. Molly was puzzled. Had young Keech begun to &ldquo;spark&ldquo; her in this romantic manner? It was highly improbable, because if he had fetched her presents he would most assuredly want to see her reaction to them. No! That package was Jim Traft&rsquo;s first message. Molly divined it, and passionately she reproached herself for her doubt of him. Still, he might have forgotten her. This might be a joke, or anything. Molly watched her chance to carry the parcel in unobserved, and up to the sanctity of her attic. Then, with trembling hands and beating heart, she opened it. Inside were three smaller parcels, and some books, one old and several new. One parcel was a box of candy tied with ribbons. Another contained the most beautiful silk scarf Molly had ever seen. The third was a little sewing-kit, containing everything imaginable. &ldquo;Shore it&rsquo;s from Jim&mdash;bless his heart!&rdquo; she murmured, and searched frantically for a note. There appeared to be none, and her wild hopes subsided. She examined the books and thrilled at their titles. The old book was a school grammar and had the look of long thumbing. Then on the fly-leaf she discovered handwriting: &ldquo;James Traft. Wellsville High School. Sept. 1, 1878.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ll be dog-goned! He&rsquo;s sent me his school-book, of ten years ago! ... Now what in the dickens does he mean by that?&rdquo; Molly was thrilled, puzzled, and then suddenly dismayed. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s a hint for me to study how to talk correct,&rdquo; she muttered, resentfully. But anger would not abide with her. One single query drove it away. Why should Jim Traft want her to make up for her lack of education? There could be only one answer. And straightway Molly was lifted to the skies and plunged into the depths. She had imagined&mdash;most absurdly&mdash;that he had repented of his rash offer, and had let her go at her own valuation. But he had not. He wished her to study. He believed in her. He meant&mdash;But Molly dared not follow on that thought. Molly hugged the grammar, miserably sure of her mingled pain and joy. She put it under her pillow. She kissed the silken scarf and tied it round her neck. And all the rest of that afternoon she sat by her window, devouring both the candy and the story-books. A hundred times she looked dreamily out, to whisper: &ldquo;Why didn&rsquo;t he write me a letter?&rdquo; And after a while it dawned upon her wondrously that Jim Traft, despite his demands, was trying to make it easy for her. All during the next week, Molly studied, day after day until her eyes ached. She taunted herself, but she could not help it. The brooding thoughts of making away with herself recurred, but they could not gain any hold in this new and strange consciousness. There seemed to be a joy even in her torment. Why could not her life be like that of the girls in the story-books? When she had punished herself greatly with their power over her, then she went back to the grammar. The next Sunday Molly waited all day for another possible visit from young Keech. But he did not come and she had to fight bitter disappointment. Reason came back to her, however, after she had become thoroughly wretched. Keech would have to ride to Flagerstown&mdash;a whole day, and another back home. Then it would take the same time to make the trip down the Cibeque and back. Four days out of one week! That was too much. But he might come again the following Sunday, and she lived on hope of that. Meanwhile September was far on its way. The golden flowers of autumn had begun to line the trails and fields. In the glen the ferns were turning brown. High up in the notches under the Diamond the scarlet, and purple, and gold, had begun to burn in the sunlight. Fall had come&mdash;the season Molly loved best. Slinger still stayed at home, though now he went to the village; and Molly detected that he drank. Once or twice he had spoken gruffly to her. And he had cut the corn and packed it in to the crib; he had repaired the fence to keep the hogs out; he had begun to haul and chop the winter supply of firewood. Molly marveled and sadly divined the torment in him. No doubt he had expected a visit from the Flagerstown sheriff, or the cowboys of the Diamond. But they did not appear. And no word of Jim Traft having been shot by fence-cutters had yet reached West Fork. Molly believed now that it never would, and so she told her brother. Other news and rumors were rife, however, some of which might be true. Work on the drift fence had not progressed beyond Tobe&rsquo;s Well. All the labor that the Diamond put in was in repairing sections that had been laid low. Small herds of stock had been reported on the trails, driven by riders whose names were not even whispered in West Fork. Money appeared plentiful in the village. There had been several shooting frays, of small consequence, one of which had been an attack on Andy Stoneham while walking the trail to Molly&rsquo;s home. Another Sunday arrived, and with it, after Molly had waited long agonizing hours, young Keech with more of a mysterious air, more assurance that he shared a great secret, more winks and grins. He nearly drove Molly mad. He stayed for a whole insupportable hour, during which, before her drooping father and her inquisitive mother, and once or twice her sharp-eyed brother, Molly had to maintain her equilibrium as well as the demeanor of a village maiden entertaining a visitor. At last Keech said he wanted to run down to West Fork, and Molly accompanied him as far as the lane that led out from the barn, where he had left his horse. &ldquo;Thar&rsquo;s a pack fer you inside, hid in the corner,&rdquo; he informed her, with a meaning grin. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll drop in on my way back, in case you want to send a letter.&rdquo; &ldquo;Letter!&rdquo; faltered Molly. &ldquo;How&mdash;how soon&rsquo;ll you be back?&rdquo; &ldquo;Reckon aboot sundown. I&rsquo;ll whistle,&rdquo; he returned, and rode off down the lane. Molly watched him guiltily. He seemed to take it for granted that she was a party to this courtship of Jim Traft&rsquo;s. The affair certainly could not be kept secret very long, even if young Keech justified the trust Jim had in him. Molly knew these youngsters. Soon everybody would learn that she was accepting gifts from the nephew of rich old Traft, and that would only be another black mark for Molly Dunn of the Cibeque. Not that Molly cared on her own account&mdash;she was thinking of Jim, and how her people and neighbors of West Fork would only have more against him. It would never enter their heads that he might have honorable intentions. Oh, if she could only let them know his honesty&mdash;his goodness! She went in the barn and found the pack. Three times as large and heavy as the first one! Molly&rsquo;s fingers burned to open it, but she resolutely hid it and ran back to the house. She would return it. But no, she had not the strength to do that, let alone hurt Jim&rsquo;s feelings. She would write and tell him not to send any more. Up by her loft window, Molly labored over that letter and her tears fell upon it. She was aware that she was placing in Jim&rsquo;s hands documental proof of her lack of education. It hurt her, but she was not ashamed, and she wrote as she would have talked. She had meant to make it short, which intention turned out to be impossible. For she had to explain to Jim why he must not send more presents, why he must forget her. And then she seemed driven to add more, the content of which she scarcely realized and that she dared not read over. She was sad while she waited in the lane for Keech, yet conscious of something she had not felt before. She was proving to herself and to Jim that she did not wholly lack decency and consideration. A melancholy consolation came to her. This was the end of her brief little romance. Keech trotted down the lane, and received the letter as a matter of course. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll rustle along, Molly. I&rsquo;m stoppin&rsquo; at the head of the Cibeque. Have a little sparkin&rsquo; to do on my own hook. Haw! Haw! ... Good-by till next time.&rdquo; Molly watched him ride away into the gloom of the forest. And when he had gone she stood there, looking up at the grand bulk of the Diamond, from the rugged rim of which the afterglow of sunset was fading, and felt vaguely that somehow she was a forlorn little soul, cheated of love and happiness. Yet she was glad she had arisen to the heights, even if forever afterward she must be plunged into the depths. Under cover of the thickening dusk she secured the pack and carried it to the cabin and up to her secluded abode. There was at least one exceedingly good feature about her room, and it was that nobody ever got up there. Molly could not remember when anyone, except Arch, who had patched the roof, had invaded her retreat. Panting, Molly sat down by her window, with her hand on the precious pack. She would revel in opening it, and in its contents, which must do for all time. Little chance of her ever having another suitor and less of her ever wanting one! Dusk had settled down now. Her window was open, and a cool air began to move down from the mountain. The pines stood up like a black fringe. She could see the hideous dead trees in the clearing, and the pale blot of the bare fields. A cowbell tinkled, and somewhere a hound bayed. It seemed very sweet and peaceful there. Molly felt that strife and anguish were matters of the heart. At last she lighted her lamp, and in its uncertain light she opened the pack, ashamed of her rapture, yet utterly victim to it. Carefully she assorted the parcels, little and big, searching for a letter. But again there was none. And Molly was glad. If she read written words of love and hope and demand from him she would be as unstable as water. Suppose he should take exception to her letter of renunciation and boldly come to see her? Her heart gave a wild leap, then subsided proportionately. He would not come. He had pride and he would never stand more than a second refusal from Molly Dunn. More books, and these from St. Louis! To her amaze all the parcels were marked with the name of that distant Eastern city. He had sent for things. The Flagerstown stores were not good enough for her. How could she ever resist his way of making somebody of her? Then for the ensuing hour she marveled at and gloated over a multiplicity of pretty and useful little presents. He did not submerge her with an overwhelming sense of great expenditure. He had tact and taste. &ldquo;Oh, the darlin&rsquo;,&rdquo; she murmured, &ldquo;if he ever sees me again&mdash;it&rsquo;s shore all day with me!&rdquo; Then she crawled into bed and cried herself to sleep. During the next few days Molly went through a hundred variations of mood, ranging from lofty pride to self-pity, from resignation to furious revolt. And then she seemed to fix upon a settled wretchedness. Yet the days passed and life had to be lived and neglected work done. On Saturday afternoon she went to the village with her mother, the first visit there for a month. It was the one day in the week when there was any activity at West Fork. Molly felt a reluctance to being seen, yet a tingling curiosity in regard to possible rumors. She knew she could tell instantly, when she met any of the girls or young men, if they had heard of the marvelous romance that had enmeshed her. But she was to discover that, so far as her acquaintances were concerned, Molly Dunn was the same old Molly Dunn as ever. Strange to realize, Molly was chagrined. If they could know how far she had been removed from the ordinary life of West Fork! Old Enoch Summers&rsquo; establishment, that combined several stores in a row, all connected, stood on the main corner of the village street, and next to it was Mace&rsquo;s Saloon, a place of doubtful character, and one which Summers openly frowned upon, but in which rumor gave him a large interest. Board porches high off the ground, with wide steps in the middle and at each end, were a feature of West Fork&rsquo;s few business houses. Saddle-horses and buckboards, along the hitching-rails, attested to visitors in town. And in the next block all the available room along the plank sidewalk was taken up by wagons and buggies. Molly for once was glad she had come with her mother, of whom it had been said that she had scant civility for Molly&rsquo;s admirers. They came at length to Summers&rsquo; store, where Molly had to run the gauntlet of numerous men, old and young, strangers and acquaintances. It was a busy hour for the village merchant and his clerks. Mrs. Dunn and Molly had to await their turn, and when Mrs. Dunn met the worst gossip in West Fork, to become avidly absorbed at once, Molly, as a matter of self-preservation as well as taste, strolled on through this store into the next. Presently she found herself quite near the door, and curiously took a peep out. A young man had just ridden up to the porch. And when Molly recognized Jim Traft she nearly dropped to the floor. Fate had led her aimlessly to that door&mdash;for this. Then as the usual hum of talk of the loungers outside abruptly ceased, Molly realized that she was indeed not the only interested one. Jim surveyed the group on the porch, then dismounted and threw his bridle, to step up half the steps. &ldquo;Good afternoon, gentlemen,&rdquo; he said, coolly, and his resonant voice carried. &ldquo;My name&rsquo;s Jim Traft and I&rsquo;m looking for Slinger Dunn.&rdquo; Molly bit her tongue to keep from crying out. Her next impulse was to rush to Jim. But that would be a crazy thing to do. He certainly would resent it. Her quick glance flashed over his lithe figure. He wore corduroys, high top-boots, and a brown shirt. Manifestly he could not have had a weapon upon him. Relief mitigated Molly&rsquo;s terror. He did not mean to fight, and certainly Slinger, no matter how provoked, could not shoot an unarmed man before all these people. Jim did not look exactly pleasant, but how fine, Molly thought, and manly and clean-eyed. &ldquo;Wal, Mister Traft, you&rsquo;ll find Slinger in Mace&rsquo;s saloon,&rdquo; drawled some one. &ldquo;Thanks,&rdquo; replied Traft, and flipping a silver dollar to an urchin there, he said, &ldquo;Johnny, go in and tell Slinger Dunn that if he isn&rsquo;t afraid to come out Jim Traft would like to see him.&rdquo; The boy snatched up the coin and bounded off the porch. Jim stepped up another step. He appeared composed, but pale. He had some deep motive in bearding Slinger Dunn right in his own den. Molly fell prey to a tumult of thrill and shudder. She could not have rushed out now, for she was riveted to the spot. Traft&rsquo;s message had caused a low exclamation to run through the occupants of the porch, a dozen or more of whom Molly could see through door and window. Jim surely would have espied her had he looked in. But he faced down the porch, toward Mace&rsquo;s saloon. &ldquo;By gum! Slinger&rsquo;s a-comin&rsquo;!&rdquo; said some one. &ldquo;Reckoned thet&rsquo;d fetch him,&rdquo; replied another. These two stood near the door and partly shielded Molly. She wrenched at her fettered feet, to move a little forward, the better to see. However, she could not see Slinger yet. People in the store behind her approached. Then the slow step of a spurred boot sent a combined fire and ice over Molly. Slinger came into view, crossed the porch to confront Traft. &ldquo;Did you send thet kid in heah,&rdquo; and Slinger jerked his left hand backward. His right hung significantly free and low, &ldquo;askin&rsquo; me out if I wasn&rsquo;t afeard?&rdquo; &ldquo;Howdy, Slinger. Yes, I did,&rdquo; replied Jim, and he stepped up the last step. After a keen fearless glance straight into Slinger&rsquo;s eyes, he extinguished the cigarette he carried and dropped it off the porch. It was noticeable that he wore gloves. &ldquo;Wal, you want to be careful aboot sendin&rsquo; fer me thet way.&rdquo; &ldquo;No offense. I just wanted you outside.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; what fer, Mister Traft?&rdquo; &ldquo;Several things, Slinger, and for that reason I&rsquo;m glad some of your friends and town folks are present,&rdquo; replied Traft. A small group of men had followed Dunn out of the saloon, but did not come up on the porch. In fact, they edged out toward the hitching-rail. &ldquo;What you want?&rdquo; demanded Dunn, in mingled anger and amaze. &ldquo;First I want to tell you I&rsquo;m sorry my outfit suspected you of cutting our drift fence. We found out who did it, and though we never said it was you, we think we owe you an apology. So I&rsquo;m apologizing for myself and the Diamond&mdash;to you, publicly.&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckon I did, Mister Traft,&rdquo; replied Slinger, not shorn of his personality, because nothing could have done that, but plainly staggered. &ldquo;I cain&rsquo;t see it thet way.&rdquo; &ldquo;No, it couldn&rsquo;t be,&rdquo; declared Dunn. &ldquo;Thanks again.... Well, Molly refused me. And one of the reasons she gave me was that she was Slinger Dunn&rsquo;s sister.&rdquo; Molly saw Arch flinch, and tremendously agitated as she was she felt a pang for him. This Jim Traft had a tongue as deadly as a bullet. Without the slightest hesitation Slinger unbuckled his belt, containing the heavy gun, and handed it shakily over to some one. He sailed his sombrero off the porch, exposing a livid face. He made a gesture, eloquent of supreme fury, and added to it with incoherent speech. Then like a panther he leaped at Traft. Molly saw Jim move as quickly, just as Slinger reached him, and appeared to strike at the same instant. The blow cracked. Its force, added to Slinger&rsquo;s momentum, sent him off the porch, where only a remarkable agility kept him on his feet. But he thumped solidly against the hitching-rail, which broke. Horses snorted and jumped. The crowd let out a whoop. Fights were mostly as common as meals in the Cibeque and infinitely more amusing. People inside the store crowded out, and joining the men cut off Molly&rsquo;s view. She edged out, back to the wall, fearful, yet tremendously impelled. She had gloried in Jim&rsquo;s brave front, but she felt he would be helpless in a fight with Slinger. Bumping against a bench, Molly stepped up on it. She saw Jim go down off the steps to meet Slinger. Then began a fierce exchange of blows, with Slinger slowly forced backward in front of Mace&rsquo;s saloon. Something was wrong, or unusual, Molly vaguely gathered from the exclamations and whispers of the villagers. Slinger Dunn had the reputation of being able to whip his weight in wildcats. But evidently he was slow in getting started here. Suddenly a blow upset him, and he plumped down ridiculously. The crowd warming to the fight greeted that with yells. Slinger bounced up, only to be knocked down again. Then pandemonium broke loose. The young man from the Diamond might not be going to get mauled into a pulp. He might be cordially hated, but that had nothing to do with the surprise and glee of the West Forkers. Molly could not longer distinguish the shouts, the jeers, the egging on of the contestants, the riotous advice. Bounding up with the agility he was noted for, Slinger took a couple of nasty digs in order to get hold of Traft. He clinched and plainly sought to trip his antagonist or wrestle him down. But Jim was the heavier and stronger, for with a whirl and a fling he sent Slinger sprawling. This occasioned a sudden silence. Was it possible for Slinger Dunn to be worsted? &ldquo;Stand up and fight&mdash;you Indian!&rdquo; yelled Traft. And indeed Dunn had the look and the suppleness of an Indian. Dunn, now bloody and dirty, responded as if he had no control over himself, as if this taunting voice could drive him to anything. He crouched and bored in, fighting low, until Traft swung up under his guard. Dunn&rsquo;s head jerked up. Another blow sent it back, and a third, square on the nose, making the blood fly, landed him on his back. This time Slinger did not bounce up. Something was being battered into his consciousness&mdash;something that had already dawned upon the crowd. He slowly and cautiously rose, a stream of red running from his nose, down across his tight lips and protruding chin. Again he changed his tactics, proving that a fury of confidence had succeeded to grim realization, and that where an ordinary fighter would have been whipped he still had resource to spirit and energy. He tried a square stand up, give and take. It grew evident that had he adopted this style in the beginning he would at least have done better, for he hit Traft now and then. But the latter could take punishment. If it hurt he gave no sign. His method grew clear to the bystanders, and wagers were shouted out, backing him to win. Molly, in a fit of wild joy at Jim&rsquo;s unexpected and wonderful ability, jumped up and down on the bench, and it was not certain that she did not cry out. Soon down went Slinger again. The blow that prostrated him was from Traft&rsquo;s right, and was a swing, coming at the end of a succession of short blows, delivered fast and closely, no doubt to beat his antagonist back and out of balance. Anyway, Molly saw her brother go piling into the dust. &ldquo;Reckon now Slinger will rooster him, an&rsquo; it&rsquo;s shore aboot time,&rdquo; declared a young fellow in front of Molly. &ldquo;Yep. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m damn curious,&rdquo; replied his companion. Other remarks were not wanting. Evidently Slinger Dunn was not yet beaten. Molly had heard of the &ldquo;rooster&rdquo; trick in fighting, but had never seen it. And her lot had been to see many an encounter between boys of the Cibeque. She had seen more than one dance interrupted, with the dancers fleeing to the walls, while a fierce battle ensued in the middle of the floor. But fear for Jim had fled from Molly. He could meet any of Slinger&rsquo;s backwoods tricks. Slinger slowly circled Traft, keeping well away. Undoubtedly Traft was ready for a new attack. When he got rather close to the wall he divined that Dunn was trying to back him into such a position, whereupon he stood stock-still and waited. Suddenly Slinger dove down with incredible swiftness, on the back of his head and neck and elevated his feet even higher than his arms had been. His boots were armed with long spurs. He began to kick at Jim. He actually appeared to stand on the back of his neck and his elbows. &ldquo;Rooster him, Slinger!&rdquo; bawled a lusty-lunged lout. And the crowd of West Forkers roared. Molly saw Jim back from this amazing onslaught, and that was what he should not have done. For Slinger, hunching himself on his elbows, quick as a cat, forced Jim to the wall. He dodged one vicious kick that raked the wall. Another caught him on his extended arm, tearing his sleeve from wrist to shoulder. Molly saw a glimpse of red. Then a cruel spur cut open Jim&rsquo;s chin. At this Molly screamed at her brother, but her voice was lost in the din. If Slinger did not kill Jim he would surely disfigure him for life. She leaped off the bench and darted here and there to get through the circle of men. Suddenly a louder yell, hoarse and thrilling, made Molly desperate. She squeezed into the front. Jim, in bent position, had both arms round Slinger&rsquo;s legs. The terrible spurs stuck up, but they scarcely moved. Jim threw Slinger from him with such force that he turned clear over, his head and shoulders acting as a pivot. He fell with a flop. Jim made one jump and landed square on him with both heavy boots. This overbalanced Jim, who went down, but he went down kicking. Rolling over, he was up and at Slinger just as that hideous blood-and-dirt begrimed individual tried to rise. Jim fastened both hands in his neck and lifted him and flung him sheer against the wall, where his head rang like bone on wood. But Jim did not stop. As Slinger, eyes rolling, tongue hanging out, was sinking down, Jim banged him against the wall again, and finished with a terrific sodden blow. Slinger sank down limp and senseless. The crowd grew silent. Molly had sense enough left to hide behind some one. Jim gazed down a long moment at his beaten antagonist, and then with scarf he wiped the blood and sweat and dirt from his face. He turned sidewise, so that Molly saw a pale tense cheek. &ldquo;See here&mdash;you fellows,&rdquo; panted Jim, &ldquo;I come down&mdash;here&mdash;to lick him&mdash;and to offer him&mdash;a job.... Reckon he&rsquo;s not worth it.... But I&rsquo;ll go through with my part. ... Tell him&mdash;when he comes to&mdash;that if he can play square&mdash;there&rsquo;s a place on the Diamond for him.&rdquo; Then Jim parted the crowd and disappeared. Molly slipped back up on the porch into the store, and never even thinking of her mother, she hurried through to the other corner store and went out the side entrance. Sobbing, and in a terrible condition of mind, she ran home. Not one of the romances Jim had sent her in book form could reach up to the heights of this true happening in West Fork. How impossible to believe! Yet Molly knew her eyes and ears were to be trusted, if not her heart. Jim Traft had done a marvelous thing. It was breath-taking. He had a noble spirit that might not be wholly realized in the valley, but his ability to whip the wildcat of the Cibeque and then offer him a job on the greatest outfit of the range, would be appreciated. That kind of language was understood down in the brakes. Molly began to divine that Jim was invincible. He had brought brains and brawn with him, and the West had taken stock of it. He had given his enemies a hard row to hoe. Molly had spent so many hours dreaming and thinking about Jim that now in the light of his decisive and open stand she could understand him. And she summed up her reaction to it all in a tragic whisper: &ldquo;My land! If he comes heah I&mdash;I&rsquo;ll fall at his feet!&rdquo; Her mother&rsquo;s return warned Molly that she was liable to have a bad half-hour, and she tried to fortify herself. &ldquo;Molly, you up there?&rdquo; came a trenchant call. &ldquo;Yes, ma.&rdquo; &ldquo;Come down pronto.&rdquo; Molly started promptly, but lagged slower and slower, and she thought she would drop off the last steps of the ladder. Her mother stood there, arms akimbo, gazing at her with an entirely new and astounding expression. &ldquo;Why&rsquo;d you run home?&rdquo; she demanded. &ldquo;There was a fight&mdash;an&rsquo;&mdash;an&rsquo; it scared me.&rdquo; &ldquo;It needn&rsquo;t have&mdash;since it was in your honor.... Molly Dunn, did you hear what that young Traft told the crowd?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, ma. I was there.&rdquo; Molly nodded mournfully. Presently she would be treated to a terrible harangue, and she had already stood enough for one day. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s all over town. Crowds on the corners talking. I was told by ten or a dozen people. But I couldn&rsquo;t believe it. You&rsquo;re sure it&rsquo;s no trick? I ran everywhere, hunting you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Mother, it&rsquo;s the bitter truth,&rdquo; said Molly, steadily. &ldquo;Bitter! Are you crazy, girl? ... It tastes pretty sweet to me... . Did you say &lsquo;No&rsquo; to young Traft?&rdquo; &ldquo;Of course I did.&rdquo; &ldquo;But you&rsquo;re in love with him. That&rsquo;s what has ailed you ever since you went to Flag. Anybody could see you were lovesick. Aren&rsquo;t you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Ma, I&rsquo;m shore sick aboot somethin&rsquo;,&rdquo; replied Molly. &ldquo;For Heaven&rsquo;s sake, then, why didn&rsquo;t you accept him?&rdquo; &ldquo;Because I&rsquo;m Molly Dunn of the Cibeque.&rdquo; Then indeed the storm broke over Molly, though it was so vastly different from what she had anticipated that instead of casting her down it began to do the opposite. In fact her mother presented a most interesting and amusing study, after the first tirade about Molly&rsquo;s lack of family pride. Molly learned that she was a granddaughter of Rose Hillyard of Virginia, and had bluer blood than any Traft who ever lived. Molly had to listen and she dared not voice her protest. Moreover she was as much amazed as her mother was indignant. She could not understand this sudden right about face in regard to her admirers. Temporary relief, however, came with an interruption in shape of the arrival of several young men, bringing Slinger home. For once her mother&rsquo;s tongue stopped its wagging. Arch Dunn was a spectacle to behold. He could walk, but that was about all, and he rewarded the kindly offices of those who had escorted him by driving them off. Both Mrs. Dunn and Molly stood back, afraid to approach or speak, and almost afraid to look. Slinger dragged himself around to the back porch, where he sagged to his bed. &ldquo;Arch&mdash;can I do anythin&rsquo;?&rdquo; faltered Molly. &ldquo;Wal, I reckon, since I cain&rsquo;t do for myself,&rdquo; was the surprising answer. Molly hastened to get a pan of water, soap and bandages and salve, and hurried to his side. She divined that by this incident she would either gain or lose a brother. She unbuckled the long spurs, shuddering at the blood-stains on one of them. Arch had got what he deserved. The imprint of hobnails on his face appeared to be a brand. He had been treated to a dose of his own medicine. But the fatal issue might be now that he would kill Jim Traft. Molly prayed and hoped. Could not the same thing, almost, that had happened to her, happen to Arch? She pulled off his boots, and then his wet and torn shirt. The mixture of blood, sweat, and dirt actually made it heavy. It was not pleasant to look into her brother&rsquo;s visage. Yet pity and tenderness came to her aid. &ldquo;Much obliged, Molly,&rdquo; he said, when she had finished what little she could do. &ldquo;Was you in town when the cyclone hit?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, Arch. I&mdash;I saw it,&rdquo; she replied, thrilled that he would talk to her in such wise. She prayed for something to make the moment helpful for this wayward brother. &ldquo;Did you see him lick me?&rdquo; She murmured an affirmative. &ldquo;My Gawd! who&rsquo;d ever thunk it? A Missourie tenderfoot!&mdash;But, Molly, he had it on me. I never was so hurted in all my fights. He must have binged my nose a thousand times. I could have bellared out.&rdquo; &ldquo;Arch, you must have&mdash;have hurt him very much, too,&rdquo; said Molly. &ldquo;He was all bloody.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. I reckon so. I&rsquo;d have cut him to pieces&mdash;I was thet mad.... Molly, I got too mad. An&rsquo; at thet he hurt me wuss with his sharp tongue.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;d not have been so&mdash;so bad if you hadn&rsquo;t tried to &lsquo;rooster&rsquo; him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, all&rsquo;s fair in thet kind of a fight. I don&rsquo;t care aboot thet any more&rsquo;n I care aboot his lickin&rsquo; me. He&rsquo;s simply a better man. I told them so.... But I reckon there&rsquo;s another way.&rdquo; &ldquo;Arch, you&mdash;you&rsquo;ll meet Jim&mdash;force him to draw?&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;d be natural-like, wouldn&rsquo;t it?&rdquo; &ldquo;From the stand of the Cibeque, yes. But there&rsquo;s a bigger way to meet it. If you forced him to draw, you&rsquo;d kill him!&rdquo; &ldquo;Huh! I&rsquo;m not so damn shore of thet. Mister Jim Traft is a surprisin&rsquo; hombre.&rdquo; &ldquo;Pray? What fer?&rdquo; &ldquo;For you to see clear.&rdquo; &ldquo;How&rsquo;n hell can I see anyway with both eyes shet?&rdquo; &ldquo;Brother, I mean see with your spirit.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, you talk like thet parson who was shot heah onct.... Molly, did Traft want to marry you?&rdquo; Solemnly Molly nodded. &ldquo;I cain&rsquo;t see very well. Talk. Honest now&mdash;did he?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, Arch&mdash;an&rsquo; that justified my faith in him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Reckon a fellar could do no more.... An&rsquo; you wouldn&rsquo;t take him up &rsquo;cause you was Slinger Dunn&rsquo;s sister?&rdquo; &ldquo;That was one reason,&rdquo; admitted Molly. &ldquo;What you mean?&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, you&rsquo;ve a reputation that to civilized folks would look terrible.&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly, I&rsquo;ll gamble there&rsquo;s wuss men than me. It ain&rsquo;t nuthin&rsquo; to pack a gun an&rsquo; use it when you&rsquo;re crowded.... Now thet day up the canyon. I shore gave young Traft a long time to shoot. An&rsquo; he had a rifle in his hands.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s what&rsquo;s wrong aboot it, Arch. He wouldn&rsquo;t want to kill you in the first place&mdash;an&rsquo; second, when you met him he&rsquo;d forget he had a gun. Or be scared.&rdquo; &ldquo;Scared! Thet hombre? He was orful scared of me today! Nope, it&rsquo;s jest gun-slingin&rsquo; wasn&rsquo;t brought up with him.&rdquo; &ldquo;It&rsquo;d be plain murder.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ll let him off&mdash;providin&rsquo; you agree to marry him.&rdquo; &ldquo;Only deal you can make with me, an&rsquo; you bet it&rsquo;s a hard one to swaller.&rdquo; Molly knew Slinger Dunn and that she must grasp at straws. &ldquo;I&mdash;I&rsquo;ll&mdash;take-you-up, Arch,&rdquo; she said, in a strangled voice. &ldquo;If he&mdash;asks me&mdash;any more.&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t worry none, sister. Thet hombre will be heah today. An&rsquo; you can make him ask you again. Reckon, though, he&rsquo;ll wait till dark&mdash;not wantin&rsquo; you to see his mug. I shore bunged him up.&rdquo; Molly trembled on the brink of the precipice to which she had been driven. She dared not look over. She must leap with closed eyes. &ldquo;Did you&mdash;rooster him bad?&rdquo; she asked. &ldquo;Nope. Only scratched him. But I was shore leggin&rsquo; it fer him when he ducked an&rsquo; grabbed me.... Fetch me a drink. I&rsquo;m shore parchin&rsquo;.&rdquo; Presently Molly stole away to her nest under the roof, thankful to let well enough alone, so far as Slinger was concerned. To secure his promise not to kill Jim was more than she had hoped for, at any cost. And that cost! What a terrible thing to take upon herself! If Jim did not ask her again and soon, he would not live very long. Slinger kept his promises. Molly felt that she would die for Jim and it was not so very much worse to have to marry him. The prospect dazzled and terrified her. If Jim would live in a log cabin and let her work for him! That would be heaven. But he would want to take her away from the Cibeque&mdash;from the woods! from Maple Spring! and wear stockings and live in the mansion of a ranch-house at Flagerstown, and meet his rich old uncle&mdash;his other relatives&mdash;and his mother. Molly was overcome at the thought. She could not do it. But to save his life she would do anything. She sank on her bed with a sensation as if her breast was caved in. And there she lay, like a wounded deer, for hours, and never could have accounted for them. She crawled down to help get supper and she took some to Arch, who could not eat, and she sat at table more to avoid clash with her mother than to satisfy hunger. In the dusk she wandered along outside the lane, irresistibly drawn. Her mother and brother were fools. Jim would not come. She longed for him, yet prayed he would stay away. The bats were whirling in the clear cool air overhead. The Diamond stood up black and bold, somehow strengthening her. All her life she had looked up to that mighty bulk. The smell of burning pine floated from the dark forest. A night-hawk flitted by with sharp note. Then a whistle electrified Molly. She had been whistled for many times in this gloaming hour. It was one of the courting tricks of the Cibeque. But never before had a whistle sent a blade into her heart or lent her wings. Her moccasined feet pattered on the hard-packed ground. She saw a dark form at the gate, leaning on the bars. She ran on. Yes&mdash;Jim! ... Then somehow she was sitting on the top bar&mdash;one hand at her bursting breast and the other, which was her left, in Jim&rsquo;s grasp. He spoke, but she did not distinguish what he said, and she could not reply. She felt his rough strong hands. He was slipping something on her finger. A ring! Dark forms appeared out of the brush, almost without rustle. One man shoved a gun against Jim&rsquo;s back. Seth Haverly! Up went Jim&rsquo;s hands. Molly recognized Sam Haverly. The third man came close&mdash;laid a powerful hand on her. &ldquo;If you yap we&rsquo;ll kill him!&rdquo; Molly would have fallen but for his hold. It was Hackamore Jocelyn. &ldquo;Turn round, Traft, an&rsquo; march,&rdquo; ordered Seth Haverly. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s this? A hold-up?&rdquo; queried Jim, hoarsely. &ldquo;Keep still&mdash;or it&rsquo;ll be wuss!&rdquo; hissed Haverly. &ldquo;Fellars, I&rsquo;m takin&rsquo; the gurl along,&rdquo; announced Jocelyn. Haverly made a fierce gesture, which included his gun. &ldquo;You air like hell! I&rsquo;m tellin&rsquo; you agin, Jocelyn, thet I&rsquo;m runnin&rsquo; this outfit. Stay heah an&rsquo; keep her quiet till we git away with the hosses.&rdquo; &ldquo;But, Seth, I want the gurl,&rdquo; replied Jocelyn, low and doggedly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll divvy my share of Traft&rsquo;s ransom with you.&rdquo; &ldquo;&mdash;&mdash; you, Jocelyn!&rdquo; cursed Haverly. &ldquo;We&rsquo;ll have hell enough without that. Do you want Slinger Dunn on your trail? ... Stay heah an&rsquo; hug the gurl who hasn&rsquo;t no use fer you, if you&rsquo;re that much of a sucker. But foller us pronto.&rdquo; The Haverlys then forced Jim ahead of them, vanishing in the gloom of the lane. Molly heard the whinny of a horse at a distance. She seemed paralyzed. Hack Jocelyn loomed over her. &ldquo;Molly, I&rsquo;m takin&rsquo; you willin&rsquo; or by force,&rdquo; he whispered, bending down to peer at her. &ldquo;An&rsquo; don&rsquo;t fergit&mdash;if you make even a peep they&rsquo;ll shoot Traft.&rdquo; &ldquo;Where they goin&rsquo;?&rdquo; gasped Molly. &ldquo;Up back on the Diamond. Thet wasn&rsquo;t my idee. They don&rsquo;t know Curly Prentiss. But they&rsquo;re goin&rsquo; to hide Traft an&rsquo; hold him fer ransom. But when they git the money they&rsquo;ll kill him. Hang him on the drift fence!&mdash;- Thet&rsquo;s the plan. An&rsquo; the only chanct of savin&rsquo; his life is fer you to go with me.&rdquo; &ldquo;How&rsquo;ll that&mdash;save Jim?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll do it. I&rsquo;ll stick out fer lettin&rsquo; him go. An&rsquo; if it comes to a pinch I&rsquo;ll force them.&rdquo; &ldquo;You swear to Gawd!&rdquo; demanded Molly, in fierce passion. &ldquo;Shore. I swear to Gawd.&rdquo; &ldquo;Slinger will kill you. I cain&rsquo;t answer fer him. It won&rsquo;t be long till he&rsquo;s on my trail. I&rsquo;m warnin&rsquo; you, Hack Jocelyn.&rdquo; &ldquo;I heah you,&rdquo; he replied, grimly. &ldquo;Reckon Slinger is&mdash;or would be&mdash;a stumper. But he&rsquo;s crippled, an&rsquo; before he&rsquo;s out ag&rsquo;in, it&rsquo;ll all be over.... I&rsquo;ll hev my stake an&rsquo; we&rsquo;ll ride out of the country.&rdquo; &ldquo;You an&rsquo; me?&rdquo; she queried, marveling at the man&rsquo;s egotism and stupidity. &ldquo;You an&rsquo; me, Molly Dunn. Shore it&rsquo;s been long in my mind.... Now, will I hev to hawg-tie you?&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll go willin&rsquo;. But keep your hands off me.&rdquo; He led her down the darkening lane, out of the clearing into the forest. Behind Molly he saw a dark form rise and loom. He recognized it. Hack Jocelyn! With a muttered curse at his heedless disregard of Andy Stoneham&rsquo;s warning he lifted his hands above his head. Then another dark form clinked into sight. He was ordered to face round. A swift glance was the last he had of Molly. Jocelyn&rsquo;s looming over her further added to his dismay. The men had their short and disgruntled argument, then Jim was forced down the lane. Jocelyn&rsquo;s staying behind to &ldquo;hug&rdquo; Molly, as Haverly so vulgarly put it, made the heat dance back into Jim&rsquo;s veins. He turned once to call out to Molly, but a hiss and a move from Haverly dissuaded him. He could scarcely help her, and after a moment&rsquo;s reflection he saw the fallacy of Jocelyn&rsquo;s kidnapping her, too, and he had faith in Molly&rsquo;s wit and nerve. It galled him horribly to leave her there, to be subjected to rudeness, perhaps insult. And the uncertainty of the situation would grow on him until Jocelyn joined them again. At the point where the lane entered the road to town three other men waited with horses. Jim was amazed to make out his own horse, saddled and bridled. These kidnappers evidently were thorough and bold. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s your game, Haverly?&rdquo; queried Jim, breaking into a whispered colloquy. &ldquo;We&rsquo;re holdin&rsquo; you fer ransom,&rdquo; came the gruff reply. &ldquo;We&rsquo;ll take you to a hidin&rsquo;-place an&rsquo; send a rider in to Flag.&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, I&rsquo;ll recommend my uncle&rsquo;s paying it, provided you agree to two things.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; what&rsquo;s them?&rdquo; &ldquo;That the ransom isn&rsquo;t made out of all reason&mdash;and that Hack Jocelyn doesn&rsquo;t share in it.&rdquo; Haverly let out a grunt which might have meant anything. Jim conceived an idea, which he proceeded to put into execution at once. &ldquo;This man Jocelyn is no good,&rdquo; went on Jim. &ldquo;He had friends on the Diamond and he double-crossed them. He&rsquo;s not a straight shooter. He plays both ends against the middle. You fellows are a lot of suckers.&rdquo; &ldquo;Sam&mdash;you heah thet?&rdquo; sharply ejaculated Haverly. &ldquo;Hell! I ain&rsquo;t deaf,&rdquo; replied one of the uneasy listeners. &ldquo;Jocelyn is using you Cibeque fellows to his own end and you can bet on it,&rdquo; concluded Jim, thinking this entering wedge enough for the moment. &ldquo;Seth, I&rsquo;m gettin&rsquo; leary myself,&rdquo; replied Sam Haverly. &ldquo;An&rsquo; I&rsquo;m sorry I plugged Jocelyn&rsquo;s game. When he killed Andy Stoneham tonight, right in the road, I shore throwed a fit. Nobody but Boyd an&rsquo; Hart an&rsquo; me seen it done. But you can gamble West Fork will lay thet on to us.&rdquo; &ldquo;Killed Andy Stoneham!&rdquo; ejaculated Seth, leaning forward. &ldquo;When?&rdquo; &ldquo;Jest now, almost. You ought to have heahed the shot.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ll be &mdash;&mdash;! What on earth fer?&rdquo; &ldquo;Jocelyn swore Stoneham was spyin&rsquo; on him an&rsquo; givin&rsquo; us away to Molly Dunn.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Jealous of poor Andy!&mdash;Gawd! but thet fellar is crazy aboot Molly Dunn.... We better not wait fer him. Tie his hoss, Boyd, an&rsquo; we&rsquo;ll hit the trail.&rdquo; Jim was told to climb into his saddle. &ldquo;Hart, you lead his hoss. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll foller behind him.&rdquo; They started off at a trot and soon turned off the road into a trail. It was dark and overgrown with branches of trees that had to be dodged or brushed aside. Jim knew that he had not ridden down to West Fork by that trail. He settled himself for what he anticipated a long night ride and his thoughts were gloomy. He had been shocked to hear that Andy Stoneham had been shot by Jocelyn. Not an hour after Andy had warned Jim to keep indoors! But Jim had disregarded this advice, to his bitter regret. If only Jocelyn would catch up with them! His distrust of this cowboy had been more than justified. Jim realized now that Molly would be in peril. Jocelyn had insisted on kidnapping her, too. The chances were he would do it. Then, when he brought her along with him, there would be precipitated a complex situation. It might well be that Jocelyn in his passion had bitten off more than he could chew. Jim grew darkly active in thought. He contended with the problem that presupposed Jocelyn was following with Molly. And while he cudgled his brains he rode on into the denser forest. His captors were silent, and kept to a trot on level ground, and a walk over rough places and upgrades. The moon arose and sent a shadowed blanching into the forest. They rode down into a gully, where water ran over rocks, and the unshod horses slipped and splashed. It was weird and wonderful under the great pines, that moaned fitfully above. They climbed out of the gully to zigzag up a steep trail, soft and full of rocks. And after one of the halts to let the horses rest Jim made certain they were climbing the Diamond. The nerve of these Cibeque riders! Jim knew the Diamond was a big country, exceedingly wild and rough on the high west slope, but he would have staked a good deal on Curly Prentiss trailing them to their lair. Curly&rsquo;s reputation as to tracker would have to be sustained. How the outfit would drive him! As to that, they would each and every one ride out on the man-hurt. This aspect of the situation was thrilling, but Jim felt qualms at the possible outcome. He had come to know his men now. The moon went down; the sky grew dark blue and star-fired. Jim&rsquo;s captors climbed out on top of the rim, and when Jim looked down into that black abyss he caught his breath. They rode on into the forest, toward the east, and after a long ride they headed a thick-timbered canyon, and turned west again. They climbed a ridge and kept to its summit for what seemed hours to the wearying Jim, and at last they cut down into wild dense forest. In the gray of dawn they rode up again, into open grassy parks where no hoof-tracks would show, down again and up once more, to descend a pine-and spruce-timbered slope, to enter at daylight a beautiful white-grassed park, fringed by forest. Jim saw a shaggy gray log cabin in the edge of the pines. A flock of wild turkeys, all gobblers, stately and bronze-flecked, walked across the open. Far down the valley-like park, near a willow-bordered stream that wound through, stood deer motionless, with heads and long ears up. Smoke issued from the rude outside stone chimney of the cabin. Then a man appeared, rifle in hand. Haverly let out a shrill whistle. Another man appeared, and he shouted in hoarse welcome. The cabin was very old, built of peeled pine logs and split shingles, which in places had rotted through. The front of the roof had an overhang of ten feet or more, with one post at each corner. That end of the cabin was open and there were no windows or doors. The fire was inside. Saddles and pack outfits were stacked under this projecting shed-like roof. &ldquo;Wal, we&rsquo;re heah,&rdquo; announced Seth Haverly to Jim, as they came to a halt at the cabin. &ldquo;I cain&rsquo;t say that you squealed a hell of a lot on the way.&rdquo; He was a thin, wiry young man, blond, with hazel eyes, clear as light and which certainly did not have any shifty quality. His beard was like fine amber moss. &ldquo;No, Haverly, I&rsquo;m not a squealer,&rdquo; replied Jim. &ldquo;And I could be worse off.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore you could,&rdquo; said the other, after a moment of surprise. &ldquo;I reckon Jocelyn had you figgered wrong or he lied like a streak.&rdquo; &ldquo;What notion of me did he give you?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, you was a dude tenderfoot, afeered of your shadder. Swore he licked stuffin&rsquo;s out of you.&rdquo; Jim laughed. &ldquo;Am I to get down?&rdquo; he inquired. &ldquo;Shore. Pile off.&rdquo; &ldquo;Was Jocelyn in West Fork yesterday when I had a little set-to with Dunn?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nope. But I was an&rsquo; I seen your little set-to.... Might I ask, Mister Traft, what you&rsquo;d call a big set-to?&rdquo; &ldquo;Well, there&rsquo;ll be a pretty fair one when Curly Prentiss and the Diamond hole you fellows here,&rdquo; responded Jim, cheerfully. &ldquo;And a real one when Slinger Dunn meets Hack Jocelyn. I wouldn&rsquo;t be in Jocelyn&rsquo;s boots for a million.&rdquo; &ldquo;Huh!&mdash;Wal, I kinda like you, Jim Traft, an&rsquo; I reckon I did thet fust day at Limestone, when I rid into your camp with Slinger. Do you recollect?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, I remember you, and that you seemed to have some sense.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I used to. But I&rsquo;m damned if I don&rsquo;t think it left me when I fell out with Slinger.&rdquo; Meanwhile the horses were unsaddled, hobbled, and turned loose. And Jim had a chance to look over the men. They were all young and they looked the part they were playing. Jim did not, however, conceive a very unfavorable impression. They were a group of cowboys misled&mdash;gone wrong; and that appeared to be a common enough thing in Arizona during the last of the &rsquo;eighties. Sam Haverly was also blond, almost red of hair and complexion, more sturdy than his brother, less lean of face and intelligent of eye. Hart Merriwell could not have been picked out of a group of cowboys for any distinct individuality. Boyd Flick was dark, small, and as he hobbled around in his enormous chaps Jim thought that he could never have stopped a pig in a lane. Jim had yet to hear the names of the two members of Haverly&rsquo;s outfit that had been keeping camp here in the park.
