They certainly struck him forcibly, and the bigger one, a brawny fellow for a rider, had very pale eyes and hair like tow. &ldquo;Whar&rsquo;s our new boss?&rdquo; he inquired, after greeting Seth, and his tone did not lack sarcasm. &ldquo;Matty, you can cut thet talk,&rdquo; replied Haverly. &ldquo;Or I&rsquo;m gonna get sore.... Jocelyn stayed behind.&rdquo; And in succinct words he gave his questioner a few details of what had detained him. And these elicited a short terse comment, mostly profane. &ldquo;If you&rsquo;re hungry come an&rsquo; git it before I throw it out,&rdquo; called the other fellow, from the cabin. &ldquo;Make yourself to home,&rdquo; said Haverly to Jim, leading him in. Whereupon these backwoods riders, precisely after the manner of cowboys, ate their breakfast standing or sitting or squatting, and one of them knelt with his. Jim very soon could have recommended the cook, and he made a hearty meal. &ldquo;What am I supposed to do now?&rdquo; he asked, genially. &ldquo;Wal, you shore wouldn&rsquo;t expect me to sing fer your amoosement,&rdquo; replied Seth, dryly. &ldquo;No. I&rsquo;m feeling pretty good now, considering, and I don&rsquo;t want to take risks,&rdquo; returned Jim, just as dryly. This retort went home, for remarks were forthcoming anent Seth&rsquo;s opinion of his vocal powers. The men looked frankly at Jim. &ldquo;Wal, Traft, do I hawg-tie you or not?&rdquo; queried Haverly. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sure I can&rsquo;t read your mind. As a matter of fact, Haverly, I don&rsquo;t think you&rsquo;ve got much gray matter. Or you&rsquo;d never have let Jocelyn persuade you into this deal.&rdquo; Haverly took that retort gravely, as well as corroborative ones from Matty and the cook. &ldquo;If you&rsquo;ll give your word not to make a break I won&rsquo;t tie you up,&rdquo; went on Haverly to Jim. &ldquo;Man, I can&rsquo;t promise that. Would you?&rdquo; protested Jim. &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ve done it, an&rsquo; lied like hell.... Mister Jim, you peer to be a straight-talkin&rsquo; fellar. Reckon we&rsquo;ll jest cut this idee of you bein&rsquo; a tenderfoot.... You can set around heah in comfort. But if you run we&rsquo;ll wing you. Thet&rsquo;s my order. Do you savvy?&rdquo; &ldquo;Sure. And much obliged. Molly was right when she said you were a pretty decent fellow. It was only bad company that was making an outlaw of you. She was glad Slinger quit your outfit,&rdquo; replied Jim, departing somewhat from literal veracity. But he had a deep game to play and he knew he could work hard in this simple, primitive mind. &ldquo;Wal, so Molly said thet!&rdquo; he ejaculated, with a queer look on his face. And he fell into a profound reverie. &ldquo;Hyar we air,&rdquo; announced Matty, spreading his hands. &ldquo;An&rsquo; thet Jocelyn not in the outfit. Air we goin&rsquo; to wait around, riskin&rsquo; our necks, while he sparks a gurl?&rdquo; &ldquo;Sparks nuthin&rsquo;. He&rsquo;ll have to hawg-tie Molly Dunn, if he wants her near him,&rdquo; replied Seth, snorting. Jim imagined he saw an opening. &ldquo;Say, didn&rsquo;t you see Jocelyn grab Molly last night? If he hadn&rsquo;t she&rsquo;d have run.&rdquo; &ldquo;I seen him. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll bet you-all a hundred thet he packs her up heah,&rdquo; replied Haverly. &ldquo;Why, the man&rsquo;d be out of his haid,&rdquo; said Matty. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s bad enough to kidnap this heah Traft boy fer money. But to haul a Cibeque gurl up heah&mdash;for doubtful reasons!&mdash;Seth, this Jocelyn ain&rsquo;t no Cibeque fellar nohow.&rdquo; &ldquo;Matty, you&rsquo;d needn&rsquo;t rag me,&rdquo; rejoined Seth, sullenly. &ldquo;I cussed him fierce last night. An&rsquo; I said &lsquo;No!&rsquo; If he fetches her &mdash;&mdash; &mdash;&mdash;; it&rsquo;ll be the wuss fer him!&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; it&rsquo;d be wuss fer us, don&rsquo;t you fergit thet.&rdquo; Sam Haverly spoke up, &ldquo;Matty, you ain&rsquo;t heerd aboot Jocelyn shootin&rsquo; Andy Stoneham last night?&rdquo; &ldquo;Andy! Fer Heaven&rsquo;s sake, thet good-natured clerk of Enoch&rsquo;s!&rdquo; exclaimed Matty, aghast. &ldquo;Hack swore Andy was informin&rsquo; ag&rsquo;in&rsquo; us.&rdquo; They all looked puzzled, and Seth Haverly shook his blond head broodingly. The expression of thoughts, opinions, criticisms, animosities, by these woodsmen seemed equivalent to the amassing of evidence. &ldquo;Fellows,&rdquo; broke in Jim, &ldquo;Hack Jocelyn is too deep for you. He had the Diamond standing on its head. Even after I saw through him I had a job convincing some of them.&rdquo; &ldquo;S&rsquo;pose you give us a hunch aboot this deal,&rdquo; suggested Seth Haverly. &ldquo;I will,&rdquo; declared Jim, &ldquo;provided you agree to what I asked.&rdquo; Seth turned to Matty, who evidently was an important member of the outfit. &ldquo;Traft says he&rsquo;ll advise his uncle to pay a ransom, providin&rsquo; it&rsquo;s not out of all reason, an&rsquo; thet Jocelyn don&rsquo;t git no share.&rdquo; &ldquo;Strikes me hard,&rdquo; said Matty. &ldquo;Jocelyn will raise hell. He wanted to name the amount of ransom an&rsquo; write the letter to old Traft. But he&rsquo;s not heah an&rsquo; we&rsquo;ve shore no time to lose.&rdquo; That rendered the eager group speechless. Before Haverly regained his voice the cook, who had gone outside to empty a pan, whistled low and sharp. They all jumped, and it certainly startled Jim. Then Seth strode out, to be followed by the others. &ldquo;Jocelyn comin&rsquo; down out of the woods&mdash;ridin&rsquo; double,&rdquo; said the cook, pointing. &ldquo;Fletch, if you ain&rsquo;t correct!&rdquo; ejaculated Matty. To this Seth Haverly added a volley of curses. Jim&rsquo;s eyes roved and strained, at last to espy a horse coming under the pines carrying double. He stood stock-still, suddenly galvanized. How slowly the tired horse descended the slope! It was some moments before Jim made out Molly clearly. She was riding astride, behind Jocelyn&rsquo;s saddle, and appeared to sit there easily. Her dark head grew distinct&mdash;then her face&mdash;then her eyes. Jim wondered how he would ever have the courage to look into them. A long night ride through the wild forest with that vicious cowboy! Jocelyn had a gray corded face&mdash;eyes like gimlets. He threw the bridle and, hands on hips, regarded the silent group. &ldquo;Missed the trail an&rsquo; was lost till daylight,&rdquo; he explained. Seth Haverly took a step out in front of his comrades. He was slow, guarded, but not afraid. &ldquo;So, Jocelyn, you kidnapped Molly?&rdquo; he queried, harshly. &ldquo;Nope. She come willin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;What!&rdquo; &ldquo;She come willin&rsquo;. Ask her yourself,&rdquo; returned Jocelyn, coolly. &ldquo;Molly, is he tellin&rsquo; me straight? Did you come up heah of your own accord?&rdquo; demanded Haverly. &ldquo;Yes, I did, Seth,&rdquo; replied Molly, calmly. Haverly stepped closer and peered up into Molly&rsquo;s face, as if to read not only confirmation of Molly&rsquo;s admission but of suspicions of his own. Again he made that striking gesture with his hand, and turning to Jim, baffled, eyes afire, he said, &ldquo;Traft, will you take a look at Molly an&rsquo; tell me if she&rsquo;s drunk, crazy or&mdash;or&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; He did not finish. There seemed to be eloquent manifestations about him that he had loved Molly Dunn. Jim dragged himself forward to obey, and there might have been a chain with iron balls attached to his legs. Yet something sustained him despite the icy clutch at his heart. Molly&rsquo;s face was wan. The big dark eyes gazed straight down into Jim&rsquo;s. He read in them love, hope, meaning. &ldquo;Mawnin&rsquo;, Jim!&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;Good morning, Molly! How&mdash;are you?&rdquo; he managed to get out. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m all right, Jim.&rdquo; &ldquo;All&mdash;right?&rdquo; he echoed. But he took little stock of her words. Jocelyn had acquired some control over her, probably lying to her about his authority in this kidnapping deal. Jim&rsquo;s whole inner being seemed to collapse with his sudden relief. Her eyes told him all he wanted to know. &ldquo;Shore. Only awful sleepy, hungry, an&rsquo; sore,&rdquo; she answered. &ldquo;Did you come with Jocelyn willingly?&rdquo; &ldquo;Reckon so, Jim. But I didn&rsquo;t have a lot of choice,&rdquo; drawled Molly, and from behind Jocelyn&rsquo;s shoulder she gave him a deep warning look that certainly could not have been lost by the others. Jocelyn laughed sardonically. &ldquo;My word goes a hell of a long way in this heah outfit.&rdquo; &ldquo;You fetched her ag&rsquo;in&rsquo; my order,&rdquo; declared Seth Haverly. &ldquo;Tell that to the chipmunks,&rdquo; retorted Seth, contemptuously, and turning to Molly: &ldquo;Get down, gurl. You shore look fagged. Fletch will fix you somethin&rsquo; to eat. An&rsquo; I&rsquo;ll make a bed for you.&rdquo; He picked up an ax and strode off toward a clump of spruce. Molly slid down, and limping to a seat under the extension she asked for a drink of water. Matty hastened to get a dipperful from a bucket and proffered it, not without kindliness. Here was a girl of the Cibeque&mdash;their own kind&mdash;placed in a queer predicament by a comparative stranger. Jim&rsquo;s gaze had followed Molly devouringly. Happening then to shift it to Jocelyn, he was struck by that worthy&rsquo;s deep-set eyes, smoldering fire. Jim read intuitively that his very life was in peril, right at the moment. There did not appear to be any reason why Jocelyn could not and would not shoot him, as he had Andy Stoneham. It stilled Jim&rsquo;s emotion. Sitting down, back to a log, he studied the ground and tried to catch at whirling thoughts. And the first one he got hold of was that Molly&rsquo;s strength and composure were due to sacrifice. She was in possession of facts unknown to him or Haverly&rsquo;s outfit. Jim believed he could unravel the plot presently. Seth returned carrying a huge bundle of spruce boughs, which he carried into the back of the cabin, where a stall-like partition hid a corner. Evidently behind this he made a bed, for he came out to get a blanket. After which task he reappeared, to approach Molly. &ldquo;You can sleep back there an&rsquo; be out of the way,&rdquo; he said. Finally Jocelyn dismounted, and uncinching the wet horse he threw saddle and blankets, unbuckled the bridle, and let him go. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll eat somethin&rsquo;,&rdquo; he said, &ldquo;an&rsquo; then we&rsquo;ll talk.&rdquo; He went in to the fireplace and sat on a box, where he could not see Molly. And when he bent ravenously to his meal Jim ventured to look at her. Deliberately she held up her left hand, and slipping the ring round to the back of her finger, where the big diamond caught the sunlight, she gave Jim a smile that seemed reward for all his agony. Yet, on second thought, there seemed, beside love and loyalty, a sadness that might be renunciation. Seth Haverly saw Molly&rsquo;s look, also the ring, and if he did not put two and two together Jim missed his guess. Moreover, Seth did not react sullenly to this revelation. Presently Molly had satisfied her hunger and thirst, and she repaired out of sight behind or in the stall at the back of the cabin. Jocelyn, about finished with his meal, watched her go, and there seemed that of a lean wolf in his gaze. A moment later he got up, and giving his belt a hitch he stalked out under the overhang of the roof, a strong figure, sure of himself and his resourcefulness. Among these men, anyway, he had no superior, and knew it. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s on your mind, Seth?&rdquo; he queried. &ldquo;Shore there&rsquo;s aplenty, Jocelyn, an&rsquo; you&rsquo;re part of it,&rdquo; replied Haverly, gruffly. &ldquo;Ahuh. Mister Traft&rsquo;s been talkin&rsquo; to you. The man who gets your ear last has you on his side.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, if I&rsquo;m not loco you stuffed both my ears prutty full.&rdquo; &ldquo;Come away from these crawfish of yours, an&rsquo; we&rsquo;ll settle this deal pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;Nope, not no more. Sam an&rsquo; the rest of my outfit air goin&rsquo; to heah every word I say.&rdquo; Jocelyn rolled and lighted a cigarette. It struck Jim that he was prepared for most anything, and had little respect for the minds and abilities of these Cibeque riders. He leaned against the log post of the cabin and smoked, his hard eyes studying every one of them, and not missing Jim. Something Ring Locke had said to Jim seemed to be justified here. Jocelyn was one of the breed of far-riding cowboys, outlawed from many ranges, perhaps, and a dangerous man. He looked it now. Seth Haverly, after a pause, went on speaking: &ldquo;I&rsquo;m sore aboot your shootin&rsquo; Andy Stoneham.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s none of your bizness,&rdquo; snapped Jocelyn. &ldquo;But it is. You&rsquo;re in my outfit now. We&rsquo;ll all be held part responsible for thet. I reckon it don&rsquo;t make a hell of a lot of difference&mdash;if we get away with this ransom deal. But your stealin&rsquo; Molly Dunn from her home&mdash;thet&rsquo;ll let us out.&rdquo; &ldquo;Haverly, I didn&rsquo;t steal her. You&rsquo;ve got ears, man. You heered her say she came willin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bah!&rdquo; That nettled Jocelyn. He had lost grip here. &ldquo;Haverly, you&rsquo;ve no call to worry on account of Molly. She&rsquo;s goin&rsquo; away with me to marry me.&rdquo; &ldquo;She is!&mdash;an&rsquo; you&rsquo;re leavin&rsquo; us heah?&rdquo; &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s your affair. I told you it&rsquo;d be a good idea to light out fer another range or hole up till the thing blowed over.&rdquo; Haverly got up, red in the face. &ldquo;Thanks. But it ain&rsquo;t so much of a compliment.... You&rsquo;ve no kick comin&rsquo;, if you get your stake out of it.&rdquo; &ldquo;I wouldn&rsquo;t of had. But your murderin&rsquo; Stoneham an&rsquo; runnin&rsquo; off with Molly&mdash;thet puts a bad complexion on the whole deal. I&rsquo;ve a mind to back out of it.&rdquo; &ldquo;You will like hell!&rdquo; asserted Jocelyn. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll go through on this deal or you&rsquo;ll have me to square up with.&rdquo; The scarcely veiled threat had its solid effect upon Haverly. A break was imminent between them&mdash;a fact Jocelyn probably saw, but it would not come at once or openly. &ldquo;Wal, we ain&rsquo;t got a lot of time to palaver,&rdquo; said Seth. &ldquo;Curly Prentiss is on Traft&rsquo;s trail, you can bet. The West Forkers air goin&rsquo; to give him a hunch. An&rsquo; wurst, Slinger Dunn has got to be considered.&rdquo; &ldquo;Dunn was beat so bad thet he&rsquo;ll be layin&rsquo; up fer a week. Mister Traft played into our hands there.&rdquo; &ldquo;Jocelyn, you cain&rsquo;t figger Slinger, an, thet&rsquo;s why I&rsquo;ve my doubts aboot you. Shore as Gawd made little apples Slinger Dunn is on your trail right this minnit.&rdquo; Jocelyn looked up at the threatening clouds rolling from the southwest. &ldquo;It&rsquo;ll rain tonight an&rsquo; then we can&rsquo;t be trailed,&rdquo; he said, confidently. &ldquo;Wal, thet&rsquo;ll only delay Slinger. He knows every hole in these hills, an&rsquo; he&rsquo;s shore a hound on a scent.&rdquo; Suddenly Jocelyn took hold of Haverly and dragged him out of earshot of the listeners. There in plain sight under a pine they argued, with Haverly growing less and less protesting. Finally they reached some kind of an agreement and returned to the cabin. &ldquo;Sam, we&rsquo;ve made the best of a bad bargain,&rdquo; announced Seth. &ldquo;The hitch was Molly. I jest don&rsquo;t care to be around if Slinger happens along. Wal, Jocelyn has agreed to take the gurl an&rsquo; go to the haid of the draw up heah, an&rsquo; stay hid in thet trapper&rsquo;s cabin at Turkey Spring. A rifle-shot will warn him to rustle.... As fer the deal&mdash;we&rsquo;ll send Boyd to Flag pronto with the letter to old Traft. Boyd&rsquo;s got the fastest hoss, an&rsquo; allowin&rsquo; half a day to get the money he can make it in lessen two days. What you say?&rdquo; &ldquo;Suits me,&rdquo; said Sam, laconically. &ldquo;Wal, it ain&rsquo;t so slick,&rdquo; added Matty. &ldquo;Tomorrer is Sunday an&rsquo; no bank is open. Suppose Boyd don&rsquo;t find old Traft at home?&rdquo; Jocelyn waved this aside as if unworthy of consideration. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s the letter thet&rsquo;ll fetch results. Blodgett or any of Traft&rsquo;s friends would dig up ten thousand.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh, will they?&rdquo; queried Matty, dubiously. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll write the letter. It&rsquo;ll be short an&rsquo; sweet. It&rsquo;ll say if thet money isn&rsquo;t heah with Boyd in twenty-four hours we&rsquo;ll kill Jim Traft an&rsquo; hang his body over his drift fence!&rdquo; Jim shared Seth Haverly&rsquo;s surprise at this act. &ldquo;Reckon it ain&rsquo;t no time fer drinkin&rsquo;, onless your nerve&rsquo;s pore,&rdquo; he said, caustically. The other wiped his mouth with the back of his hand. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re a bright fellar, Seth,&rdquo; rejoined Jocelyn, rising with the packet. &ldquo;But you ain&rsquo;t as bright as I am. My idee is to make Traft write this letter to his uncle.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shucks! I thought of thet long ago. An&rsquo; fer thet matter, he offered to do it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who offered?&rdquo; &ldquo;Traft heah. But he made it providin&rsquo; I didn&rsquo;t ask fer too much ransom, an&rsquo; thet you wasn&rsquo;t to hev no share.&rdquo; &ldquo;Oh! The hell you say!&rdquo; returned Jocelyn, with a dark glance at Jim. Then he opened the packet and laid writing-paper, pen, and ink on the woodpile. &ldquo;Fetch a box.&rdquo; One of the party procured a box from inside. &ldquo;Now, Mister Jim Traft, I reckon you&rsquo;ll put one of your accomplishments to good use,&rdquo; declared Jocelyn. As Jim rose from his seat Seth Haverly made that singular gesture. &ldquo;Wait,&rdquo; he said, in lower tone. &ldquo;Jocelyn, I&rsquo;ll agree aboot sendin&rsquo; fer the money. But I&rsquo;m daid set ag&rsquo;in&rsquo; your double-crossin&rsquo; old Traft.&rdquo; &ldquo;Talk low, you &mdash;&mdash; &mdash;&mdash; &mdash;&mdash;!&rdquo; rejoined Jocelyn, swiftly. &ldquo;She might be awake an&rsquo; heah you... . Git it off your chest quick.&rdquo; Haverly had paled so that his thin amber beard stood out in contrast. He knelt on one knee and whispered, huskily, &ldquo;I weaken on two points of your deal.&rdquo; &ldquo;An&rsquo; what&rsquo;s them?&rdquo; &ldquo;Layin&rsquo; the blame on Slinger Dunn, fer one&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Too late. I&rsquo;ve already fixed thet in West Fork. Never mind your other point.&rdquo; Jim had heard enough to divine Jocelyn&rsquo;s diabolical plot. Whether that ransom was paid or not, he realized Jocelyn meant to decorate the drift fence with his dead body, like a scarecrow hung up in a cornfield. There was one instant when it seemed Jim&rsquo;s whole internal machinery would fail. Then he rallied in desperate spirit. Right then and there he would have snatched a gun from one of these men, had he been close enough. He nursed the inspiration. If he could kill Jocelyn, that at least would save Molly. Slowly his muscles set to the terrible determination. &ldquo;You already fixed the blame&mdash;on Slinger!&rdquo; ejaculated Haverly. &ldquo;Jocelyn, you&rsquo;re orful smart, but you cain&rsquo;t work miracles,&rdquo; said Haverly. &ldquo;Gawd-Almighty!&rdquo; gasped Haverly. At this juncture Jim, whose eyes kept continually traveling back to the corner of the cabin, made the startling discovery that Molly was peeping round the corner of the stall. This added to Jim&rsquo;s uneasy sense of premonition and made him more restless than ever. He tried to move round where he could warn Molly to be careful. &ldquo;Somebody tie up this fellar,&rdquo; ordered Jocelyn, who apparently could see in all directions at once. &ldquo;He&rsquo;s snoopin&rsquo; around. Reckon he&rsquo;d like to get back there with my lady love.&rdquo; But nobody lifted hand or foot to comply with his order. Snatching up a lasso he made at Jim, and swung the loose loops viciously, cracking on Jim&rsquo;s hip. It was just possible for Jim to resist leaping at Jocelyn, who he knew would shoot him upon the slightest excuse. &ldquo;Turn round an&rsquo; lean ag&rsquo;in&rsquo; the post,&rdquo; he commanded. &ldquo;An&rsquo; put your hands behind you.&rdquo; With that he proceeded to bind Jim&rsquo;s hands and feet, and tied the last in intricate knots. &ldquo;It ain&rsquo;t my rope, an&rsquo; it&rsquo;ll have to be cut.&rdquo; Then he kicked Jim&rsquo;s feet from under him, letting him down. It so happened that Jim slid round in falling, and there he sat, back to the post, helpless and so devoured by wrath that he felt he was sweating blood. Whereupon Jocelyn drew his bottle from his hip pocket and took a drink. &ldquo;To celebrate the day, Mister Traft,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;An&rsquo; when I drink the rest of this I&rsquo;ll set the bottle on your head an&rsquo; treat you to some real shootin&rsquo;.&rdquo; Repairing to the box, he sat down again before the writing-materials. &ldquo;Hell! I was goin&rsquo; to make him write the letter, an&rsquo; now I&rsquo;ve tied him up.... Wal, I&rsquo;ll write it myself.&rdquo; And he did, laboring over the task like a schoolboy. Then he read it over with evident satisfaction. Seth Haverly held put a lean hand. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m askin&rsquo; to see what you writ.&rdquo; Imperturbably Jocelyn sealed the letter. &ldquo;Reckon you couldn&rsquo;t read it, if I let you,&rdquo; said Jocelyn. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re demandin&rsquo; more&rsquo;n we agreed on,&rdquo; shouted Haverly, beside himself with rage. &ldquo;Yes, an&rsquo; he&rsquo;ll pay all in vain,&rdquo; snarled Haverly, white to the lips. Jocelyn gave little heed to him, and turning to Flick he presented the letter. &ldquo;Ride like hell, now. An&rsquo; get this in Traft&rsquo;s hands today, or Blodgett&rsquo;s. An&rsquo; if they ain&rsquo;t handy go to Tobin at the bank. Anyone of them will pile out the greenbacks. An&rsquo; you ride back, pronto.&rdquo; &ldquo;I ain&rsquo;t stuck on the job,&rdquo; declared Flick, brusquely, rising to his feet. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s safer than waitin&rsquo; here, you can gamble.... An&rsquo; I reckon I needn&rsquo;t tell you what I&rsquo;ll do to you&mdash;if you don&rsquo;t come back.&rdquo; Flick threw the letter in Jocelyn&rsquo;s face. &ldquo;Go to hell! You cain&rsquo;t make me do your dirty job an&rsquo; insult me to boot.&rdquo; But Seth and Matty quickly intervened, one to rescue the letter and the other to lead Flick aside. Between them they persuaded him. &ldquo;All right, I&rsquo;ll go an&rsquo; do my damndest,&rdquo; said Flick. &ldquo;But I&rsquo;m tellin&rsquo; you straight, Jocelyn has busted the Cibeque.&rdquo; With that he picked up his saddle, blanket, and bridle, and disappeared round the corner of the cabin. Jocelyn stood up, to tilt his bottle and drain the contents. He was about to fling the empty bottle aside when he remembered something. Stalking over to Jim, he pulled him up to an erect sitting posture, rapped his head hard against the post and set the bottle upon it. &ldquo;There!&rdquo; he exclaimed, with elation, and drawing his gun he flipped it in the air, catching it by the handle with great dexterity. &ldquo;Anybody want to bet me?&rdquo; No one answered. Seth Haverly looked a protest he knew it would be useless to voice. Jocelyn had dominated the group. He had alienated them, but had them under his thumb. Jocelyn stepped off ten long paces and turned, his face singularly bright, to level the gun. Jim saw that the hammer of the Colt was up. Next instant he looked into the small black tube-like barrel of the quivering weapon. All faculties but sight seemed to be in abeyance. And that deadly little hole suddenly belched fire. He felt a slight jar, just before the gun boomed. Particles of shattered glass fell on his head and shoulders. &ldquo;Ha! How&rsquo;s thet, Seth? Shot the neck off first crack!&rdquo; cried the grinning devil. &ldquo;Wal, it&rsquo;s damn bad, if you ask me,&rdquo; growled Haverly. &ldquo;Thet gun of yourn can be heerd fer miles.&rdquo; When Jocelyn leveled it again Jim&rsquo;s consciousness seemed undamped. Jocelyn was aiming a little lower this time. Back of the gun gleamed the eyes of a cat playing with a mouse. Then came a rush and patter of moccasined feet. Molly flashed out. She struck up the gun so violently that it went off harmlessly in the air. &ldquo;Hey, gurl! You might get hurt,&rdquo; protested Jocelyn, as if he had been interrupted at a favorite sport. &ldquo;Hack Jocelyn, put away that gun,&rdquo; demanded Molly, furiously. &ldquo;Say, little lady, you cut quite a figger in my eyes, but you ain&rsquo;t givin&rsquo; orders yet,&rdquo; drawled Jocelyn. But a keen person could have observed that here he was on uncertain ground. &ldquo;Put it away&mdash;or I&rsquo;ll&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; Seth Haverly, his brother, and their three comrades showed undisguised satisfaction at the turn of affairs. Seth, in fact, exhibited more. Molly Dunn, whatever her relation to this man or his villainy, was certainly not afraid of him. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m liable to do anythin&rsquo;, Hack Jocelyn,&rdquo; she said, and she looked it. Seth stepped out unsteadily. &ldquo;Molly, you better stay back. Honest, gurl, Hack was only foolin&rsquo;. He ain&rsquo;t really goin&rsquo; to hurt Traft.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shet up,&rdquo; ordered Jocelyn, sheathing his gun. &ldquo;Molly, you&rsquo;re damn pretty when you&rsquo;re riled, but you&rsquo;re goin&rsquo; too far. Shet your mouth now, or I&rsquo;ll slap it good an&rsquo; hard.&rdquo; Jim, loving Molly so deeply, and feeling her emotion so tremendously, had an intuition of a change in her that was not wholly explained by her fears for him. She had heard intimations not intended for her. She had realized Jocelyn&rsquo;s perfidy&mdash;that if murder was to be averted she would have to do it. This, of course, could have roused Molly&rsquo;s wild spirit to any extreme. Still, there seemed to be something else. Was Molly talking to gain time? Her suppressed air of suspense, her furtive glances, were slight indeed, compared with stronger expressions, yet they did not escape Jim. &ldquo;Wal, I&rsquo;ll touch you aplenty an&rsquo; pronto,&rdquo; replied Jocelyn. And that fetched Jim&rsquo;s gaze back to the leader in this woodland drama. He was in time to note Jocelyn, as he espied the ring on Molly&rsquo;s left hand. &ldquo;Where&rsquo;d you get thet ring?&rdquo; demanded Jocelyn, suddenly hot. &ldquo;On your third finger! An&rsquo; a big sparkler!&rdquo; &ldquo;None of your mix, Hack Jocelyn,&rdquo; retorted Molly, and she put her hands behind her back. It was then that Jim imagined he saw her look over Jocelyn or behind him. Jim peered across the grassy open to the wooded slope. On that side the pines were scattered, as by an expert landscape gardener who wanted a beautiful open forest. And on the instant Jim sighted the flash of a dark form vanishing behind a tree trunk. It resembled what he imagined an Indian would look like, and all his being responded in a concerted shock. Had Molly seen it? &ldquo;You two-faced hussy!&rdquo; ejaculated Jocelyn, dark with jealous wrath. &ldquo;Stick out your hand.... Lemme see.&rdquo; Molly coolly brought her hand round and extended it, fingers spread, upper side exposed, and she preened it before the exasperated cowboy. She might have been actuated by many motives, but one of them assuredly was a woman&rsquo;s desire to inflict pain. &ldquo;Reckoned you was hidin&rsquo; thet from me,&rdquo; declared Jocelyn, tragically. &ldquo;You just had that idea. Why should I hide it from you&mdash;or anybody else? ... It&rsquo;s my engagement ring.&rdquo; &ldquo;Engagement!&rdquo; echoed Jocelyn. &ldquo;Shore.... An&rsquo; I may as well announce it.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wimmen shore air hell,&rdquo; muttered Jocelyn, more to himself, as if the puzzle of Molly Dunn&rsquo;s dual nature was beyond him. He seemed to be studying her as a species new to him, as fascinating as mysterious. And his doubt grew. Jim took advantage of the moment to glance across the open to the slope, up and down, and to each side. He was about to believe he had been deceived, when far down to the left, at the edge of the forest, he saw something move&mdash;a shadow of a branch or the tail of a horse or&mdash;&mdash; &ldquo;Molly, who gave you thet ring?&rdquo; went on Jocelyn, coming to himself. &ldquo;Mister James Traft,&rdquo; replied Molly, blushing deeply. Even though she was playing a woman&rsquo;s wit against this jealous lover, she could not conceal her maidenly confusion, her pride and joy. Jocelyn bellowed like a bull. &ldquo;Since when? How long you bin engaged to this &mdash;&mdash; tenderfoot?&rdquo; Perhaps nothing else could have shaken Jocelyn as this mortal blow to his vanity. Perhaps Molly was working on that very weakness. If so, she was taking desperate chances and she knew it. A transformation took place in Jocelyn, not a swift one, but a gradual breaking up of illusive force, of the one vital vulnerable link in his personality, and it left him a cold, hard fiend. As he whipped up his gun Molly stepped in front of him, to shield Jim. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll have to kill me first,&rdquo; she declared, resolutely. Certain it was, however, that she knew he would not shoot her. Jocelyn was in no hurry now. He had a gun in his hand. He would torture as well as slay. Seth Haverly, however, took him as seriously menacing Molly. &ldquo;My Gawd, Jocelyn&mdash;put thet down!&rdquo; &ldquo;Nope. I feel too much at home with my gun stickin&rsquo; out in front.&rdquo; Molly seemed to Jim to be at the end of her rope. Slowly Jocelyn backed her toward where Jim sat, his head against the post, still holding the broken whisky bottle. &ldquo;Oh, Seth Haverly, but you&rsquo;re a rotten coward!&rdquo; cried Molly. &ldquo;You leave it for a girl to face this devil.&rdquo; &ldquo;But, Molly, devil or not, I&rsquo;m in a deal with him,&rdquo; expostulated Seth, as if stung. &ldquo;An&rsquo; it ain&rsquo;t no call fer me to risk my hide.&rdquo; &ldquo;Deal, yes&mdash;a dirtier deal than you know. He&rsquo;s&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly Dunn,&rdquo; cut in Jocelyn, &ldquo;shet your jaw&mdash;or it&rsquo;ll be too late.&rdquo; &ldquo;Too late! Why, you poison-tongued snake&mdash;do you think I&rsquo;d believe you again? ... You can kill me an&rsquo; Jim&mdash;an&rsquo; this yellow bunch, for all I care. But, by God! I&rsquo;m givin&rsquo; you away.&rdquo; Jocelyn made a fierce reach for her. But he did not quite lose himself in the passion of the moment. He had to stand clear, to be free, to watch the Haverlys. So he dared not close in with her. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m warnin&rsquo; you onct more.&rdquo; Molly must have kept her burning eyes on him, at bay, while she denounced him to the Cibeque. &ldquo;You pop-eyed cat,&rdquo; yelled Jocelyn, stridently. &ldquo;Who wants you to give in?&mdash;I&rsquo;ll rope you like I would a mean hawse!&rdquo; Suddenly the gun banged. Jim felt the bottle blown off his head. Molly screamed. &ldquo;Right under your arm, Molly,&rdquo; said Jocelyn. &ldquo;How&rsquo;s thet fer shootin&rsquo;?&rdquo; And he began to step from one side to the other, the gun extended about even with his hip. Molly did not back away from his formidable advance. She blocked his every move, interposing her body between the gun and Jim. Then like a cat she pounced upon Jocelyn&rsquo;s hand, shoving it out of line. Bang! The bullet scattered dust and gravel over Jim. Both her hands and then her teeth were locked on Jocelyn&rsquo;s wrist. &ldquo;Leggo!&rdquo; he yelled, lustily, wrestling to get free. But he could not free himself. With left hand he lifted Molly by her hair. He swung her clear of the ground, her weight nothing to his powerful arms. But he could not shake her grip. Blood began to drip from his wrist. &ldquo;Bite, &mdash;&mdash;!&rdquo; he cursed. And lifting her he tried to get the gun on Jim. But as he pulled the trigger she swung desperately, spoiling his aim. The gun roared and a bullet tore splinters out of the post beside Jim&rsquo;s ear, and whirred away into the forest. The recoil of the heavy Colt loosened Molly&rsquo;s teeth, but not her hands. She screeched like a wild creature. Before Jocelyn could take advantage of this, she again buried her teeth in his wrist. He swung her aside, but she alighted on her feet. He fought her to and fro, until they entered the cabin. Haverly and his men were caught in a trap. Like rats they ran to dodge behind the stall, yet to peep out at this extraordinary encounter. Jocelyn took another snap shot at Jim, narrowly missing him. The shock of this explosion, right at her ear, appeared to weaken Molly, for she let go with her teeth, and her weight sagged on Jocelyn&rsquo;s arm. He shifted his left hand from her hair to her neck, where his long fingers shut like a vise. Yet on the instant he could not get loose. His malignant cry, however, hoarse and exultant, attested to the victory he saw. Molly could no longer move. He was lifting her, and the gun. Jim&rsquo;s distended sight caught a shadow of something passing him. He could not move, even his eyes, but out of the tail of his right he saw a buckskin-clad figure that had appeared as if by magic. Jocelyn jerked up his head and a fleeting consternation showed on his convulsed face. He let go of Molly&rsquo;s neck. And as her hands slipped loose she slid down. Like a whipcord Jocelyn&rsquo;s gun leaped. But as it leaped Dunn&rsquo;s crashed. Jocelyn appeared to be arrested. Then shot through the heart, he staggered forward, with an awful look that set blankly, and plunged step by halting step, to fall clear outside of the cabin. &ldquo;&rsquo;Pears like I didn&rsquo;t get heah none too soon,&rdquo; he drawled. &ldquo;Slinger! Just&mdash;in time!&rdquo; gasped Jim. Molly sat up dazedly, her hair disordered, blood on her chin and nerveless hands. &ldquo;Oh!&mdash;Oh!&mdash;Oh!&rdquo; she moaned. &ldquo;Air you hurted, Molly?&rdquo; asked Dunn. &ldquo;Wal, I reckon, onless he wears a big watch in his breast pocket.&rdquo; Molly got shakily to her feet and ran unevenly to Jim, where she fell, still game, still proof against the collapse that had taken her strength. &ldquo;Cut&mdash;this&mdash;rope,&rdquo; she whispered, huskily, plucking at Jim&rsquo;s bonds. Dunn, with a wary glance at the back of the cabin, dropped a knife in front of Molly. &ldquo;Jim&mdash;did he&mdash;hit you?&rdquo; she asked, fearfully, as she freed his hands. &ldquo;No. But I sure know what a bullet sounds like.... Let me have the knife.... Molly!&rdquo; &ldquo;I cain&rsquo;t heah you,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;His gun deafened me.&rdquo; Jim severed the knotted rope and got up, lifting Molly with him, which action assured her that he was uninjured. &ldquo;Oh&mdash;thank heaven!&rdquo; She cried, sinking against him. &ldquo;Jim! Jim! I thought&mdash;he&rsquo;d hit you.&rdquo; Jim held her tight, and probably no other moment of his life could ever equal that one. Following it he became aware of Dunn sheathing his gun. &ldquo;Come out heah!&rdquo; he called, and his voice was piercing enough to penetrate more than a board stall. Seth Haverly came out first, livid of face, and Matty followed, visibly shaken, but unafraid. &ldquo;Slinger, he had us buffaloed,&rdquo; explained Seth. &ldquo;Who else back there?&rdquo; Sam slouched out, then Hart Merriwell, and lastly Fletch. &ldquo;Where&rsquo;s Boyd?&rdquo; &ldquo;Jocelyn sent him with a letter to old Jim Traft.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh.&rdquo; Then Dunn turned to his sister. &ldquo;Molly, you was shore fightin&rsquo; thet skunk, an&rsquo; I needn&rsquo;t ask if he got the best of you. But Seth, heah, an&rsquo; Sam&mdash;did they stand around an&rsquo; let thet hombre bulldog you?&rdquo; The moment was critical and Molly reacted to it as might have been expected from Slinger Dunn&rsquo;s sister. If Jim had had the impulse to check her he suppressed it. &ldquo;Thet squares you with me, Jim Traft,&rdquo; replied Slinger, gruffly. &ldquo;Take her away from the cabin.... An&rsquo; wash the blood offen her.&rdquo; Jim was not loath to lead Molly away, half supporting her in his arm. He lifted her across the brook, surprised and pleased to find she was a pretty heavy little chunk. He led her on, across the open grassy flat and up the first gentle slope to a pine tree, where a fragrant brown matt, and shade, invited a stop. &ldquo;This is far enough,&rdquo; he said, letting her down. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll run back&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t leave me. There&rsquo;ll be a fight,&rdquo; she cried, clinging to him. &ldquo;But only to the brook to wet my scarf.... You&rsquo;re all bloody.&rdquo; &ldquo;Then hurry.&rdquo; Jim made short work of the trip down to the brook, and while soaking his silken scarf he heard a loud angry protest of Haverly in the cabin, and the cold ring of Slinger&rsquo;s voice. He ran back to Molly. She was spitting like an angry cat. &ldquo;I bit him, Jim, I bit him! I&rsquo;d have chewed him to pieces... . But now it&rsquo;s over I&rsquo;m sick&mdash;sick with the taste an&rsquo; smell an&rsquo; sight of his blood.&rdquo; And as she spat out she did look sick. &ldquo;Never mind, darling. Your biting saved my life.... My God! how wonderful you were&mdash;how I love you!&rdquo; And he kissed her passionately, stained lips and chin and hands. Evidently this treatment effectually checked her nausea. &ldquo;Oh&mdash;Jim&mdash;somebody&rsquo;ll see,&rdquo; she whispered. &ldquo;Who cares?&mdash;But let me scrub you good,&rdquo; laughed Jim, and he did scrub her mouth inside and outside, and nearly washed the skin off her chin, and likewise the little strong brown hands. &ldquo;There goes one. Slinger&rsquo;s let him off. Look!&rdquo; said Molly. &ldquo;That&rsquo;s Matty,&rdquo; replied Jim, recognizing the tall member of Seth&rsquo;s outfit. He was in a hurry, and snatching up a saddle and bridle he strode off up the park, looking back over his shoulder. &ldquo;He didn&rsquo;t seem a bad sort of fellow.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;m glad. Slinger is hell when he&rsquo;s&mdash;like you saw him.... Oh, but wasn&rsquo;t Hack Jocelyn the dastardliest&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Sweetheart, don&rsquo;t think of him,&rdquo; entreated Jim. &ldquo;But he&rsquo;s daid! An&rsquo; Slinger killed him&mdash;all on my account.&rdquo; &ldquo;No, not all. Some on mine, and some on his own.... Honey, did you really mean you&rsquo;d accepted me&mdash;when you threw it up to Jocelyn&mdash;that we were engaged?&rdquo; &ldquo;Funny tenderfoot from Missourie you are&mdash;I guess not,&rdquo; she declared. &ldquo;You&rsquo;re almost another Curly Prentiss.&rdquo; &ldquo;Thanks. You couldn&rsquo;t pay me a compliment that&rsquo;d please me more.&rdquo; &ldquo;Jim, I&rsquo;d have liked Curly if he hadn&rsquo;t the cowboys&rsquo; weakness,&rdquo; said Molly, thoughtfully. &ldquo;And what&rsquo;s that?&rdquo; &ldquo;Bein&rsquo; too gay with a girl&mdash;the very first thing&mdash;all same like an Easterner I met once.&rdquo; &ldquo;I reckon it was in Flag.... Oh&mdash;Jim&mdash;&rdquo; She was surrendering to his arm, when suddenly she started up. &ldquo;Look! Another leavin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s the cook. Fletch, they called him,&rdquo; said Jim, watching the man, who lost no time in imitating Matty&rsquo;s example in making tracks from the cabin, burdened by his saddle. &ldquo;Hart Merriwell left, besides Seth an&rsquo; Sam. Slinger will let Hart go. Now you watch.&rdquo; Presently Jim espied Merriwell come out, in no great hurry, and instead of striding away he slipped round to the back of the cabin and peered through a chink. &ldquo;Well, there must be something up,&rdquo; declared Jim anxiously. &ldquo;Listen! That&rsquo;s Slinger shoutin&rsquo;,&rdquo; rejoined Molly. Jim did distinguish Slinger&rsquo;s voice, with its high ringing note, but only the sound carried so far. What meaning he could attach to the harangue he had to supply himself. But as Slinger kept on it was no great task to imagine the storm of violent backwoods profanity with which he was berating the brothers who had betrayed him. &ldquo;Just like I heahed once at a dance,&rdquo; said Molly, with a sigh. &ldquo;There!&rdquo; She jerked spasmodically at the crack of a gun, and clapping her hands over her ears she sagged against Jim. &ldquo;Gosh!&mdash;That didn&rsquo;t sound like Slinger&rsquo;s gun!&rdquo; ejaculated Jim, and all his being seemed suspended upon his hearing. Bang! &ldquo;That was Slinger&rsquo;s,&rdquo; he went on, huskily, tightening an arm round Molly. Shots followed, three or four, so swiftly as to be hardly separated. Then again came the heavy boom and a volley of lighter reports. There was a pause that might have been a suspension of hostilities. Jim dared believe it was over. Then followed loud reports, a heavy one&mdash;more of the sharp rifle shots&mdash;another heavy, and quickly the boom. Silence! Smoke drifted out of the cabin, showing blue against the green pines. Hart Merriwell, who had evidently run off, appeared coming slowly back, halting, waiting, then approaching again. But there were no more shots. &ldquo;All&mdash;daid!&rdquo; whispered Molly, lifting her head. She must have been able to hear the shots even with her ears covered. &ldquo;I&mdash;I&rsquo;m afraid so,&rdquo; replied Jim, huskily. &ldquo;I see Merriwell coming back.... There. He&rsquo;s going in.... Molly, I&rsquo;ll go, too.&rdquo; &ldquo;That&rsquo;s the end&mdash;of the Cibeque,&rdquo; she murmured. &ldquo;Poor Arch! ... He wasn&rsquo;t all bad.&rdquo; &ldquo;Stay here, Molly,&rdquo; admonished Jim. &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll come back&mdash;if&mdash;if&mdash;&rdquo; He squeezed her hand, and getting up he ran down the slope, leaped the brook, and soon reached the cabin. As he peeped round the post, against which he had sat not many minutes past, his hand came in contact with the place splintered by Jocelyn&rsquo;s bullet. The interior of the cabin was still smoky. Jim saw one of the men huddled in a heap. Then on the other side he espied Merriwell kneeling beside Slinger, whom he had propped up. &ldquo;Is he&mdash;alive yet?&rdquo; called Jim, breathlessly, and he ran in. &ldquo;Hullo! Shore, Slinger&rsquo;s alive, but shot all up,&rdquo; replied Merriwell. &ldquo;Help me carry him out of this smoke.&rdquo; They lifted the bleeding, broken body and carried it out to the shady side, where they laid it on a blanket. While Jim lifted Slinger&rsquo;s head Merriwell placed a saddle under it. &ldquo;Reckon you&rsquo;ll&mdash;cash in, Slinger, ole pard,&rdquo; he said, gulping. &ldquo;Anythin&rsquo; I can do?&rdquo; &ldquo;Where&rsquo;s Molly?&rdquo; asked Dunn, faintly. &ldquo;She&rsquo;s across the brook. I&rsquo;ll call her,&rdquo; replied Jim, and he got up to go round the cabin. Molly was coming. She had seen them carry her brother out. Jim went out to meet her. &ldquo;Molly, he&rsquo;s alive. He asked for you.&rdquo; He had to run to keep up to her. &ldquo;Oh, Arch!&rdquo; burst out Molly, dropping on her knees. &ldquo;Are you&mdash;bad hurt?&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Molly, I ain&rsquo;t hurt atall&mdash;but I reckon&mdash;I&rsquo;m done fer,&rdquo; he said, feebly. &ldquo;Where there&rsquo;s life there&rsquo;s hope,&rdquo; interposed Jim, as he too knelt. &ldquo;Oh, cain&rsquo;t we do anythin&rsquo;?&rdquo; cried Molly. &ldquo;You don&rsquo;t happen&mdash;to have some&mdash;whisky?&rdquo; replied Slinger. &ldquo;There&rsquo;s some here,&rdquo; said Jim, leaping up. &ldquo;Where did I see it? ... Jocelyn had it.&rdquo; Jim ran to the saddle-bags, and procuring the flask he rushed back. Dunn took a stiff drink. &ldquo;Wal, Jim Traft, you&rsquo;re lucky&mdash;thet you&rsquo;re not layin&rsquo; around heah full of bullet holes,&rdquo; he said, in stronger voice, not devoid of humor. &ldquo;I guess yes,&rdquo; said Jim, fervently. &ldquo;I was fer killin&rsquo; you myself&mdash;till Molly made me&mdash;a promise.&rdquo; &ldquo;What promise?&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly, air you goin&rsquo; to keep it?&rdquo; &ldquo;Yes, Arch,&rdquo; replied Molly, as she bent over him, her bright tears falling. &ldquo;Good! Reckon thet&rsquo;ll be aboot all,&rdquo; said Slinger, with satisfaction. &ldquo;Molly dear, tell me the promise,&rdquo; rejoined Jim, earnestly. &ldquo;Jim, he was shore set on killin&rsquo; you. I begged him not to. An&rsquo; he said he&rsquo;d let you off&mdash;if I&mdash;I would make you ask me again&mdash;to marry you&mdash;an&rsquo; take you up.... So I promised.&rdquo; Jim took Dunn&rsquo;s limp hand. &ldquo;Slinger, she&rsquo;d never have had to make me. I&rsquo;d kept asking till doomsday.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Then you&rsquo;re powerful fond of my kid sister.... Wal, I cain&rsquo;t thank Gawd fer much&mdash;but I do fer thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Lord! he&rsquo;s shot bad all over,&rdquo; declared Merriwell. &ldquo;Jim, I tell you bullet holes are nothin&rsquo; to Slinger Dunn. He&rsquo;s not bleedin&rsquo; at the lungs, because he&rsquo;d be spittin&rsquo; blood. This cut on his haid isn&rsquo;t bad. His left arm is broke. There&rsquo;s a hole in his right shoulder.... Heah, low down is the bad one.... Oh, my Heavens!&mdash;But let&rsquo;s tie it tight.&rdquo; The practical Molly prompted the stricken men to get busy and do what was possible for the wounded one. Jim believed the gun-shot in the abdomen would have killed any ordinary man. It had gone clear through. They padded that and bound it up. He had another dangerous wound in the hip, which, according to Merriwell, like the hole in his abdomen, had been made with a rifle. While they washed and dressed his wounds, as best they could do it with limited means, Slinger talked. &ldquo;Sam throwed fust on me, when I was keepin&rsquo; two eyes on Seth. An&rsquo; he got me thet crack in the belly. It keeled me over, or the fight&rsquo;d been short an&rsquo; sweet.... I nailed Sam. Then Seth showed his true color at last. He dove fer the stall an&rsquo; began shootin&rsquo; from behind. He had a Colt an&rsquo; a forty-four Winchester. An&rsquo; he shore pumped lead into me.... I sent a couple through the stall. Hit him, fer he cussed hard. But he come back at me. Then I flopped over along the wall, where he couldn&rsquo;t see me onless he come out. He&rsquo;d used up all his shells fer the Colt. Anyway, he didn&rsquo;t take risk to load, an&rsquo; stuck to the rifle. Reckon thet beat him. I got to the corner by the stall&mdash;leaned over quick&mdash;an&rsquo; bored him&mdash;when he was tryin&rsquo; to shift the rifle round.&rdquo; Beyond a slight huskiness Dunn&rsquo;s voice did not depart radically from a tone of ordinary conversation. There was no trace of emotion, unless the mere fact of lengthy recital of the fray was one. He had a remarkable vitality. Jim knew that in case of injury, especially from gun-shots, the next perilous thing to the wound itself was the shock to the consciousness. This probably had not occurred in Dunn at all. &ldquo;Now, Merriwell, what more is to be done?&rdquo; queried Jim. &ldquo;I reckon nuthin&rsquo;, onless he wants us to pray. How aboot it, Slinger?&rdquo; &ldquo;Nope,&rdquo; was the laconic reply. He lay still, with closed eyes, a limp and ghastly sight. Molly sat beside him, supporting his head. &ldquo;I heah hosses,&rdquo; suddenly spoke up Dunn, opening his eyes. They all listened, and Jim shook his head. &ldquo;You ain&rsquo;t close enough to the ground. I shore heah hosses,&rdquo; added Dunn. Merriwell got up to walk out away from the cabin. &ldquo;Bunch of cowboys comin&rsquo;,&rdquo; he said, excitedly. &ldquo;Reckon I&rsquo;d better make myself scarce.&rdquo; Jim joined him. &ldquo;No need to run, Merriwell. That&rsquo;s Curly Prentiss and some of my outfit.&rdquo; &ldquo;By gum! An&rsquo; they&rsquo;ve ketched Matty an&rsquo; Fletch,&rdquo; ejaculated the other. So it appeared to be, and presently Jim made certain of it. Curly rode down the slope, followed by Bud and Lonestar and Jackson Way, who had in tow the late fleeing members of the Cibeque. Curly threw his bridle and made one sweep of his long leg and slid to the ground. A blue flash of keen eyes took in the situation. &ldquo;Howdy, boss! Mawnin&rsquo;, Miss Molly!&rdquo; he said, and lifted his sombrero from his bright hair. Then he bent his gaze upon the prostrate Dunn. &ldquo;Prentiss, you&rsquo;re a little late in the day,&rdquo; said Dunn. &ldquo;But I&rsquo;m recommendin&rsquo; you let Matty an&rsquo; Fletch go. I did.... It was Jocelyn an&rsquo; Seth an&rsquo; Sam. Take a look in the cabin.&rdquo; Curly did as he was bidden, returning promptly, a queer cold look in his eyes. &ldquo;Wal, Slinger, it shore was a good job. But I&rsquo;m sorry they got you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, I ain&rsquo;t cashed yet, an&rsquo; if I do it shore won&rsquo;t be owin&rsquo; to Seth or Jocelyn. Sam&rsquo;s the one who bored me. I wasn&rsquo;t lookin&rsquo; fer it from him.&rdquo; Bud Chalfack and the cowboys came riding down, to halt before the group. &ldquo;Dog-gone, Jim, if it ain&rsquo;t fine to see you on yore feet,&rdquo; said Bud, heartily. &ldquo;How do, Molly! ... Somebody tell me what&rsquo;s come off.&rdquo; Whereupon Jim briefly related the circumstances leading up to the stop at this cabin, the wrangle over the conditions of the ransom, the blackguard conduct of Jocelyn, and the several fights that succeeded. If Jim emphasized anything it was the wit and courage and ferocity of Molly. &ldquo;Dog-gone me!&rdquo; burst out Bud, with worshipful eyes on the girl. &ldquo;Molly, I always knowed you was the sweetest an&rsquo; wonderfulest little devil in Arizonie, but now I jest haf to take off my hat to you.&rdquo; And he did it, quite gallantly. Curly&rsquo;s encomium was directed at Jim. &ldquo;You lucky son-of-a-gun! To come out heah an&rsquo; steal her from us!&rdquo; Slinger had missed nothing of all this. He seemed not only incredibly tenacious of life, but singularly possessed of receptive faculties. &ldquo;Wal, fellars, she&rsquo;s Molly Dunn of the Cibeque,&rdquo; he drawled. Molly took exception to compliments on the moment. &ldquo;You heartless cowpunchers! Heah&rsquo;s daid men all aboot an&rsquo; Slinger dyin&rsquo;&mdash;yet you talk an&rsquo; make eyes at me. Jim, you&rsquo;re just as bad. Curly, haven&rsquo;t you got some sense?&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly, I&rsquo;m shore beggin&rsquo; your pardon,&rdquo; replied Curly, contritely, as he threw off his gloves and knelt by Slinger. &ldquo;Tell me jest where he&rsquo;s shot.&rdquo; Molly and Merriwell together gave him the desired information. Curly got up, decisive though grave. &ldquo;Mebbe he&rsquo;s-a chance. You cain&rsquo;t kill some fellars.... Jack, ride to Flag an&rsquo; fetch back the doctor. He can drive a buckboard clear to Cottonwood. You lead an extra saddlehoss, an&rsquo; then come rustlin&rsquo; across country.... Holliday, you an&rsquo; Bud go back to camp an&rsquo; pack some of our outfit over heah. Fetch the boss&rsquo;s pack an&rsquo; don&rsquo;t fergit everythin&rsquo; to make Miss Molly comfortable.... Boss, do you want to hold these Cibeque men heah? I reckon if we let them go they&rsquo;ll haid Flick off with thet ransom money. An&rsquo; we shore don&rsquo;t want to lose thet.&rdquo; &ldquo;Hold them, then, till Flick comes back,&rdquo; replied Jim. &ldquo;You and I can take turns on guard.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, Traft,&rdquo; spoke up Matty, &ldquo;if you&rsquo;d like to know, we&rsquo;re so darn glad the way things hev come off thet we won&rsquo;t need watchin&rsquo;.&rdquo; Night fell, and the wind moaned the threatening storm. Coyotes ranged the park and yelped a staccato whining protest at the camp fire. Weird flickering shadows played on the cabin and the improvised tent. Molly sat close to her brother, leaning against the wall, silent and watchful. The only way she could ascertain that Dunn still lived was by touch, and when his pulse grew imperceptible and his hands cold she laid her head on his breast to listen to his heart. Jim stayed up with her, and seldom left her, except to replenish the fire and take a look at the inside of the cabin, where the prisoners slept. Curly lay across the opening, and every time Jim stepped near, though ever so noiselessly, he would awaken. The dead men had been moved to a corner of the cabin and covered with canvas. Sometimes Jim paced up and down, finding action helpful to the surcharged condition of his mind. He never closed an eye, and seldom indeed was there an interval of any length when he was not watching Molly. In the dead of night there in that lonely forest he came to full appreciation of her sterling worth and a deep respect for the primitive life that had developed her. Many things considered by civilized people as necessary were merely superficial. Molly had the great qualities, virtue, courage, and love. When he would approach her, as he did often, and sink down on one knee to peer at the pale Slinger, Molly would take hold of him. By the firelight he could see the big eyes, black as night, rest upon him with a glance that made him wonder how life had come to reward him so beautifully. Towards dawn the wind ceased and despite the overhanging clouds the air chilled until it grew bitter cold. Jim wrapped his coat round Molly and laid his saddle blanket over Slinger. He kept the fire going, and stood close to it, burning his palms, turning from front to back. It grew very black and still, the formidable hour before dawn when even the life of the forest seemed at lowest ebb. Slinger would go soon, Jim thought. Molly called him. &ldquo;He whispered somethin&rsquo; just now,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;I think he called for Seth&rsquo;s sister. He was sweet on her. ... She&rsquo;ll hate even his memory now.&rdquo; Jim sat with her then, silently awaiting the end, holding her against him. It seemed the most melancholy and poignant of all the long night hours. How welcome the first gray streaks! Something ghastly and ghostly stole away into the forest. A fine misty rain began to fall. Daylight at last with Slinger Dunn still not dead! Curly appeared around the corner of the cabin, yawning, and stretching his lithe length. &ldquo;Mawnin&rsquo;, you babes in the woods,&rdquo; he said, and dropped beside them, to lean over Dunn. &ldquo;Wal, Slinger, old man, I reckoned you&rsquo;d be long where the blue flags an&rsquo; the cornflowers blow.&rdquo; &ldquo;Curly, not yet,&rdquo; replied Jim. &ldquo;All night we&rsquo;ve been expecting him to die. And every moment the last hour.&rdquo; &ldquo;It takes so long,&rdquo; said Molly, sadly. Curly placed his ear over Dunn&rsquo;s heart and listened. &ldquo;Wal, you both got the willies,&rdquo; he declared, as he rose with a glad smile. &ldquo;He ain&rsquo;t goin&rsquo; to croak.&rdquo; &ldquo;What!&rdquo; ejaculated Jim. &ldquo;Oh, Curly!&rdquo; cried Molly, in an agony of hope. &ldquo;Shore he&rsquo;ll live. I ain&rsquo;t no doctor, but I&rsquo;ve seen a heap of fellars pass out. An&rsquo; Slinger don&rsquo;t show no earmarks of thet this mawnin&rsquo;.&rdquo; Molly&rsquo;s little remaining strength eked out here and she collapsed in Jim&rsquo;s arms. He let her gently down on the bed of spruce and covered her over. &ldquo;Go to sleep, honey,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Boss, it&rsquo;s goin&rsquo; to rain,&rdquo; announced Curly, when indeed it was raining already. &ldquo;Let&rsquo;s rig up another tarp heah fer Slinger.... Look! Molly asleep all in a jiffy. Poor kid! ... Jim, I don&rsquo;t see how I kin give her up to you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t you? Well, please be reasonable about it,&rdquo; replied Jim, dryly. They stretched a tarpaulin over the makeshift canvas shelter. &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;ll do fer a spell. Mebbe we kin move him into the cabin after Doc Shields looks him over.... But say, boss, how aboot them stiffs in there?&rdquo; &ldquo;They&rsquo;ll have to be buried, of course.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, the Haverlys ought to have decent burial, I s&rsquo;pose, but thet Hack Jocelyn, &mdash;&mdash;, he ought to be throwed to the coyotes.&rdquo; Jim followed Curly inside. He stripped the scant covering off the bodies. It was not a pretty spectacle. Jim recoiled. But Curly viewed it differently. &ldquo;Daid center on thet left vest pocket,&rdquo; he muttered, bending over Jocelyn. &ldquo;Slinger&rsquo;s fancy shot, I&rsquo;ve heahed say. Wal, I&rsquo;m durned if I want the jasper slingin&rsquo; one at me.&rdquo; Jim went back to rouse the three prisoners. &ldquo;Roll out, you buckaroos, and help get breakfast.&rdquo; Matty lumbered up cheerfully, and the others followed with an alacrity that presupposed a desire to please. Probably they did not hold themselves guilty of the deal hatched by the Haverlys and Jocelyn. &ldquo;Say, you Cibeque duffers,&rdquo; said Curly, facetiously, &ldquo;I fell asleep purpose last night so you could sneak off.&rdquo; &ldquo;Prentiss, I&rsquo;d hate to of tried it. But all we own in the world is this pack outfit,&rdquo; explained Matty. &ldquo;Wal, thet ain&rsquo;t a hell uv a lot.&rdquo; While they ate breakfast the rain ceased and blue sky showed overhead, soon to be obscured, however, by fast scudding clouds out of the southwest. Molly slept on, and even the bells on the Diamond pack-horses ringing down the slope did not wake her. Jim did not recall ever being so glad to see cowboys as then. &ldquo;The whole night long!&rdquo; sang out Bud. &ldquo;The whole night long!&rdquo; &ldquo;Say, Bud, if it was long for you, what do you suppose it was for Molly and me?&rdquo; retorted Jim. &ldquo;I see you haven&rsquo;t covered Stinger&rsquo;s face, so he mustn&rsquo;t hev croaked yet,&rdquo; replied Bud, practically. &ldquo;Howdy, boys! Sure glad to see you,&rdquo; said Jim, running his eye over the group. &ldquo;Where&rsquo;s Hump and Uphill?&rdquo; &ldquo;Don&rsquo;t know. Cherry will tell you what he thinks, an&rsquo; it ain&rsquo;t no cheerful news,&rdquo; announced Bud. &ldquo;We left some uv the hosses an&rsquo; packs. Jeff is comin&rsquo; somewhere back. He shore hates a saddle.&rdquo; &ldquo;What&rsquo;s up, Cherry?&rdquo; queried Jim, approaching Winters, who was dismounting. &ldquo;Boss, I reckon Stevens an&rsquo; Frost hev struck somethin&rsquo; out at the head of Derrick. We left a note fer them to hit our trail. An&rsquo; I reckon they&rsquo;ll be along, mebbe tonight.&rdquo; Jim did not see in Cherry&rsquo;s words much to be worried about. But still less did he like Cherry&rsquo;s expression than the hint in Bud&rsquo;s remark. &ldquo;Well, unpack and make camp,&rdquo; he ordered. &ldquo;If Dunn pulls through we&rsquo;ll have to stay here for days. Put up the little tent for Molly, if you fetched it. But first thing after you unpack bury these dead men.&rdquo; One by one, Bud leading, the cowboys went up to have a look at Slinger and Molly. Strange to say, no remarks were forthcoming, which was probably owing to solicitude for the girl. Then, after the fashion of cowboys, they set to work. Jeff Davis rode down into camp, and added his efficient hands to the task. The rain held off till early in the afternoon, when it began to fall in earnest. Molly slept on through it all, dry and snug under shelter and blanket. Slinger showed no change. The three Cibeque men had been unceremoniously moved out of the cabin, which the cowboys had thoroughly cleaned. Jim, after a short stay beside Molly and her brother, watchful to see any change in the latter, went inside the cabin to wait. There was nothing much to do now, but wait. Bud appeared to be industriously working over something. It was a section of aspen tree, split in half, on the white surface of which he had carved some words. Jim bent over Curly, who was intent on the artist. Hackamore Jocelyn, N. G. Drift Fence Cutter Died with his boots on, Sept. 1889. &ldquo;I clean forgit the date,&rdquo; said Bud, viewing his work with satisfaction. &ldquo;Reckon it&rsquo;s aboot the twenty-third. Towards the end of September, anyhow. An&rsquo; what&rsquo;s the date amount to? Jocelyn should never hev bin borned. All we aim to celebrate with this heah tombstone is thet he&rsquo;s damn good an&rsquo; daid,&rdquo; replied Curly. The other cowboys noted Bud&rsquo;s work with quite different points of view. &ldquo;Bud, anyone could see you onct worked in a graveyard,&rdquo; said Cherry. &ldquo;What&rsquo;s the idee&mdash;cuttin&rsquo; out a haidpiece fer thet greaser?&rdquo; inquired Lonestar, scornfully. &ldquo;There&rsquo;d be sum sense in it if we&rsquo;d buried Slinger.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. But it&rsquo;s good practice in case Slinger croaks,&rdquo; observed Bud. &ldquo;Hev I gotta tell you fellars any more thet Slinger will live?&rdquo; queried Curly, annoyed that his judgment was questioned. &ldquo;He might, Curly. Life is darn oncertain. I&rsquo;ll bet you Slinger croaks,&rdquo; returned Bud, much animated at the prospect of a wager. &ldquo;Aw hell!&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, then, I&rsquo;ll bet you he croaks before Doc Shields gits hyar,&rdquo; went on Bud. &ldquo;You&rsquo;d gamble on your grandma&rsquo;s coffin,&rdquo; asserted Curly, in supreme disgust. &ldquo;Funniest thing, too. You always lose.&rdquo; &ldquo;Like hob I do! Right this minnit your spurs an&rsquo; chaps belong to me. You&rsquo;d be naked &rsquo;most but fer my magnamity.&rdquo; &ldquo;Bud, it shore ain&rsquo;t no wonder no gurl will stick to you,&rdquo; replied Curly. &ldquo;Wot&rsquo;s a gurl got to do with games uv chance? I&rsquo;m a born better, as you know to your cost.&rdquo; &ldquo;But you&rsquo;ll bet on anythin&rsquo; in Gawd&rsquo;s world,&rdquo; protested Curly. &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s the secret. How much did I clean up when the boss licked you&mdash;an&rsquo; Cherry an&rsquo; Up an&rsquo; Lonestar? Huh! I got it all down, an&rsquo; if we live to reach Flag ag&rsquo;in, I&rsquo;ll be rich.&rdquo; &ldquo;Shore. An&rsquo; you&rsquo;ll be drunk, too.&rdquo; &ldquo;Curly, you make me orful tired. If I wasn&rsquo;t so all-fired fond uv you&mdash;fer no reason thet any human bein&rsquo; could see&mdash;I&rsquo;d be riled at your insults.... Now listen. Money talks. Put up or shet up. Hyar&rsquo;s my roll.&rdquo; Bud exhibited a roll of greenbacks that made Curly&rsquo;s eyes stick out and likewise excited Lonestar and Cherry. &ldquo;Where&rsquo;n hell&rsquo;d you git thet?&rdquo; Curly&rsquo;s face was saved, and the risk of his money, too, by the arrival of Jack Way and Dr. Shields. The latter was a little man, lost in a heavy slicker. From the exchange of greetings he was well acquainted with several of the Diamond. He got off, and out of the slicker, then removed a small medicine-satchel and a parcel from the saddle-bag. &ldquo;Where&rsquo;s your man?&rdquo; he queried, after being introduced to Jim. &ldquo;Fetch clean hot water.&rdquo; Jim did not think it necessary to awaken Molly. The job would be gruesome and she would insist on helping. Dr. Shields felt of Dunn&rsquo;s pulse and heart, and said, &ldquo;Hum!&rdquo; which meant little to Jim. Then he cut open Dunn&rsquo;s blood-caked garments, and first removed the bandages from the wound in the abdomen. At sight of this he shook his head. When he saw that the bullet had gone clear through he nodded his head. So Jim inferred it was both bad and good. Cherry fetched a pan of hot water. Whereupon the doctor went to work, and with Jim&rsquo;s assistance, in an hour had all six wounds dressed. To Jim&rsquo;s amaze Slinger opened his eyes. No doubt all the while he had been conscious. &ldquo;You ought to be a hoss-doctor,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Hurtin&rsquo; me wuss&rsquo;n the bullets.... Am I goin&rsquo; to cash?&rdquo; &ldquo;Dunn, that shot in your belly ought to have killed you long ago,&rdquo; replied the doctor. &ldquo;The one in your hip is bad, but not critical. The others just gun-shots.&rdquo; &ldquo;Did you fetch any whisky?&rdquo; Shields made haste to supply this evident pressing need. &ldquo;Jim,&rdquo; said Slinger, &ldquo;I knowed you gave me some whisky of Jocelyn&rsquo;s. But I shore didn&rsquo;t want to live on thet.&rdquo; Suddenly Molly sat up, wide-eyed and bewildered. &ldquo;Hullo, wood-mouse! You&rsquo;ve shore slept a lot,&rdquo; said Dunn. &ldquo;Oh, Arch!&mdash;Jim!&rdquo; &ldquo;Molly, this is Dr. Shields from Flag. He has just attended to your brother&rsquo;s wounds, and&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; But Jim could not reply to Molly&rsquo;s mutely questioning eyes. And Dr. Shields, blunt as he was, shrank from their wonderful eager look. &ldquo;Molly, I&rsquo;m further from bein&rsquo; daid than last night,&rdquo; spoke up Slinger for himself. The rain dripped through the roof of the old cabin, in some places in little streams, one of which happened to go down Bud Chalfack&rsquo;s neck. It had the effect of a lighted match dropped in gunpowder. &ldquo;Bud, why don&rsquo;t you git used to water?&rdquo; complained Curly, when the explosion had subsided. Molly had dinner in the cabin and she was the object of much solicitude. She went back to her shelter, claiming it dry, at least. The three Cibeque men became restless as the afternoon grew late. Boyd Flick did not return. All the cowboys save Bud passed the time playing cards. Bud had evidently become enamoured of his carving ability, for he spent hours over the other half of the aspen log. When he exhibited his latest bit of sculptural genius the cowboys were spellbound. Bud had hewn out a masterpiece of a wooden headpiece for a grave. And he had engraved upon it: &ldquo;Curly Prentiss. Died &mdash;&mdash;. Unmarried.&rdquo; This he presented to Curly, before the bursting cowboys. &ldquo;You bow-legged, kangaroo-rat!&rdquo; declared Curly, who could not see the joke. &ldquo;I could of married a hundred gurls.&rdquo; And so the day passed, every minute of it with less strain for Jim. He was attentive to Molly, who began another vigil over her brother. The rain roared on the canvas, but Molly was comfortable. &ldquo;Traft, you look fagged out,&rdquo; said Dr. Shields, when night fell. &ldquo;Go to bed. I&rsquo;ll keep tab on Dunn and wake you if there&rsquo;s any turn for the worse. He simply amazes me. Jim slipped under Molly&rsquo;s shelter to encourage her and say good night. In the dark he was feeling round for her when she tugged at him. &ldquo;&rsquo;Sssh! He&rsquo;s asleep,&rdquo; she whispered. &ldquo;Molly, you&rsquo;ll not sit up all night again? Dr. Shields said he&rsquo;d watch.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll lay down when I get sleepy,&rdquo; she said. &ldquo;Jim, I&rsquo;m beginnin&rsquo; to hope.&rdquo; &ldquo;So am I,&rdquo; replied Jim, and put his arms around her. Molly gave a start and then slowly relaxed. Jim waited to see what she would do. After a long moment she stirred, and in the dim light of the smoldering camp fire he saw her head come up. Then shyly and sweetly, but surely, she returned his embrace, and just brushed his cheek with her lips. Thrilled and utterly grateful and content, he bade her good night. He sought his own bed in the stall behind which Seth Haverly had met death. Once stretched out, Jim discovered how weary he actually was, and that was the extent of his conscious mental activity for this trying day. He slept till the ring of ax and stirring of men roused him. It was a dark, wet, gray dawn, but evidently the rain had ceased. While pulling on his boots, he heard Curly speak: &ldquo;Wal, Doc, how&rsquo;s your patient this mawnin&rsquo;?&rdquo; &ldquo;I give up, Prentiss. You cowboys are not made of flesh and blood. Dunn just asked for a cigarette,&rdquo; replied the doctor. Jim went to the fire in a hurry. &ldquo;Then he must be&mdash;be&mdash;&rdquo; he faltered, just overcome with hope for Molly. &ldquo;Traft, I reckon he&rsquo;ll pull through. I&rsquo;ll look him over after breakfast. Then you can send a man with me. I left my horses tied in a corral over here where the road ends. Guess they&rsquo;ll be all right, but I&rsquo;d like to hurry.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, it shore is good,&rdquo; said Curly. &ldquo;My hunch was all right. But I was scared stiff fer the little gurl.&rdquo; Jim felt too grateful for expression and had to restrain himself from rushing in to Molly. At the breakfast call she appeared, pale, yet somehow glowing, and to Jim&rsquo;s delight she chose a seat beside him. The morning showed a prospect of clearing weather. A bit of sunlight tipped the spear-pointed spruces on the ridge. Jays and squirrels were noisily in attendance upon the cabin. Dr. Shields seemed vastly relieved, and not a little glad. &ldquo;Miss Molly, you can rest easy,&rdquo; he said. &ldquo;Your brother will live.... Traft, I&rsquo;m leaving only a few instructions. Give Dunn a little whisky and water now and then, for a couple of days. If fever sets in send for me. But there&rsquo;s no sign of that or other complications. His wounds have closed clean in this forest air. Then begin to feed him nourishing soups, beef tea, and presently light food. But don&rsquo;t overdo. In about ten days make a canvas litter and have four men pack him to the road, where you can safely haul him in a wagon to Flag.&rdquo; When the genial doctor had said good-by to all and ridden away with his guide, Bud ejaculated plaintively: &ldquo;Nourishin&rsquo; soup! Beef tea! I need somethin&rsquo; like thet myself.&rdquo; &ldquo;My Gawd!&rdquo; added Curly, his loyal champion, at times. &ldquo;So do I. This heah fence-buildin&rsquo; has run me down to skin an&rsquo; bones.&rdquo; &ldquo;I&rsquo;ll bet a two-dollar bill thet if I got shot up our deaf-an&rsquo; dumb cook would feed me salt mackerel,&rdquo; ended Bud, in disgust. Long since Jim had learned this apparent discontent and garrulousness were just their perennial spirit of fun, but he still found occasion to pretend he did not understand them. &ldquo;Curly, you and Bud are so darn full of energy&mdash;suppose you split some shingles and repair the cabin roof,&rdquo; he suggested, dryly. &ldquo;Boss, it ain&rsquo;t a-goin&rsquo; to rain no more,&rdquo; replied Bud. &ldquo;I think it might. It&rsquo;s clouding up just like Curly&rsquo;s face now.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, I&rsquo;m as weak as a sick cat,&rdquo; protested Curly. &ldquo;You&rsquo;ll feel better at work. That&rsquo;s what you boys need. Good hard work.&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, fer cripe&rsquo;s sake!&rdquo; exploded Bud. &ldquo;Come on, Bud,&rdquo; said Curly, with resignation. &ldquo;No turkey-huntin&rsquo; this heah mawnin&rsquo;. We gotta work while our Mizzourie boss looks at his gurl like a dyin&rsquo; duck in a thunderstorm.&rdquo; They hailed Cherry on the way out, &ldquo;You&rsquo;re helpin&rsquo; us split shingles.&rdquo; &ldquo;Who said so?&rdquo; demanded that worthy, affronted. &ldquo;We said so. Orders from the boss.&rdquo; Jim saw Molly at meal-times. She had grown shyer as the strain of anxiety lessened. And the cowboys, now that her cause for distress had been removed, began to pay pretty compliments and make sly little speeches and eye her with flirtatious longing. She seemed even shyer with Jim. But he was happy, and when night fell, with the rain teeming down, he could not resist a moment alone with her under the canvas. Slinger, however, was awake, evidently suffering, and cursing Dr. Shields about the limited nips of whisky. Jim could only squeeze Molly&rsquo;s hand and say good night. It stormed that night, a regular equinoctial gale. The old cabin groaned and threatened to fly away on the winds of the wind. Jim thought there really was risk, for the rafters were heavy. Nevertheless, like the cowboys, he took the risk. It was wonderful to lie snug and warm, however, and listen to the crash and roar of the gale through the forest, and the intermittent and varying spells of rain on the roof. Now and then a faint flash of lightning showed the lashing pines, and dull thunder reverberated across the heavens. Toward morning the fury of the storm abated and it turned cold. When daylight came the sky was fast clearing, the air had a keen cold edge, and the color of the forest had perceptibly changed. There were hints of red in the maples, brown in the oaks, and the aspens showed a splash of gold. The willow leaves were gone. Autumn had come to this low part of the Diamond, and he ventured a guess that winter had laid a white mantle on the high promontories. This caused Jim concern, for he had hoped to get the drift fence finished before the snow fell. He went outside to wash. The morning was beautiful, with rose and gold clouds skimming the tree tops, and the sky a deep blue. The brook was a rushing torrent of amber water, full of floating aspen leaves, and it was cold enough to make his fingers ache. He was wiping his face with his scarf when Curly and Bud appeared, each with a rifle and a fine turkey gobbler. &ldquo;Mawnin&rsquo; boss, did you hev enough sleep?&rdquo; drawled Curly. &ldquo;Boss, you ain&rsquo;t risin&rsquo; with the larks no more,&rdquo; added Bud, with pretended solicitude. &ldquo;I hope you&rsquo;ve no wuss ailment than&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; Jim swore mildly at them. &ldquo;You went wild-turkey-hunting without taking me! It&rsquo;s a low-down trick. When I&rsquo;ve been waiting and longing to go.... I&rsquo;ll get even with you.&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, I&rsquo;m downright sorry,&rdquo; replied Curly, with profound regret. &ldquo;But it was dark an&rsquo; cold, an&rsquo; Bud says&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; &ldquo;Aw, don&rsquo;t lie it on to me,&rdquo; interrupted Bud, scornfully. &ldquo;You know you said, &lsquo;It&rsquo;ll jest aboot make the boss weep if we waddle in with a couple of turks.&rsquo;&rdquo; &ldquo;It just has done that,&rdquo; said Jim. &ldquo;Wal, wait till Jeff roasts these young birds!&rdquo; added Curly. &ldquo;Boy, yore mouth&rsquo;ll water so you&rsquo;ll swear you&rsquo;ve got dropsy.&rdquo; &ldquo;Ahuh. Do I get to go along next time? Of course there&rsquo;s a lot of odd jobs around camp&mdash;&mdash;&rdquo; Vociferously and in unison Curly and Bud gave remarkable evidence of their regret and how they would make amends that very day. It turned out that Jim would not leave Molly. Her brother was in agony most of the time, with fever threatening, and not until late did he grow easier. They were all concerned about Slinger, except Curly, who said: &ldquo;Wal, the third day is the wust. From now on he&rsquo;ll mend fast.&rdquo; Next morning, however, Jim routed Curly and Bud out so early that they could not see even a tree for half an hour. &ldquo;Boss, wild turkeys is wild, shore, but they ain&rsquo;t owls,&rdquo; complained Bud. And Curly, loyal as always, vowed that Jim was not going to be permitted to shoot a turkey while still on the roost. &ldquo;Thet&rsquo;s pot-huntin&rsquo;, boss, an&rsquo; not sporty atall.&rdquo; &ldquo;After eating that turkey drumstick last night, I&rsquo;d perpetrate any kind of a dark deed to get another,&rdquo; averred Jim. &ldquo;Didn&rsquo;t I tell you? Wal, now don&rsquo;t talk so much, an&rsquo; mebbe, if it ever gits daytime, we&rsquo;ll see a flock,&rdquo; rejoined Curly. Jim, therefore, kept his exuberant spirits under restraint. He noticed, however, that Bud and Curly talked right on as usual, and after a few moments he dryly mentioned the fact. &ldquo;Wal, you see, these heah turkeys air used to us cowboys ridin&rsquo; around in the woods,&rdquo; explained Curly. &ldquo;They know our voices.&rdquo; Daylight came, with rosy color and nipping air, with frost like diamonds on the gramma grass, and so quiet that a falling aspen leaf rustled loudly. Evidently they had come up to the head of the park, for they crossed a brook and passed a tumble-down log cabin, where Hack Jocelyn had elected to hide with Molly and wait for the ransom. Curly remarked about how the plans of crooked men went wrong more often than right. &ldquo;Flick ought to ride in today,&rdquo; he added. &ldquo;He might be cute enough to savvy the way the deal has turned out an&rsquo; skedaddle with the money.&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t believe&mdash;&rdquo; began Jim, when he got a prod from Bud. &ldquo;Hist!&rdquo; he whispered. &ldquo;Shet up, you elocuteners! Turkey!&rdquo; Jim heard the well-known gobble of a turkey, and it certainly resembled that of a tame turkey at home. Bud led the way over a rise of ground, under green and gold aspens, into an open glade, wildly closed at its upper end by a confusion of maples, oak, and pines. There were logs on the ground, and one huge pine across them. Something crashed ahead through the brush, snapping twigs. Then came a rap of bone on wood. &ldquo;Where? ... That thing? It&rsquo;s a stump.&rdquo; &ldquo;Take a chanct, boss,&rdquo; said Curly, with a chuckle. &ldquo;If it&rsquo;s a stump it ain&rsquo;t a-goin&rsquo; to run. An&rsquo; if it&rsquo;s a turkey, keep on shootin&rsquo;.&rdquo; &ldquo;Aim low an&rsquo; be quick,&rdquo; added Bud. &ldquo;But I tell you that thing is a black stump,&rdquo; remonstrated Jim, always and forever looking for tricks from these boys. &ldquo;Honest to Gawd, boss, it&rsquo;s a turk,&rdquo; appealed Bud. &ldquo;Stump, huh? Why didn&rsquo;t you peg him while he was runnin&rsquo;?&rdquo; queried Bud. &ldquo;Gosh! I forgot I had a gun. What a sight! Say, that wasn&rsquo;t a turkey, but an ostrich!&rdquo; &ldquo;Wal, it was shore a rotten shot,&rdquo; said Curly. &ldquo;But he didn&rsquo;t scare the flock.... See them scratchin&rsquo; over heah&mdash;under the jackpines?&rdquo; Jim did see them and grew wildly excited. It was an enormous flock, some of which exhibited signs of nervousness. &ldquo;Duck now, an&rsquo; crawl after me,&rdquo; whispered Bud, getting down on his knees. &ldquo;They&rsquo;ve seen or heerd us, but we&rsquo;ll get a shot if we&rsquo;re smart.... Now, boss, don&rsquo;t crawl like a elephant with a broken laig.&rdquo; Jim did not see anything but grass and leaves, the ground, logs, and tree trunks for several strenuous moments. But he heard turkeys gobble, and then, close at hand: put-put, put-put-put. &ldquo;Damn!&rdquo; swore Bud. &ldquo;It&rsquo;s always the hens thet bust up everythin&rsquo;.... O Lord! hyar they come.... Now stand up an&rsquo; shoot.&rdquo; Jim got to his feet and certain was it that he shook. The great black and bronze gobblers, the sleek, smaller, less conspicuous hens, were coming under the scrub pines, out into the open. They no longer resembled tame turkeys. The huge-breasted gobblers, with their long beards, their stately nodding walk, suddenly halted. Jim picked out one that looked as high as a church and pulled the trigger. &ldquo;Dog-gone!&rdquo; gasped Curly, as soon as he could speak. &ldquo;They shore stampeded us. Boss, aboot how many did you knock?&rdquo; &ldquo;Gosh! I never touched a feather, that I could see. Oh, it was wonderful! ... But why in the dickens didn&rsquo;t you prepare me for such a charge?&rdquo; &ldquo;Boss, I know turkeys orful well, but I didn&rsquo;t hev this bunch figgered,&rdquo; said Bud. &ldquo;Didn&rsquo;t you plug one?&rdquo; Jim shook his head sorrowfully. &ldquo;I&rsquo;m as bum a hunter&mdash;as&mdash;as everything else.&rdquo; &ldquo;How many times did you shoot?&rdquo; &ldquo;I don&rsquo;t know.... The gun&rsquo;s empty.&rdquo; &ldquo;Gee whiz! So &rsquo;tis. It had ten shells in the magazine, one in the barrel. Countin&rsquo; thet stump thet wus a turkey, you&rsquo;ve shot eleven times.&rdquo; &ldquo;Good Lord! I must have.
